<p>Chapter VII 371</p>
<p><a href="#pf17d"></a></p>
<p>PREFACE</p>
<p>THE event on which this ﬁction is founded has been</p>
<p>supposed, by Dr. Darwin, and some of the physio-</p>
<p>logical writers of Germany, as not of impossible oc-</p>
<p>currence. I shall not be supposed as according the</p>
<p>remotest degree of serious faith to such an imagi-</p>
<p>nation; yet, in assuming it as the basis of a work of</p>
<p>fancy, I have not considered myself as merely weav-</p>
<p>ing a series of supernatural terrors. The event on</p>
<p>which the interest of the story depends is exempt</p>
<p>from the disadvantages of a mere tale of spectres or</p>
<p>enchantment. It was recommended by the novelty</p>
<p>of the situations which it developes; and, however</p>
<p>vi</p>
<p>impossible as a physical fact, affords a point of view</p>
<p>to the imagination for the delineating of human pas-</p>
<p>sions more comprehensive and commanding than</p>
<p>any which the ordinary relations of existing events</p>
<p>can yield.</p>
<p>I have thus endeavoured to preserve the truth</p>
<p>of the elementary principles of human nature, while</p>
<p>I have not scrupled to innovate upon their com-</p>
<p>binations. The Iliad, the tragic poetry of Greece,</p>
<p>— Shakespeare, in the Tempest and Midsummer</p>
<p>Night’s Dream, — and most especially Milton, in</p>
<p>Paradise Lost, conform to this rule; and the most</p>
<p>humble novelist, who seeks to confer or receive</p>
<p>amusement from his labours, may, without pre-</p>
<p>sumption, apply to prose ﬁction a licence, or rather</p>
<p>a rule, from the adoption of which so many exquisite</p>
<p>combinations of human feeling have resulted in the</p>
<p>highest specimens of poetry.</p>
<p>The circumstance on which my story rests was</p>
<p>suggested in casual conversation. It was com-</p>
<p>menced, partly as a source of amusement, and</p>
<p>partly as an expedient for exercising any untried re-</p>
<p>sources of mind. Other motives were mingled with</p>
<p>these, as the work proceeded. I am by no means</p>
<p>indifferent to the manner in which whatever moral</p>
<p>tendencies exist in the sentiments or characters it</p>
<p>contains shall affect the reader; yet my chief con-</p>
<p>cern in this respect has been limited to the avoiding</p>
<p>of the enervating effects of the novels of the present</p>
<p>day, and to the exhibition of the amiableness of do-</p>
<p>mestic affection, and the excellence of universal</p>
<p>virtue. The opinions which naturally spring from</p>
<p>the character and situation of the hero are by no</p>
<p>means to be conceived as existing always in my</p>
<p>own conviction; nor is any inference justly to be</p>
<p>drawn from the following pages as prejudicing any</p>
<p>philosophical doctrine of whatever kind.</p>
<p>It is a subject also of additional interest to the</p>
<p>author, that this story was begun in the majestic</p>
<p>region where the scene is principally laid, and in so-</p>
<p>ciety which cannot cease to be regretted. I passed</p>
<p>the summer of 1816 in the environs of Geneva. The</p>
<p>season was cold and rainy, and in the evenings</p>
<p>we crowded around a blazing wood ﬁre, and oc-</p>
<p>casionally amused ourselves with some German</p>
<p>stories of ghosts, which happened to fall into our</p>
<p>hands. These tales excited in us a playful desire of</p>
<p>imitation. Two other friends (a tale from the pen of</p>
<p>one of whom would be far more acceptable to the</p>
<p>public than any thing I can ever hope to produce)</p>
<p>and myself agreed to write each a story, founded</p>
<p>on some supernatural occurrence.</p>
<p>The weather, however, suddenly became ser-</p>
<p>ene; and my two friends left me on a journey among</p>
<p>the Alps, and lost, in the magniﬁcent scenes which</p>
<p>they present, all memory of their ghostly visions.</p>
<p>The following tale is the only one which has been</p>
<p>completed.</p>
<p>Volume I</p>
<p>1</p>
<p>Letter I</p>
<p>To Mrs. SAVILLE, England.</p>
<p>St. Petersburgh, Dec. 11th, 17—.</p>
<p>YOU will rejoice to hear that no disaster has accom-</p>
<p>panied the commencement of an enterprise which</p>
<p>you have regarded with such evil forebodings. I</p>
<p>arrived here yesterday; and my ﬁrst task is to as-</p>
<p>sure my dear sister of my welfare, and increasing</p>
<p>conﬁdence in the success of my undertaking.</p>
<p>I am already far north of London; and as I walk</p>
<p>in the streets of Petersburgh, I feel a cold north-</p>
<p>ern breeze play upon my cheeks, which braces my</p>
<p>2</p>
<p>nerves, and ﬁlls me with delight. Do you understand</p>
<p>this feeling? This breeze, which has travelled from</p>
<p>the regions towards which I am advancing, gives</p>
<p>me a foretaste of those icy climes. Inspirited by</p>
<p>this wind of promise, my day dreams become more</p>
<p>fervent and vivid. I try in vain to be persuaded that</p>
<p>the pole is the seat of frost and desolation; it ever</p>
<p>presents itself to my imagination as the region of</p>
<p>beauty and delight. There, Margaret, the sun is for</p>
<p>ever visible; its broad disk just skirting the horizon,</p>
<p>and diffusing a perpetual splendour. There — for</p>
<p>with your leave, my sister, I will put some trust in</p>
<p>preceding navigators — there snow and frost are</p>
<p>banished; and, sailing over a calm sea, we may</p>
<p>be wafted to a land surpassing in wonders and in</p>
<p>beauty every region hitherto discovered on the hab-</p>
<p>itable globe. Its productions and features may be</p>
<p>without example, as the phænomena of the heav-</p>
<p>enly bodies undoubtedly are in those undiscovered</p>
<p>solitudes. What may not be expected in a country</p>
<p>of eternal light? I may there discover the wondrous</p>
<p>power which attracts the needle; and may regulate</p>
<p>a thousand celestial observations, that require only</p>
<p>this voyage to render their seeming eccentricities</p>
<p>consistent for ever. I shall satiate my ardent cu-</p>
<p>riosity with the sight of a part of the world never</p>
<p>before visited, and may tread a land never before</p>
<p>imprinted by the foot of man. These are my entice-</p>
<p>ments, and they are sufﬁcient to conquer all fear of</p>
<p>danger or death, and to induce me to commence</p>
<p>this laborious voyage with the joy a child feels when</p>
<p>he embarks in a little boat, with his holiday mates,</p>
<p>on an expedition of discovery up his native river.</p>
<p>But, supposing all these conjectures to be false,</p>
<p>you cannot contest the inestimable beneﬁt which I</p>
<p>shall confer on all mankind to the last generation, by</p>
<p>discovering a passage near the pole to those coun-</p>
<p>tries, to reach which at present so many months are</p>
<p>requisite; or by ascertaining the secret of the mag-</p>
<p>net, which, if at all possible, can only be effected by</p>
<p>an undertaking such as mine.</p>
<p>These reﬂections have dispelled the agitation</p>
<p>with which I began my letter, and I feel my heart</p>
<p>glow with an enthusiasm which elevates me to</p>
<p>heaven; for nothing contributes so much to tran-</p>
<p>quillize the mind as a steady purpose, — a point on</p>
<p>which the soul may ﬁx its intellectual eye. This</p>
<p>expedition has been the favourite dream of my</p>
<p>early years. I have read with ardour the accounts</p>
<p>of the various voyages which have been made in</p>
<p>the prospect of arriving at the North Paciﬁc Ocean</p>
<p>through the seas which surround the pole. You may</p>
<p>remember, that a history of all the voyages made</p>
<p>for purposes of discovery composed the whole of</p>
<p>our good uncle Thomas’s library. My education was</p>
<p>neglected, yet I was passionately fond of reading.</p>
<p>These volumes were my study day and night, and</p>
<p>my familiarity with them increased that regret which</p>
<p>I had felt, as a child, on learning that my father’s</p>
<p>dying injunction had forbidden my uncle to allow me</p>
<p>to embark in a sea-faring life.</p>
<p>These visions faded when I perused, for the</p>
<p>ﬁrst time, those poets whose effusions entranced</p>
<p>my soul, and lifted it to heaven. I also became a</p>
<p>poet, and for one year lived in a Paradise of my</p>
<p>own creation; I imagined that I also might obtain</p>
<p>a niche in the temple where the names of Homer</p>
<p>and Shakespeare are consecrated. You are well</p>
<p>acquainted with my failure, and how heavily I bore</p>
<p>the disappointment. But just at that time I inherited</p>
<p>the fortune of my cousin, and my thoughts were</p>
<p>turned into the channel of their earlier bent.</p>
<p>Six years have passed since I resolved on my</p>
<p>present undertaking. I can, even now, remember</p>
<p>the hour from which I dedicated myself to this great</p>
<p>enterprise. I commenced by inuring my body to</p>
<p>hardship. I accompanied the whale-ﬁshers on sev-</p>
<p>eral expeditions to the North Sea; I voluntarily en-</p>
<p>dured cold, famine, thirst, and want of sleep; I of-</p>
<p>ten worked harder than the common sailors dur-</p>
<p>ing the day, and devoted my nights to the study</p>
<p>of mathematics, the theory of medicine, and those</p>
<p>branches of physical science from which a naval</p>
<p>adventurer might derive the greatest practical ad-</p>
<p>vantage. Twice I actually hired myself as an under-</p>
<p>mate in a Greenland whaler, and acquitted myself</p>
<p>to admiration. I must own I felt a little proud, when</p>
<p>my captain offered me the second dignity in the</p>
<p>vessel, and entreated me to remain with the great-</p>
<p>est earnestness; so valuable did he consider my</p>
<p>services.</p>
<p>And now, dear Margaret, do I not deserve to ac-</p>
<p>complish some great purpose. My life might have</p>
<p>been passed in ease and luxury; but I preferred</p>
<p>glory to every enticement that wealth placed in my</p>
<p>path. Oh, that some encouraging voice would an-</p>
<p>swer in the afﬁrmative! My courage and my resolu-</p>
<p>tion is ﬁrm; but my hopes ﬂuctuate, and my spirits</p>
<p>are often depressed. I am about to proceed on a</p>
<p>long and difﬁcult voyage; the emergencies of which</p>
<p>will demand all my fortitude: I am required not only</p>
<p>to raise the spirits of others, but sometimes to sus-</p>
<p>tain my own, when their’s are failing.</p>
<p>This is the most favourable period for travelling</p>
<p>in Russia. They ﬂy quickly over the snow in their</p>
<p>sledges; the motion is pleasant, and, in my opinion,</p>
<p>far more agreeable than that of an English stage-</p>
<p>coach. The cold is not excessive, if you are wrapt in</p>
<p>furs, a dress which I have already adopted; for there</p>
<p>is a great difference between walking the deck and</p>
<p>remaining seated motionless for hours, when no</p>
<p>exercise prevents the blood from actually freezing in</p>
<p>your veins. I have no ambition to lose my life on the</p>
<p>post-road between St. Petersburgh and Archangel.</p>
<p>I shall depart for the latter town in a fortnight or</p>
<p>three weeks; and my intention is to hire a ship there,</p>
<p>which can easily be done by paying the insurance</p>
<p>for the owner, and to engage as many sailors as I</p>
<p>think necessary among those who are accustomed</p>
<p>to the whale-ﬁshing. I do not intend to sail until</p>
<p>the month of June: and when shall I return? Ah,</p>
<p>dear sister, how can I answer this question? If I</p>
<p>succeed, many, many months, perhaps years, will</p>
<p>pass before you and I may meet. If I fail, you will</p>
<p>see me again soon, or never.</p>
<p>Farewell, my dear, excellent, Margaret. Heaven</p>
<p>shower down blessings on you, and save me, that I</p>
<p>may again and again testify my gratitude for all your</p>
<p>love and kindness.</p>
<p>Your affectionate brother,</p>
<p>R. WALTON.</p>
<p>Letter II</p>
<p>To Mrs. SAVILLE, England.</p>
<p>Archangel, 28th March, 17—.</p>
<p>HOW slowly the time passes here, encompassed</p>
<p>as I am by frost and snow; yet a second step is</p>
<p>taken towards my enterprise. I have hired a vessel,</p>
<p>and am occupied in collecting my sailors; those</p>
<p>whom I have already engaged appear to be men on</p>
<p>whom I can depend, and are certainly possessed</p>
<p>of dauntless courage.</p>
<p>But I have one want which I have never yet</p>
<p>been able to satisfy; and the absence of the object</p>
<p>9</p>
<p>of which I now feel as a most severe evil. I have no</p>
<p>friend, Margaret: when I am glowing with the enthu-</p>
<p>siasm of success, there will be none to participate</p>
<p>my joy; if I am assailed by disappointment, no one</p>
<p>will endeavour to sustain me in dejection. I shall</p>
<p>commit my thoughts to paper, it is true; but that is a</p>
<p>poor medium for the communication of feeling. I de-</p>
<p>sire the company of a man who could sympathize</p>
<p>with me; whose eyes would reply to mine. You may</p>
<p>deem me romantic, my dear sister, but I bitterly feel</p>
<p>the want of a friend. I have no one near me, gentle</p>
<p>yet courageous, possessed of a cultivated as well</p>
<p>as of a capacious mind, whose tastes are like my</p>
<p>own, to approve or amend my plans. How would</p>
<p>such a friend repair the faults of your poor brother!</p>
<p>I am too ardent in execution, and too impatient of</p>
<p>difﬁculties. But it is a still greater evil to me that I</p>
<p>am self-educated: for the ﬁrst fourteen years of my</p>
<p>life I ran wild on a common, and read nothing but</p>
<p>our uncle Thomas’s books of voyages. At that age I</p>
<p>became acquainted with the celebrated poets of our</p>
<p>own country; but it was only when it had ceased to</p>
<p><img src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAeMAAAJiCAIAAAB7ET2PAAAACXBIWXMAABYlAAAWJQFJUiTwAAAGpUlEQVR42u3WsRHDMAwEQQiwCnCm/rskxBm4BmcMdkv44OavtVZVVVUAcJK998zcd36qKjMtAnCazJyZiLpmxhwARyfbBABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUpsAQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqEwAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASm0CAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpTQCg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AD8q7O7996WADjN+77dHfHENy53HOBkP3zoILYnBJBFAAAAAElFTkSuQmCC" alt=""></p>
<p>be in my power to derive its most important beneﬁts</p>
<p>from such a conviction, that I perceived the neces-</p>
<p>sity of becoming acquainted with more languages</p>
<p>than that of my native country. Now I am twenty-</p>
<p>eight, and am in reality more illiterate than many</p>
<p>school-boys of ﬁfteen. It is true that I have thought</p>
<p>more, and that my day dreams are more extended</p>
<p>and magniﬁcent; but they want (as the painters call</p>
<p>it) keeping; and I greatly need a friend who would</p>
<p>have sense enough not to despise me as roman-</p>
<p>tic, and affection enough for me to endeavour to</p>
<p>regulate my mind.</p>
<p>Well, these are useless complaints; I shall cer-</p>
<p>tainly ﬁnd no friend on the wide ocean, nor even</p>
<p>here in Archangel, among merchants and seamen.</p>
<p>Yet some feelings, unallied to the dross of human</p>
<p>nature, beat even in these rugged bosoms. My lieu-</p>
<p>tenant, for instance, is a man of wonderful courage</p>
<p>and enterprise; he is madly desirous of glory. He</p>
<p>is an Englishman, and in the midst of national and</p>
<p>professional prejudices, unsoftened by cultivation,</p>
<p>retains some of the noblest endowments of human-</p>
<p>ity. I ﬁrst became acquainted with him on board</p>
<p>a whale vessel: ﬁnding that he was unemployed</p>
<p>in this city, I easily engaged him to assist in my</p>
<p>enterprise.</p>
<p>The master is a person of an excellent dispo-</p>
<p>sition, and is remarkable in the ship for his gen-</p>
<p>tleness, and the mildness of his discipline. He is,</p>
<p>indeed, of so amiable a nature, that he will not hunt</p>
<p>(a favourite, and almost the only amusement here),</p>
<p>because he cannot endure to spill blood. He is,</p>
<p>moreover, heroically generous. Some years ago he</p>
<p>loved a young Russian lady, of moderate fortune;</p>
<p>and having amassed a considerable sum in prize-</p>
<p>money, the father of the girl consented to the match.</p>
<p>He saw his mistress once before the destined cere-</p>
<p>mony; but she was bathed in tears, and, throwing</p>
<p>herself at his feet, entreated him to spare her, con-</p>
<p>fessing at the same time that she loved another, but</p>
<p>that he was poor, and that her father would never</p>
<p>consent to the union. My generous friend reassured</p>
<p>the suppliant, and on being informed of the name of</p>
<p>her lover instantly abandoned his pursuit. He had</p>
<p>already bought a farm with his money, on which he</p>
<p>had designed to pass the remainder of his life; but</p>
<p>he bestowed the whole on his rival, together with</p>
<p>the remains of his prize-money to purchase stock,</p>
<p>and then himself solicited the young woman’s father</p>
<p>to consent to her marriage with her lover. But the</p>
<p>old man decidedly refused, thinking himself bound</p>
<p>in honour to my friend; who, when he found the</p>
<p>father inexorable, quitted his country, nor returned</p>
<p>until he heard that his former mistress was married</p>
<p>according to her inclinations. “What a noble fellow!”</p>
<p>you will exclaim. He is so; but then he has passed</p>
<p>all his life on board a vessel, and has scarcely an</p>
<p>idea beyond the rope and the shroud.</p>
<p>But do not suppose that, because I complain a</p>
<p>little, or because I can conceive a consolation for</p>
<p>my toils which I may never know, that I am wavering</p>
<p>in my resolutions. Those are as ﬁxed as fate; and</p>
<p>my voyage is only now delayed until the weather</p>
<p>shall permit my embarkation. The winter has been</p>
<p>dreadfully severe; but the spring promises well, and</p>
<p>it is considered as a remarkably early season; so</p>
<p>that, perhaps, I may sail sooner than I expected. I</p>
<p>shall do nothing rashly; you know me sufﬁciently</p>
<p>to conﬁde in my prudence and considerateness</p>
<p>whenever the safety of others is committed to my</p>
<p>care.</p>
<p>I cannot describe to you my sensations on the</p>
<p>near prospect of my undertaking. It is impossible to</p>
<p>communicate to you a conception of the trembling</p>
<p>sensation, half pleasurable and half fearful, with</p>
<p>which I am preparing to depart. I am going to unex-</p>
<p>plored regions, to “the land of mist and snow;” but I</p>
<p>shall kill no albatross, therefore do not be alarmed</p>
<p>for my safety.</p>
<p>Shall I meet you again, after having traversed</p>
<p>immense seas, and returned by the most southern</p>
<p>cape of Africa or America? I dare not expect such</p>
<p>success, yet I cannot bear to look on the reverse</p>
<p>of the picture. Continue to write to me by every</p>
<p>opportunity: I may receive your letters (though the</p>
<p>chance is very doubtful) on some occasions when</p>
<p>I need them most to support my spirits. I love you</p>
<p>very tenderly. Remember me with affection, should</p>
<p>you never hear from me again.</p>
<p>Your affectionate brother,</p>
<p>ROBERT WALTON.</p>

src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAeMAAAJiCAIAAAB7ET2PAAAACXBIWXMAABYlAAAWJQFJUiTwAAAJM0lEQVR42u3UT2sUZxzA8fmTzWQDDeMmMRUvxUIEi+hqsQUvhVDw6jvoy+g7Eg/Fg6f+seClKqlIkNgSaOnNZLtr0inJujszOz3MjrstWNqL5PD5XOLD7Mw88/PhG45GoziO4zgOADhNiqKoqqrVihbiOI6iyEQATpsoiqqqCoI4rKrKOABOdbKNAECpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQaiMAUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZTaCACUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApTYCAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpjQBAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQH4v8bReDwuisIkAE6bPM/H43EQvB+e+SA4/G3uypd7Fz//9MrWq28+vPjV4dzf88tXH1xJlvIg74/Kc//4x80bwYNx+sfiVu/Jt2/90fmzwa8vzyxFraLV29pN7362l774Yu/mn2d3d9KjG3tr3231bz8/cy89TI/eC4P4cDVYy5PJYhlE/e4nvacPL4fxQVWutpcHH++nDzfC+KAq15MsH64nWT7ceHN1eLIRxgfXDtPtTpxN8vpC2s6C4/Uk2x2tbLaz4Hi1vTy4tJ9up+0sOO7E2STvxNkkT5trw5O0nV1/mW6vJ1k+TNtZcFw/qBNnP5crs71c2r91J/1hfm8X7ncfp7/826y+f3Tuvw3p9t2Lb5vHs0ebxUIr2c9H9Vs7j9Lpej3JyjIt4jA5qEYbYXwQxmkRLrR+L/J6MJ2dtKiSZPA8X7k8vTyJF1+V4+md9aL+uM5Os+7EWRDWN45GaTuL8vqpL8qVzXZ2/WVnp3nn9GK9m+lts61e2r91p/Pj3zf/bkZWH6GoWsvjyeLgZr8+RFG1li+0lvrD1+tJNhmt5YtVq1fmq+3lwfh1vbo6SLc7cRaW9fKncmWzncXD5r60XsSTxUG3n25Pl2G4cJgX09uCcumoet08MyiXjq71ek9XPqrfWF9tNlPfuDG31W6/OWOzzb/bUzbJm/c252yS158/mtT/7VFeD6cK62NRlc3ses/WNosqSQbF3E8m8eKroJg9olnXn9ucuqCox7EbzD0iKJeOwsnsLfU0m+MXzf2i2eebR8x/Src/O4ezz7twv/u4+/XJvZNTl+y/APAQhMG+jVM9AAAAAElFTkSuQmCC" alt=""></p>
<p>Letter III</p>
<p>To Mrs. SAVILLE, England.</p>
<p>July 7th, 17—.</p>
<p>MY DEAR SISTER,</p>
<p>I WRITE a few lines in haste, to say that I am safe,</p>
<p>and well advanced on my voyage. This letter will</p>
<p>reach England by a merchant-man now on its home-</p>
<p>ward voyage from Archangel; more fortunate than I,</p>
<p>who may not see my native land, perhaps, for many</p>
<p>years. I am, however, in good spirits: my men are</p>
<p>bold, and apparently ﬁrm of purpose; nor do the</p>
<p>16</p>
<p><img src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAeMAAAJiCAIAAAB7ET2PAAAACXBIWXMAABYlAAAWJQFJUiTwAAAHC0lEQVR42u3UIUoEUQDG8Tfv7RZBGD2DIEZtBsOyQY1bBE8g2E0ewSNYBeNE2WRXixfwAopOWpg3sz67TTBM+P3K17/wr7quSymllAIAYzIMQyllOo2TlFKM0SMAYxNjLKWEkKpSijsARp1sFwAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASu0CAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpXQCg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKLULAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCldgGAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgPwVznmnIdh8ATA2PR9n3MO4aSa3YfH8xBCfbd39Xp2fTM73lk/H8w/L3/t02Jjsbxodpv6q2632npdhxgn7ffH/Ci8vJ+mt4ftw7B5u79cNSv/AvyjH1XcRdPyE7ZnAAAAAElFTkSuQmCC" alt=""></p>
<p>ﬂoating sheets of ice that continually pass us, indi-</p>
<p>cating the dangers of the region towards which we</p>
<p>are advancing, appear to dismay them. We have</p>
<p>already reached a very high latitude; but it is the</p>
<p>height of summer, and although not so warm as in</p>
<p>England, the southern gales, which blow us speed-</p>
<p>ily towards those shores which I so ardently desire</p>
<p>to attain, breathe a degree of renovating warmth</p>
<p>which I had not expected.</p>
<p>No incidents have hitherto befallen us, that</p>
<p>would make a ﬁgure in a letter. One or two stiff</p>
<p>gales, and the breaking of a mast, are accidents</p>
<p>which experienced navigators scarcely remember</p>
<p>to record; and I shall be well content, if nothing</p>
<p>worse happen to us during our voyage.</p>
<p>Adieu, my dear Margaret. Be assured, that for</p>
<p>my own sake, as well as your’s, I will not rashly</p>
<p>encounter danger. I will be cool, persevering, and</p>
<p>prudent.</p>
<p>Remember me to all my English friends.</p>
<p><img src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAeMAAAJiCAIAAAB7ET2PAAAACXBIWXMAABYlAAAWJQFJUiTwAAAJO0lEQVR42u3Uz28UZRzA4fnR3ek2sRm2LQ3hRkyNGCILBg9cJD3IlYuJN8/ePfkn+G+YEE4cOBlFw0UwlZCGFA2JXmnXXVrHtMvuzOx4mB22kmD0Qnp4nkt5Mzsz73x58wnH43Ecx3EcBwCcJEVRVFXVakULcRxHUWQiACdNFEVVVQVBHFZVZRwAJzrZRgCg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKLURACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKbQQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIbAYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AU <p>Most affectionately yours,</p>
<p>R. W.</p>

g2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyi1CwCUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGoC/KqmU0ve9JwCGZrValVIizkez22gvIyLu969fzq5udk52t54Opx8Pvxbbs7XZ/KLda5vPptvomq8mUhp33+/T43h+O82vj5tHsX53MF+0C/8C/KMf2Ss84+6FGNIAAAAASUVORK5CYII=" alt=""></p>
<p>Letter IV</p>
<p>To Mrs. SAVILLE, England.</p>
<p>August 5th, 17—.</p>
<p>SO strange an accident has happened to us, that</p>
<p>I cannot forbear recording it, although it is very</p>
<p>probable that you will see me before these papers</p>
<p>can come into your possession.</p>
<p>Last Monday (July 31st), we were nearly sur-</p>
<p>rounded by ice, which closed in the ship on all</p>
<p>sides, scarcely leaving her the sea room in which</p>
<p>she ﬂoated. Our situation was somewhat danger-</p>
<p>ous, especially as we were compassed round by a</p>
<p>19</p>
<p><img src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAeMAAAJiCAIAAAB7ET2PAAAACXBIWXMAABYlAAAWJQFJUiTwAAAG90lEQVR42u3UIU4DQRiG4ekMNSt7gkFiYNrU4LnAGoIlGBCEVKBJmvQknIM7YXd2kkEgqkkQK57H/MkvP/GupmlKKaWUAgBL0lrrva/X8SKlFGO0CMDSxBh77yGkVe/dHACLTrYJAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClNgGAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQmAFBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGU2gQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFKbAECpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAfirGmutrTVLACzNPM+11hCew+Pp9/P9eXV6/zjfcrz82tyfbx6Hw3DI21xyydtcrkspu30u+ek13zy87G7z3Vseh3EwLsD/+gHPcTSzwgj1OAAAAABJRU5ErkJggg==" alt=""></p>
<p>very thick fog. We accordingly lay to, hoping that</p>
<p>some change would take place in the atmosphere</p>
<p>and weather.</p>
<p>About two o’clock the mist cleared away, and we</p>
<p>beheld, stretched out in every direction, vast and</p>
<p>irregular plains of ice, which seemed to have no</p>
<p>end. Some of my comrades groaned, and my own</p>
<p>mind began to grow watchful with anxious thoughts,</p>
<p>when a strange sight suddenly attracted our at-</p>
<p>tention, and diverted our solicitude from our own</p>
<p>situation. We perceived a low carriage, ﬁxed on a</p>
<p>sledge and drawn by dogs, pass on towards the</p>
<p>north, at the distance of half a mile: a being which</p>
<p>had the shape of a man, but apparently of gigan-</p>
<p>tic stature, sat in the sledge, and guided the dogs.</p>
<p>We watched the rapid progress of the traveller with</p>
<p>our telescopes, until he was lost among the distant</p>
<p>inequalities of the ice.</p>
<p>This appearance excited our unqualiﬁed won-</p>
<p>der. We were, as we believed, many hundred miles</p>
<p>from any land; but this apparition seemed to denote</p>
<p>that it was not, in reality, so distant as we had sup-</p>
<p><img src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAeMAAAJiCAIAAAB7ET2PAAAACXBIWXMAABYlAAAWJQFJUiTwAAAHWElEQVR42u3XMWoUURzH8d+8l5UkIIZtbIdY2JkQrMXCC2yzWAg5glikjtfwMHuFQM5ikezswLPY2bhWIiIs8vlU897MvGX/xRemW6/XtdZaawA4JOM4ttZms3JUay2lmAjAoSmltNaS2rXWjAPgoJNtBABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUhsBgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDURgCg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKLURACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKbQQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg3AnxrKMAzjOJoEwKHZbDbDMCQfs3g5bb17vZovky9Jbm6T/vpiNV8mSZdkf73VJbl621/u1pf9h8/94nRxvNv4mrxI0m6n++P5L+e9H1+t5svk7PrNz3P33pk9nfO9tdZaSXL3sN26f9xef9s9c5aULjmZTu+zXdfpo+E4yel0z0cE/D9qkqPp+tne/slTaf5e95vf3/fp+T/6oz8AFaNCga1T01IAAAAASUVORK5CYII=" alt=""></p>
<p>posed. Shut in, however, by ice, it was impossible</p>
<p>to follow his track, which we had observed with the</p>
<p>greatest attention.</p>
<p>About two hours after this occurrence, we heard</p>
<p>the ground sea; and before night the ice broke, and</p>
<p>freed our ship. We, however, lay to until the morn-</p>
<p>ing, fearing to encounter in the dark those large</p>
<p>loose masses which ﬂoat about after the breaking</p>
<p>up of the ice. I proﬁted of this time to rest for a few</p>
<p>hours.</p>
<p>In the morning, however, as soon as it was light,</p>
<p>I went upon deck, and found all the sailors busy on</p>
<p>one side of the vessel, apparently talking to some</p>
<p>one in the sea. It was, in fact, a sledge, like that we</p>
<p>had seen before, which had drifted towards us in</p>
<p>the night, on a large fragment of ice. Only one dog</p>
<p>remained alive; but there was a human being within</p>
<p>it, whom the sailors were persuading to enter the</p>
<p>vessel. He was not, as the other traveller seemed</p>
<p>to be, a savage inhabitant of some undiscovered</p>
<p>island, but an European. When I appeared on deck,</p>
<p>the master said, “Here is our captain, and he will</p>

src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAeMAAAJiCAIAAAB7ET2PAAAACXBIWXMAABYlAAAWJQFJUiTwAAAGqElEQVR42u3UsRECMQwAQb3E10XtZLT0smdEDWQOdku44K7neaqqqgKAk+y9Z+a+81VVmakIwGkyc2Yi6poZOQCOXrYEAE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODeDUADg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NYBTA+DUADg1gFMD4NQAODWAUwPg1ABODYBTA+DUAE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODYBTAzg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NQBODeDUADg1gFMD4NQAODWAUwPg1AA4NYBTA+DUAE4NgFMD4NQATg2AUwM4NQBODYBTAzg1AE4NgFMDODUATg3g1AA4NQBODeDUADg1AE4N4NQAODWAUwPg1AA4NYBTA+DUAE4NgFMD4NQATg2AUwPg1ABODYBTAzg1AE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODeDUADg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NYBTA+DUADg1gFMD4NQATg2AUwPg1ABODYBTA+DUAE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODYBTAzg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NYBTSwDg1AA4NYBTA+DUADg1gFMD4NQATg2AUwPg1ABODYBTA+DUAE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODYBTAzg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NYBTA+DUADg1gFMD4NQAODWAUwPg1ABODYBTA+DUAE4NgFMD4NQATg2AUwM4NQBODYBTAzg1AE4NgFMDODUATg3g1AA4NQBODeDUADg1gFMD4NQAODWAUwPg1AA4NYBTA+DUAE4NgFMD4NQATg2AUwPg1ABODYBTAzg1AE4NgFMDODUATg3g1AA4NQBODeDUADg1AE4N4NQAODWAUwPg1AA4NYBTA+DUADg1gFMD4NQATg2AUwPg1ABODYBTA+DUAE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODeDUADg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NYBTA+DUADg1gFMD4NQAODWAUwPg1ABODYBTA+DUAE4NgFMDOLUEAE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODeDUADg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NYBTA+DUADg1gFMD4NQAODWAUwPg1ABODYBTA+DUAE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODYBTAzg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NQBODeDUADg1gFMD4NQAODWAUwPg1AA4NYBTA+DUAE4NgFMD4NQATg2AUwM4NQBODYBTAzg1AE4NgFMDODUATg3g1AA4NQBODeDUADg1AE4N4NQAODWAUwPg1AA4NYBTA+DUAE4NgFMD4NQATg2AUwPg1ABODYBTAzg1AE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODeDUADg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NYBTA+DUADg1gFMD4NQATg2AUwPg1ABODYBTA+DUAE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODYBTAzg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NYBTSwDg1AA4NYBTA+DUADg1gFMD4NQATg2AUwPg1ABODYBTA+DUAE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODYBTAzg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NYBTA+DUADg1gFMD4NQAODWAUwPg1ABODYBTA+DUAE4NgFMD4NQATg2AUwM4NQBODYBTAzg1AE4NgFMDODUATg3g1AA4NQBODeDUADg1gFMD4NQAODWAUwPg1AA4NYBTA+DUAE4NgFMD4NQATg2AUwPg1ABODYBTAzg1AE4NgFMDODUATg3g1AA4NQBODeDUADg1AE4N4NQAODWAUwPg1AA4NYBTA+DUADg1gFMD4NQATg2AUwPg1ABODYBTA+DUAE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODeDUADg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NYBTA+DUADg1gFMD4NQAODWAUwPg1ABODYBTA+DUAE4NgFMDOLUEAE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODeDUADg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NYBTA+DUADg1gFMD4NQAODWAUwPg1ABODYBTA+DUAE4NgFMDODUATg2AUwM4NQBODYBTAzg1AE4N4NQAODUATg3g1AA4NQBODeDUADg1gFMD4NQAODWAUwPg1AA4NYBTA+DUAE4NgFMD4NQATg2AUwM4NQBODYBTAzg1AE4NgFMDODUATg3g1AA4NQBODeDUADg1AE4N4NQAODWAUwPg1AA4NYBTA+DUAE4NgFMD4NQATg2AUwPg1ABODYBTAzg1AE4NwL86u3vvrQTAadZa3R3xvT53vFdExMzoAnCgHxK5JlAGlVcXAAAAAElFTkSuQmCC" alt=""></p>
<p>not allow you to perish on the open sea.”</p>
<p>On perceiving me, the stranger addressed me</p>
<p>in English, although with a foreign accent. “Before I</p>
<p>come on board your vessel,” said he, “will you have</p>
<p>the kindness to inform me whither you are bound?”</p>
<p>You may conceive my astonishment on hearing</p>
<p>such a question addressed to me from a man on</p>
<p>the brink of destruction, and to whom I should have</p>
<p>supposed that my vessel would have been a re-</p>
<p>source which he would not have exchanged for the</p>
<p>most precious wealth the earth can afford. I replied,</p>
<p>however, that we were on a voyage of discovery</p>
<p>towards the northern pole.</p>
<p>Upon hearing this he appeared satisﬁed, and</p>
<p>consented to come on board. Good God! Margaret,</p>
<p>if you had seen the man who thus capitulated for</p>
<p>his safety, your surprise would have been bound-</p>
<p>less. His limbs were nearly frozen, and his body</p>
<p>dreadfully emaciated by fatigue and suffering. I</p>
<p>never saw a man in so wretched a condition. We at-</p>
<p>tempted to carry him into the cabin; but as soon as</p>
<p>he had quitted the fresh air, he fainted. We accord-</p>
<p><img src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAeMAAAJiCAIAAAB7ET2PAAAACXBIWXMAABYlAAAWJQFJUiTwAAAJe0lEQVR42u3UX4hUVRzA8d/OpJauoRPVggVXS03LnNUW3Y1w0EVLzcQ/A1mRq067C+6s7q7lBhGkKQSFleBD/3zIQiKwHnwwV32QoihEsJKokHzoJWghQhyXpoe5CzEk0VMGnw8sZ869516Wcw/fhsuXL2ez2Ww2GwBcS0ZGRqrV6pgxmeuy2Wwmk7EjANeaTCZTrVYjsg3VatV2AFzTybYFAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBS2wIApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKltAYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1LYAQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGUGoAlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQGUGgClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZQagCUGgClBlBqAJQaQKkBUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAZQaAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqAJQaAKUGUGoAlBpAqQFQagCUGkCpAVBqAJQaQKkBUGoApQZAqQFQagClBkCpAVBqAKUGQKkBlBoApQZAqQGUGgClBlBqWwCg1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDKDUASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNoNQAKDUASg2g1AAoNYBSA6DUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1ABKDYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgMoNQBKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1gFIDoNQAKDWAUgOg1AAoNYBSA6DUAEoNgFIDoNQASg2AUgOg1ABKDYBSAyg1AEoNgFIDKDUASg2g1AAoNQBKDaDUACg1AEoNoNQAKDWAUgOg1AD8W5VMpVIZGRmxEwDXmitXrlQqlYjro6GhdiWZvfR4rhjRdPb347liRDm369QXDVMLTx57qDHzQWvhrV/f/3TFgvOL/v595xdNy0ZMX9JX2nem9nypLVnXtSN5eN+zb36SK0acSsd8fv746dOX9JU6Bnr72pLV9W86sf/wwvad2wuFXVOHcsWIC3XjwbdnDeWKEZPeqY1JOg6n9/ftro3D6Xg17aWnS1t7t/V8lM7z3zYO5YoRR98b/GzWsfZC/fqG//iLJS35efnW/LzJ6T+yOSK6IqI3IrZGxGBEdEREOSK2pH+liOiPiG3pve6I6EvHckR0RsT9dc/1p+/sSZ8fSNf3/OV9XenvzvS5jrrr/RFRHljV0VNK5q5cXHxw7Ya7V3X3bNrRv7ajvHLOmlLnjmRL6anxc5f2lgcWL+7dmTw2p3nBfcmceQtbkuZ8vjlpbZmfPNFX6tjSW946WFu7vty9fUfpofbRfahdXd3xT+d33cVDb5zeeHPhx0dePlDOXf38Ls9GbC4ld42e34Hk8XxLfv2a+vM7kDS3trYmmwbT+/Vv6m5fduupd/sLsSc9r3XjkXTctjedp+PGsdOGcsWIxnG1cWM6Xs3o3vxfzm95c++W7nJnMnp+s+n153av3t92aUbhhnR+4NIzkxt+mlGYUHd/Yjp/Ib1/Szp/Lb3fVDefUrf+tnT+/b1HXrm497uTU9P5A+n6O+rW31m3fmbd+y/PXL5hwtFfLt304VBu49ffnF48seX5c3uWjR/4eHjTlEcPL2g78dXJeZWuoRWFEw2n7jn00sGZP/8298sJf1z4fPa5xrFNr796+w+NNzY1nRndj8LZ2veetG/4eK5Y258Xx0XsGVf7dtk/AeB3MK+9HhDhAAAAAElFTkSuQmCC" alt=""></p>
<p>ingly brought him back to the deck, and restored</p>
<p>him to animation by rubbing him with brandy, and</p>
<p>forcing him to swallow a small quantity. As soon</p>
<p>as he shewed signs of life, we wrapped him up in</p>
<p>blankets, and placed him near the chimney of the</p>
<p>kitchen-stove. By slow degrees he recovered, and</p>
<p>ate a little soup, which restored him wonderfully.</p>
<p>Two days passed in this manner before he was</p>
<p>able to speak; and I often feared that his sufferings</p>
<p>had deprived him of understanding. When he had</p>
<p>in some measure recovered, I removed him to my</p>
<p>own cabin, and attended on him as much as my</p>
<p>duty would permit. I never saw a more interesting</p>
<p>creature: his eyes have generally an expression</p>
<p>of wildness, and even madness; but there are mo-</p>
<p>ments when, if any one performs an act of kindness</p>
<p>towards him, or does him any the most triﬂing ser-</p>
<p>vice, his whole countenance is lighted up, as it were,</p>
<p>with a beam of benevolence and sweetness that</p>
<p>I never saw equalled. But he is generally melan-</p>
<p>choly and despairing; and sometimes he gnashes</p>
<p>his teeth, as if impatient of the weight of woes that</p>
<p>oppresses him.</p>
<p>When my guest was a little recovered, I had</p>
<p>great trouble to keep off the men, who wished to</p>
<p>ask him a thousand questions; but I would not allow</p>
<p>him to be tormented by their idle curiosity, in a</p>
<p>state of body and mind whose restoration evidently</p>
<p>depended upon entire repose. Once, however, the</p>
<p>lieutenant asked, Why he had come so far upon the</p>
<p>ice in so strange a vehicle?</p>
<p>His countenance instantly assumed an aspect</p>
<p>of the deepest gloom; and he replied, “To seek one</p>
<p>who ﬂed from me.”</p>
<p>“And did the man whom you pursued travel in</p>
<p>the same fashion?”</p>
<p>“Yes.”</p>
<p>“Then I fancy we have seen him; for, the day be-</p>
<p>fore we picked you up, we saw some dogs drawing</p>
<p>a sledge, with a man in it, across the ice.”</p>
<p>This aroused the stranger’s attention; and he</p>
<p>asked a multitude of questions concerning the route</p>
<p>which the dæmon, as he called him, had pursued.</p>
<p>Soon after, when he was alone with me, he said, “I</p>
<p>have, doubtless, excited your curiosity, as well as</p>
<p>that of these good people; but you are too consid-</p>
<p>erate to make inquiries.”</p>
<p>“Certainly; it would indeed be very impertinent</p>
<p>and inhuman in me to trouble you with any inquisi-</p>
<p>tiveness of mine.”</p>
<p>“And yet you rescued me from a strange and</p>
<p>perilous situation; you have benevolently restored</p>
<p>me to life.”</p>
<p>Soon after this he inquired, if I thought that</p>
<p>the breaking up of the ice had destroyed the other</p>
<p>sledge? I replied, that I could not answer with any</p>
<p>degree of certainty; for the ice had not broken until</p>
<p>near midnight, and the traveller might have arrived</p>
<p>at a place of safety before that time; but of this I</p>
<p>could not judge.</p>
<p>From this time the stranger seemed very eager</p>
<p>to be upon deck, to watch for the sledge which</p>
<p>had before appeared; but I have persuaded him</p>
<p>to remain in the cabin, for he is far too weak to</p>
<p>sustain the rawness of the atmosphere. But I have</p>
<p>promised that some one should watch for him, and</p>
<p>give him instant notice if any new object should</p>
<p>appear in sight.</p>
<p>Such is my journal of what relates to this</p>
<p>strange occurrence up to the present day. The</p>
<p>stranger has gradually improved in health, but is</p>
<p>very silent, and appears uneasy when any one</p>
<p>except myself enters his cabin. Yet his manners are</p>
<p>so conciliating and gentle, that the sailors are all</p>
<p>interested in him, although they have had very little</p>
<p>communication with him. For my own part, I begin</p>
<p>to love him as a brother; and his constant and deep</p>
<p>grief ﬁlls me with sympathy and compassion. He</p>
<p>must have been a noble creature in his better days,</p>
<p>being even now in wreck so attractive and amiable.</p>
<p>I said in one of my letters, my dear Margaret,</p>
<p>that I should ﬁnd no friend on the wide ocean; yet I</p>
<p>have found a man who, before his spirit had been</p>
<p>broken by misery, I should have been happy to have</p>
<p>possessed as the brother of my heart.</p>
<p>I shall continue my journal concerning the</p>
<p>stranger at intervals, should I have any fresh inci-</p>
<p>dents to record.</p>
<p>August 13th, 17—.</p>
<p>My affection for my guest increases every day.</p>
<p>He excites at once my admiration and my pity to</p>
<p>an astonishing degree. How can I see so noble</p>
<p>a creature destroyed by misery without feeling the</p>
<p>most poignant grief? He is so gentle, yet so wise;</p>
<p>his mind is so cultivated; and when he speaks,</p>
<p>although his words are culled with the choicest</p>
<p>art, yet they ﬂow with rapidity and unparalleled elo-</p>
<p>quence.</p>
<p>He is now much recovered from his illness, and</p>
<p>is continually on the deck, apparently watching for</p>
<p>the sledge that preceded his own. Yet, although</p>
<p>unhappy, he is not so utterly occupied by his own</p>
<p>misery, but that he interests himself deeply in the</p>
<p>employments of others. He has asked me many</p>
<p>questions concerning my design; and I have re-</p>
<p>lated my little history frankly to him. He appeared</p>
<p>pleased with the conﬁdence, and suggested several</p>
<p>alterations in my plan, which I shall ﬁnd exceedingly</p>
<p>useful. There is no pedantry in his manner; but</p>
<p>all he does appears to spring solely from the in-</p>
<p>terest he instinctively takes in the welfare of those</p>
<p>who surround him. He is often overcome by gloom,</p>
<p>and then he sits by himself, and tries to overcome</p>
<p>all that is sullen or unsocial in his humour. These</p>
<p>paroxysms pass from him like a cloud from before</p>
<p>the sun, though his dejection never leaves him. I</p>
<p>have endeavoured to win his conﬁdence; and I trust</p>
<p>that I have succeeded. One day I mentioned to him</p>
<p>the desire I had always felt of ﬁnding a friend who</p>
<p>might sympathize with me, and direct me by his</p>
<p>counsel. I said, I did not belong to that class of men</p>
<p>who are offended by advice. “I am self-educated,</p>
<p>and perhaps I hardly rely sufﬁciently upon my own</p>
<p>powers. I wish therefore that my companion should</p>
<p>be wiser and more experienced than myself, to</p>
<p>conﬁrm and support me; nor have I believed it im-</p>
<p>possible to ﬁnd a true friend.”</p>
<p>“I agree with you,” replied the stranger, “in be-</p>
<p>lieving that friendship is not only a desirable, but</p>
<p>a possible acquisition. I once had a friend, the</p>
<p>most noble of human creatures, and am entitled,</p>
<p>therefore, to judge respecting friendship. You have</p>
<p>hope, and the world before you, and have no cause</p>
<p>for despair. But I —— I have lost every thing, and</p>
<p>cannot begin life anew.”</p>
<p>As he said this, his countenance became ex-</p>
<p>pressive of a calm settled grief, that touched me to</p>
<p>the heart. But he was silent, and presently retired</p>
<p>to his cabin.</p>
<p>Even broken in spirit as he is, no one can feel</p>
<p>more deeply than he does the beauties of nature.</p>
<p>The starry sky, the sea, and every sight afforded</p>
<p>by these wonderful regions, seems still to have the</p>
<p>power of elevating his soul from earth. Such a man</p>
<p>has a double existence: he may suffer misery, and</p>
<p>be overwhelmed by disappointments; yet when he</p>
<p>has retired into himself, he will be like a celestial</p>
<p>spirit, that has a halo around him, within whose</p>
<p>circle no grief or folly ventures.</p>
<p>Will you laugh at the enthusiasm I express con-</p>
<p>cerning this divine wanderer? If you do, you must</p>
<p>have certainly lost that simplicity which was once</p>
<p>your characteristic charm. Yet, if you will, smile at</p>
<p>the warmth of my expressions, while I ﬁnd every</p>
<p>day new causes for repeating them.</p>
<p>August 19th, 17—.</p>
<p>Yesterday the stranger said to me, “You may</p>
<p>easily perceive, Captain Walton, that I have suf-</p>
<p>fered great and unparalleled misfortunes. I had</p>
<p>determined, once, that the memory of these evils</p>
<p>should die with me; but you have won me to alter</p>
<p>my determination. You seek for knowledge and wis-</p>
<p>dom, as I once did; and I ardently hope that the</p>
<p>gratiﬁcation of your wishes may not be a serpent to</p>
<p>sting you, as mine has been. I do not know that the</p>
<p>relation of my misfortunes will be useful to you, yet,</p>
<p>if you are inclined, listen to my tale. I believe that</p>
<p>the strange incidents connected with it will afford</p>
<p>a view of nature, which may enlarge your faculties</p>
<p>and understanding. You will hear of powers and</p>
<p>occurrences, such as you have been accustomed</p>
<p>to believe impossible: but I do not doubt that my</p>
<p>tale conveys in its series internal evidence of the</p>
<p>truth of the events of which it is composed.”</p>
<p>You may easily conceive that I was much grati-</p>
<p>ﬁed by the offered communication; yet I could not</p>
<p>endure that he should renew his grief by a recital</p>
<p>of his misfortunes. I felt the greatest eagerness</p>
<p>to hear the promised narrative, partly from curios-</p>
<p>ity, and partly from a strong desire to ameliorate</p>
<p>his fate, if it were in my power. I expressed these</p>
<p>feelings in my answer.</p>
<p>“I thank you,” he replied, “for your sympathy, but</p>
<p>it is useless; my fate is nearly fulﬁlled. I wait but for</p>
<p>one event, and then I shall repose in peace. I under-</p>
<p>stand your feeling,” continued he, perceiving that I</p>
<p>wished to interrupt him; “but you are mistaken, my</p>
<p>friend, if thus you will allow me to name you; noth-</p>
<p>ing can alter my destiny: listen to my history, and</p>
<p>you will perceive how irrevocably it is determined.”</p>
<p>He then told me, that he would commence his</p>
<p>narrative the next day when I should be at leisure.</p>
<p>This promise drew from me the warmest thanks. I</p>
<p>have resolved every night, when I am not engaged,</p>
<p>to record, as nearly as possible in his own words,</p>
<p>what he has related during the day. If I should be</p>
<p>engaged, I will at least make notes. This manuscript</p>
<p>will doubtless afford you the greatest pleasure: but</p>
<p>to me, who know him, and who hear it from his own</p>
<p>lips, with what interest and sympathy shall I read it</p>
<p>in some future day!</p>
<p>Chapter I</p>
<p>I AM by birth a Genevese; and my family is one of</p>
<p>the most distinguished of that republic. My ances-</p>
<p>tors had been for many years counsellors and syn-</p>
<p>dics; and my father had ﬁlled several public situa-</p>
<p>tions with honour and reputation. He was respected</p>
<p>by all who knew him for his integrity and indefati-</p>
<p>gable attention to public business. He passed his</p>
<p>younger days perpetually occupied by the affairs</p>
<p>of his country; and it was not until the decline of</p>
<p>life that he thought of marrying, and bestowing on</p>
<p>the state sons who might carry his virtues and his</p>
<p>name down to posterity.</p>
<p>33</p>
<p>As the circumstances of his marriage illustrate</p>
<p>his character, I cannot refrain from relating them.</p>
<p>One of his most intimate friends was a merchant,</p>
<p>who, from a ﬂourishing state, fell, through numerous</p>
<p>mischances, into poverty. This man, whose name</p>
<p>was Beaufort, was of a proud and unbending dis-</p>
<p>position, and could not bear to live in poverty and</p>
<p>oblivion in the same country where he had formerly</p>
<p>been distinguished for his rank and magniﬁcence.</p>
<p>Having paid his debts, therefore, in the most hon-</p>
<p>ourable manner, he retreated with his daughter to</p>
<p>the town of Lucerne, where he lived unknown and</p>
<p>in wretchedness. My father loved Beaufort with</p>
<p>the truest friendship, and was deeply grieved by</p>
<p>his retreat in these unfortunate circumstances. He</p>
<p>grieved also for the loss of his society, and resolved</p>
<p>to seek him out and endeavour to persuade him to</p>
<p>begin the world again through his credit and assis-</p>
<p>tance.</p>
<p>Beaufort had taken effectual measures to con-</p>
<p>ceal himself; and it was ten months before my father</p>
<p>discovered his abode. Overjoyed at this discovery,</p>
<p>he hastened to the house, which was situated in a</p>
<p>mean street, near the Reuss. But when he entered,</p>
<p>misery and despair alone welcomed him. Beaufort</p>
<p>had saved but a very small sum of money from the</p>
<p>wreck of his fortunes; but it was sufﬁcient to provide</p>
<p>him with sustenance for some months, and in the</p>
<p>mean time he hoped to procure some respectable</p>
<p>employment in a merchant’s house. The interval</p>
<p>was consequently spent in inaction; his grief only</p>
<p>became more deep and rankling, when he had</p>
<p>leisure for reﬂection; and at length it took so fast</p>
<p>hold of his mind, that at the end of three months he</p>
<p>lay on a bed of sickness, incapable of any exertion.</p>
<p>His daughter attended him with the greatest</p>
<p>tenderness; but she saw with despair that their little</p>
<p>fund was rapidly decreasing, and that there was no</p>
<p>other prospect of support. But Caroline Beaufort</p>
<p>possessed a mind of an uncommon mould; and</p>
<p>her courage rose to support her in her adversity.</p>
<p>She procured plain work; she plaited straw; and by</p>
<p>various means contrived to earn a pittance scarcely</p>
<p>sufﬁcient to support life.</p>
<p>Several months passed in this manner. Her</p>
<p>father grew worse; her time was more entirely oc-</p>
<p>cupied in attending him; her means of subsistence</p>
<p>decreased; and in the tenth month her father died in</p>
<p>her arms, leaving her an orphan and a beggar. This</p>
<p>last blow overcame her; and she knelt by Beaufort’s</p>
<p>cofﬁn, weeping bitterly, when my father entered the</p>
<p>chamber. He came like a protecting spirit to the</p>
<p>poor girl, who committed herself to his care, and</p>
<p>after the interment of his friend he conducted her</p>
<p>to Geneva, and placed her under the protection</p>
<p>of a relation. Two years after this event Caroline</p>
<p>became his wife.</p>
<p>When my father became a husband and a par-</p>
<p>ent, he found his time so occupied by the duties</p>
<p>of his new situation, that he relinquished many of</p>
<p>his public employments, and devoted himself to the</p>
<p>education of his children. Of these I was the eldest,</p>
<p>and the destined successor to all his labours and</p>
<p>utility. No creature could have more tender parents</p>
<p>than mine. My improvement and health were their</p>
<p>constant care, especially as I remained for several</p>
<p>years their only child. But before I continue my nar-</p>
<p>rative, I must record an incident which took place</p>
<p>when I was four years of age.</p>
<p>My father had a sister, whom he tenderly loved,</p>
<p>and who had married early in life an Italian gentle-</p>
<p>man. Soon after her marriage, she had accompa-</p>
<p>nied her husband into her native country, and for</p>
<p>some years my father had very little communication</p>
<p>with her. About the time I mentioned she died; and</p>
<p>a few months afterwards he received a letter from</p>
<p>her husband, acquainting him with his intention of</p>
<p>marrying an Italian lady, and requesting my father</p>
<p>to take charge of the infant Elizabeth, the only child</p>
<p>of his deceased sister. “It is my wish,” he said, “that</p>
<p>you should consider her as your own daughter, and</p>
<p>educate her thus. Her mother’s fortune is secured</p>
<p>to her, the documents of which I will commit to your</p>
<p>keeping. Reﬂect upon this proposition; and de-</p>
<p>cide whether you would prefer educating your niece</p>
<p>yourself to her being brought up by a stepmother.”</p>
<p>My father did not hestitate, and immediately</p>
<p>went to Italy, that he might accompany the little Eliz-</p>
<p>abeth to her future home. I have often heard my</p>
<p>mother say, that she was at that time the most beau-</p>
<p>tiful child she had ever seen, and shewed signs</p>
<p>even then of a gentle and affectionate disposition.</p>
<p>These indications, and a desire to bind as closely</p>
<p>as possible the ties of domestic love, determined</p>
<p>my mother to consider Elizabeth as my future wife;</p>
<p>a design which she never found reason to repent.</p>
<p>From this time Elizabeth Lavenza became my</p>
<p>playfellow, and, as we grew older, my friend. She</p>
<p>was docile and good tempered, yet gay and play-</p>
<p>ful as a summer insect. Although she was lively</p>
<p>and animated, her feelings were strong and deep,</p>
<p>and her disposition uncommonly affectionate. No</p>
<p>one could better enjoy liberty, yet no one could sub-</p>
<p>mit with more grace than she did to constraint and</p>
<p>caprice. Her imagination was luxuriant, yet her ca-</p>
<p>pability of application was great. Her person was</p>
<p>the image of her mind; her hazel eyes, although as</p>
<p>lively as a bird’s, possessed an attractive softness.</p>
<p>Her ﬁgure was light and airy; and, though capable</p>
<p>of enduring great fatigue, she appeared the most</p>
<p>fragile creature in the world. While I admired her</p>
<p>understanding and fancy, I loved to tend on her, as</p>
<p>I should on a favourite animal; and I never saw so</p>
<p>much grace both of person and mind united to so</p>
<p>little pretension.</p>
<p>Every one adored Elizabeth. If the servants</p>
<p>had any request to make, it was always through</p>
<p>her intercession. We were strangers to any species</p>
<p>of disunion and dispute; for although there was</p>
<p>a great dissimilitude in our characters, there was</p>
<p>an harmony in that very dissimilitude. I was more</p>
<p>calm and philosophical than my companion; yet my</p>
<p>temper was not so yielding. My application was of</p>
<p>longer endurance; but it was not so severe whilst</p>
<p>it endured. I delighted in investigating the facts</p>
<p>relative to the actual world; she busied herself in fol-</p>
<p>lowing the aërial creations of the poets. The world</p>
<p>was to me a secret, which I desired to discover; to</p>
<p>her it was a vacancy, which she sought to people</p>
<p>with imaginations of her own.</p>
<p>My brothers were considerably younger than</p>
<p>myself; but I had a friend in one of my schoolfel-</p>
<p>lows, who compensated for this deﬁciency. Henry</p>
<p>Clerval was the son of a merchant of Geneva, an</p>
<p>intimate friend of my father. He was a boy of sin-</p>
<p>gular talent and fancy. I remember, when he was</p>
<p>nine years old, he wrote a fairy tale, which was the</p>
<p>delight and amazement of all his companions. His</p>
<p>favourite study consisted in books of chivalry and</p>
<p>romance; and when very young, I can remember,</p>
<p>that we used to act plays composed by him out</p>
<p>of these favourite books, the principal characters</p>
<p>of which were Orlando, Robin Hood, Amadis, and</p>
<p>St. George.</p>
<p>No youth could have passed more happily than</p>
<p>mine. My parents were indulgent, and my compan-</p>
<p>ions amiable. Our studies were never forced; and</p>
<p>by some means we always had an end placed in</p>
<p>view, which excited us to ardour in the prosecution</p>
<p>of them. It was by this method, and not by emula-</p>
<p>tion, that we were urged to application. Elizabeth</p>
<p>was not incited to apply herself to drawing, that her</p>
<p>companions might not outstrip her; but through the</p>
<p>desire of pleasing her aunt, by the representation</p>
<p>of some favourite scene done by her own hand. We</p>
<p>learned Latin and English, that we might read the</p>
<p>writings in those languages; and so far from study</p>
<p>being made odious to us through punishment, we</p>
<p>loved application, and our amusements would have</p>
<p>been the labours of other children. Perhaps we</p>
<p>did not read so many books, or learn languages</p>
<p>so quickly, as those who are disciplined according</p>
<p>to the ordinary methods; but what we learned was</p>
<p>impressed the more deeply on our memories.</p>
<p>In this description of our domestic circle I in-</p>
<p>clude Henry Clerval; for he was constantly with us.</p>
<p>He went to school with me, and generally passed</p>
<p>the afternoon at our house; for being an only child,</p>
<p>and destitute of companions at home, his father</p>
<p>was well pleased that he should ﬁnd associates at</p>
<p>our house; and we were never completely happy</p>
<p>when Clerval was absent.</p>
<p>I feel pleasure in dwelling on the recollections of</p>
<p>childhood, before misfortune had tainted my mind,</p>
<p>and changed its bright visions of extensive useful-</p>
<p>ness into gloomy and narrow reﬂections upon self.</p>
<p>But, in drawing the picture of my early days, I must</p>
<p>not omit to record those events which led, by insen-</p>
<p>sible steps to my after tale of misery: for when I</p>
<p>would account to myself for the birth of that passion,</p>
<p>which afterwards ruled my destiny, I ﬁnd it arise, like</p>
<p>a mountain river, from ignoble and almost forgotten</p>
<p>sources; but, swelling as it proceeded, it became</p>
<p>the torrent which, in its course, has swept away all</p>
<p>my hopes and joys.</p>
<p>Natural philosophy is the genius that has reg-</p>
<p>ulated my fate; I desire therefore, in this narration,</p>
<p>to state those facts which led to my predilection for</p>
<p>that science. When I was thirteen years of age, we</p>
<p>all went on a party of pleasure to the baths near</p>
<p>Thonon: the inclemency of the weather obliged us</p>
<p>to remain a day conﬁned to the inn. In this house</p>
<p>I chanced to ﬁnd a volume of the works of Cor-</p>
<p>nelius Agrippa. I opened it with apathy; the theory</p>
<p>which he attempts to demonstrate, and the wonder-</p>
<p>ful facts which he relates, soon changed this feeling</p>
<p>into enthusiasm. A new light seemed to dawn upon</p>
<p>my mind; and, bounding with joy, I communicated</p>
<p>my discovery to my father. I cannot help remarking</p>
<p>here the many opportunities instructors possess</p>
<p>of directing the attention of their pupils to useful</p>
<p>knowledge, which they utterly neglect. My father</p>
<p>looked carelessly at the title-page of my book, and</p>
<p>said, “Ah! Cornelius Agrippa! My dear Victor, do</p>
<p>not waste your time upon this; it is sad trash.”</p>
<p>If, instead of this remark, my father had taken</p>
<p>the pains, to explain to me, that the principles of</p>
<p>Agrippa had been entirely exploded, and that a mod-</p>
<p>ern system of science had been introduced, which</p>
<p>possessed much greater powers than the ancient,</p>
<p>because the powers of the latter were chimerical,</p>
<p>while those of the former were real and practical;</p>
<p>under such circumstances, I should certainly have</p>
<p>thrown Agrippa aside, and, with my imagination</p>
<p>warmed as it was, should probably have applied my-</p>
<p>self to the more rational theory of chemistry which</p>
<p>has resulted from modern discoveries. It is even</p>
<p>possible, that the train of my ideas would never</p>
<p>have received the fatal impulse that led to my ruin.</p>
<p>But the cursory glance my father had taken of my</p>
<p>volume by no means assured me that he was ac-</p>
<p>quainted with its contents; and I continued to read</p>
<p>with the greatest avidity.</p>
<p>When I returned home, my ﬁrst care was to</p>
<p>procure the whole works of this author, and after-</p>
<p>wards of Paracelsus and Albertus Magnus. I read</p>
<p>and studied the wild fancies of these writers with</p>
<p>delight; they appeared to me treasures known to</p>
<p>few beside myself; and although I often wished to</p>
<p>communicate these secret stores of knowledge to</p>
<p>my father, yet his indeﬁnite censure of my favourite</p>
<p>Agrippa always withheld me. I disclosed my dis-</p>
<p>coveries to Elizabeth, therefore, under a promise of</p>
<p>strict secrecy; but she did not interest herself in the</p>
<p>subject, and I was left by her to pursue my studies</p>
<p>alone.</p>
<p>It may appear very strange, that a disciple of</p>
<p>Albertus Magnus should arise in the eighteenth cen-</p>
<p>tury; but our family was not scientiﬁcal, and I had</p>
<p>not attended any of the lectures given at the schools</p>
<p>of Geneva. My dreams were therefore undisturbed</p>
<p>by reality; and I entered with the greatest diligence</p>
<p>into the search of the philosopher’s stone and the</p>
<p>elixir of life. But the latter obtained my most undi-</p>
<p>vided attention: wealth was an inferior object; but</p>
<p>what glory would attend the discovery, if I could</p>
<p>banish disease from the human frame, and render</p>
<p>man invulnerable to any but a violent death!</p>
<p>Nor were these my only visions. The raising of</p>
<p>ghosts or devils was a promise liberally accorded</p>
<p>by my favourite authors, the fulﬁlment of which I</p>
<p>most eagerly sought; and if my incantations were</p>
<p>always unsuccessful, I attributed the failure rather</p>
<p>to my own inexperience and mistake, than to a want</p>
<p>of skill or ﬁdelity in my instructors.</p>
<p>The natural phænomena that take place ev-</p>
<p>ery day before our eyes did not escape my exam-</p>
<p>inations. Distillation, and the wonderful effects of</p>
<p>steam, processes of which my favourite authors</p>
<p>were utterly ignorant, excited my astonishment; but</p>
<p>my utmost wonder was engaged by some experi-</p>
<p>ments on an air-pump, which I saw employed by a</p>
<p>gentleman whom we were in the habit of visiting.</p>
<p>The ignorance of the early philosophers on</p>
<p>these and several other points served to decrease</p>
<p>their credit with me: but I could not entirely throw</p>
<p>them aside, before some other system should oc-</p>
<p>cupy their place in my mind.</p>
<p>When I was about ﬁfteen years old, we had re-</p>
<p>tired to our house near Belrive, when we witnessed</p>
<p>a most violent and terrible thunder-storm. It ad-</p>
<p>vanced from behind the mountains of Jura; and</p>
<p>the thunder burst at once with frightful loudness</p>
<p>from various quarters of the heavens. I remained,</p>
<p>while the storm lasted, watching its progress with</p>
<p>curiosity and delight. As I stood at the door, on a</p>
<p>sudden I beheld a stream of ﬁre issue from an old</p>
<p>and beautiful oak, which stood about twenty yards</p>
<p>from our house; and so soon as the dazzling light</p>
<p>vanished, the oak had disappeared, and nothing</p>
<p>remained but a blasted stump. When we visited it</p>
<p>the next morning, we found the tree shattered in a</p>
<p>singular manner. It was not splintered by the shock,</p>
<p>but entirely reduced to thin ribbands of wood. I</p>
<p>never beheld any thing so utterly destroyed.</p>
<p>The catastrophe of this tree excited my extreme</p>
<p>astonishment; and I eagerly inquired of my father</p>
<p>the nature and origin of thunder and lightning. He</p>
<p>replied, “Electricity;” describing at the same time</p>
<p>the various effects of that power. He constructed a</p>
<p>small electrical machine, and exhibited a few exper-</p>
<p>iments; he made also a kite, with a wire and string,</p>
<p>which drew down that ﬂuid from the clouds.</p>
<p>This last stroke completed the overthrow of Cor-</p>
<p>nelius Agrippa, Albertus Magnus, and Paracelsus,</p>
<p>who had so long reigned the lords of my imagina-</p>
<p>tion. But by some fatality I did not feel inclined to</p>
<p>commence the study of any modern system; and</p>
<p>this disinclination was inﬂuenced by the following</p>
<p>circumstance.</p>
<p>My father expressed a wish that I should attend</p>
<p>a course of lectures upon natural philosophy, to</p>
<p>which I cheerfully consented. Some accident pre-</p>
<p>vented my attending these lectures until the course</p>
<p>was nearly ﬁnished. The lecture, being therefore</p>
<p>one of the last, was entirely incomprehensible to</p>
<p>me. The professor discoursed with the greatest</p>
<p>ﬂuency of potassium and boron, of sulphates and</p>
<p>oxyds, terms to which I could afﬁx no idea; and I</p>
<p>became disgusted with the science of natural phi-</p>
<p>losophy, although I still read Pliny and Buffon with</p>
<p>delight, authors, in my estimation, of nearly equal</p>
<p>interest and utility.</p>
<p>My occupations at this age were principally the</p>
<p>mathematics, and most of the branches of study</p>
<p>appertaining to that science. I was busily employed</p>
<p>in learning languages; Latin was already familiar</p>
<p>to me, and I began to read some of the easiest</p>
<p>Greek authors without the help of a lexicon. I also</p>
<p>perfectly understood English and German. This</p>
<p>is the list of my accomplishments at the age of</p>
<p>seventeen; and you may conceive that my hours</p>
<p>were fully employed in acquiring and maintaining a</p>
<p>knowledge of this various literature.</p>
<p>Another task also devolved upon me, when I</p>
<p>became the instructor of my brothers. Ernest was</p>
<p>six years younger than myself, and was my principal</p>
<p>pupil. He had been afﬂicted with ill health from his</p>
<p>infancy, through which Elizabeth and I had been his</p>
<p>constant nurses: his disposition was gentle, but he</p>
<p>was incapable of any severe application. William,</p>
<p>the youngest of our family, was yet an infant, and</p>
<p>the most beautiful little fellow in the world; his lively</p>
<p>blue eyes, dimpled cheeks, and endearing manners,</p>
<p>inspired the tenderest affection.</p>
<p>Such was our domestic circle, from which care</p>
<p>and pain seemed for ever banished. My father</p>
<p>directed our studies, and my mother partook of our</p>
<p>enjoyments. Neither of us possessed the slightest</p>
<p>pre-eminence over the other; the voice of command</p>
<p>was never heard amongst us; but mutual affection</p>
<p>engaged us all to comply with and obey the slightest</p>
<p>desire of each other.</p>
<p>Chapter II</p>
<p>WHEN I had attained the age of seventeen, my</p>
<p>parents resolved that I should become a student at</p>
<p>the university of Ingolstadt. I had hitherto attended</p>
<p>the schools of Geneva; but my father thought it</p>
<p>necessary, for the completion of my education, that</p>
<p>I should be made acquainted with other customs</p>
<p>than those of my native country. My departure was</p>
<p>therefore ﬁxed at an early date; but, before the day</p>
<p>resolved upon could arrive, the ﬁrst misfortune of</p>
<p>my life occurred — an omen, as it were, of my future</p>
<p>misery.</p>
<p>50</p>
<p>Elizabeth had caught the scarlet fever; but her</p>
<p>illness was not severe, and she quickly recovered.</p>
<p>During her conﬁnement, many arguments had been</p>
<p>urged to persuade my mother to refrain from at-</p>
<p>tending upon her. She had, at ﬁrst, yielded to our</p>
<p>entreaties; but when she heard that her favourite</p>
<p>was recovering, she could no longer debar herself</p>
<p>from her society, and entered her chamber long</p>
<p>before the danger of infection was past. The con-</p>
<p>sequences of this imprudence were fatal. On the</p>
<p>third day my mother sickened; her fever was very</p>
<p>malignant, and the looks of her attendants prognos-</p>
<p>ticated the worst event. On her death-bed the forti-</p>
<p>tude and benignity of this admirable woman did not</p>
<p>desert her. She joined the hands of Elizabeth and</p>
<p>myself: “My children,” she said, “my ﬁrmest hopes</p>
<p>of future happiness were placed on the prospect of</p>
<p>your union. This expectation will now be the conso-</p>
<p>lation of your father. Elizabeth, my love, you must</p>
<p>supply my place to your younger cousins. Alas! I</p>
<p>regret that I am taken from you; and, happy and</p>
<p>beloved as I have been, is it not hard to quit you</p>
<p>all? But these are not thoughts beﬁtting me; I will</p>
<p>endeavour to resign myself cheerfully to death, and</p>
<p>will indulge a hope of meeting you in another world.”</p>
<p>She died calmly; and her countenance ex-</p>
<p>pressed affection even in death. I need not de-</p>
<p>scribe the feelings of those whose dearest ties</p>
<p>are rent by that most irreparable evil, the void that</p>
<p>presents itself to the soul, and the despair that is</p>
<p>exhibited on the countenance. It is so long before</p>
<p>the mind can persuade itself that she, whom we</p>
<p>saw every day, and whose very existence appeared</p>
<p>a part of our own, can have departed for ever —</p>
<p>that the brightness of a beloved eye can have been</p>
<p>extinguished, and the sound of a voice so familiar,</p>
<p>and dear to the ear, can be hushed, never more to</p>
<p>be heard. These are the reﬂections of the ﬁrst days;</p>
<p>but when the lapse of time proves the reality of the</p>
<p>evil, then the actual bitterness of grief commences.</p>
<p>Yet from whom has not that rude hand rent away</p>
<p>some dear connexion; and why should I describe a</p>
<p>sorrow which all have felt, and must feel? The time</p>
<p>at length arrives, when grief is rather an indulgence</p>
<p>than a necessity; and the smile that plays upon</p>
