<p>the lips, although it may be deemed a sacrilege, is</p>
<p>not banished. My mother was dead, but we had</p>
<p>still duties which we ought to perform; we must</p>
<p>continue our course with the rest, and learn to think</p>
<p>ourselves fortunate, whilst one remains whom the</p>
<p>spoiler has not seized.</p>
<p>My journey to Ingolstadt, which had been de-</p>
<p>ferred by these events, was now again determined</p>
<p>upon. I obtained from my father a respite of some</p>
<p>weeks. This period was spent sadly; my mother’s</p>
<p>death, and my speedy departure, depressed our</p>
<p>spirits; but Elizabeth endeavoured to renew the</p>
<p>spirit of cheerfulness in our little society. Since the</p>
<p>death of her aunt, her mind had acquired new ﬁrm-</p>
<p>ness and vigour. She determined to fulﬁl her duties</p>
<p>with the greatest exactness; and she felt that that</p>
<p>most imperious duty, of rendering her uncle and</p>
<p>cousins happy, had devolved upon her. She con-</p>
<p>soled me, amused her uncle, instructed my broth-</p>
<p>ers; and I never beheld her so enchanting as at</p>
<p>this time, when she was continually endeavouring</p>
<p>to contribute to the happiness of others, entirely</p>
<p>forgetful of herself.</p>
<p>The day of my departure at length arrived. I</p>
<p>had taken leave of all my friends, excepting Clerval,</p>
<p>who spent the last evening with us. He bitterly</p>
<p>lamented that he was unable to accompany me: but</p>
<p>his father could not be persuaded to part with him,</p>
<p>intending that he should become a partner with him</p>
<p>in business, in compliance with his favourite theory,</p>
<p>that learning was superﬂuous in the commerce of</p>
<p>ordinary life. Henry had a reﬁned mind; he had no</p>
<p>desire to be idle, and was well pleased to become</p>
<p>his father’s partner, but he believed that a man</p>
<p>might be a very good trader, and yet possess a</p>
<p>cultivated understanding.</p>
<p>We sat late, listening to his complaints, and</p>
<p>making many little arrangements for the future. The</p>
<p>next morning early I departed. Tears gushed from</p>
<p>the eyes of Elizabeth; they proceeded partly from</p>
<p>sorrow at my departure, and partly because she</p>
<p>reﬂected that the same journey was to have taken</p>
<p>place three months before, when a mother’s bless-</p>
<p>ing would have accompanied me.</p>
<p>I threw myself into the chaise that was to convey</p>
<p>me away, and indulged in the most melancholy re-</p>
<p>ﬂections. I, who had ever been surrounded by ami-</p>
<p>able companions, continually engaged in endeav-</p>
<p>ouring to bestow mutual pleasure, I was now alone.</p>
<p>In the university, whither I was going, I must form</p>
<p>my own friends, and be my own protector. My life</p>
<p>had hitherto been remarkably secluded and domes-</p>
<p>tic; and this had given me invincible repugnance to</p>
<p>new countenances. I loved my brothers, Elizabeth,</p>
<p>and Clerval; these were “old familiar faces;” but</p>
<p>I believed myself totally unﬁtted for the company</p>
<p>of strangers. Such were my reﬂections as I com-</p>
<p>menced my journey; but as I proceeded, my spirits</p>
<p>and hopes rose. I ardently desired the acquisition</p>
<p>of knowledge. I had often, when at home, thought</p>
<p>it hard to remain during my youth cooped up in one</p>
<p>place, and had longed to enter the world, and take</p>
<p>my station among other human beings. Now my</p>
<p>desires were complied with, and it would, indeed,</p>
<p>have been folly to repent.</p>
<p>I had sufﬁcient leisure for these and many other</p>
<p>reﬂections during my journey to Ingolstadt, which</p>
<p>was long and fatiguing. At length the high white</p>
<p>steeple of the town met my eyes. I alighted, and</p>
<p>was conducted to my solitary apartment, to spend</p>
<p>the evening as I pleased.</p>
<p>The next morning I delivered my letters of intro-</p>
<p>duction, and paid a visit to some of the principal</p>
<p>professors, and among others to M. Krempe, pro-</p>
<p>fessor of natural philosophy. He received me with</p>
<p>politeness, and asked me several questions con-</p>
<p>cerning my progress in the different branches of</p>
<p>science appertaining to natural philosophy. I men-</p>
<p>tioned, it is true, with fear and trembling, the only</p>
<p>authors I had ever read upon those subjects. The</p>
<p>professor stared: “Have you,” he said, “really spent</p>
<p>your time in studying such nonsense?”</p>
<p>I replied in the afﬁrmative. “Every minute,” con-</p>
<p>tinued M. Krempe with warmth, “every instant that</p>
<p>you have wasted on those books is utterly and en-</p>
<p>tirely lost. You have burdened your memory with</p>
<p>exploded systems, and useless names. Good God!</p>
<p>in what desert land have you lived, where no one</p>
<p>was kind enough to inform you that these fancies,</p>
<p>which you have so greedily imbibed, are a thousand</p>
<p>years old, and as musty as they are ancient? I little</p>
<p>expected in this enlightened and scientiﬁc age to</p>
<p>ﬁnd a disciple of Albertus Magnus and Paracelsus.</p>
<p>My dear Sir, you must begin your studies entirely</p>
<p>anew.”</p>
<p>So saying, he stept aside, and wrote down a</p>
<p>list of several books treating of natural philosophy,</p>
<p>which he desired me to procure, and dismissed</p>
<p>me, after mentioning that in the beginning of the</p>
<p>following week he intended to commence a course</p>
<p>of lectures upon natural philosophy in its general</p>
<p>relations, and that M. Waldman, a fellow-professor,</p>
<p>would lecture upon chemistry the alternate days</p>
<p>that he missed.</p>
<p>I returned home, not disappointed, for I had</p>
<p>long considered those authors useless whom the</p>
<p>professor had so strongly reprobated; but I did not</p>
<p>feel much inclined to study the books which I pro-</p>
<p>cured at his recommendation. M. Krempe was a</p>
<p>little squat man, with a gruff voice and repulsive</p>
<p>countenance; the teacher, therefore, did not pre-</p>
<p>possess me in favour of his doctrine. Besides, I had</p>
<p>a contempt for the uses of modern natural philoso-</p>
<p>phy. It was very different, when the masters of the</p>
<p>science sought immortality and power; such views,</p>
<p>although futile, were grand: but now the scene was</p>
<p>changed. The ambition of the inquirer seemed to</p>
<p>limit itself to the annihilation of those visions on</p>
<p>which my interest in science was chieﬂy founded. I</p>
<p>was required to exchange chimeras of boundless</p>
<p>grandeur for realities of little worth.</p>
<p>Such were my reﬂections during the ﬁrst two</p>
<p>or three days spent almost in solitude. But as the</p>
<p>ensuing week commenced, I thought of the infor-</p>
<p>mation which M. Krempe had given me concerning</p>
<p>the lectures. And although I could not consent to</p>
<p>go and hear that little conceited fellow deliver sen-</p>
<p>tences out of a pulpit, I recollected what he had</p>
<p>said of M. Waldman, whom I had never seen, as he</p>
<p>had hitherto been out of town.</p>
<p>Partly from curiosity, and partly from idleness,</p>
<p>I went into the lecturing room, which M. Waldman</p>
<p>entered shortly after. This professor was very un-</p>
<p>like his colleague. He appeared about ﬁfty years of</p>
<p>age, but with an aspect expressive of the greatest</p>
<p>benevolence; a few gray hairs covered his temples,</p>
<p>but those at the back of his head were nearly black.</p>
<p>His person was short, but remarkably erect; and</p>
<p>his voice the sweetest I had ever heard. He be-</p>
<p>gan his lecture by a recapitulation of the history of</p>
<p>chemistry and the various improvements made by</p>
<p>different men of learning, pronouncing with fervour</p>
<p>the names of the most distinguished discoverers.</p>
<p>He then took a cursory view of the present state</p>
<p>of the science, and explained many of its elemen-</p>
<p>tary terms. After having made a few preparatory</p>
<p>experiments, he concluded with a panegyric upon</p>
<p>modern chemistry, the terms of which I shall never</p>
<p>forget:—</p>
<p>“The ancient teachers of this science,” said he,</p>
<p>“promised impossibilities, and performed nothing.</p>
<p>The modern masters promise very little; they know</p>
<p>that metals cannot be transmuted, and that the elixir</p>
<p>of life is a chimera. But these philosophers, whose</p>
<p>hands seem only made to dabble in dirt, and their</p>
<p>eyes to pour over the microscope or crucible, have</p>
<p>indeed performed miracles. They penetrate into</p>
<p>the recesses of nature, and shew how she works</p>
<p>in her hiding places. They ascend into the heav-</p>
<p>ens; they have discovered how the blood circulates,</p>
<p>and the nature of the air we breathe. They have</p>
<p>acquired new and almost unlimited powers; they</p>
<p>can command the thunders of heaven, mimic the</p>
<p>earthquake, and even mock the invisible world with</p>
<p>its own shadows.”</p>
<p>I departed highly pleased with the professor and</p>
<p>his lecture, and paid him a visit the same evening.</p>
<p>His manners in private were even more mild and</p>
<p>attractive than in public; for there was a certain dig-</p>
<p>nity in his mien during his lecture, which in his own</p>
<p>house was replaced by the greatest affability and</p>
<p>kindness. He heard with attention my little narration</p>
<p>concerning my studies, and smiled at the names</p>
<p>of Cornelius Agrippa, and Paracelsus, but without</p>
<p>the contempt that M. Krempe had exhibited. He</p>
<p>said, that “these were men to whose indefatigable</p>
<p>zeal modern philosophers were indebted for most</p>
<p>of the foundations of their knowledge. They had</p>
<p>left to us, as an easier task, to give new names,</p>
<p>and arrange in connected classiﬁcations, the facts</p>
<p>which they in a great degree had been the instru-</p>
<p>ments of bringing to light. The labours of men</p>
<p>of genius, however erroneously directed, scarcely</p>
<p>ever fail in ultimately turning to the solid advantage</p>
<p>of mankind.” I listened to his statement, which was</p>
<p>delivered without any presumption or affectation;</p>
<p>and then added, that his lecture had removed my</p>
<p>prejudices against modern chemists; and I, at the</p>
<p>same time, requested his advice concerning the</p>
<p>books I ought to procure.</p>
<p>“I am happy,” said M. Waldman, “to have gained</p>
<p>a disciple; and if your application equals your ability,</p>
<p>I have no doubt of your success. Chemistry is that</p>
<p>branch of natural philosophy in which the greatest</p>
<p>improvements have been and may be made; it is</p>
<p>on that account that I have made it my peculiar</p>
<p>study; but at the same time I have not neglected</p>
<p>the other branches of science. A man would make</p>
<p>but a very sorry chemist, if he attended to that</p>
<p>department of human knowledge alone. If your</p>
<p>wish is to become really a man of science, and</p>
<p>not merely a petty experimentalist, I should advise</p>
<p>you to apply to every branch of natural philosophy,</p>
<p>including mathematics.”</p>
<p>He then took me into his laboratory, and ex-</p>
<p>plained to me the uses of his various machines;</p>
<p>instructing me as to what I ought to procure, and</p>
<p>promising me the use of his own, when I should</p>
<p>have advanced far enough in the science not to</p>
<p>derange their mechanism. He also gave me the</p>
<p>list of books which I had requested; and I took my</p>
<p>leave.</p>
<p>Thus ended a day memorable to me; it decided</p>
<p>my future destiny.</p>
<p>Chapter III</p>
<p>FROM this day natural philosophy, and particularly</p>
<p>chemistry, in the most comprehensive sense of the</p>
<p>term, became nearly my sole occupation. I read</p>
<p>with ardour those works, so full of genius and dis-</p>
<p>crimination, which modern inquirers have written</p>
<p>on these subjects. I attended the lectures, and culti-</p>
<p>vated the acquaintance, of the men of science of the</p>
<p>university; and I found even in M. Krempe a great</p>
<p>deal of sound sense and real information, com-</p>
<p>bined, it is true, with a repulsive physiognomy and</p>
<p>manners, but not on that account the less valuable.</p>
<p>In M. Waldman I found a true friend. His gentleness</p>
<p>63</p>
<p>was never tinged by dogmatism; and his instruc-</p>
<p>tions were given with an air of frankness and good</p>
<p>nature, that banished every idea of pedantry. It was,</p>
<p>perhaps, the amiable character of this man that in-</p>
<p>clined me more to that branch of natural philosophy</p>
<p>which he professed, than an intrinsic love for the</p>
<p>science itself. But this state of mind had place only</p>
<p>in the ﬁrst steps towards knowledge: the more fully</p>
<p>I entered into the science, the more exclusively I</p>
<p>pursued it for its own sake. That application, which</p>
<p>at ﬁrst had been a matter of duty and resolution,</p>
<p>now became so ardent and eager, that the stars</p>
<p>often disappeared in the light of morning whilst I</p>
<p>was yet engaged in my laboratory.</p>
<p>As I applied so closely, it may be easily con-</p>
<p>ceived that I improved rapidly. My ardour was in-</p>
<p>deed the astonishment of the students; and my</p>
<p>proﬁciency, that of the masters. Professor Krempe</p>
<p>often asked me, with a sly smile, how Cornelius</p>
<p>Agrippa went on? whilst M. Waldman expressed</p>
<p>the most heart-felt exultation in my progress. Two</p>
<p>years passed in this manner, during which I paid no</p>
<p>visit to Geneva, but was engaged, heart and soul,</p>
<p>in the pursuit of some discoveries, which I hoped</p>
<p>to make. None but those who have experienced</p>
<p>them can conceive of the enticements of science.</p>
<p>In other studies you go as far as others have gone</p>
<p>before you, and there is nothing more to know; but</p>
<p>in a scientiﬁc pursuit there is continual food for dis-</p>
<p>covery and wonder. A mind of moderate capacity,</p>
<p>which closely pursues one study, must infallibly ar-</p>
<p>rive at great proﬁciency in that study; and I, who</p>
<p>continually sought the attainment of one object of</p>
<p>pursuit, and was solely wrapt up in this, improved so</p>
<p>rapidly, that, at the end of two years, I made some</p>
<p>discoveries in the improvement of some chemical</p>
<p>instruments, which procured me great esteem and</p>
<p>admiration at the university. When I had arrived at</p>
<p>this point, and had become as well acquainted with</p>
<p>the theory and practice of natural philosophy as de-</p>
<p>pended on the lessons of any of the professors at</p>
<p>Ingolstadt, my residence there being no longer con-</p>
<p>ducive to my improvements, I thought of returning</p>
<p>to my friends and my native town, when an incident</p>
<p>happened that protracted my stay.</p>
<p>One of the phænonema which had peculiarly</p>
<p>attracted my attention was the structure of the hu-</p>
<p>man frame, and, indeed, any animal endued with</p>
<p>life. Whence, I often asked myself, did the princi-</p>
<p>ple of life proceed? It was a bold question, and</p>
<p>one which has ever been considered as a mys-</p>
<p>tery; yet with how many things are we upon the</p>
<p>brink of becoming acquainted, if cowardice or care-</p>
<p>lessness did not restrain our inquiries. I revolved</p>
<p>these circumstances in my mind, and determined</p>
<p>thenceforth to apply myself more particularly to</p>
<p>those branches of natural philosophy which relate</p>
<p>to physiology. Unless I had been animated by an</p>
<p>almost supernatural enthusiasm, my application to</p>
<p>this study would have been irksome, and almost</p>
<p>intolerable. To examine the causes of life, we must</p>
<p>ﬁrst have recourse to death. I became acquainted</p>
<p>with the science of anatomy: but this was not suf-</p>
<p>ﬁcient; I must also observe the natural decay and</p>
<p>corruption of the human body. In my education</p>
<p>my father had taken the greatest precautions that</p>
<p>my mind should be impressed with no supernatural</p>
<p>horrors. I do not ever remember to have trembled</p>
<p>at a tale of superstition, or to have feared the ap-</p>
<p>parition of a spirit. Darkness had no effect upon</p>
<p>my fancy; and a church-yard was to me merely</p>
<p>the receptacle of bodies deprived of life, which,</p>
<p>from being the seat of beauty and strength, had</p>
<p>become food for the worm. Now I was led to ex-</p>
<p>amine the cause and progress of this decay, and</p>
<p>forced to spend days and nights in vaults and char-</p>
<p>nel houses. My attention was ﬁxed upon every</p>
<p>object the most insupportable to the delicacy of the</p>
<p>human feelings. I saw how the ﬁne form of man</p>
<p>was degraded and wasted; I beheld the corruption</p>
<p>of death succeed to the blooming cheek of life; I</p>
<p>saw how the worm inherited the wonders of the</p>
<p>eye and brain. I paused, examining and analysing</p>
<p>all the minutiæ of causation, as exempliﬁed in the</p>
<p>change from life to death, and death to life, un-</p>
<p>til from the midst of this darkness a sudden light</p>
<p>broke in upon me — a light so brilliant and won-</p>
<p>drous, yet so simple, that while I became dizzy with</p>
<p>the immensity of the prospect which it illustrated, I</p>
<p>was surprised that among so many men of genius,</p>
<p>who had directed their inquiries towards the same</p>
<p>science, that I alone should be reserved to discover</p>
<p>so astonishing a secret.</p>
<p>Remember, I am not recording the vision of a</p>
<p>madman. The sun does not more certainly shine</p>
<p>in the heavens, than that which I now afﬁrm is</p>
<p>true. Some miracle might have produced it, yet</p>
<p>the stages of the discovery were distinct and proba-</p>
<p>ble. After days and nights of incredible labour and</p>
<p>fatigue, I succeeded in discovering the cause of</p>
<p>generation and life; nay, more, I became myself ca-</p>
<p>pable of bestowing animation upon lifeless matter.</p>
<p>The astonishment which I had at ﬁrst experi-</p>
<p>enced on this discovery soon gave place to delight</p>
<p>and rapture. After so much time spent in painful</p>
<p>labour, to arrive at once at the summit of my desires,</p>
<p>was the most gratifying consummation of my toils.</p>
<p>But this discovery was so great and overwhelming,</p>
<p>that all the steps by which I had been progressively</p>
<p>led to it were obliterated, and I beheld only the re-</p>
<p>sult. What had been the study and desire of the</p>
<p>wisest men since the creation of the world, was</p>
<p>now within my grasp. Not that, like a magic scene,</p>
<p>it all opened upon me at once: the information I</p>
<p>had obtained was of a nature rather to direct my</p>
<p>endeavours so soon as I should point them towards</p>
<p>the object of my search, than to exhibit that ob-</p>
<p>ject already accomplished. I was like the Arabian</p>
<p>who had been buried with the dead, and found a</p>
<p>passage to life aided only by one glimmering, and</p>
<p>seemingly ineffectual light.</p>
<p>I see by your eagerness, and the wonder and</p>
<p>hope which your eyes express, my friend, that you</p>
<p>expect to be informed of the secret with which I am</p>
<p>acquainted; that cannot be: listen patiently until the</p>
<p>end of my story, and you will easily perceive why I</p>
<p>am reserved upon that subject. I will not lead you</p>
<p>on, unguarded and ardent as I then was, to your</p>
<p>destruction and infallible misery. Learn from me, if</p>
<p>not by my precepts, at least by my example, how</p>
<p>dangerous is the acquirement of knowledge, and</p>
<p>how much happier that man is who believes his</p>
<p>native town to be the world, than he who aspires to</p>
<p>become greater than his nature will allow.</p>
<p>When I found so astonishing a power placed</p>
<p>within my hands, I hesitated a long time concerning</p>
<p>the manner in which I should employ it. Although</p>
<p>I possessed the capacity of bestowing animation,</p>
<p>yet to prepare a frame for the reception of it, with all</p>
<p>its intricacies of ﬁbres, muscles, and veins, still re-</p>
<p>mained a work of inconceivable difﬁculty and labour.</p>
<p>I doubted at ﬁrst whether I should attempt the cre-</p>
<p>ation of a being like myself or one of simpler orga-</p>
<p>nization; but my imagination was too much exalted</p>
<p>by my ﬁrst success to permit me to doubt of my</p>
<p>ability to give life to an animal as complex and won-</p>
<p>derful as man. The materials at present within my</p>
<p>command hardly appeared adequate to so arduous</p>
<p>an undertaking; but I doubted not that I should ulti-</p>
<p>mately succeed. I prepared myself for a multitude</p>
<p>of reverses; my operations might be incessantly baf-</p>
<p>ﬂed, and at last my work be imperfect: yet, when I</p>
<p>considered the improvement which every day takes</p>
<p>place in science and mechanics, I was encouraged</p>
<p>to hope my present attempts would at least lay the</p>
<p>foundations of future success. Nor could I con-</p>
<p>sider the magnitude and complexity of my plan as</p>
<p>any argument of its impracticability. It was with</p>
<p>these feelings that I began the creation of a human</p>
<p>being. As the minuteness of the parts formed a</p>
<p>great hindrance to my speed, I resolved, contrary</p>
<p>to my ﬁrst intention, to make the being of a gigantic</p>
<p>stature; that is to say, about eight feet in height,</p>
<p>and proportionably large. After having formed this</p>
<p>determination, and having spent some months in</p>
<p>successfully collecting and arranging my materials,</p>
<p>I began.</p>
<p>No one can conceive the variety of feelings</p>
<p>which bore me onwards, like a hurricane, in the ﬁrst</p>
<p>enthusiasm of success. Life and death appeared to</p>
<p>me ideal bounds, which I should ﬁrst break through,</p>
<p>and pour a torrent of light into our dark world. A new</p>
<p>species would bless me as its creator and source;</p>
<p>many happy and excellent natures would owe their</p>
<p>being to me. No father could claim the gratitude of</p>
<p>his child so completely as I should deserve their’s.</p>
<p>Pursuing these reﬂections, I thought, that if I could</p>
<p>bestow animation upon lifeless matter, I might in</p>
<p>process of time (although I now found it impossible)</p>
<p>renew life where death had apparently devoted the</p>
<p>body to corruption.</p>
<p>These thoughts supported my spirits, while I</p>
<p>pursued my undertaking with unremitting ardour.</p>
<p>My cheek had grown pale with study, and my person</p>
<p>had become emaciated with conﬁnement. Some-</p>
<p>times, on the very brink of certainty, I failed; yet still</p>
<p>I clung to the hope which the next day or the next</p>
<p>hour might realize. One secret which I alone pos-</p>
<p>sessed was the hope to which I had dedicated my-</p>
<p>self; and the moon gazed on my midnight labours,</p>
<p>while, with unrelaxed and breathless eagerness,</p>
<p>I pursued nature to her hiding places. Who shall</p>
<p>conceive the horrors of my secret toil, as I dab-</p>
<p>bled among the unhallowed damps of the grave,</p>
<p>or tortured the living animal to animate the lifeless</p>
<p>clay? My limbs now tremble, and my eyes swim with</p>
<p>the remembrance; but then a resistless, and almost</p>
<p>frantic impulse, urged me forward; I seemed to have</p>
<p>lost all soul or sensation but for this one pursuit. It</p>
<p>was indeed but a passing trance, that only made</p>
<p>me feel with renewed acuteness so soon as, the un-</p>
<p>natural stimulus ceasing to operate, I had returned</p>
<p>to my old habits. I collected bones from charnel</p>
<p>houses; and disturbed, with profane ﬁngers, the</p>
<p>tremendous secrets of the human frame. In a soli-</p>
<p>tary chamber, or rather cell, at the top of the house,</p>
<p>and separated from all the other apartments by a</p>
<p>gallery and staircase, I kept my workshop of ﬁlthy</p>
<p>creation; my eyeballs were starting from their sock-</p>
<p>ets in attending to the details of my employment.</p>
<p>The dissecting room and the slaughter-house fur-</p>
<p>nished many of my materials; and often did my</p>
<p>human nature turn with loathing from my occupa-</p>
<p>tion, whilst, still urged on by an eagerness which</p>
<p>perpetually increased, I brought my work near to a</p>
<p>conclusion.</p>
<p>The summer months passed while I was thus</p>
<p>engaged, heart and soul, in one pursuit. It was a</p>
<p>most beautiful season; never did the ﬁelds bestow</p>
<p>a more plentiful harvest, or the vines yield a more</p>
<p>luxuriant vintage: but my eyes were insensible to</p>
<p>the charms of nature. And the same feelings which</p>
<p>made me neglect the scenes around me caused</p>
<p>me also to forget those friends who were so many</p>
<p>miles absent, and whom I had not seen for so long a</p>
<p>time. I knew my silence disquieted them; and I well</p>
<p>remembered the words of my father: “I know that</p>
<p>while you are pleased with yourself, you will think</p>
<p>of us with affection, and we shall hear regularly</p>
<p>from you. You must pardon me, if I regard any</p>
<p>interruption in your correspondence as a proof that</p>
<p>your other duties are equally neglected.”</p>
<p>I knew well therefore what would be my fa-</p>
<p>ther’s feelings; but I could not tear my thoughts</p>
<p>from my employment, loathsome in itself, but which</p>
<p>had taken an irresistible hold of my imagination. I</p>
<p>wished, as it were, to procrastinate all that related to</p>
<p>my feelings of affection until the great object, which</p>
<p>swallowed up every habit of my nature, should be</p>
<p>completed.</p>
<p>I then thought that my father would be unjust if</p>
<p>he ascribed my neglect to vice, or faultiness on my</p>
<p>part; but I am now convinced that he was justiﬁed</p>
<p>in conceiving that I should not be altogether free</p>
<p>from blame. A human being in perfection ought</p>
<p>always to preserve a calm and peaceful mind, and</p>
<p>never to allow passion or a transitory desire to dis-</p>
<p>turb his tranquillity. I do not think that the pursuit</p>
<p>of knowledge is an exception to this rule. If the</p>
<p>study to which you apply yourself has a tendency</p>
<p>to weaken your affections, and to destroy your taste</p>
<p>for those simple pleasures in which no alloy can</p>
<p>possibly mix, then that study is certainly unlawful,</p>
<p>that is to say, not beﬁtting the human mind. If this</p>
<p>rule were always observed; if no man allowed any</p>
<p>pursuit whatsoever to interfere with the tranquillity</p>
<p>of his domestic affections, Greece had not been</p>
<p>enslaved; Cæsar would have spared his country;</p>
<p>America would have been discovered more gradu-</p>
<p>ally; and the empires of Mexico and Peru had not</p>
<p>been destroyed.</p>
<p>But I forget that I am moralizing in the most</p>
<p>interesting part of my tale; and your looks remind</p>
<p>me to proceed.</p>
<p>My father made no reproach in his letters; and</p>
<p>only took notice of my silence by inquiring into my</p>
<p>occupations more particularly than before. Win-</p>
<p>ter, spring, and summer, passed away during my</p>
<p>labours; but I did not watch the blossom or the</p>
<p>expanding leaves — sights which before always</p>
<p>yielded me supreme delight, so deeply was I en-</p>
<p>grossed in my occupation. The leaves of that year</p>
<p>had withered before my work drew near to a close;</p>
<p>and now every day shewed me more plainly how</p>
<p>well I had succeeded. But my enthusiasm was</p>
<p>checked by my anxiety, and I appeared rather like</p>
<p>one doomed by slavery to toil in the mines, or any</p>
<p>other unwholesome trade, than an artist occupied</p>
<p>by his favourite employment. Every night I was op-</p>
<p>pressed by a slow fever, and I became nervous to a</p>
<p>most painful degree; a disease that I regretted the</p>
<p>more because I had hitherto enjoyed most excellent</p>
<p>health, and had always boasted of the ﬁrmness of</p>
<p>my nerves. But I believed that exercise and amuse-</p>
<p>ment would soon drive away such symptoms; and I</p>
<p>promised myself both of these, when my creation</p>
<p>should be complete.</p>
<p>Chapter IV</p>
<p>IT was on a dreary night of November, that I beheld</p>
<p>the accomplishment of my toils. With an anxiety</p>
<p>that almost amounted to agony, I collected the in-</p>
<p>struments of life around me, that I might infuse a</p>
<p>spark of being into the lifeless thing that lay at my</p>
<p>feet. It was already one in the morning; the rain</p>
<p>pattered dismally against the panes, and my can-</p>
<p>dle was nearly burnt out, when, by the glimmer</p>
<p>of the half-extinguished light, I saw the dull yellow</p>
<p>eye of the creature open; it breathed hard, and a</p>
<p>convulsive motion agitated its limbs.</p>
<p>78</p>
<p>How can I describe my emotions at this catas-</p>
<p>trophe, or how delineate the wretch whom with such</p>
<p>inﬁnite pains and care I had endeavoured to form?</p>
<p>His limbs were in proportion, and I had selected his</p>
<p>features as beautiful. Beautiful! — Great God! His</p>
<p>yellow skin scarcely covered the work of muscles</p>
<p>and arteries beneath; his hair was of a lustrous</p>
<p>black, and ﬂowing; his teeth of a pearly whiteness;</p>
<p>but these luxuriances only formed a more horrid</p>
<p>contrast with his watery eyes, that seemed almost</p>
<p>of the same colour as the dun white sockets in</p>
<p>which they were set, his shrivelled complexion, and</p>
<p>straight black lips.</p>
<p>The different accidents of life are not so change-</p>
<p>able as the feelings of human nature. I had worked</p>
<p>hard for nearly two years, for the sole purpose of</p>
<p>infusing life into an inanimate body. For this I had</p>
<p>deprived myself of rest and health. I had desired</p>
<p>it with an ardour that far exceeded moderation; but</p>
<p>now that I had ﬁnished, the beauty of the dream</p>
<p>vanished, and breathless horror and disgust ﬁlled</p>
<p>my heart. Unable to endure the aspect of the being</p>
<p>I had created, I rushed out of the room, and contin-</p>
<p>ued a long time traversing my bed-chamber, unable</p>
<p>to compose my mind to sleep. At length lassitude</p>
<p>succeeded to the tumult I had before endured; and</p>
<p>I threw myself on the bed in my clothes, endeavour-</p>
<p>ing to seek a few moments of forgetfulness. But it</p>
<p>was in vain: I slept indeed, but I was disturbed by</p>
<p>the wildest dreams. I thought I saw Elizabeth, in the</p>
<p>bloom of health, walking in the streets of Ingolstadt.</p>
<p>Delighted and surprised, I embraced her; but as</p>
<p>I imprinted the ﬁrst kiss on her lips, they became</p>
<p>livid with the hue of death; her features appeared</p>
<p>to change, and I thought that I held the corpse of</p>
<p>my dead mother in my arms; a shroud enveloped</p>
<p>her form, and I saw the grave-worms crawling in</p>
<p>the folds of the ﬂannel. I started from my sleep</p>
<p>with horror; a cold dew covered my forehead, my</p>
<p>teeth chattered, and every limb became convulsed;</p>
<p>when, by the dim and yellow light of the moon, as</p>
<p>it forced its way through the window-shutters, I be-</p>
<p>held the wretch — the miserable monster whom I</p>
<p>had created. He held up the curtain of the bed; and</p>
<p>his eyes, if eyes they may be called, were ﬁxed on</p>
<p>me. His jaws opened, and he muttered some inar-</p>
<p>ticulate sounds, while a grin wrinkled his cheeks.</p>
<p>He might have spoken, but I did not hear; one hand</p>
<p>was stretched out, seemingly to detain me, but I</p>
<p>escaped, and rushed down stairs. I took refuge</p>
<p>in the court-yard belonging to the house which I</p>
<p>inhabited; where I remained during the rest of the</p>
<p>night, walking up and down in the greatest agita-</p>
<p>tion, listening attentively, catching and fearing each</p>
<p>sound as if it were to announce the approach of</p>
<p>the demoniacal corpse to which I had so miserably</p>
<p>given life.</p>
<p>Oh! no mortal could support the horror of that</p>
<p>countenance. A mummy again endued with anima-</p>
<p>tion could not be so hideous as that wretch. I had</p>
<p>gazed on him while unﬁnished; he was ugly then;</p>
<p>but when those muscles and joints were rendered</p>
<p>capable of motion, it became a thing such as even</p>
<p>Dante could not have conceived.</p>
<p>I passed the night wretchedly. Sometimes my</p>
<p>pulse beat so quickly and hardly, that I felt the pal-</p>
<p>pitation of every artery; at others, I nearly sank to</p>
<p>the ground through languor and extreme weakness.</p>
<p>Mingled with this horror, I felt the bitterness of dis-</p>
<p>appointment: dreams that had been my food and</p>
<p>pleasant rest for so long a space, were now be-</p>
<p>come a hell to me; and the change was so rapid,</p>
<p>the overthrow so complete!</p>
<p>Morning, dismal and wet, at length dawned,</p>
<p>and discovered to my sleepless and aching eyes</p>
<p>the church of Ingolstadt, its white steeple and clock,</p>
<p>which indicated the sixth hour. The porter opened</p>
<p>the gates of the court, which had that night been</p>
<p>my asylum, and I issued into the streets, pacing</p>
<p>them with quick steps, as if I sought to avoid the</p>
<p>wretch whom I feared every turning of the street</p>
<p>would present to my view. I did not dare return to</p>
<p>the apartment which I inhabited, but felt impelled to</p>
<p>hurry on, although wetted by the rain, which poured</p>
<p>from a black and comfortless sky.</p>
<p>I continued walking in this manner for some</p>
<p>time, endeavouring, by bodily exercise, to ease the</p>
<p>load that weighed upon my mind. I traversed the</p>
<p>streets, without any clear conception of where I</p>
<p>was, or what I was doing. My heart palpitated in</p>
<p>the sickness of fear; and I hurried on with irregular</p>
<p>steps, not daring to look about me:</p>
<p>Like one who, on a lonely road,</p>
<p>Doth walk in fear and dread,</p>
<p>And, having once turn’d round, walks on,</p>
<p>And turns no more his head;</p>
<p>Because he knows a frightful ﬁend</p>
<p>Doth close behind him tread.</p>
<p>Continuing thus, I came at length opposite to</p>
<p>the inn at which the various diligences and car-</p>
<p>riages usually stopped. Here I paused, I knew not</p>
<p>why; but I remained some minutes with my eyes</p>
<p>ﬁxed on a coach that was coming towards me from</p>
<p>the other end of the street. As it drew nearer, I</p>
<p>observed that it was the Swiss diligence: it stopped</p>
<p>just where I was standing; and, on the door being</p>
<p>opened, I perceived Henry Clerval, who, on seeing</p>
<p>me, instantly sprung out. “My dear Frankenstein,”</p>
<p>exclaimed he, “how glad I am to see you! how for-</p>
<p>tunate that you should be here at the very moment</p>
<p>of my alighting!”</p>
<p>Nothing could equal my delight on seeing Cler-</p>
<p>val; his presence brought back to my thoughts my</p>
<p>father, Elizabeth, and all those scenes of home so</p>
<p>dear to my recollection. I grasped his hand, and</p>
<p>in a moment forgot my horror and misfortune; I felt</p>
<p>suddenly, and for the ﬁrst time during many months,</p>
<p>calm and serene joy. I welcomed my friend, there-</p>
<p>fore, in the most cordial manner, and we walked</p>
<p>towards my college. Clerval continued talking for</p>
<p>some time about our mutual friends, and his own</p>
<p>good fortune in being permitted to come to Ingol-</p>
<p>stadt. “You may easily believe,” said he, “how great</p>
<p>was the difﬁculty to persuade my father that it was</p>
<p>not absolutely necessary for a merchant not to un-</p>
<p>derstand any thing except book-keeping; and, in-</p>
<p>deed, I believe I left him incredulous to the last, for</p>
<p>his constant answer to my unwearied entreaties</p>
<p>was the same as that of the Dutch schoolmaster</p>
<p>in the Vicar of Wakeﬁeld: ‘I have ten thousand</p>
<p>ﬂorins a year without Greek, I eat heartily without</p>
<p>Greek.’ But his affection for me at length overcame</p>
<p>his dislike of learning, and he has permitted me</p>
<p>to undertake a voyage of discovery to the land of</p>
<p>knowledge.”</p>
<p>“It gives me the greatest delight to see you;</p>
<p>but tell me how you left my father, brothers, and</p>
<p>Elizabeth.”</p>
<p>“Very well, and very happy, only a little uneasy</p>
<p>that they hear from you so seldom. By the bye,</p>
<p>I mean to lecture you a little upon their account</p>
<p>myself. — But, my dear Frankenstein,” continued</p>
<p>he, stopping short, and gazing full in my face, “I did</p>
<p>not before remark how very ill you appear; so thin</p>
<p>and pale; you look as if you had been watching for</p>
<p>several nights.”</p>
<p>“You have guessed right; I have lately been so</p>
<p>deeply engaged in one occupation, that I have not</p>
<p>allowed myself sufﬁcient rest, as you see: but I</p>
<p>hope, I sincerely hope, that all these employments</p>
<p>are now at an end, and that I am at length free.”</p>
<p>I trembled excessively; I could not endure to</p>
<p>think of, and far less to allude to the occurrences</p>
<p>of the preceding night. I walked with a quick pace,</p>
<p>and we soon arrived at my college. I then reﬂected,</p>
<p>and the thought made me shiver, that the creature</p>
<p>whom I had left in my apartment might still be there,</p>
<p>alive, and walking about. I dreaded to behold this</p>
<p>monster; but I feared still more that Henry should</p>
<p>see him. Entreating him therefore to remain a few</p>
<p>minutes at the bottom of the stairs, I darted up</p>
<p>towards my own room. My hand was already on the</p>
<p>lock of the door before I recollected myself. I then</p>
<p>paused; and a cold shivering came over me. I threw</p>
<p>the door forcibly open, as children are accustomed</p>
<p>to do when they expect a spectre to stand in waiting</p>
<p>for them on the other side; but nothing appeared.</p>
<p>I stepped fearfully in: the apartment was empty;</p>
<p>and my bedroom was also freed from its hideous</p>
<p>guest. I could hardly believe that so great a good-</p>
<p>fortune could have befallen me; but when I became</p>
<p>assured that my enemy had indeed ﬂed, I clapped</p>
<p>my hands for joy, and ran down to Clerval.</p>
<p>We ascended into my room, and the servant</p>
<p>presently brought breakfast; but I was unable to con-</p>
<p>tain myself. It was not joy only that possessed me;</p>
<p>I felt my ﬂesh tingle with excess of sensitiveness,</p>
<p>and my pulse beat rapidly. I was unable to remain</p>
<p>for a single instant in the same place; I jumped over</p>
<p>the chairs, clapped my hands, and laughed aloud.</p>
<p>Clerval at ﬁrst attributed my unusual spirits to joy</p>
<p>on his arrival; but when he observed me more at-</p>
<p>tentively, he saw a wildness in my eyes for which</p>
<p>he could not account; and my loud, unrestrained,</p>
<p>heartless laughter, frightened and astonished him.</p>
<p>“My dear Victor,” cried he, “what, for God’s sake,</p>
<p>is the matter? Do not laugh in that manner. How ill</p>
<p>you are! What is the cause of all this?”</p>
<p>“Do not ask me,” cried I, putting my hands be-</p>
<p>fore my eyes, for I thought I saw the dreaded spec-</p>
<p>tre glide into the room; “he can tell. — Oh, save</p>
<p>me! save me!” I imagined that the monster seized</p>
<p>me; I struggled furiously, and fell down in a ﬁt.</p>
<p>Poor Clerval! what must have been his feelings?</p>
<p>A meeting, which he anticipated with such joy, so</p>
<p>strangely turned to bitterness. But I was not the</p>
<p>witness of his grief; for I was lifeless, and did not</p>
<p>recover my senses for a long, long time.</p>
<p>This was the commencement of a nervous fever,</p>
<p>which conﬁned me for several months. During</p>
<p>all that time Henry was my only nurse. I after-</p>
<p>wards learned that, knowing my father’s advanced</p>
<p>age, and unﬁtness for so long a journey, and how</p>
<p>wretched my sickness would make Elizabeth, he</p>
<p>spared them this grief by concealing the extent of</p>
<p>my disorder. He knew that I could not have a more</p>
<p>kind and attentive nurse than himself; and, ﬁrm in</p>
<p>the hope he felt of my recovery, he did not doubt</p>
<p>that, instead of doing harm, he performed the kind-</p>
<p>est action that he could towards them.</p>
<p>But I was in reality very ill; and surely noth-</p>
<p>ing but the unbounded and unremitting attentions</p>
<p>of my friend could have restored me to life. The</p>
<p>form of the monster on whom I had bestowed ex-</p>
<p>istence was for ever before my eyes, and I raved</p>
<p>incessantly concerning him. Doubtless my words</p>
<p>surprised Henry: he at ﬁrst believed them to be</p>
<p>the wanderings of my disturbed imagination; but</p>
<p>the pertinacity with which I continually recurred to</p>
<p>the same subject persuaded him that my disorder</p>
<p>indeed owed its origin to some uncommon and ter-</p>
<p>rible event.</p>
<p>By very slow degrees, and with frequent re-</p>
<p>lapses, that alarmed and grieved my friend, I recov-</p>
<p>ered. I remember the ﬁrst time I became capable of</p>
<p>observing outward objects with any kind of pleasure,</p>
<p>I perceived that the fallen leaves had disappeared,</p>
<p>and that the young buds were shooting forth from</p>
<p>the trees that shaded my window. It was a divine</p>
<p>spring; and the season contributed greatly to my</p>
<p>convalescence. I felt also sentiments of joy and af-</p>
<p>fection revive in my bosom; my gloom disappeared,</p>
<p>and in a short time I became as cheerful as before</p>
<p>I was attacked by the fatal passion.</p>
<p>“Dearest Clerval,” exclaimed I, “how kind, how</p>
<p>very good you are to me. This whole winter, instead</p>
<p>of being spent in study, as you promised yourself,</p>
<p>has been consumed in my sick room. How shall I</p>
<p>ever repay you? I feel the greatest remorse for the</p>
<p>disappointment of which I have been the occasion;</p>
<p>but you will forgive me.”</p>
<p>“You will repay me entirely, if you do not discom-</p>
<p>pose yourself, but get well as fast as you can; and</p>
<p>since you appear in such good spirits, I may speak</p>
<p>to you on one subject, may I not?”</p>
<p>I trembled. One subject! what could it be?</p>
<p>Could he allude to an object on whom I dared not</p>
<p>even think?</p>
<p>“Compose yourself,” said Clerval, who observed</p>
<p>my change of colour, “I will not mention it, if it agi-</p>
<p>tates you; but your father and cousin would be very</p>
<p>happy if they received a letter from you in your own</p>
<p>hand-writing. They hardly know how ill you have</p>
<p>been, and are uneasy at your long silence.”</p>
<p>“Is that all? my dear Henry. How could you</p>
<p>suppose that my ﬁrst thought would not ﬂy towards</p>
<p>those dear, dear friends whom I love, and who are</p>
<p>so deserving of my love.”</p>
<p>“If this is your present temper, my friend, you</p>
<p>will perhaps be glad to see a letter that has been</p>
<p>lying here some days for you: it is from your cousin,</p>
<p>I believe.”</p>
<p>Chapter V</p>
<p>CLERVAL then put the following letter into my hands.</p>
<p>“To V. FRANKENSTEIN.</p>
<p>“MY DEAR COUSIN,</p>
<p>“I cannot describe to you the uneasiness we</p>
<p>have all felt concerning your health. We cannot</p>
<p>help imagining that your friend Clerval conceals the</p>
<p>extent of your disorder: for it is now several months</p>
<p>since we have seen your hand-writing; and all this</p>
<p>time you have been obliged to dictate your letters</p>
<p>91</p>
<p>to Henry. Surely, Victor, you must have been ex-</p>
<p>ceedingly ill; and this makes us all very wretched,</p>
<p>as much so nearly as after the death of your dear</p>
<p>mother. My uncle was almost persuaded that you</p>
<p>were indeed dangerously ill, and could hardly be</p>
<p>restrained from undertaking a journey to Ingolstadt.</p>
<p>Clerval always writes that you are getting better; I</p>
<p>eagerly hope that you will conﬁrm this intelligence</p>
<p>soon in your own hand-writing; for indeed, indeed,</p>
<p>Victor, we are all very miserable on this account.</p>
<p>Relieve us from this fear, and we shall be the hap-</p>
<p>piest creatures in the world. Your father’s health is</p>
<p>now so vigorous, that he appears ten years younger</p>
<p>since last winter. Ernest also is so much improved,</p>
<p>that you would hardly know him: he is now nearly</p>
<p>sixteen, and has lost that sickly appearance which</p>
<p>he had some years ago; he is grown quite robust</p>
<p>and active.</p>
<p>“My uncle and I conversed a long time last night</p>
<p>about what profession Ernest should follow. His</p>
<p>constant illness when young has deprived him of</p>
<p>the habits of application; and now that he enjoys</p>
<p>good health, he is continually in the open air, climb-</p>
<p>ing the hills, or rowing on the lake. I therefore pro-</p>
<p>posed that he should be a farmer; which you know,</p>
<p>Cousin, is a favourite scheme of mine. A farmer’s</p>
<p>is a very healthy happy life; and the least hurtful,</p>
<p>or rather the most beneﬁcial profession of any. My</p>
<p>uncle had an idea of his being educated as an ad-</p>
<p>vocate, that through his interest he might become</p>
<p>a judge. But, besides that he is not at all ﬁtted for</p>
<p>such an occupation, it is certainly more creditable to</p>
<p>cultivate the earth for the sustenance of man, than</p>
<p>to be the conﬁdant, and sometimes the accomplice,</p>
<p>of his vices; which is the profession of a lawyer. I</p>
<p>said, that the employments of a prosperous farmer,</p>
<p>if they were not a more honourable, they were at</p>
<p>least a happier species of occupation than that of</p>
<p>a judge, whose misfortune it was always to med-</p>
<p>dle with the dark side of human nature. My uncle</p>
<p>smiled, and said, that I ought to be an advocate</p>
<p>myself, which put an end to the conversation on</p>
<p>that subject.</p>
<p>“And now I must tell you a little story that will</p>
<p>please, and perhaps amuse you. Do you not re-</p>
<p>member Justine Moritz? Probably you do not; I</p>
<p>will relate her history, therefore, in a few words.</p>
<p>Madame Moritz, her mother, was a widow with</p>
<p>four children, of whom Justine was the third. This</p>
<p>girl had always been the favourite of her father;</p>
<p>but, through a strange perversity, her mother could</p>
<p>not endure her, and, after the death of M. Moritz,</p>
<p>treated her very ill. My aunt observed this; and,</p>
<p>when Justine was twelve years of age, prevailed on</p>
<p>her mother to allow her to live at her house. The</p>
<p>republican institutions of our country have produced</p>
<p>simpler and happier manners than those which pre-</p>
<p>vail in the great monarchies that surround it. Hence</p>
<p>there is less distinction between the several classes</p>
<p>of its inhabitants; and the lower orders being nei-</p>
<p>ther so poor nor so despised, their manners are</p>
<p>more reﬁned and moral. A servant in Geneva does</p>
<p>not mean the same thing as a servant in France</p>
<p>and England. Justine, thus received in our family,</p>
<p>learned the duties of a servant; a condition which,</p>
<p>in our fortunate country, does not include the idea of</p>
<p>ignorance, and a sacriﬁce of the dignity of a human</p>
<p>being.</p>
<p>“After what I have said, I dare say you well re-</p>
<p>member the heroine of my little tale: for Justine was</p>
<p>a great favourite of your’s; and I recollect you once</p>
<p>remarked, that if you were in an ill humour, one</p>
<p>glance from Justine could dissipate it, for the same</p>
<p>reason that Ariosto gives concerning the beauty of</p>
<p>Angelica — she looked so frank-hearted and happy.</p>
<p>My aunt conceived a great attachment for her, by</p>
<p>which she was induced to give her an education su-</p>
<p>perior to that which she had at ﬁrst intended. This</p>
<p>beneﬁt was fully repaid; Justine was the most grate-</p>
<p>ful little creature in the world: I do not mean that she</p>
<p>made any professions, I never heard one pass her</p>
<p>lips; but you could see by her eyes that she almost</p>
<p>adored her protectress. Although her disposition</p>
<p>was gay, and in many respects inconsiderate, yet</p>
<p>she paid the greatest attention to every gesture of</p>
<p>my aunt. She thought her the model of all excel-</p>
<p>lence, and endeavoured to imitate her phraseology</p>
<p>and manners, so that even now she often reminds</p>
<p>me of her.</p>
<p>“When my dearest aunt died, every one was</p>
<p>too much occupied in their own grief to notice poor</p>
<p>Justine, who had attended her during her illness</p>
<p>with the most anxious affection. Poor Justine was</p>
<p>very ill; but other trials were reserved for her.</p>
<p>“One by one, her brothers and sister died; and</p>
<p>her mother, with the exception of her neglected</p>
<p>daughter, was left childless. The conscience of</p>
<p>the woman was troubled; she began to think that</p>
<p>the deaths of her favourites was a judgment from</p>
<p>heaven to chastise her partiality. She was a Roman</p>
<p>Catholic; and I believe her confessor conﬁrmed the</p>
<p>idea which she had conceived. Accordingly, a few</p>
<p>months after your departure for Ingolstadt, Justine</p>
<p>was called home by her repentant mother. Poor</p>
<p>girl! she wept when she quitted our house: she was</p>
<p>much altered since the death of my aunt; grief had</p>
<p>given softness and a winning mildness to her man-</p>
<p>ners, which had before been remarkable for vivacity.</p>
<p>Nor was her residence at her mother’s house of a</p>
<p>nature to restore her gaiety. The poor woman was</p>
<p>very vacillating in her repentance. She sometimes</p>
<p>begged Justine to forgive her unkindness, but much</p>
<p>oftener accused her of having caused the deaths of</p>
<p>her brothers and sister. Perpetual fretting at length</p>
<p>threw Madame Moritz into a decline, which at ﬁrst</p>
<p>increased her irritability, but she is now at peace</p>
<p>for ever. She died on the ﬁrst approach of cold</p>
<p>weather, at the beginning of this last winter. Justine</p>
<p>has returned to us; and I assure you I love her ten-</p>
<p>derly. She is very clever and gentle, and extremely</p>
<p>pretty; as I mentioned before, her mien and her</p>
<p>expressions continually remind me of my dear aunt.</p>
<p>“I must say also a few words to you, my dear</p>
<p>cousin, of little darling William. I wish you could see</p>
<p>him; he is very tall of his age, with sweet laughing</p>
<p>blue eyes, dark eye-lashes, and curling hair. When</p>
<p>he smiles, two little dimples appear on each cheek,</p>
<p>which are rosy with health. He has already had one</p>
<p>or two little wives, but Louisa Biron is his favourite,</p>
<p>a pretty little girl of ﬁve years of age.</p>
<p>“Now, dear Victor, I dare say you wish to be</p>
<p>indulged in a little gossip concerning the good peo-</p>
<p>ple of Geneva. The pretty Miss Mansﬁeld has al-</p>
<p>ready received the congratulatory visits on her ap-</p>
<p>proaching marriage with a young Englishman, John</p>
<p>Melbourne, Esq. Her ugly sister, Manon, married</p>
<p>M. Duvillard, the rich banker, last autumn. Your</p>
<p>favourite schoolfellow, Louis Manoir, has suffered</p>
<p>several misfortunes since the departure of Clerval</p>
<p>from Geneva. But he has already recovered his spir-</p>
<p>its, and is reported to be on the point of marrying a</p>
<p>very lively pretty Frenchwoman, Madame Tavernier.</p>
<p>She is a widow, and much older than Manoir; but</p>
<p>she is very much admired, and a favourite with</p>
<p>every body.</p>
<p>“I have written myself into good spirits, dear</p>
<p>cousin; yet I cannot conclude without again anx-</p>
<p>iously inquiring concerning your health. Dear Victor,</p>
<p>if you are not very ill, write yourself, and make your</p>
<p>father and all of us happy; or —— I cannot bear</p>
<p>to think of the other side of the question; my tears</p>
<p>already ﬂow. Adieu, my dearest cousin.”</p>
<p>“ELIZABETH LAVENZA.</p>
<p>“Geneva, March 18th, 17— .”</p>
<p>“Dear, dear Elizabeth!” I exclaimed when I had</p>
<p>read her letter, “I will write instantly, and relieve</p>
<p>them from the anxiety they must feel.” I wrote, and</p>
<p>this exertion greatly fatigued me; but my convales-</p>
<p>cence had commenced, and proceeded regularly.</p>
<p>In another fortnight I was able to leave my chamber.</p>
<p>One of my ﬁrst duties on my recovery was to</p>
<p>introduce Clerval to the several professors of the</p>
<p>university. In doing this, I underwent a kind of rough</p>
<p>usage, ill beﬁtting the wounds that my mind had</p>
<p>sustained. Ever since the fatal night, the end of my</p>
<p>labours, and the beginning of my misfortunes, I had</p>
<p>conceived a violent antipathy even to the name of</p>
<p>natural philosophy. When I was otherwise quite re-</p>
<p>stored to health, the sight of a chemical instrument</p>
<p>would renew all the agony of my nervous symptoms.</p>
<p>Henry saw this, and had removed all my apparatus</p>
<p>from my view. He had also changed my apartment;</p>
<p>for he perceived that I had acquired a dislike for the</p>
<p>room which had previously been my laboratory. But</p>
<p>these cares of Clerval were made of no avail when I</p>
<p>visited the professors. M. Waldman inﬂicted torture</p>
<p>when he praised, with kindness and warmth, the</p>
<p>astonishing progress I had made in the sciences.</p>
<p>He soon perceived that I disliked the subject; but,</p>
<p>not guessing the real cause, he attributed my feel-</p>
<p>ings to modesty, and changed the subject from my</p>
<p>improvement to the science itself, with a desire, as</p>
<p>I evidently saw, of drawing me out. What could I</p>
<p>do? He meant to please, and he tormented me.</p>
<p>I felt as if he had placed carefully, one by one, in</p>
<p>my view those instruments which were to be after-</p>
<p>wards used in putting me to a slow and cruel death.</p>
<p>I writhed under his words, yet dared not exhibit the</p>
<p>pain I felt. Clerval, whose eyes and feelings were</p>
<p>always quick in discerning the sensations of others,</p>
<p>declined the subject, alleging, in excuse, his total ig-</p>
<p>norance; and the conversation took a more general</p>
<p>turn. I thanked my friend from my heart, but I did</p>
<p>not speak. I saw plainly that he was surprised, but</p>
<p>he never attempted to draw my secret from me; and</p>
<p>although I loved him with a mixture of affection and</p>
<p>reverence that knew no bounds, yet I could never</p>
<p>persuade myself to conﬁde to him that event which</p>
<p>was so often present to my recollection, but which</p>
<p>I feared the detail to another would only impress</p>
<p>more deeply.</p>
<p>M. Krempe was not equally docile; and in my</p>
<p>condition at that time, of almost insupportable sen-</p>
<p>sitiveness, his harsh blunt encomiums gave me</p>
<p>even more pain than the benevolent approbation</p>
<p>of M. Waldman. “D—n the fellow!” cried he; “why,</p>
<p>M. Clerval, I assure you he has outstript us all. Aye,</p>
<p>stare if you please; but it is nevertheless true. A</p>
<p>youngster who, but a few years ago, believed Cor-</p>
<p>nelius Agrippa as ﬁrmly as the gospel, has now set</p>
<p>himself at the head of the university; and if he is</p>
<p>not soon pulled down, we shall all be out of coun-</p>
<p>tenance. — Aye, aye,” continued he, observing my</p>
<p>face expressive of suffering, “M. Frankenstein is</p>
<p>modest; an excellent quality in a young man. Young</p>
<p>men should be difﬁdent of themselves, you know,</p>
<p>M. Clerval; I was myself when young: but that wears</p>
<p>out in a very short time.”</p>
<p>M. Krempe had now commenced an eulogy on</p>
<p>himself, which happily turned the conversation from</p>
<p>a subject that was so annoying to me.</p>
<p>Clerval was no natural philosopher. His imag-</p>
<p>ination was too vivid for the minutiæ of science.</p>
<p>Languages were his principal study; and he sought,</p>
<p>by acquiring their elements, to open a ﬁeld for self-</p>
<p>instruction on his return to Geneva. Persian, Ara-</p>
<p>bic, and Hebrew, gained his attention, after he had</p>
<p>made himself perfectly master of Greek and Latin.</p>
<p>For my own part, idleness had ever been irksome</p>
<p>to me; and now that I wished to ﬂy from reﬂection,</p>
<p>and hated my former studies, I felt great relief in</p>
<p>being the fellow-pupil with my friend, and found not</p>
<p>only instruction but consolation in the works of the</p>
<p>orientalists. Their melancholy is soothing, and their</p>
<p>joy elevating to a degree I never experienced in</p>
<p>studying the authors of any other country. When</p>
<p>you read their writings, life appears to consist in a</p>
<p>warm sun and garden of roses, — in the smiles and</p>
<p>frowns of a fair enemy, and the ﬁre that consumes</p>
<p>your own heart. How different from the manly and</p>
<p>heroical poetry of Greece and Rome.</p>
<p>Summer passed away in these occupations,</p>
<p>and my return to Geneva was ﬁxed for the latter</p>
<p>end of autumn; but being delayed by several ac-</p>
<p>cidents, winter and snow arrived, the roads were</p>
<p>deemed impassable, and my journey was retarded</p>
<p>until the ensuing spring. I felt this delay very bitterly;</p>
<p>for I longed to see my native town, and my beloved</p>
<p>friends. My return had only been delayed so long</p>
<p>from an unwillingness to leave Clerval in a strange</p>
<p>place, before he had become acquainted with any</p>
<p>of its inhabitants. The winter, however, was spent</p>
<p>cheerfully; and although the spring was uncom-</p>
<p>monly late, when it came, its beauty compensated</p>
<p>for its dilatoriness.</p>
<p>The month of May had already commenced,</p>
<p>and I expected the letter daily which was to ﬁx</p>
<p>the date of my departure, when Henry proposed a</p>
<p>pedestrian tour in the environs of Ingolstadt that I</p>
<p>might bid a personal farewell to the country I had</p>
<p>so long inhabited. I acceded with pleasure to this</p>
<p>proposition: I was fond of exercise, and Clerval had</p>
<p>always been my favourite companion in the rambles</p>
<p>of this nature that I had taken among the scenes of</p>
<p>my native country.</p>
<p>We passed a fortnight in these perambulations:</p>
<p>my health and spirits had long been restored, and</p>
<p>they gained additional strength from the salubrious</p>
<p>air I breathed, the natural incidents of our progress,</p>
<p>and the conversation of my friend. Study had be-</p>
<p>fore secluded me from the intercourse of my fellow-</p>
<p>creatures, and rendered me unsocial; but Clerval</p>
<p>called forth the better feelings of my heart; he again</p>
<p>taught me to love the aspect of nature, and the</p>
<p>cheerful faces of children. Excellent friend! how</p>
<p>sincerely did you love me, and endeavour to elevate</p>
<p>my mind, until it was on a level with your own. A</p>
<p>selﬁsh pursuit had cramped and narrowed me, until</p>
<p>your gentleness and affection warmed and opened</p>
<p>my senses; I became the same happy creature who,</p>
<p>a few years ago, loving and beloved by all, had no</p>
<p>sorrow or care. When happy, inanimate nature had</p>
<p>the power of bestowing on me the most delightful</p>
<p>sensations. A serene sky and verdant ﬁelds ﬁlled</p>
<p>me with ecstacy. The present season was indeed</p>
<p>divine; the ﬂowers of spring bloomed in the hedges,</p>
<p>while those of summer were already in bud: I was</p>
<p>undisturbed by thoughts which during the preced-</p>
<p>ing year had pressed upon me, notwithstanding my</p>
<p>endeavours to throw them off, with an invincible</p>
<p>burden.</p>
<p>Henry rejoiced in my gaiety, and sincerely sym-</p>
<p>pathized in my feelings: he exerted himself to</p>
<p>amuse me, while he expressed the sensations that</p>
<p>ﬁlled his soul. The resources of his mind on this</p>
<p>occasion were truly astonishing: his conversation</p>
<p>was full of imagination; and very often, in imitation</p>
<p>of the Persian and Arabic writers, he invented tales</p>
<p>of wonderful fancy and passion. At other times</p>
<p>he repeated my favourite poems, or drew me out</p>
<p>into arguments, which he supported with great</p>
<p>ingenuity.</p>
<p>We returned to our college on a Sunday after-</p>
<p>noon: the peasants were dancing, and every one</p>
<p>we met appeared gay and happy. My own spirits</p>
<p>were high, and I bounded along with feelings of</p>
<p>unbridled joy and hilarity.</p>
<p>Chapter VI</p>
<p>ON my return, I found the following letter from my</p>
<p>father:</p>
<p>“To V. FRANKENSTEIN.</p>
<p>“MY DEAR VICTOR,</p>
<p>“You have probably waited impatiently for a let-</p>
<p>ter to ﬁx the date of your return to us; and I was</p>
<p>at ﬁrst tempted to write only a few lines, merely</p>
<p>mentioning the day on which I should expect you.</p>
<p>But that would be a cruel kindness, and I dare not</p>
<p>do it. What would be your surprise, my son, when</p>
<p>107</p>
<p>you expected a happy and gay welcome, to behold,</p>
<p>on the contrary, tears and wretchedness? And how,</p>
<p>Victor, can I relate our misfortune? Absence cannot</p>
<p>have rendered you callous to our joys and griefs;</p>
<p>and how shall I inﬂict pain on an absent child? I</p>
<p>wish to prepare you for the woeful news, but I know</p>
<p>it is impossible; even now your eye skims over the</p>
<p>page, to seek the words which are to convey to you</p>
<p>the horrible tidings.</p>
<p>“William is dead! — that sweet child, whose</p>
<p>smiles delighted and warmed my heart, who was</p>
<p>so gentle, yet so gay! Victor, he is murdered!</p>
<p>“I will not attempt to console you; but will simply</p>
<p>relate the circumstances of the transaction.</p>
<p>“Last Thursday (May 7th) I, my niece, and your</p>
<p>two brothers, went to walk in Plainpalais. The</p>
<p>evening was warm and serene, and we prolonged</p>
<p>our walk farther than usual. It was already dusk</p>
<p>before we thought of returning; and then we dis-</p>
<p>covered that William and Ernest, who had gone on</p>
<p>before, were not to be found. We accordingly rested</p>
<p>on a seat until they should return. Presently Ernest</p>
<p>came, and inquired if we had seen his brother:</p>
<p>he said, that they had been playing together, that</p>
<p>William had run away to hide himself, and that he</p>
<p>vainly sought for him, and afterwards waited for him</p>
<p>a long time, but that he did not return.</p>
<p>“This account rather alarmed us, and we con-</p>
<p>tinued to search for him until night fell, when Eliza-</p>
<p>beth conjectured that he might have returned to the</p>
<p>house. He was not there. We returned again, with</p>
<p>torches; for I could not rest, when I thought that my</p>
<p>sweet boy had lost himself, and was exposed to all</p>
<p>the damps and dews of night: Elizabeth also suf-</p>
<p>fered extreme anguish. About ﬁve in the morning I</p>
<p>discovered my lovely boy, whom the night before I</p>
<p>had seen blooming and active in health, stretched</p>
<p>on the grass livid and motionless: the print of the</p>
<p>murderer’s ﬁnger was on his neck.</p>
<p>“He was conveyed home, and the anguish that</p>
<p>was visible in my countenance betrayed the secret</p>
<p>to Elizabeth. She was very earnest to see the</p>
<p>corpse. At ﬁrst I attempted to prevent her; but she</p>
<p>persisted, and entering the room where it lay, hastily</p>
<p>examined the neck of the victim, and clasping her</p>
<p>hands exclaimed, ‘O God! I have murdered my</p>
<p>darling infant!’</p>
<p>“She fainted, and was restored with extreme</p>
<p>difﬁculty. When she again lived, it was only to weep</p>
<p>and sigh. She told me, that that same evening</p>
<p>William had teazed her to let him wear a very valu-</p>
<p>able miniature that she possessed of your mother.</p>
<p>This picture is gone, and was doubtless the temp-</p>
<p>tation which urged the murderer to the deed. We</p>
<p>have no trace of him at present, although our exer-</p>
<p>tions to discover him are unremitted; but they will</p>
<p>not restore my beloved William.</p>
<p>“Come, dearest Victor; you alone can console</p>
<p>Elizabeth. She weeps continually, and accuses</p>
<p>herself unjustly as the cause of his death; her words</p>
<p>pierce my heart. We are all unhappy; but will not</p>
<p>that be an additional motive for you, my son, to</p>
<p>return and be our comforter? Your dear mother!</p>
<p>Alas, Victor! I now say, Thank God she did not</p>
<p>live to witness the cruel, miserable death of her</p>
<p>youngest darling!</p>
<p>“Come, Victor; not brooding thoughts of veng-</p>
<p>eance against the assassin, but with feelings of</p>
<p>peace and gentleness, that will heal, instead of fes-</p>
<p>tering the wounds of our minds. Enter the house of</p>
<p>mourning, my friend, but with kindness and affec-</p>
<p>tion for those who love you, and not with hatred for</p>
<p>your enemies.</p>
<p>“Your affectionate and afﬂicted father,</p>
<p>“ALPHONSE FRANKENSTEIN.</p>
<p>“Geneva, May 12th, 17—.”</p>
<p>Clerval, who had watched my countenance as</p>
<p>I read this letter, was surprised to observe the de-</p>
<p>spair that succeeded to the joy I at ﬁrst expressed</p>
<p>on receiving news from my friends. I threw the letter</p>
<p>on the table, and covered my face with my hands.</p>
<p>“My dear Frankenstein,” exclaimed Henry, when</p>
<p>he perceived me weep with bitterness, “are you</p>
<p>always to be unhappy? My dear friend, what has</p>
<p>happened?”</p>
<p>I motioned to him to take up the letter, while I</p>
<p>walked up and down the room in the extremest agi-</p>
<p>tation. Tears also gushed from the eyes of Clerval,</p>
<p>as he read the account of my misfortune.</p>
<p>“I can offer you no consolation, my friend,” said</p>
<p>he; “your disaster is irreparable. What do you intend</p>
<p>to do?”</p>
<p>“To go instantly to Geneva: come with me,</p>
<p>Henry, to order the horses.”</p>
<p>During our walk, Clerval endeavoured to raise</p>
<p>my spirits. He did not do this by common topics of</p>
<p>consolation, but by exhibiting the truest sympathy.</p>
<p>“Poor William!” said he, “that dear child; he now</p>
<p>sleeps with his angel mother. His friends mourn</p>
<p>and weep, but he is at rest: he does not now feel</p>
<p>the murderer’s grasp; a sod covers his gentle form,</p>
<p>and he knows no pain. He can no longer be a</p>
<p>ﬁt subject for pity; the survivors are the greatest</p>
<p>sufferers, and for them time is the only consolation.</p>
<p>Those maxims of the Stoics, that death was no evil,</p>
<p>and that the mind of man ought to be superior to</p>
<p>despair on the eternal absence of a beloved object,</p>
<p>ought not to be urged. Even Cato wept over the</p>
<p>dead body of his brother.”</p>
<p>Clerval spoke thus as we hurried through the</p>
<p>streets; the words impressed themselves on my</p>
<p>mind, and I remembered them afterwards in soli-</p>
<p>tude. But now, as soon as the horses arrived, I</p>
<p>hurried into a cabriole, and bade farewell to my</p>
<p>friend.</p>
<p>My journey was very melancholy. At ﬁrst I</p>
<p>wished to hurry on, for I longed to console and</p>
<p>sympathize with my loved and sorrowing friends;</p>
<p>but when I drew near my native town, I slackened</p>
<p>my progress. I could hardly sustain the multitude</p>
<p>of feelings that crowded into my mind. I passed</p>
<p>through scenes familiar to my youth, but which I</p>
<p>had not seen for nearly six years. How altered ev-</p>
<p>ery thing might be during that time? One sudden</p>
<p>and desolating change had taken place; but a thou-</p>
<p>sand little circumstances might have by degrees</p>
<p>worked other alterations which, although they were</p>
<p>done more tranquilly, might not be the less decisive.</p>
<p>Fear overcame me; I dared not advance, dreading</p>
<p>a thousand nameless evils that made me tremble,</p>
<p>although I was unable to deﬁne them.</p>
<p>I remained two days at Lausanne, in this painful</p>
<p>state of mind. I contemplated the lake: the wa-</p>
<p>ters were placid; all around was calm, and the</p>
<p>snowy mountains, “the palaces of nature,” were</p>
<p>not changed. By degrees the calm and heavenly</p>
<p>scene restored me, and I continued my journey</p>
<p>towards Geneva.</p>
<p>The road ran by the side of the lake, which</p>
<p>became narrower as I approached my native town.</p>
<p>I discovered more distinctly the black sides of Jura,</p>
<p>and the bright summit of Mont Blânc; I wept like a</p>
<p>child: “Dear mountains! my own beautiful lake! how</p>
<p>do you welcome your wanderer? Your summits</p>
<p>are clear; the sky and lake are blue and placid.</p>
<p>Is this to prognosticate peace, or to mock at my</p>
<p>unhappiness?”</p>
<p>I fear, my friend, that I shall render myself</p>
<p>tedious by dwelling on these preliminary circum-</p>
<p>stances; but they were days of comparative happi-</p>
<p>ness, and I think of them with pleasure. My country,</p>
<p>my beloved country! who but a native can tell the</p>
<p>delight I took in again beholding thy streams, thy</p>
<p>mountains, and, more than all, thy lovely lake.</p>
<p>Yet, as I drew nearer home, grief and fear again</p>
<p>overcame me. Night also closed around; and when</p>
<p>I could hardly see the dark mountains, I felt still</p>
<p>more gloomily. The picture appeared a vast and</p>
<p>dim scene of evil, and I foresaw obscurely that I was</p>
<p>destined to become the most wretched of human</p>
<p>beings. Alas! I prophesied truly, and failed only</p>
<p>in one single circumstance, that in all the misery</p>
<p>I imagined and dreaded, I did not conceive the</p>
<p>hundredth part of the anguish I was destined to</p>
<p>endure.</p>
<p>It was completely dark when I arrived in the</p>
<p>environs of Geneva; the gates of the town were</p>
<p>already shut; and I was obliged to pass the night</p>
<p>at Secheron, a village half a league to the east</p>
<p>of the city. The sky was serene; and, as I was</p>
<p>unable to rest, I resolved to visit the spot where</p>
<p>my poor William had been murdered. As I could</p>
<p>not pass through the town, I was obliged to cross</p>
<p>the lake in a boat to arrive at Plainpalais. During</p>
<p>this short voyage I saw the lightnings playing on</p>
<p>the summit of Mont Blânc in the most beautiful</p>
<p>ﬁgures. The storm appeared to approach rapidly;</p>
<p>and, on landing, I ascended a low hill, that I might</p>
<p>observe its progress. It advanced; the heavens</p>
<p>were clouded, and I soon felt the rain coming slowly</p>
<p>in large drops, but its violence quickly increased.</p>
<p>I quitted my seat, and walked on, although the</p>
<p>darkness and storm increased every minute, and</p>
<p>the thunder burst with a terriﬁc crash over my head.</p>
<p>It was echoed from Salêve, the Juras, and the Alps</p>
<p>of Savoy; vivid ﬂashes of lightning dazzled my eyes,</p>
<p>illuminating the lake, making it appear like a vast</p>
<p>sheet of ﬁre; then for an instant every thing seemed</p>
<p>of a pitchy darkness, until the eye recovered itself</p>
<p>from the preceding ﬂash. The storm, as is often the</p>
<p>case in Switzerland, appeared at once in various</p>
<p>parts of the heavens. The most violent storm hung</p>
<p>exactly north of the town, over that part of the lake</p>
<p>which lies between the promontory of Belrive and</p>
<p>the village of Copêt. Another storm enlightened</p>
<p>Jura with faint ﬂashes; and another darkened and</p>
<p>sometimes disclosed the Môle, a peaked mountain</p>
<p>to the east of the lake.</p>
<p>While I watched the storm, so beautiful yet ter-</p>
<p>riﬁc, I wandered on with a hasty step. This noble</p>
<p>war in the sky elevated my spirits; I clasped my</p>
<p>hands, and exclaimed aloud, “William, dear angel!</p>
<p>this is thy funeral, this thy dirge!” As I said these</p>
<p>words, I perceived in the gloom a ﬁgure which stole</p>
<p>from behind a clump of trees near me; I stood ﬁxed,</p>
<p>gazing intently: I could not be mistaken. A ﬂash</p>
<p>of lightning illuminated the object, and discovered</p>
<p>its shape plainly to me; its gigantic stature, and the</p>
<p>deformity of its aspect, more hideous than belongs</p>
<p>to humanity, instantly informed me that it was the</p>
<p>wretch, the ﬁlthy dæmon to whom I had given life.</p>
<p>What did he there? Could he be (I shuddered at the</p>
<p>conception) the murderer of my brother? No sooner</p>
<p>did that idea cross my imagination, than I became</p>
<p>convinced of its truth; my teeth chattered, and I</p>
<p>was forced to lean against a tree for support. The</p>
<p>ﬁgure passed me quickly, and I lost it in the gloom.</p>
<p>Nothing in human shape could have destroyed that</p>
<p>fair child. He was the murderer! I could not doubt it.</p>
<p>The mere presence of the idea was an irresistible</p>
<p>proof of the fact. I thought of pursuing the devil;</p>
<p>but it would have been in vain, for another ﬂash</p>
<p>discovered him to me hanging among the rocks of</p>
<p>the nearly perpendicular ascent of Mont Salêve, a</p>
<p>hill that bounds Plainpalais on the south. He soon</p>
<p>reached the summit, and disappeared.</p>
<p>I remained motionless. The thunder ceased;</p>
<p>but the rain still continued, and the scene was en-</p>
<p>veloped in an impenetrable darkness. I revolved in</p>
<p>my mind the events which I had until now sought</p>
<p>to forget: the whole train of my progress towards</p>
<p>the creation; the appearance of the work of my own</p>
<p>hands alive at my bed side; its departure. Two years</p>
<p>had now nearly elapsed since the night on which</p>
<p>he ﬁrst received life; and was this his ﬁrst crime?</p>
<p>Alas! I had turned loose into the world a depraved</p>
<p>wretch, whose delight was in carnage and misery;</p>
<p>had he not murdered my brother?</p>
<p>No one can conceive the anguish I suffered</p>
<p>during the remainder of the night, which I spent,</p>
<p>cold and wet, in the open air. But I did not feel the</p>
<p>inconvenience of the weather; my imagination was</p>
<p>busy in scenes of evil and despair. I considered</p>
<p>the being whom I had cast among mankind, and</p>
<p>endowed with the will and power to effect purposes</p>
<p>of horror, such as the deed which he had now done,</p>
<p>nearly in the light of my own vampire, my own spirit</p>
<p>let loose from the grave, and forced to destroy all</p>
<p>that was dear to me.</p>
<p>Day dawned; and I directed my steps towards</p>
<p>the town. The gates were open; and I hastened to</p>
<p>my father’s house. My ﬁrst thought was to discover</p>
<p>what I knew of the murderer, and cause instant pur-</p>
<p>suit to be made. But I paused when I reﬂected on</p>
<p>the story that I had to tell. A being whom I myself</p>
<p>had formed, and endued with life, had met me at</p>
<p>midnight among the precipices of an inaccessible</p>
<p>mountain. I remembered also the nervous fever</p>
<p>with which I had been seized just at the time that</p>
<p>I dated my creation, and which would give an air</p>
<p>of delirium to a tale otherwise so utterly improba-</p>
<p>ble. I well knew that if any other had communicated</p>
<p>such a relation to me, I should have looked upon</p>
<p>it as the ravings of insanity. Besides, the strange</p>
<p>nature of the animal would elude all pursuit, even</p>
<p>if I were so far credited as to persuade my rela-</p>
<p>tives to commence it. Besides, of what use would</p>
<p>be pursuit? Who could arrest a creature capable</p>
<p>of scaling the overhanging sides of Mont Salêve?</p>
<p>These reﬂections determined me, and I resolved to</p>
<p>remain silent.</p>
<p>It was about ﬁve in the morning when I entered</p>
<p>my father’s house. I told the servants not to disturb</p>
<p>the family, and went into the library to attend their</p>
<p>usual hour of rising.</p>
<p>Six years had elapsed, passed as a dream but</p>
<p>for one indelible trace, and I stood in the same place</p>
<p>where I had last embraced my father before my</p>
<p>departure for Ingolstadt. Beloved and respectable</p>
<p>parent! He still remained to me. I gazed on the</p>
<p>picture of my mother, which stood over the mantle-</p>
<p>piece. It was an historical subject, painted at my</p>
<p>father’s desire, and represented Caroline Beaufort</p>
<p>in an agony of despair, kneeling by the cofﬁn of her</p>
<p>dead father. Her garb was rustic, and her cheek</p>
<p>pale; but there was an air of dignity and beauty, that</p>
<p>hardly permitted the sentiment of pity. Below this</p>
<p>picture was a miniature of William; and my tears</p>
<p>ﬂowed when I looked upon it. While I was thus</p>
<p>engaged, Ernest entered: he had heard me arrive,</p>
<p>and hastened to welcome me. He expressed a</p>
<p>sorrowful delight to see me: “Welcome, my dearest</p>
<p>Victor,” said he. “Ah! I wish you had come three</p>
<p>months ago, and then you would have found us all</p>
<p>joyous and delighted. But we are now unhappy;</p>
<p>and, I am afraid, tears instead of smiles will be</p>
<p>your welcome. Our father looks so sorrowful: this</p>
<p>dreadful event seems to have revived in his mind</p>
<p>his grief on the death of Mamma. Poor Elizabeth</p>
<p>also is quite inconsolable.” Ernest began to weep</p>
<p>as he said these words.</p>
<p>“Do not,” said I, “welcome me thus; try to be</p>
<p>more calm, that I may not be absolutely miserable</p>
<p>the moment I enter my father’s house after so long</p>
<p>an absence. But, tell me, how does my father sup-</p>
<p>port his misfortunes? and how is my poor Eliza-</p>
<p>beth?”</p>
<p>“She indeed requires consolation; she accused</p>
<p>herself of having caused the death of my brother,</p>
<p>and that made her very wretched. But since the</p>
<p>murderer has been discovered —— ”</p>
<p>“The murderer discovered! Good God! how can</p>
<p>that be? who could attempt to pursue him? It is</p>
<p>impossible; one might as well try to overtake the</p>
<p>winds, or conﬁne a mountain-stream with a straw.”</p>
<p>“I do not know what you mean; but we were all</p>
<p>very unhappy when she was discovered. No one</p>
<p>would believe it at ﬁrst; and even now Elizabeth will</p>
<p>not be convinced, notwithstanding all the evidence.</p>
<p>Indeed, who would credit that Justine Moritz, who</p>
<p>was so amiable, and fond of all the family, could all</p>
<p>at once become so extremely wicked?”</p>
<p>“Justine Moritz! Poor, poor girl, is she the ac-</p>
<p>cused? But it is wrongfully; every one knows that;</p>
<p>no one believes it, surely, Ernest?”</p>
<p>“No one did at ﬁrst; but several circumstances</p>
<p>came out, that have almost forced conviction upon</p>
<p>us: and her own behaviour has been so confused,</p>
<p>as to add to the evidence of facts a weight that, I</p>
<p>fear, leaves no hope for doubt. But she will be tried</p>
<p>to-day, and you will then hear all.”</p>
<p>He related that, the morning on which the mur-</p>
<p>der of poor William had been discovered, Justine</p>
<p>had been taken ill, and conﬁned to her bed; and,</p>
<p>after several days, one of the servants, happen-</p>
<p>ing to examine the apparel she had worn on the</p>
<p>night of the murder, had discovered in her pocket</p>
<p>the picture of my mother, which had been judged</p>
<p>to be the temptation of the murderer. The servant</p>
<p>instantly shewed it to one of the others, who, with-</p>
<p>out saying a word to any of the family, went to a</p>
<p>magistrate; and, upon their deposition, Justine was</p>
<p>apprehended. On being charged with the fact, the</p>
