<p>poor girl conﬁrmed the suspicion in a great measure</p>
<p>by her extreme confusion of manner.</p>
<p>This was a strange tale, but it did not shake my</p>
<p>faith; and I replied earnestly, “You are all mistaken;</p>
<p>I know the murderer. Justine, poor, good Justine, is</p>
<p>innocent.”</p>
<p>At that instant my father entered. I saw unhappi-</p>
<p>ness deeply impressed on his countenance, but he</p>
<p>endeavoured to welcome me cheerfully; and, after</p>
<p>we had exchanged our mournful greeting, would</p>
<p>have introduced some other topic than that of our</p>
<p>disaster, had not Ernest exclaimed, “Good God,</p>
<p>Papa! Victor says that he knows who was the mur-</p>
<p>derer of poor William.”</p>
<p>“We do also, unfortunately,” replied my father;</p>
<p>“for indeed I had rather have been for ever igno-</p>
<p>rant than have discovered so much depravity and</p>
<p>ingratitude in one I valued so highly.”</p>
<p>“My dear father, you are mistaken; Justine is</p>
<p>innocent.”</p>
<p>“If she is, God forbid that she should suffer as</p>
<p>guilty. She is to be tried to-day, and I hope, I sin-</p>
<p>cerely hope, that she will be acquitted.”</p>
<p>This speech calmed me. I was ﬁrmly convinced</p>
<p>in my own mind that Justine, and indeed every</p>
<p>human being, was guiltless of this murder. I had</p>
<p>no fear, therefore, that any circumstantial evidence</p>
<p>could be brought forward strong enough to con-</p>
<p>vict her; and, in this assurance, I calmed myself,</p>
<p>expecting the trial with eagerness, but without prog-</p>
<p>nosticating an evil result.</p>
<p>We were soon joined by Elizabeth. Time had</p>
<p>made great alterations in her form since I had last</p>
<p>beheld her. Six years before she had been a pretty,</p>
<p>good-humoured girl, whom every one loved and</p>
<p>caressed. She was now a woman in stature and ex-</p>
<p>pression of countenance, which was uncommonly</p>
<p>lovely. An open and capacious forehead gave in-</p>
<p>dications of a good understanding, joined to great</p>
<p>frankness of disposition. Her eyes were hazel, and</p>
<p>expressive of mildness, now through recent afﬂic-</p>
<p>tion allied to sadness. Her hair was of a rich, dark</p>
<p>auburn, her complexion fair, and her ﬁgure slight</p>
<p>and graceful. She welcomed me with the great-</p>
<p>est affection. “Your arrival, my dear cousin,” said</p>
<p>she, “ﬁlls me with hope. You perhaps will ﬁnd some</p>
<p>means to justify my poor guiltless Justine. Alas!</p>
<p>who is safe, if she be convicted of crime? I rely on</p>
<p>her innocence as certainly as I do upon my own.</p>
<p>Our misfortune is doubly hard to us; we have not</p>
<p>only lost that lovely darling boy, but this poor girl,</p>
<p>whom I sincerely love, is to be torn away by even a</p>
<p>worse fate. If she is condemned, I never shall know</p>
<p>joy more. But she will not, I am sure she will not;</p>
<p>and then I shall be happy again, even after the sad</p>
<p>death of my little William.”</p>
<p>“She is innocent, my Elizabeth,” said I, “and that</p>
<p>shall be proved; fear nothing, but let your spirits be</p>
<p>cheered by the assurance of her acquittal.”</p>
<p>“How kind you are! every one else believes in</p>
<p>her guilt, and that made me wretched; for I knew</p>
<p>that it was impossible: and to see every one else</p>
<p>prejudiced in so deadly a manner, rendered me</p>
<p>hopeless and despairing.” She wept.</p>
<p>“Sweet niece,” said my father, “dry your tears. If</p>
<p>she is, as you believe, innocent, rely on the justice</p>
<p>of our judges, and the activity with which I shall</p>
<p>prevent the slightest shadow of partiality.”</p>
<p>Chapter VII</p>
<p>WE passed a few sad hours, until eleven o’clock,</p>
<p>when the trial was to commence. My father and</p>
<p>the rest of the family being obliged to attend as wit-</p>
<p>nesses, I accompanied them to the court. During</p>
<p>the whole of this wretched mockery of justice, I suf-</p>
<p>fered living torture. It was to be decided, whether</p>
<p>the result of my curiosity and lawless devices would</p>
<p>cause the death of two of my fellow-beings: one a</p>
<p>smiling babe, full of innocence and joy; the other far</p>
<p>more dreadfully murdered, with every aggravation</p>
<p>of infamy that could make the murder memorable</p>
<p>127</p>
<p>in horror. Justine also was a girl of merit, and pos-</p>
<p>sessed qualities which promised to render her life</p>
<p>happy: now all was to be obliterated in an igno-</p>
<p>minious grave; and I the cause! A thousand times</p>
<p>rather would I have confessed myself guilty of the</p>
<p>crime ascribed to Justine; but I was absent when</p>
<p>it was committed, and such a declaration would</p>
<p>have been considered as the ravings of a madman,</p>
<p>and would not have exculpated her who suffered</p>
<p>through me.</p>
<p>The appearance of Justine was calm. She was</p>
<p>dressed in mourning; and her countenance, always</p>
<p>engaging, was rendered, by the solemnity of her</p>
<p>feelings, exquisitely beautiful. Yet she appeared</p>
<p>conﬁdent in innocence, and did not tremble, al-</p>
<p>though gazed on and execrated by thousands; for</p>
<p>all the kindness which her beauty might otherwise</p>
<p>have excited, was obliterated in the minds of the</p>
<p>spectators by the imagination of the enormity she</p>
<p>was supposed to have committed. She was tranquil,</p>
<p>yet her tranquillity was evidently constrained; and</p>
<p>as her confusion had before been adduced as a</p>
<p>proof of her guilt, she worked up her mind to an ap-</p>
<p>pearance of courage. When she entered the court,</p>
<p>she threw her eyes round it, and quickly discovered</p>
<p>where we were seated. A tear seemed to dim her</p>
<p>eye when she saw us; but she quickly recovered</p>
<p>herself, and a look of sorrowful affection seemed to</p>
<p>attest her utter guiltlessness.</p>
<p>The trial began; and after the advocate against</p>
<p>her had stated the charge, several witnesses were</p>
<p>called. Several strange facts combined against her,</p>
<p>which might have staggered any one who had not</p>
<p>such proof of her innocence as I had. She had been</p>
<p>out the whole of the night on which the murder had</p>
<p>been committed, and towards morning had been</p>
<p>perceived by a market-woman not far from the spot</p>
<p>where the body of the murdered child had been</p>
<p>afterwards found. The woman asked her what she</p>
<p>did there; but she looked very strangely, and only</p>
<p>returned a confused and unintelligible answer. She</p>
<p>returned to the house about eight o’clock; and when</p>
<p>one inquired where she had passed the night, she</p>
<p>replied, that she had been looking for the child, and</p>
<p>demanded earnestly, if any thing had been heard</p>
<p>concerning him. When shewn the body, she fell into</p>
<p>violent hysterics, and kept her bed for several days.</p>
<p>The picture was then produced, which the servant</p>
<p>had found in her pocket; and when Elizabeth, in a</p>
<p>faltering voice, proved that it was the same which,</p>
<p>an hour before the child had been missed, she had</p>
<p>placed round his neck, a murmur of horror and</p>
<p>indignation ﬁlled the court.</p>
<p>Justine was called on for her defence. As the</p>
<p>trial had proceeded, her countenance had altered.</p>
<p>Surprise, horror, and misery, were strongly ex-</p>
<p>pressed. Sometimes she struggled with her tears;</p>
<p>but when she was desired to plead, she collected</p>
<p>her powers, and spoke in an audible although</p>
<p>variable voice:—</p>
<p>“God knows,” she said, “how entirely I am in-</p>
<p>nocent. But I do not pretend that my protestations</p>
<p>should acquit me: I rest my innocence on a plain</p>
<p>and simple explanation of the facts which have</p>
<p>been adduced against me; and I hope the char-</p>
<p>acter I have always borne will incline my judges to a</p>
<p>favourable interpretation, where any circumstance</p>
<p>appears doubtful or suspicious.”</p>
<p>She then related that, by the permission of Eliz-</p>
<p>abeth, she had passed the evening of the night on</p>
<p>which the murder had been committed, at the house</p>
<p>of an aunt at Chêne, a village situated at about a</p>
<p>league from Geneva. On her return, at about nine</p>
<p>o’clock, she met a man, who asked her if she had</p>
<p>seen any thing of the child who was lost. She was</p>
<p>alarmed by this account, and passed several hours</p>
<p>in looking for him, when the gates of Geneva were</p>
<p>shut, and she was forced to remain several hours</p>
<p>of the night in a barn belonging to a cottage, being</p>
<p>unwilling to call up the inhabitants, to whom she</p>
<p>was well known. Unable to rest or sleep, she quitted</p>
<p>her asylum early, that she might again endeavour</p>
<p>to ﬁnd my brother. If she had gone near the spot</p>
<p>where his body lay, it was without her knowledge.</p>
<p>That she had been bewildered when questioned by</p>
<p>the market-woman, was not surprising, since she</p>
<p>had passed a sleepless night, and the fate of poor</p>
<p>William was yet uncertain. Concerning the picture</p>
<p>she could give no account.</p>
<p>“I know,” continued the unhappy victim, “how</p>
<p>heavily and fatally this one circumstance weighs</p>
<p>against me, but I have no power of explaining it; and</p>
<p>when I have expressed my utter ignorance, I am</p>
<p>only left to conjecture concerning the probabilities</p>
<p>by which it might have been placed in my pocket.</p>
<p>But here also I am checked. I believe that I have</p>
<p>no enemy on earth, and none surely would have</p>
<p>been so wicked as to destroy me wantonly. Did the</p>
<p>murderer place it there? I know of no opportunity</p>
<p>afforded him for so doing; or if I had, why should he</p>
<p>have stolen the jewel, to part with it again so soon?</p>
<p>“I commit my cause to the justice of my judges,</p>
<p>yet I see no room for hope. I beg permission to have</p>
<p>a few witnesses examined concerning my charac-</p>
<p>ter; and if their testimony shall not overweigh my</p>
<p>supposed guilt, I must be condemned, although I</p>
<p>would pledge my salvation on my innocence.”</p>
<p>Several witnesses were called, who had known</p>
<p>her for many years, and they spoke well of her; but</p>
<p>fear, and hatred of the crime of which they sup-</p>
<p>posed her guilty, rendered them timorous, and un-</p>
<p>willing to come forward. Elizabeth saw even this last</p>
<p>resource, her excellent dispositions and irreproach-</p>
<p>able conduct, about to fail the accused, when, al-</p>
<p>though violently agitated, she desired permission</p>
<p>to address the court.</p>
<p>“I am,” said she, “the cousin of the unhappy</p>
<p>child who was murdered, or rather his sister, for I</p>
<p>was educated by and have lived with his parents</p>
<p>ever since and even long before his birth. It may</p>
<p>therefore be judged indecent in me to come forward</p>
<p>on this occasion; but when I see a fellow-creature</p>
<p>about to perish through the cowardice of her pre-</p>
<p>tended friends, I wish to be allowed to speak, that I</p>
<p>may say what I know of her character. I am well ac-</p>
<p>quainted with the accused. I have lived in the same</p>
<p>house with her, at one time for ﬁve, and at another</p>
<p>for nearly two years. During all that period she ap-</p>
<p>peared to me the most amiable and benevolent of</p>
<p>human creatures. She nursed Madame Franken-</p>
<p>stein, my aunt, in her last illness with the greatest af-</p>
<p>fection and care; and afterwards attended her own</p>
<p>mother during a tedious illness, in a manner that</p>
<p>excited the admiration of all who knew her. After</p>
<p>which she again lived in my uncle’s house, where</p>
<p>she was beloved by all the family. She was warmly</p>
<p>attached to the child who is now dead, and acted</p>
<p>towards him like a most affectionate mother. For</p>
<p>my own part, I do not hesitate to say, that, notwith-</p>
<p>standing all the evidence produced against her, I</p>
<p>believe and rely on her perfect innocence. She had</p>
<p>no temptation for such an action: as to the bauble</p>
<p>on which the chief proof rests, if she had earnestly</p>
<p>desired it, I should have willingly given it to her; so</p>
<p>much do I esteem and value her.”</p>
<p>Excellent Elizabeth! A murmur of approbation</p>
<p>was heard; but it was excited by her generous inter-</p>
<p>ference, and not in favour of poor Justine, on whom</p>
<p>the public indignation was turned with renewed vi-</p>
<p>olence, charging her with the blackest ingratitude.</p>
<p>She herself wept as Elizabeth spoke, but she did</p>
<p>not answer. My own agitation and anguish was</p>
<p>extreme during the whole trial. I believed in her</p>
<p>innocence; I knew it. Could the dæmon, who had</p>
<p>(I did not for a minute doubt) murdered my brother,</p>
<p>also in his hellish sport have betrayed the innocent</p>
<p>to death and ignominy. I could not sustain the horror</p>
<p>of my situation; and when I perceived that the popu-</p>
<p>lar voice, and the countenances of the judges, had</p>
<p>already condemned my unhappy victim, I rushed</p>
<p>out of the court in agony. The tortures of the ac-</p>
<p>cused did not equal mine; she was sustained by</p>
<p>innocence, but the fangs of remorse tore my bosom,</p>
<p>and would not forego their hold.</p>
<p>I passed a night of unmingled wretchedness. In</p>
<p>the morning I went to the court; my lips and throat</p>
<p>were parched. I dared not ask the fatal question;</p>
<p>but I was known, and the ofﬁcer guessed the cause</p>
<p>of my visit. The ballots had been thrown; they were</p>
<p>all black, and Justine was condemned.</p>
<p>I cannot pretend to describe what I then felt. I</p>
<p>had before experienced sensations of horror; and I</p>
<p>have endeavoured to bestow upon them adequate</p>
<p>expressions, but words cannot convey an idea of</p>
<p>the heart-sickening despair that I then endured.</p>
<p>The person to whom I addressed myself added,</p>
<p>that Justine had already confessed her guilt. “That</p>
<p>evidence,” he observed, “was hardly required in so</p>
<p>glaring a case, but I am glad of it; and, indeed,</p>
<p>none of our judges like to condemn a criminal upon</p>
<p>circumstantial evidence, be it ever so decisive.”</p>
<p>When I returned home, Elizabeth eagerly de-</p>
<p>manded the result.</p>
<p>“My cousin,” replied I, “it is decided as you may</p>
<p>have expected; all judges had rather that ten in-</p>
<p>nocent should suffer, than that one guilty should</p>
<p>escape. But she has confessed.”</p>
<p>This was a dire blow to poor Elizabeth, who</p>
<p>had relied with ﬁrmness upon Justine’s innocence.</p>
<p>“Alas!” said she, “how shall I ever again believe in</p>
<p>human benevolence? Justine, whom I loved and</p>
<p>esteemed as my sister, how could she put on those</p>
<p>smiles of innocence only to betray; her mild eyes</p>
<p>seemed incapable of any severity or ill-humour, and</p>
<p>yet she has committed a murder.”</p>
<p>Soon after we heard that the poor victim had</p>
<p>expressed a wish to see my cousin. My father</p>
<p>wished her not to go; but said, that he left it to her</p>
<p>own judgment and feelings to decide. “Yes,” said</p>
<p>Elizabeth, “I will go, although she is guilty; and you,</p>
<p>Victor, shall accompany me: I cannot go alone.”</p>
<p>The idea of this visit was torture to me, yet I could</p>
<p>not refuse.</p>
<p>We entered the gloomy prison-chamber, and</p>
<p>beheld Justine sitting on some straw at the fur-</p>
<p>ther end; her hands were manacled, and her head</p>
<p>rested on her knees. She rose on seeing us enter;</p>
<p>and when we were left alone with her, she threw</p>
<p>herself at the feet of Elizabeth, weeping bitterly. My</p>
<p>cousin wept also.</p>
<p>“Oh, Justine!” said she, “why did you rob me</p>
<p>of my last consolation. I relied on your innocence;</p>
<p>and although I was then very wretched, I was not</p>
<p>so miserable as I am now.”</p>
<p>“And do you also believe that I am so very, very</p>
<p>wicked? Do you also join with my enemies to crush</p>
<p>me?” Her voice was suffocated with sobs.</p>
<p>“Rise, my poor girl,” said Elizabeth, “why do you</p>
<p>kneel, if you are innocent? I am not one of your</p>
<p>enemies; I believed you guiltless, notwithstanding</p>
<p>every evidence, until I heard that you had your-</p>
<p>self declared your guilt. That report, you say, is</p>
<p>false; and be assured, dear Justine, that nothing</p>
<p>can shake my conﬁdence in you for a moment, but</p>
<p>your own confession.”</p>
<p>“I did confess; but I confessed a lie. I confessed,</p>
<p>that I might obtain absolution; but now that false-</p>
<p>hood lies heavier at my heart than all my other sins.</p>
<p>The God of heaven forgive me! Ever since I was</p>
<p>condemned, my confessor has besieged me; he</p>
<p>threatened and menaced, until I almost began to</p>
<p>think that I was the monster that he said I was. He</p>
<p>threatened excommunication and hell ﬁre in my last</p>
<p>moments, if I continued obdurate. Dear lady, I had</p>
<p>none to support me; all looked on me as a wretch</p>
<p>doomed to ignominy and perdition. What could I</p>
<p>do? In an evil hour I subscribed to a lie; and now</p>
<p>only am I truly miserable.”</p>
<p>She paused, weeping, and then continued — “I</p>
<p>thought with horror, my sweet lady, that you should</p>
<p>believe your Justine, whom your blessed aunt had</p>
<p>so highly honoured, and whom you loved, was a</p>
<p>creature capable of a crime which none but the</p>
<p>devil himself could have perpetrated. Dear William!</p>
<p>dearest blessed child! I soon shall see you again</p>
<p>in heaven, where we shall all be happy; and that</p>
<p>consoles me, going as I am to suffer ignominy and</p>
<p>death.”</p>
<p>“Oh, Justine! forgive me for having for one mo-</p>
<p>ment distrusted you. Why did you confess? But do</p>
<p>not mourn, my dear girl; I will every where proclaim</p>
<p>your innocence, and force belief. Yet you must die;</p>
<p>you, my playfellow, my companion, my more than</p>
<p>sister. I never can survive so horrible a misfortune.”</p>
<p>“Dear, sweet Elizabeth, do not weep. You ought</p>
<p>to raise me with thoughts of a better life, and elevate</p>
<p>me from the petty cares of this world of injustice</p>
<p>and strife. Do not you, excellent friend, drive me to</p>
<p>despair.”</p>
<p>“I will try to comfort you; but this, I fear, is an evil</p>
<p>too deep and poignant to admit of consolation, for</p>
<p>there is no hope. Yet heaven bless thee, my dearest</p>
<p>Justine, with resignation, and a conﬁdence elevated</p>
<p>beyond this world. Oh! how I hate its shews and</p>
<p>mockeries! when one creature is murdered, another</p>
<p>is immediately deprived of life in a slow torturing</p>
<p>manner; then the executioners, their hands yet reek-</p>
<p>ing with the blood of innocence, believe that they</p>
<p>have done a great deed. They call this retribution.</p>
<p>Hateful name! When that word is pronounced, I</p>
<p>know greater and more horrid punishments are go-</p>
<p>ing to be inﬂicted than the gloomiest tyrant has ever</p>
<p>invented to satiate his utmost revenge. Yet this is</p>
<p>not consolation for you, my Justine, unless indeed</p>
<p>that you may glory in escaping from so miserable</p>
<p>a den. Alas! I would I were in peace with my aunt</p>
<p>and my lovely William, escaped from a world which</p>
<p>is hateful to me, and the visages of men which I</p>
<p>abhor.”</p>
<p>Justine smiled languidly. “This, dear lady, is</p>
<p>despair, and not resignation. I must not learn the</p>
<p>lesson that you would teach me. Talk of something</p>
<p>else, something that will bring peace, and not in-</p>
<p>crease of misery.”</p>
<p>During this conversation I had retired to a corner</p>
<p>of the prison-room, where I could conceal the horrid</p>
<p>anguish that possessed me. Despair! Who dared</p>
<p>talk of that? The poor victim, who on the morrow</p>
<p>was to pass the dreary boundary between life and</p>
<p>death, felt not as I did, such deep and bitter agony.</p>
<p>I gnashed my teeth, and ground them together,</p>
<p>uttering a groan that came from my inmost soul.</p>
<p>Justine started. When she saw who it was, she</p>
<p>approached me, and said, “Dear Sir, you are very</p>
<p>kind to visit me; you, I hope, do not believe that I</p>
<p>am guilty.”</p>
<p>I could not answer. “No, Justine,” said Elizabeth;</p>
<p>“he is more convinced of your innocence than I was;</p>
<p>for even when he heard that you had confessed, he</p>
<p>did not credit it.”</p>
<p>“I truly thank him. In these last moments I feel</p>
<p>the sincerest gratitude towards those who think of</p>
<p>me with kindness. How sweet is the affection of</p>
<p>others to such a wretch as I am! It removes more</p>
<p>than half my misfortune; and I feel as if I could die</p>
<p>in peace, now that my innocence is acknowledged</p>
<p>by you, dear lady, and your cousin.”</p>
<p>Thus the poor sufferer tried to comfort others</p>
<p>and herself. She indeed gained the resignation she</p>
<p>desired. But I, the true murderer, felt the never-</p>
<p>dying worm alive in my bosom, which allowed of</p>
<p>no hope or consolation. Elizabeth also wept, and</p>
<p>was unhappy; but her’s also was the misery of in-</p>
<p>nocence, which, like a cloud that passes over the</p>
<p>fair moon, for a while hides, but cannot tarnish its</p>
<p>brightness. Anguish and despair had penetrated</p>
<p>into the core of my heart; I bore a hell within me,</p>
<p>which nothing could extinguish. We staid several</p>
<p>hours with Justine; and it was with great difﬁculty</p>
<p>that Elizabeth could tear herself away. “I wish,” cried</p>
<p>she, “that I were to die with you; I cannot live in this</p>
<p>world of misery.”</p>
<p>Justine assumed an air of cheerfulness, while</p>
<p>she with difﬁculty repressed her bitter tears. She</p>
<p>embraced Elizabeth, and said, in a voice of half-</p>
<p>suppressed emotion, “Farewell, sweet lady, dearest</p>
<p>Elizabeth, my beloved and only friend; may heaven</p>
<p>in its bounty bless and preserve you; may this be</p>
<p>the last misfortune that you will ever suffer. Live,</p>
<p>and be happy, and make others so.”</p>
<p>As we returned, Elizabeth said, “You know not,</p>
<p>my dear Victor, how much I am relieved, now that</p>
<p>I trust in the innocence of this unfortunate girl. I</p>
<p>never could again have known peace, if I had been</p>
<p>deceived in my reliance on her. For the moment</p>
<p>that I did believe her guilty, I felt an anguish that</p>
<p>I could not have long sustained. Now my heart</p>
<p>is lightened. The innocent suffers; but she whom</p>
<p>I thought amiable and good has not betrayed the</p>
<p>trust I reposed in her, and I am consoled.”</p>
<p>Amiable cousin! such were your thoughts, mild</p>
<p>and gentle as your own dear eyes and voice. But I</p>
<p>— I was a wretch, and none ever conceived of the</p>
<p>misery that I then endured.</p>
<p>Volume II</p>
<p>144</p>
<p>Chapter I</p>
<p>NOTHING is more painful to the human mind, than,</p>
<p>after the feelings have been worked up by a quick</p>
<p>succession of events, the dead calmness of inac-</p>
<p>tion and certainty which follows, and deprives the</p>
<p>soul both of hope and fear. Justine died; she rested;</p>
<p>and I was alive. The blood ﬂowed freely in my veins,</p>
<p>but a weight of despair and remorse pressed on</p>
<p>my heart, which nothing could remove. Sleep ﬂed</p>
<p>from my eyes; I wandered like an evil spirit, for I</p>
<p>had committed deeds of mischief beyond descrip-</p>
<p>tion horrible, and more, much more, (I persuaded</p>
<p>myself) was yet behind. Yet my heart overﬂowed</p>
<p>145</p>
<p>with kindness, and the love of virtue. I had begun</p>
<p>life with benevolent intentions, and thirsted for the</p>
<p>moment when I should put them in practice, and</p>
<p>make myself useful to my fellow-beings. Now all</p>
<p>was blasted: instead of that serenity of conscience,</p>
<p>which allowed me to look back upon the past with</p>
<p>self-satisfaction, and from thence to gather promise</p>
<p>of new hopes, I was seized by remorse and the</p>
<p>sense of guilt, which hurried me away to a hell of</p>
<p>intense tortures, such as no language can describe.</p>
<p>This state of mind preyed upon my health, which</p>
<p>had entirely recovered from the ﬁrst shock it had</p>
<p>sustained. I shunned the face of man; all sound</p>
<p>of joy or complacency was torture to me; solitude</p>
<p>was my only consolation — deep, dark, death-like</p>
<p>solitude.</p>
<p>My father observed with pain the alteration per-</p>
<p>ceptible in my disposition and habits, and endeav-</p>
<p>oured to reason with me on the folly of giving way</p>
<p>to immoderate grief. “Do you think, Victor,” said he,</p>
<p>“that I do not suffer also? No one could love a child</p>
<p>more than I loved your brother;” (tears came into</p>
<p>his eyes as he spoke); “but is it not a duty to the</p>
<p>survivors, that we should refrain from augmenting</p>
<p>their unhappiness by an appearance of immoderate</p>
<p>grief? It is also a duty owed to yourself; for exces-</p>
<p>sive sorrow prevents improvement or enjoyment,</p>
<p>or even the discharge of daily usefulness, without</p>
<p>which no man is ﬁt for society.”</p>
<p>This advice, although good, was totally inappli-</p>
<p>cable to my case; I should have been the ﬁrst to</p>
<p>hide my grief, and console my friends, if remorse</p>
<p>had not mingled its bitterness with my other sen-</p>
<p>sations. Now I could only answer my father with a</p>
<p>look of despair, and endeavour to hide myself from</p>
<p>his view.</p>
<p>About this time we retired to our house at Bel-</p>
<p>rive. This change was particularly agreeable to me.</p>
<p>The shutting of the gates regularly at ten o’clock,</p>
<p>and the impossibility of remaining on the lake after</p>
<p>that hour, had rendered our residence within the</p>
<p>walls of Geneva very irksome to me. I was now</p>
<p>free. Often, after the rest of the family had retired</p>
<p>for the night, I took the boat, and passed many</p>
<p>hours upon the water. Sometimes, with my sails</p>
<p>set, I was carried by the wind; and sometimes, after</p>
<p>rowing into the middle of the lake, I left the boat to</p>
<p>pursue its own course, and gave way to my own</p>
<p>miserable reﬂections. I was often tempted, when</p>
<p>all was at peace around me, and I the only unquiet</p>
<p>thing that wandered restless in a scene so beautiful</p>
<p>and heavenly, if I except some bat, or the frogs,</p>
<p>whose harsh and interrupted croaking was heard</p>
<p>only when I approached the shore — often, I say,</p>
<p>I was tempted to plunge into the silent lake, that</p>
<p>the waters might close over me and my calamities</p>
<p>for ever. But I was restrained, when I thought of</p>
<p>the heroic and suffering Elizabeth, whom I tenderly</p>
<p>loved, and whose existence was bound up in mine.</p>
<p>I thought also of my father, and surviving brother:</p>
<p>should I by my base desertion leave them exposed</p>
<p>and unprotected to the malice of the ﬁend whom I</p>
<p>had let loose among them?</p>
<p>At these moments I wept bitterly, and wished</p>
<p>that peace would revisit my mind only that I might</p>
<p>afford them consolation and happiness. But that</p>
<p>could not be. Remorse extinguished every hope. I</p>
<p>had been the author of unalterable evils; and I lived</p>
<p>in daily fear, lest the monster whom I had created</p>
<p>should perpetrate some new wickedness. I had an</p>
<p>obscure feeling that all was not over, and that he</p>
<p>would still commit some signal crime, which by its</p>
<p>enormity should almost efface the recollection of</p>
<p>the past. There was always scope for fear, so long</p>
<p>as any thing I loved remained behind. My abhor-</p>
<p>rence of this ﬁend cannot be conceived. When I</p>
<p>thought of him, I gnashed my teeth, my eyes be-</p>
<p>came inﬂamed, and I ardently wished to extinguish</p>
<p>that life which I had so thoughtlessly bestowed.</p>
<p>When I reﬂected on his crimes and malice, my ha-</p>
<p>tred and revenge burst all bounds of moderation. I</p>
<p>would have made a pilgrimage to the highest peak</p>
<p>of the Andes, could I, when there, have precipitated</p>
<p>him to their base. I wished to see him again, that I</p>
<p>might wreak the utmost extent of anger on his head,</p>
<p>and avenge the deaths of William and Justine.</p>
<p>Our house was the house of mourning. My fa-</p>
<p>ther’s health was deeply shaken by the horror of the</p>
<p>recent events. Elizabeth was sad and desponding;</p>
<p>she no longer took delight in her ordinary occupa-</p>
<p>tions; all pleasure seemed to her sacrilege toward</p>
<p>the dead; eternal woe and tears she then thought</p>
<p>was the just tribute she should pay to innocence</p>
<p>so blasted and destroyed. She was no longer that</p>
<p>happy creature, who in earlier youth wandered with</p>
<p>me on the banks of the lake, and talked with ec-</p>
<p>stacy of our future prospects. She had become</p>
<p>grave, and often conversed of the inconstancy of</p>
<p>fortune, and the instability of human life.</p>
<p>“When I reﬂect, my dear cousin,” said she, “on</p>
<p>the miserable death of Justine Moritz, I no longer</p>
<p>see the world and its works as they before appeared</p>
<p>to me. Before, I looked upon the accounts of vice</p>
<p>and injustice, that I read in books or heard from</p>
<p>others, as tales of ancient days, or imaginary evils;</p>
<p>at least they were remote, and more familiar to</p>
<p>reason than to the imagination; but now misery has</p>
<p>come home, and men appear to me as monsters</p>
<p>thirsting for each other’s blood. Yet I am certainly</p>
<p>unjust. Every body believed that poor girl to be</p>
<p>guilty; and if she could have committed the crime</p>
<p>for which she suffered, assuredly she would have</p>
<p>been the most depraved of human creatures. For</p>
<p>the sake of a few jewels, to have murdered the son</p>
<p>of her benefactor and friend, a child whom she had</p>
<p>nursed from its birth, and appeared to love as if</p>
<p>it had been her own! I could not consent to the</p>
<p>death of any human being; but certainly I should</p>
<p>have thought such a creature unﬁt to remain in the</p>
<p>society of men. Yet she was innocent. I know, I feel</p>
<p>she was innocent; you are of the same opinion, and</p>
<p>that conﬁrms me. Alas! Victor, when falsehood can</p>
<p>look so like the truth, who can assure themselves</p>
<p>of certain happiness? I feel as if I were walking on</p>
<p>the edge of a precipice, towards which thousands</p>
<p>are crowding, and endeavouring to plunge me into</p>
<p>the abyss. William and Justine were assassinated,</p>
<p>and the murderer escapes; he walks about the</p>
<p>world free, and perhaps respected. But even if I</p>
<p>were condemned to suffer on the scaffold for the</p>
<p>same crimes, I would not change places with such</p>
<p>a wretch.”</p>
<p>I listened to this discourse with the extremest</p>
<p>agony. I, not in deed, but in effect, was the true</p>
<p>murderer. Elizabeth read my anguish in my counte-</p>
<p>nance, and kindly taking my hand said, “My dearest</p>
<p>cousin, you must calm yourself. These events have</p>
<p>affected me, God knows how deeply; but I am not</p>
<p>so wretched as you are. There is an expression of</p>
<p>despair, and sometimes of revenge, in your counte-</p>
<p>nance, that makes me tremble. Be calm, my dear</p>
<p>Victor; I would sacriﬁce my life to your peace. We</p>
<p>surely shall be happy: quiet in our native country,</p>
<p>and not mingling in the world, what can disturb our</p>
<p>tranquillity?”</p>
<p>She shed tears as she said this, distrusting the</p>
<p>very solace that she gave; but at the same time</p>
<p>she smiled, that she might chase away the ﬁend</p>
<p>that lurked in my heart. My father, who saw in the</p>
<p>unhappiness that was painted in my face only an</p>
<p>exaggeration of that sorrow which I might naturally</p>
<p>feel, thought that an amusement suited to my taste</p>
<p>would be the best means of restoring to me my</p>
<p>wonted serenity. It was from this cause that he had</p>
<p>removed to the country; and, induced by the same</p>
<p>motive, he now proposed that we should all make</p>
<p>an excursion to the valley of Chamounix. I had been</p>
<p>there before, but Elizabeth and Ernest never had;</p>
<p>and both had often expressed an earnest desire</p>
<p>to see the scenery of this place, which had been</p>
<p>described to them as so wonderful and sublime.</p>
<p>Accordingly we departed from Geneva on this tour</p>
<p>about the middle of the month of August, nearly two</p>
<p>months after the death of Justine.</p>
<p>The weather was uncommonly ﬁne; and if mine</p>
<p>had been a sorrow to be chased away by any ﬂeet-</p>
<p>ing circumstance, this excursion would certainly</p>
<p>have had the effect intended by my father. As it was,</p>
<p>I was somewhat interested in the scene; it some-</p>
<p>times lulled, although it could not extinguish my</p>
<p>grief. During the ﬁrst day we travelled in a carriage.</p>
<p>In the morning we had seen the mountains at a dis-</p>
<p>tance, towards which we gradually advanced. We</p>
<p>perceived that the valley through which we wound,</p>
<p>and which was formed by the river Arve, whose</p>
<p>course we followed, closed in upon us by degrees;</p>
<p>and when the sun had set, we beheld immense</p>
<p>mountains and precipices overhanging us on every</p>
<p>side, and heard the sound of the river raging among</p>
<p>rocks, and the dashing of water-falls around.</p>
<p>The next day we pursued our journey upon</p>
<p>mules; and as we ascended still higher, the valley</p>
<p>assumed a more magniﬁcent and astonishing char-</p>
<p>acter. Ruined castles hanging on the precipices of</p>
<p>piny mountains; the impetuous Arve, and cottages</p>
<p>every here and there peeping forth from among the</p>
<p>trees, formed a scene of singular beauty. But it was</p>
<p>augmented and rendered sublime by the mighty</p>
<p>Alps, whose white and shining pyramids and domes</p>
<p>towered above all, as belonging to another earth,</p>
<p>the habitations of another race of beings.</p>
<p>We passed the bridge of Pelissier, where the</p>
<p>ravine, which the river forms, opened before us, and</p>
<p>we began to ascend the mountain that overhangs it.</p>
<p>Soon after we entered the valley of Chamounix.</p>
<p>This valley is more wonderful and sublime, but</p>
<p>not so beautiful and picturesque as that of Servox,</p>
<p>through which we had just passed. The high and</p>
<p>snowy mountains were its immediate boundaries;</p>
<p>but we saw no more ruined castles and fertile ﬁelds.</p>
<p>Immense glaciers approached the road; we heard</p>
<p>the rumbling thunder of the falling avalanche, and</p>
<p>marked the smoke of its passage. Mont Blânc, the</p>
<p>supreme and magniﬁcent Mont Blânc, raised itself</p>
<p>from the surrounding aiguilles, and its tremendous</p>
<p>dome overlooked the valley.</p>
<p>During this journey, I sometimes joined Eliza-</p>
<p>beth, and exerted myself to point out to her the</p>
<p>various beauties of the scene. I often suffered my</p>
<p>mule to lag behind, and indulged in the misery of</p>
<p>reﬂection. At other times I spurred on the animal</p>
<p>before my companions, that I might forget them,</p>
<p>the world, and, more than all, myself. When at a</p>
<p>distance, I alighted, and threw myself on the grass,</p>
<p>weighed down by horror and despair. At eight in</p>
<p>the evening I arrived at Chamounix. My father and</p>
<p>Elizabeth were very much fatigued; Ernest, who</p>
<p>accompanied us, was delighted, and in high spirits:</p>
<p>the only circumstance that detracted from his plea-</p>
<p>sure was the south wind, and the rain it seemed to</p>
<p>promise for the next day.</p>
<p>We retired early to our apartments, but not to</p>
<p>sleep; at least I did not. I remained many hours at</p>
<p>the window, watching the pallid lightning that played</p>
<p>above Mont Blânc, and listening to the rushing of</p>
<p>the Arve, which ran below my window.</p>
<p>Chapter II</p>
<p>THE next day, contrary to the prognostications of</p>
<p>our guides, was ﬁne, although clouded. We visited</p>
<p>the source of the Arveiron, and rode about the val-</p>
<p>ley until evening. These sublime and magniﬁcent</p>
<p>scenes afforded me the greatest consolation that I</p>
<p>was capable of receiving. They elevated me from</p>
<p>all littleness of feeling; and although they did not</p>
<p>remove my grief, they subdued and tranquillized it.</p>
<p>In some degree, also, they diverted my mind from</p>
<p>the thoughts over which it had brooded for the last</p>
<p>month. I returned in the evening, fatigued, but less</p>
<p>unhappy, and conversed with my family with more</p>
<p>157</p>
<p>cheerfulness than had been my custom for some</p>
<p>time. My father was pleased, and Elizabeth over-</p>
<p>joyed. “My dear cousin,” said she, “you see what</p>
<p>happiness you diffuse when you are happy; do not</p>
<p>relapse again!”</p>
<p>The following morning the rain poured down</p>
<p>in torrents, and thick mists hid the summits of the</p>
<p>mountains. I rose early, but felt unusually melan-</p>
<p>choly. The rain depressed me; my old feelings</p>
<p>recurred, and I was miserable. I knew how disap-</p>
<p>pointed my father would be at this sudden change,</p>
<p>and I wished to avoid him until I had recovered</p>
<p>myself so far as to be enabled to conceal those feel-</p>
<p>ings that overpowered me. I knew that they would</p>
<p>remain that day at the inn; and as I had ever inured</p>
<p>myself to rain, moisture, and cold, I resolved to go</p>
<p>alone to the summit of Montanvert. I remembered</p>
<p>the effect that the view of the tremendous and ever-</p>
<p>moving glacier had produced upon my mind when</p>
<p>I ﬁrst saw it. It had then ﬁlled me with a sublime</p>
<p>ecstacy that gave wings to the soul, and allowed</p>
<p>it to soar from the obscure world to light and joy.</p>
<p>The sight of the awful and majestic in nature had</p>
<p>indeed always the effect of solemnizing my mind,</p>
<p>and causing me to forget the passing cares of life. I</p>
<p>determined to go alone, for I was well acquainted</p>
<p>with the path, and the presence of another would</p>
<p>destroy the solitary grandeur of the scene.</p>
<p>The ascent is precipitous, but the path is cut</p>
<p>into continual and short windings, which enable you</p>
<p>to surmount the perpendicularity of the mountain.</p>
<p>It is a scene terriﬁcally desolate. In a thousand</p>
<p>spots the traces of the winter avalanche may be</p>
<p>perceived, where trees lie broken and strewed on</p>
<p>the ground; some entirely destroyed, others bent,</p>
<p>leaning upon the jutting rocks of the mountain, or</p>
<p>transversely upon other trees. The path, as you</p>
<p>ascend higher, is intersected by ravines of snow,</p>
<p>down which stones continually roll from above; one</p>
<p>of them is particularly dangerous, as the slightest</p>
<p>sound, such as even speaking in a loud voice, pro-</p>
<p>duces a concussion of air sufﬁcient to draw destruc-</p>
<p>tion upon the head of the speaker. The pines are</p>
<p>not tall or luxuriant, but they are sombre, and add</p>
<p>an air of severity to the scene. I looked on the val-</p>
<p>ley beneath; vast mists were rising from the rivers</p>
<p>which ran through it, and curling in thick wreaths</p>
<p>around the opposite mountains, whose summits</p>
<p>were hid in the uniform clouds, while rain poured</p>
<p>from the dark sky, and added to the melancholy</p>
<p>impression I received from the objects around me.</p>
<p>Alas! why does man boast of sensibilities supe-</p>
<p>rior to those apparent in the brute; it only renders</p>
<p>them more necessary beings. If our impulses were</p>
<p>conﬁned to hunger, thirst, and desire, we might be</p>
<p>nearly free; but now we are moved by every wind</p>
<p>that blows, and a chance word or scene that that</p>
<p>word may convey to us.</p>
<p>We rest; a dream has power to poison sleep.</p>
<p>We rise; one wand’ring thought pollutes the day.</p>
<p>We feel, conceive, or reason; laugh, or weep,</p>
<p>Embrace fond woe, or cast our cares away;</p>
<p>It is the same: for, be it joy or sorrow,</p>
<p>The path of its departure still is free.</p>
<p>Man’s yesterday may ne’er be like his morrow;</p>
<p>Nought may endure but mutability!</p>
<p>It was nearly noon when I arrived at the top of</p>
<p>the ascent. For some time I sat upon the rock that</p>
<p>overlooks the sea of ice. A mist covered both that</p>
<p>and the surrounding mountains. Presently a breeze</p>
<p>dissipated the cloud, and I descended upon the</p>
<p>glacier. The surface is very uneven, rising like the</p>
<p>waves of a troubled sea, descending low, and inter-</p>
<p>spersed by rifts that sink deep. The ﬁeld of ice is al-</p>
<p>most a league in width, but I spent nearly two hours</p>
<p>in crossing it. The opposite mountain is a bare per-</p>
<p>pendicular rock. From the side where I now stood</p>
<p>Montanvert was exactly opposite, at the distance</p>
<p>of a league; and above it rose Mont Blânc, in awful</p>
<p>majesty. I remained in a recess of the rock, gaz-</p>
<p>ing on this wonderful and stupendous scene. The</p>
<p>sea, or rather the vast river of ice, wound among its</p>
<p>dependent mountains, whose aërial summits hung</p>
<p>over its recesses. Their icy and glittering peaks</p>
<p>shone in the sunlight over the clouds. My heart,</p>
<p>which was before sorrowful, now swelled with some-</p>
<p>thing like joy; I exclaimed — “Wandering spirits, if</p>
<p>indeed ye wander, and do not rest in your narrow</p>
<p>beds, allow me this faint happiness, or take me, as</p>
<p>your companion, away from the joys of life.”</p>
<p>As I said this, I suddenly beheld the ﬁgure of a</p>
<p>man, at some distance, advancing towards me with</p>
<p>superhuman speed. He bounded over the crevices</p>
<p>in the ice, among which I had walked with caution;</p>
<p>his stature also, as he approached, seemed to ex-</p>
<p>ceed that of man. I was troubled: a mist came over</p>
<p>my eyes, and I felt a faintness seize me; but I was</p>
<p>quickly restored by the cold gale of the mountains. I</p>
<p>perceived, as the shape came nearer, (sight tremen-</p>
<p>dous and abhorred!) that it was the wretch whom I</p>
<p>had created. I trembled with rage and horror, resolv-</p>
<p>ing to wait his approach, and then close with him in</p>
<p>mortal combat. He approached; his countenance</p>
<p>bespoke bitter anguish, combined with disdain and</p>
<p>malignity, while its unearthly ugliness rendered it</p>
<p>almost too horrible for human eyes. But I scarcely</p>
<p>observed this; anger and hatred had at ﬁrst de-</p>
<p>prived me of utterance, and I recovered only to</p>
<p>overwhelm him with words expressive of furious</p>
<p>detestation and contempt.</p>
<p>“Devil!” I exclaimed, “do you dare approach</p>
<p>me? and do not you fear the ﬁerce vengeance of</p>
<p>my arm wreaked on your miserable head? Begone,</p>
<p>vile insect! or rather stay, that I may trample you</p>
<p>to dust! and, oh, that I could, with the extinction</p>
<p>of your miserable existence, restore those victims</p>
<p>whom you have so diabolically murdered!”</p>
<p>“I expected this reception,” said the dæmon. “All</p>
<p>men hate the wretched; how then must I be hated,</p>
<p>who am miserable beyond all living things! Yet you,</p>
<p>my creator, detest and spurn me, thy creature, to</p>
<p>whom thou art bound by ties only dissoluble by the</p>
<p>annihilation of one of us. You purpose to kill me.</p>
<p>How dare you sport thus with life? Do your duty</p>
<p>towards me, and I will do mine towards you and</p>
<p>the rest of mankind. If you will comply with my</p>
<p>conditions, I will leave them and you at peace; but</p>
<p>if you refuse, I will glut the maw of death, until it be</p>
<p>satiated with the blood of your remaining friends.”</p>
<p>“Abhorred monster! ﬁend that thou art! the</p>
<p>tortures of hell are too mild a vengeance for thy</p>
<p>crimes. Wretched devil! you reproach me with your</p>
<p>creation; come on then, that I may extinguish the</p>
<p>spark which I so negligently bestowed.”</p>
<p>My rage was without bounds; I sprang on him,</p>
<p>impelled by all the feelings which can arm one being</p>
<p>against the existence of another.</p>
<p>He easily eluded me, and said,</p>
<p>“Be calm! I entreat you to hear me, before you</p>
<p>give vent to your hatred on my devoted head. Have</p>
<p>I not suffered enough, that you seek to increase my</p>
<p>misery? Life, although it may only be an accumu-</p>
<p>lation of anguish, is dear to me, and I will defend</p>
<p>it. Remember, thou hast made me more powerful</p>
<p>than thyself; my height is superior to thine; my joints</p>
<p>more supple. But I will not be tempted to set myself</p>
<p>in opposition to thee. I am thy creature, and I will</p>
<p>be even mild and docile to my natural lord and king,</p>
<p>if thou wilt also perform thy part, the which thou</p>
<p>owest me. Oh, Frankenstein, be not equitable to</p>
<p>every other, and trample upon me alone, to whom</p>
<p>thy justice, and even thy clemency and affection,</p>
<p>is most due. Remember, that I am thy creature: I</p>
<p>ought to be thy Adam; but I am rather the fallen</p>
<p>angel, whom thou drivest from joy for no misdeed.</p>
<p>Every where I see bliss, from which I alone am ir-</p>
<p>revocably excluded. I was benevolent and good;</p>
<p>misery made me a ﬁend. Make me happy, and I</p>
<p>shall again be virtuous.”</p>
<p>“Begone! I will not hear you. There can be no</p>
<p>community between you and me; we are enemies.</p>
<p>Begone, or let us try our strength in a ﬁght, in which</p>
<p>one must fall.”</p>
<p>“How can I move thee? Will no entreaties cause</p>
<p>thee to turn a favourable eye upon thy creature,</p>
<p>who implores thy goodness and compassion? Be-</p>
<p>lieve me, Frankenstein: I was benevolent; my soul</p>
<p>glowed with love and humanity: but am I not alone,</p>
<p>miserably alone? You, my creator, abhor me; what</p>
<p>hope can I gather from your fellow-creatures, who</p>
<p>owe me nothing? they spurn and hate me. The</p>
<p>desert mountains and dreary glaciers are my refuge.</p>
<p>I have wandered here many days; the caves of ice,</p>
<p>which I only do not fear, are a dwelling to me, and</p>
<p>the only one which man does not grudge. These</p>
<p>bleak skies I hail, for they are kinder to me than your</p>
<p>fellow-beings. If the multitude of mankind knew of</p>
<p>my existence, they would do as you do, and arm</p>
<p>themselves for my destruction. Shall I not then hate</p>
<p>them who abhor me? I will keep no terms with</p>
<p>my enemies. I am miserable, and they shall share</p>
<p>my wretchedness. Yet it is in your power to rec-</p>
<p>ompense me, and deliver them from an evil which</p>
<p>it only remains for you to make so great, that not</p>
<p>only you and your family, but thousands of others,</p>
<p>shall be swallowed up in the whirlwinds of its rage.</p>
<p>Let your compassion be moved, and do not disdain</p>
<p>me. Listen to my tale: when you have heard that,</p>
<p>abandon or commiserate me, as you shall judge</p>
<p>that I deserve. But hear me. The guilty are allowed,</p>
<p>by human laws, bloody as they may be, to speak</p>
<p>in their own defence before they are condemned.</p>
<p>Listen to me, Frankenstein. You accuse me of mur-</p>
<p>der; and yet you would, with a satisﬁed conscience,</p>
<p>destroy your own creature. Oh, praise the eternal</p>
<p>justice of man! Yet I ask you not to spare me: listen</p>
<p>to me; and then, if you can, and if you will, destroy</p>
<p>the work of your hands.”</p>
<p>“Why do you call to my remembrance circum-</p>
<p>stances of which I shudder to reﬂect, that I have</p>
<p>been the miserable origin and author? Cursed be</p>
<p>the day, abhorred devil, in which you ﬁrst saw light!</p>
<p>Cursed (although I curse myself) be the hands that</p>
<p>formed you! You have made me wretched beyond</p>
<p>expression. You have left me no power to consider</p>
<p>whether I am just to you, or not. Begone! relieve</p>
<p>me from the sight of your detested form.”</p>
<p>“Thus I relieve thee, my creator,” he said, and</p>
<p>placed his hated hands before my eyes, which I</p>
<p>ﬂung from me with violence; “thus I take from thee</p>
<p>a sight which you abhor. Still thou canst listen to me,</p>
<p>and grant me thy compassion. By the virtues that</p>
<p>I once possessed, I demand this from you. Hear</p>
<p>my tale; it is long and strange, and the temperature</p>
<p>of this place is not ﬁtting to your ﬁne sensations;</p>
<p>come to the hut upon the mountain. The sun is</p>
<p>yet high in the heavens; before it descends to hide</p>
<p>itself behind yon snowy precipices, and illuminate</p>
<p>another world, you will have heard my story, and</p>
<p>can decide. On you it rests, whether I quit for ever</p>
<p>the neighbourhood of man, and lead a harmless</p>
<p>life, or become the scourge of your fellow-creatures,</p>
<p>and the author of your own speedy ruin.”</p>
<p>As he said this, he led the way across the ice:</p>
<p>I followed. My heart was full, and I did not answer</p>
<p>him; but, as I proceeded, I weighed the various ar-</p>
<p>guments that he had used, and determined at least</p>
<p>to listen to his tale. I was partly urged by curiosity,</p>
<p>and compassion conﬁrmed my resolution. I had</p>
<p>hitherto supposed him to be the murderer of my</p>
<p>brother, and I eagerly sought a conﬁrmation or de-</p>
<p>nial of this opinion. For the ﬁrst time, also, I felt what</p>
<p>the duties of a creator towards his creature were,</p>
<p>and that I ought to render him happy before I com-</p>
<p>plained of his wickedness. These motives urged</p>
<p>me to comply with his demand. We crossed the ice,</p>
<p>therefore, and ascended the opposite rock. The air</p>
<p>was cold, and the rain again began to descend: we</p>
<p>entered the hut, the ﬁend with an air of exultation,</p>
<p>I with a heavy heart, and depressed spirits. But I</p>
<p>consented to listen; and, seating myself by the ﬁre</p>
<p>which my odious companion had lighted, he thus</p>
<p>began his tale.</p>
<p>Chapter III</p>
<p>“IT is with considerable difﬁculty that I remember</p>
<p>the original æra of my being: all the events of that</p>
<p>period appear confused and indistinct. A strange</p>
<p>multiplicity of sensations seized me, and I saw, felt,</p>
<p>heard, and smelt, at the same time; and it was,</p>
<p>indeed, a long time before I learned to distinguish</p>
<p>between the operations of my various senses. By</p>
<p>degrees, I remember, a stronger light pressed upon</p>
<p>my nerves, so that I was obliged to shut my eyes.</p>
<p>Darkness then came over me, and troubled me; but</p>
<p>hardly had I felt this, when, by opening my eyes,</p>
<p>as I now suppose, the light poured in upon me</p>
<p>170</p>
<p>again. I walked, and, I believe, descended; but I</p>
<p>presently found a great alteration in my sensations.</p>
<p>Before, dark and opaque bodies had surrounded</p>
<p>me, impervious to my touch or sight; but I now found</p>
<p>that I could wander on at liberty, with no obstacles</p>
<p>which I could not either surmount or avoid. The</p>
<p>light became more and more oppressive to me;</p>
<p>and, the heat wearying me as I walked, I sought a</p>
<p>place where I could receive shade. This was the</p>
<p>forest near Ingolstadt; and here I lay by the side of a</p>
<p>brook resting from my fatigue, until I felt tormented</p>
<p>by hunger and thirst. This roused me from my</p>
<p>nearly dormant state, and I ate some berries which</p>
<p>I found hanging on the trees, or lying on the ground.</p>
<p>I slaked my thirst at the brook; and then lying down,</p>
<p>was overcome by sleep.</p>
<p>“It was dark when I awoke; I felt cold also, and</p>
<p>half-frightened as it were instinctively, ﬁnding myself</p>
<p>so desolate. Before I had quitted your apartment,</p>
<p>on a sensation of cold, I had covered myself with</p>
<p>some clothes; but these were insufﬁcient to secure</p>
<p>me from the dews of night. I was a poor, helpless,</p>
<p>miserable wretch; I knew, and could distinguish,</p>
<p>nothing; but, feeling pain invade me on all sides, I</p>
<p>sat down and wept.</p>
<p>“Soon a gentle light stole over the heavens, and</p>
<p>gave me a sensation of pleasure. I started up, and</p>
<p>beheld a radiant form rise from among the trees.</p>
<p>I gazed with a kind of wonder. It moved slowly,</p>
<p>but it enlightened my path; and I again went out</p>
<p>in search of berries. I was still cold, when under</p>
<p>one of the trees I found a huge cloak, with which I</p>
<p>covered myself, and sat down upon the ground. No</p>
<p>distinct ideas occupied my mind; all was confused.</p>
<p>I felt light, and hunger, and thirst, and darkness;</p>
<p>innumerable sounds rung in my ears, and on all</p>
<p>sides various scents saluted me: the only object</p>
<p>that I could distinguish was the bright moon, and I</p>
<p>ﬁxed my eyes on that with pleasure.</p>
<p>“Several changes of day and night passed, and</p>
<p>the orb of night had greatly lessened when I be-</p>
<p>gan to distinguish my sensations from each other. I</p>
<p>gradually saw plainly the clear stream that supplied</p>
<p>me with drink, and the trees that shaded me with</p>
<p>their foliage. I was delighted when I ﬁrst discov-</p>
<p>ered that a pleasant sound, which often saluted</p>
<p>my ears, proceeded from the throats of the little</p>
<p>winged animals who had often intercepted the light</p>
<p>from my eyes. I began also to observe, with greater</p>
<p>accuracy, the forms that surrounded me, and to</p>
<p>perceive the boundaries of the radiant roof of light</p>
<p>which canopied me. Sometimes I tried to imitate</p>
<p>the pleasant songs of the birds, but was unable.</p>
<p>Sometimes I wished to express my sensations in</p>
<p>my own mode, but the uncouth and inarticulate</p>
<p>sounds which broke from me frightened me into</p>
<p>silence again.</p>
<p>“The moon had disappeared from the night, and</p>
<p>again, with a lessened form, shewed itself, while I</p>
<p>still remained in the forest. My sensations had, by</p>
<p>this time, become distinct, and my mind received</p>
<p>every day additional ideas. My eyes became ac-</p>
<p>customed to the light, and to perceive objects in</p>
<p>their right forms; I distinguished the insect from</p>
<p>the herb, and, by degrees, one herb from another.</p>
<p>I found that the sparrow uttered none but harsh</p>
<p>notes, whilst those of the blackbird and thrush were</p>
<p>sweet and enticing.</p>
<p>“One day, when I was oppressed by cold, I</p>
<p>found a ﬁre which had been left by some wander-</p>
<p>ing beggars, and was overcome with delight at the</p>
<p>warmth I experienced from it. In my joy I thrust my</p>
<p>hand into the live embers, but quickly drew it out</p>
<p>again with a cry of pain. How strange, I thought,</p>
<p>that the same cause should produce such opposite</p>
<p>effects! I examined the materials of the ﬁre, and to</p>
<p>my joy found it to be composed of wood. I quickly</p>
<p>collected some branches; but they were wet, and</p>
<p>would not burn. I was pained at this, and sat still</p>
<p>watching the operation of the ﬁre. The wet wood</p>
<p>which I had placed near the heat dried, and itself</p>
<p>became inﬂamed. I reﬂected on this; and, by touch-</p>
<p>ing the various branches, I discovered the cause,</p>
<p>and busied myself in collecting a great quantity of</p>
<p>wood, that I might dry it, and have a plentiful supply</p>
<p>of ﬁre. When night came on, and brought sleep with</p>
<p>it, I was in the greatest fear lest my ﬁre should be</p>
<p>extinguished. I covered it carefully with dry wood</p>
<p>and leaves, and placed wet branches upon it; and</p>
<p>then, spreading my cloak, I lay on the ground, and</p>
<p>sunk into sleep.</p>
<p>“It was morning when I awoke, and my ﬁrst care</p>
<p>was to visit the ﬁre. I uncovered it, and a gentle</p>
<p>breeze quickly fanned it into a ﬂame. I observed this</p>
<p>also, and contrived a fan of branches, which roused</p>
<p>the embers when they were nearly extinguished.</p>
<p>When night came again, I found, with pleasure, that</p>
<p>the ﬁre gave light as well as heat; and that the</p>
<p>discovery of this element was useful to me in my</p>
<p>food; for I found some of the offals that the travellers</p>
<p>had left had been roasted, and tasted much more</p>
<p>savoury than the berries I gathered from the trees.</p>
<p>I tried, therefore, to dress my food in the same</p>
<p>manner, placing it on the live embers. I found that</p>
<p>the berries were spoiled by this operation, and the</p>
<p>nuts and roots much improved.</p>
<p>“Food, however, became scarce; and I often</p>
<p>spent the whole day searching in vain for a few</p>
<p>acorns to assuage the pangs of hunger. When I</p>
<p>found this, I resolved to quit the place that I had</p>
<p>hitherto inhabited, to seek for one where the few</p>
<p>wants I experienced would be more easily satisﬁed.</p>
<p>In this emigration, I exceedingly lamented the loss</p>
<p>of the ﬁre which I had obtained through accident,</p>
<p>and knew not how to re-produce it. I gave several</p>
<p>hours to the serious consideration of this difﬁculty;</p>
<p>but I was obliged to relinquish all attempt to supply</p>
<p>it; and, wrapping myself up in my cloak, I struck</p>
<p>across the wood towards the setting sun. I passed</p>
<p>three days in these rambles, and at length discov-</p>
<p>ered the open country. A great fall of snow had</p>
<p>taken place the night before, and the ﬁelds were of</p>
<p>one uniform white; the appearance was disconso-</p>
<p>late, and I found my feet chilled by the cold damp</p>
<p>substance that covered the ground.</p>
<p>“It was about seven in the morning, and I longed</p>
<p>to obtain food and shelter; at length I perceived a</p>
<p>small hut, on a rising ground, which had doubtless</p>
<p>been built for the convenience of some shepherd.</p>
<p>This was a new sight to me; and I examined the</p>
<p>structure with great curiosity. Finding the door open,</p>
<p>I entered. An old man sat in it, near a ﬁre, over</p>
<p>which he was preparing his breakfast. He turned</p>
<p>on hearing a noise; and, perceiving me, shrieked</p>
<p>loudly, and, quitting the hut, ran across the ﬁelds</p>
<p>with a speed of which his debilitated form hardly ap-</p>
<p>peared capable. His appearance, different from any</p>
<p>I had ever before seen, and his ﬂight, somewhat</p>
<p>surprised me. But I was enchanted by the appear-</p>
<p>ance of the hut: here the snow and rain could not</p>
<p>penetrate; the ground was dry; and it presented to</p>
<p>me then as exquisite and divine a retreat as Pandæ-</p>
<p>monium appeared to the dæmons of hell after their</p>
<p>sufferings in the lake of ﬁre. I greedily devoured the</p>
<p>remnants of the shepherd’s breakfast, which con-</p>
<p>sisted of bread, cheese, milk, and wine; the latter,</p>
<p>however, I did not like. Then overcome by fatigue, I</p>
<p>lay down among some straw, and fell asleep.</p>
<p>“It was noon when I awoke; and, allured by the</p>
<p>warmth of the sun, which shone brightly on the</p>
<p>white ground, I determined to recommence my trav-</p>
<p>els; and, depositing the remains of the peasant’s</p>
<p>breakfast in a wallet I found, I proceeded across the</p>
<p>ﬁelds for several hours, until at sunset I arrived at a</p>
<p>village. How miraculous did this appear! the huts,</p>
<p>the neater cottages, and stately houses, engaged</p>
<p>my admiration by turns. The vegetables in the gar-</p>
<p>dens, the milk and cheese that I saw placed at the</p>
<p>windows of some of the cottages, allured my ap-</p>
<p>petite. One of the best of these I entered; but I had</p>
<p>hardly placed my foot within the door, before the</p>
<p>children shrieked, and one of the women fainted.</p>
<p>The whole village was roused; some ﬂed, some at-</p>
<p>tacked me, until, grievously bruised by stones and</p>
<p>many other kinds of missile weapons, I escaped</p>
<p>to the open country, and fearfully took refuge in</p>
<p>a low hovel, quite bare, and making a wretched</p>
<p>appearance after the palaces I had beheld in the</p>
<p>village. This hovel, however, joined a cottage of a</p>
<p>neat and pleasant appearance; but, after my late</p>
<p>dearly-bought experience, I dared not enter it. My</p>
<p>place of refuge was constructed of wood, but so</p>
<p>low, that I could with difﬁculty sit upright in it. No</p>
<p>wood, however, was placed on the earth, which</p>
<p>formed the ﬂoor, but it was dry; and although the</p>
<p>wind entered it by innumerable chinks, I found it an</p>
<p>agreeable asylum from the snow and rain.</p>
<p>“Here then I retreated, and lay down, happy to</p>
<p>have found a shelter, however miserable, from the</p>
<p>inclemency of the season, and still more from the</p>
<p>barbarity of man.</p>
<p>“As soon as morning dawned, I crept from my</p>
<p>kennel, that I might view the adjacent cottage, and</p>
<p>discover if I could remain in the habitation I had</p>
<p>found. It was situated against the back of the cot-</p>
<p>tage, and surrounded on the sides which were ex-</p>
<p>posed by a pig-stye and a clear pool of water. One</p>
<p>part was open, and by that I had crept in; but now</p>
<p>I covered every crevice by which I might be per-</p>
<p>ceived with stones and wood, yet in such a manner</p>
<p>that I might move them on occasion to pass out: all</p>
<p>the light I enjoyed came through the stye, and that</p>
<p>was sufﬁcient for me.</p>
<p>“Having thus arranged my dwelling, and car-</p>
<p>peted it with clean straw, I retired; for I saw the</p>
<p>ﬁgure of a man at a distance, and I remembered</p>
<p>too well my treatment the night before, to trust my-</p>
<p>self in his power. I had ﬁrst, however, provided</p>
<p>for my sustenance for that day, by a loaf of coarse</p>
<p>bread, which I purloined, and a cup with which I</p>
<p>could drink, more conveniently than from my hand,</p>
<p>of the pure water which ﬂowed by my retreat. The</p>
<p>ﬂoor was a little raised, so that it was kept perfectly</p>
<p>dry, and by its vicinity to the chimney of the cottage</p>
<p>it was tolerably warm.</p>
<p>“Being thus provided, I resolved to reside in this</p>
<p>hovel, until something should occur which might</p>
<p>alter my determination. It was indeed a paradise,</p>
<p>compared to the bleak forest, my former residence,</p>
<p>the rain-dropping branches, and dank earth. I ate</p>
<p>my breakfast with pleasure, and was about to re-</p>
<p>move a plank to procure myself a little water, when</p>
<p>I heard a step, and, looking through a small chink,</p>
<p>I beheld a young creature, with a pail on her head,</p>
<p>passing before my hovel. The girl was young and of</p>
<p>gentle demeanour, unlike what I have since found</p>
<p>cottagers and farm-house servants to be. Yet she</p>
<p>was meanly dressed, a coarse blue petticoat and</p>
<p>a linen jacket being her only garb; her fair hair was</p>
<p>plaited, but not adorned; she looked patient, yet</p>
<p>sad. I lost sight of her; and in about a quarter of an</p>
<p>hour she returned, bearing the pail, which was now</p>
<p>partly ﬁlled with milk. As she walked along, seem-</p>
<p>ingly incommoded by the burden, a young man met</p>
<p>her, whose countenance expressed a deeper de-</p>
<p>spondence. Uttering a few sounds with an air of</p>
<p>melancholy, he took the pail from her head, and</p>
<p>bore it to the cottage himself. She followed, and</p>
<p>they disappeared. Presently I saw the young man</p>
<p>again, with some tools in his hand, cross the ﬁeld</p>
<p>behind the cottage; and the girl was also busied,</p>
<p>sometimes in the house, and sometimes in the</p>
<p>yard.</p>
<p>“On examining my dwelling, I found that one of</p>
<p>the windows of the cottage had formerly occupied</p>
<p>a part of it, but the panes had been ﬁlled up with</p>
<p>wood. In one of these was a small and almost im-</p>
<p>perceptible chink, through which the eye could just</p>
<p>penetrate. Through this crevice, a small room was</p>
<p>visible, white-washed and clean, but very bare of</p>
<p>furniture. In one corner, near a small ﬁre, sat an</p>
<p>old man, leaning his head on his hands in a dis-</p>
<p>consolate attitude. The young girl was occupied in</p>
<p>arranging the cottage; but presently she took some-</p>
<p>thing out of a drawer, which employed her hands,</p>
<p>and she sat down beside the old man, who, taking</p>
<p>up an instrument, began to play, and to produce</p>
<p>sounds, sweeter than the voice of the thrush or the</p>
<p>nightingale. It was a lovely sight, even to me, poor</p>
<p>wretch! who had never beheld aught beautiful be-</p>
<p>fore. The silver hair and benevolent countenance</p>
<p>of the aged cottager, won my reverence; while the</p>
<p>gentle manners of the girl enticed my love. He</p>
<p>played a sweet mournful air, which I perceived drew</p>
<p>tears from the eyes of his amiable companion, of</p>
<p>which the old man took no notice, until she sobbed</p>
<p>audibly; he then pronounced a few sounds, and</p>
<p>the fair creature, leaving her work, knelt at his feet.</p>
<p>He raised her, and smiled with such kindness and</p>
<p>affection, that I felt sensations of a peculiar and</p>
<p>over-powering nature: they were a mixture of pain</p>
<p>and pleasure, such as I had never before experi-</p>
<p>enced, either from hunger or cold, warmth or food;</p>
<p>and I withdrew from the window, unable to bear</p>
<p>these emotions.</p>
<p>“Soon after this the young man returned, bear-</p>
<p>ing on his shoulders a load of wood. The girl met</p>
<p>him at the door, helped to relieve him of his bur-</p>
<p>den, and, taking some of the fuel into the cottage,</p>
<p>placed it on the ﬁre; then she and the youth went</p>
<p>apart into a nook of the cottage, and he shewed her</p>
<p>a large loaf and a piece of cheese. She seemed</p>
<p>pleased; and went into the garden for some roots</p>
<p>and plants, which she placed in water, and then</p>
<p>upon the ﬁre. She afterwards continued her work,</p>
<p>whilst the young man went into the garden, and</p>
<p>appeared busily employed in digging and pulling</p>
<p>up roots. After he had been employed thus about</p>
<p>an hour, the young woman joined him, and they</p>
<p>entered the cottage together.</p>
<p>“The old man had, in the mean time, been pen-</p>
<p>sive; but, on the appearance of his companions, he</p>
<p>assumed a more cheerful air, and they sat down to</p>
<p>eat. The meal was quickly dispatched. The young</p>
<p>woman was again occupied in arranging the cot-</p>
<p>tage; the old man walked before the cottage in the</p>
<p>sun for a few minutes, leaning on the arm of the</p>
<p>youth. Nothing could exceed in beauty the contrast</p>
<p>between these two excellent creatures. One was</p>
<p>old, with silver hairs and a countenance beaming</p>
<p>with benevolence and love: the younger was slight</p>
<p>and graceful in his ﬁgure, and his features were</p>
<p>moulded with the ﬁnest symmetry; yet his eyes and</p>
<p>attitude expressed the utmost sadness and despon-</p>
<p>dency. The old man returned to the cottage; and</p>
<p>the youth, with tools different from those he had</p>
<p>used in the morning, directed his steps across the</p>
<p>ﬁelds.</p>
<p>“Night quickly shut in; but, to my extreme won-</p>
<p>der, I found that the cottagers had a means of pro-</p>
<p>longing light, by the use of tapers, and was de-</p>
<p>lighted to ﬁnd, that the setting of the sun did not put</p>
<p>an end to the pleasure I experienced in watching</p>
<p>my human neighbours. In the evening, the young</p>
<p>girl and her companion were employed in various</p>
<p>occupations which I did not understand; and the</p>
<p>old man again took up the instrument, which pro-</p>
<p>duced the divine sounds that had enchanted me</p>
<p>in the morning. So soon as he had ﬁnished, the</p>
<p>youth began, not to play, but to utter sounds that</p>
<p>were monotonous, and neither resembling the har-</p>
<p>mony of the old man’s instrument or the songs of</p>
<p>the birds; I since found that he read aloud, but at</p>
<p>that time I knew nothing of the science of words or</p>
<p>letters.</p>
<p>“The family, after having been thus occupied for</p>
<p>a short time, extinguished their lights, and retired,</p>
<p>as I conjectured, to rest.”</p>
<p>Chapter IV</p>
<p>“I LAY on my straw, but I could not sleep. I thought</p>
<p>of the occurrences of the day. What chieﬂy struck</p>
<p>me was the gentle manners of these people; and I</p>
<p>longed to join them, but dared not. I remembered</p>
<p>too well the treatment I had suffered the night before</p>
<p>from the barbarous villagers, and resolved, what-</p>
<p>ever course of conduct I might hereafter think it</p>
<p>right to pursue, that for the present I would remain</p>
<p>quietly in my hovel, watching, and endeavouring to</p>
<p>discover the motives which inﬂuenced their actions.</p>
<p>“The cottagers arose the next morning before</p>
<p>the sun. The young woman arranged the cottage,</p>
<p>186</p>
<p>and prepared the food; and the youth departed after</p>
<p>the ﬁrst meal.</p>
<p>“This day was passed in the same routine as</p>
<p>that which preceded it. The young man was con-</p>
<p>stantly employed out of doors, and the girl in various</p>
<p>laborious occupations within. The old man, whom</p>
<p>I soon perceived to be blind, employed his leisure</p>
<p>hours on his instrument, or in contemplation. Noth-</p>
<p>ing could exceed the love and respect which the</p>
<p>younger cottagers exhibited towards their venera-</p>
<p>ble companion. They performed towards him every</p>
<p>little ofﬁce of affection and duty with gentleness;</p>
<p>and he rewarded them by his benevolent smiles.</p>
<p>“They were not entirely happy. The young man</p>
<p>and his companion often went apart, and appeared</p>
<p>to weep. I saw no cause for their unhappiness;</p>
<p>but I was deeply affected by it. If such lovely crea-</p>
<p>tures were miserable, it was less strange that I, an</p>
<p>imperfect and solitary being, should be wretched.</p>
<p>Yet why were these gentle beings unhappy? They</p>
<p>possessed a delightful house (for such it was in my</p>
<p>eyes), and every luxury; they had a ﬁre to warm</p>
<p>them when chill, and delicious viands when hun-</p>
<p>gry; they were dressed in excellent clothes; and,</p>
<p>still more, they enjoyed one another’s company and</p>
<p>speech, interchanging each day looks of affection</p>
<p>and kindness. What did their tears imply? Did they</p>
<p>really express pain? I was at ﬁrst unable to solve</p>
<p>these questions; but perpetual attention, and time,</p>
<p>explained to me many appearances which were at</p>
<p>ﬁrst enigmatic.</p>
<p>“A considerable period elapsed before I discov-</p>
<p>ered one of the causes of the uneasiness of this</p>
<p>amiable family; it was poverty: and they suffered</p>
<p>that evil in a very distressing degree. Their nourish-</p>
<p>ment consisted entirely of the vegetables of their</p>
<p>garden, and the milk of one cow, who gave very little</p>
<p>during the winter, when its masters could scarcely</p>
<p>procure food to support it. They often, I believe,</p>
<p>suffered the pangs of hunger very poignantly, espe-</p>
<p>cially the two younger cottagers; for several times</p>
<p>they placed food before the old man, when they</p>
<p>reserved none for themselves.</p>
<p>“This trait of kindness moved me sensibly. I</p>
<p>had been accustomed, during the night, to steal</p>
<p>a part of their store for my own consumption; but</p>
<p>when I found that in doing this I inﬂicted pain on</p>
<p>the cottagers, I abstained, and satisﬁed myself with</p>
<p>berries, nuts, and roots, which I gathered from a</p>
<p>neighbouring wood.</p>
<p>“I discovered also another means through which</p>
<p>I was enabled to assist their labours. I found that</p>
<p>the youth spent a great part of each day in collect-</p>
<p>ing wood for the family ﬁre; and, during the night, I</p>
<p>often took his tools, the use of which I quickly dis-</p>
<p>covered, and brought home ﬁring sufﬁcient for the</p>
<p>consumption of several days.</p>
<p>“I remember, the ﬁrst time that I did this, the</p>
<p>young woman, when she opened the door in the</p>
<p>morning, appeared greatly astonished on seeing</p>
<p>a great pile of wood on the outside. She uttered</p>
<p>some words in a loud voice, and the youth joined</p>
<p>her, who also expressed surprise. I observed, with</p>
<p>pleasure, that he did not go to the forest that day,</p>
<p>but spent it in repairing the cottage, and cultivating</p>
<p>the garden.</p>
<p>“By degrees I made a discovery of still greater</p>
<p>moment. I found that these people possessed a</p>
<p>method of communicating their experience and feel-</p>
<p>ings to one another by articulate sounds. I per-</p>
<p>ceived that the words they spoke sometimes pro-</p>
<p>duced pleasure or pain, smiles or sadness, in the</p>
<p>minds and countenances of the hearers. This was</p>
<p>indeed a godlike science, and I ardently desired</p>
<p>to become acquainted with it. But I was bafﬂed in</p>
<p>every attempt I made for this purpose. Their pronun-</p>
<p>ciation was quick; and the words they uttered, not</p>
<p>having any apparent connexion with visible objects,</p>
<p>I was unable to discover any clue by which I could</p>
<p>unravel the mystery of their reference. By great</p>
<p>application, however, and after having remained</p>
<p>during the space of several revolutions of the moon</p>
<p>in my hovel, I discovered the names that were given</p>
<p>to some of the most familiar objects of discourse: I</p>
<p>learned and applied the words ﬁre, milk, bread, and</p>
<p>wood. I learned also the names of the cottagers</p>
<p>themselves. The youth and his companion had</p>
<p>each of them several names, but the old man had</p>
<p>only one, which was father. The girl was called sis-</p>
<p>ter, or Agatha; and the youth Felix, brother, or son.</p>
<p>I cannot describe the delight I felt when I learned</p>
<p>the ideas appropriated to each of these sounds,</p>
<p>and was able to pronounce them. I distinguished</p>
<p>several other words, without being able as yet to</p>
<p>understand or apply them; such as good, dearest,</p>
<p>unhappy.</p>
<p>“I spent the winter in this manner. The gentle</p>
<p>manners and beauty of the cottagers greatly en-</p>
<p>deared them to me: when they were unhappy, I felt</p>
<p>depressed; when they rejoiced, I sympathized in</p>
<p>their joys. I saw few human beings beside them;</p>
<p>and if any other happened to enter the cottage, their</p>
<p>harsh manners and rude gait only enhanced to me</p>
<p>the superior accomplishments of my friends. The</p>
<p>old man, I could perceive, often endeavoured to</p>
<p>encourage his children, as sometimes I found that</p>
<p>he called them, to cast off their melancholy. He</p>
<p>would talk in a cheerful accent, with an expression</p>
<p>of goodness that bestowed pleasure even upon me.</p>
<p>Agatha listened with respect, her eyes sometimes</p>
<p>ﬁlled with tears, which she endeavoured to wipe</p>
<p>away unperceived; but I generally found that her</p>
<p>countenance and tone were more cheerful after</p>
<p>having listened to the exhortations of her father. It</p>
<p>was not thus with Felix. He was always the saddest</p>
<p>of the groupe; and, even to my unpractised senses,</p>
<p>he appeared to have suffered more deeply than his</p>
<p>friends. But if his countenance was more sorrowful,</p>
<p>his voice was more cheerful than that of his sister,</p>
<p>especially when he addressed the old man.</p>
<p>“I could mention innumerable instances, which,</p>
<p>although slight, marked the dispositions of these</p>
<p>amiable cottagers. In the midst of poverty and want,</p>
<p>Felix carried with pleasure to his sister the ﬁrst</p>
<p>little white ﬂower that peeped out from beneath the</p>
<p>snowy ground. Early in the morning before she had</p>
<p>risen, he cleared away the snow that obstructed her</p>
<p>path to the milk-house, drew water from the well,</p>
<p>and brought the wood from the out-house, where,</p>
<p>to his perpetual astonishment, he found his store</p>
<p>always replenished by an invisible hand. In the day,</p>
<p>I believe, he worked sometimes for a neighbouring</p>
<p>farmer, because he often went forth, and did not</p>
<p>return until dinner, yet brought no wood with him. At</p>
<p>other times he worked in the garden; but, as there</p>
<p>was little to do in the frosty season, he read to the</p>
<p>old man and Agatha.</p>
<p>“This reading had puzzled me extremely at ﬁrst;</p>
<p>but, by degrees, I discovered that he uttered many</p>
<p>of the same sounds when he read as when he</p>
<p>talked. I conjectured, therefore, that he found on</p>
<p>the paper signs for speech which he understood,</p>
<p>and I ardently longed to comprehend these also;</p>
<p>but how was that possible, when I did not even un-</p>
<p>derstand the sounds for which they stood as signs?</p>
<p>I improved, however, sensibly in this science, but</p>
<p>not sufﬁciently to follow up any kind of conversation,</p>
<p>although I applied my whole mind to the endeavour:</p>
<p>for I easily perceived that, although I eagerly longed</p>
<p>to discover myself to the cottagers, I ought not to</p>
<p>make the attempt until I had ﬁrst become master of</p>
<p>their language; which knowledge might enable me</p>
<p>to make them overlook the deformity of my ﬁgure;</p>
<p>for with this also the contrast perpetually presented</p>
<p>to my eyes had made me acquainted.</p>
<p>“I had admired the perfect forms of my cottagers</p>
<p>— their grace, beauty, and delicate complexions:</p>
<p>but how was I terriﬁed, when I viewed myself in a</p>
<p>transparent pool! At ﬁrst I started back, unable to</p>
<p>believe that it was indeed I who was reﬂected in</p>
<p>the mirror; and when I became fully convinced that</p>
<p>I was in reality the monster that I am, I was ﬁlled</p>
<p>with the bitterest sensations of despondence and</p>
<p>mortiﬁcation. Alas! I did not yet entirely know the</p>
<p>fatal effects of this miserable deformity.</p>
<p>“As the sun became warmer, and the light of</p>
<p>day longer, the snow vanished, and I beheld the</p>
<p>bare trees and the black earth. From this time</p>
<p>Felix was more employed; and the heart-moving</p>
<p>indications of impending famine disappeared. Their</p>
<p>food, as I afterwards found, was coarse, but it was</p>
<p>wholesome; and they procured a sufﬁciency of it.</p>
<p>Several new kinds of plants sprung up in the garden,</p>
<p>which they dressed; and these signs of comfort</p>
<p>increased daily as the season advanced.</p>
<p>“The old man, leaning on his son, walked each</p>
<p>day at noon, when it did not rain, as I found it was</p>
<p>called when the heavens poured forth its waters.</p>
<p>This frequently took place; but a high wind quickly</p>
<p>dried the earth, and the season became far more</p>
<p>pleasant than it had been.</p>
<p>“My mode of life in my hovel was uniform. Dur-</p>
<p>ing the morning I attended the motions of the cot-</p>
<p>tagers; and when they were dispersed in various</p>
<p>occupations, I slept: the remainder of the day was</p>
<p>spent in observing my friends. When they had re-</p>
