<p>tired to rest, if there was any moon, or the night</p>
<p>was star-light, I went into the woods, and collected</p>
<p>my own food and fuel for the cottage. When I re-</p>
<p>turned, as often as it was necessary, I cleared their</p>
<p>path from the snow, and performed those ofﬁces</p>
<p>that I had seen done by Felix. I afterwards found</p>
<p>that these labours, performed by an invisible hand,</p>
<p>greatly astonished them; and once or twice I heard</p>
<p>them, on these occasions, utter the words good</p>
<p>spirit, wonderful; but I did not then understand the</p>
<p>signiﬁcation of these terms.</p>
<p>“My thoughts now became more active, and</p>
<p>I longed to discover the motives and feelings of</p>
<p>these lovely creatures; I was inquisitive to know</p>
<p>why Felix appeared so miserable, and Agatha so</p>
<p>sad. I thought (foolish wretch!) that it might be in</p>
<p>my power to restore happiness to these deserving</p>
<p>people. When I slept, or was absent, the forms of</p>
<p>the venerable blind father, the gentle Agatha, and</p>
<p>the excellent Felix, ﬂitted before me. I looked upon</p>
<p>them as superior beings, who would be the arbiters</p>
<p>of my future destiny. I formed in my imagination a</p>
<p>thousand pictures of presenting myself to them, and</p>
<p>their reception of me. I imagined that they would</p>
<p>be disgusted, until, by my gentle demeanour and</p>
<p>conciliating words, I should ﬁrst win their favour,</p>
<p>and afterwards their love.</p>
<p>“These thoughts exhilarated me, and led me</p>
<p>to apply with fresh ardour to the acquiring the art</p>
<p>of language. My organs were indeed harsh, but</p>
<p>supple; and although my voice was very unlike the</p>
<p>soft music of their tones, yet I pronounced such</p>
<p>words as I understood with tolerable ease. It was</p>
<p>as the ass and the lap-dog; yet surely the gentle</p>
<p>ass, whose intentions were affectionate, although</p>
<p>his manners were rude, deserved better treatment</p>
<p>than blows and execration.</p>
<p>“The pleasant showers and genial warmth of</p>
<p>spring greatly altered the aspect of the earth. Men,</p>
<p>who before this change seemed to have been hid in</p>
<p>caves, dispersed themselves, and were employed</p>
<p>in various arts of cultivation. The birds sang in more</p>
<p>cheerful notes, and the leaves began to bud forth</p>
<p>on the trees. Happy, happy earth! ﬁt habitation</p>
<p>for gods, which, so short a time before, was bleak,</p>
<p>damp, and unwholesome. My spirits were elevated</p>
<p>by the enchanting appearance of nature; the past</p>
<p>was blotted from my memory, the present was tran-</p>
<p>quil, and the future gilded by bright rays of hope,</p>
<p>and anticipations of joy.”</p>
<p>Chapter V</p>
<p>“I NOW hasten to the more moving part of my story.</p>
<p>I shall relate events that impressed me with feelings</p>
<p>which, from what I was, have made me what I am.</p>
<p>“Spring advanced rapidly; the weather became</p>
<p>ﬁne, and the skies cloudless. It surprised me, that</p>
<p>what before was desert and gloomy should now</p>
<p>bloom with the most beautiful ﬂowers and verdure.</p>
<p>My senses were gratiﬁed and refreshed by a thou-</p>
<p>sand scents of delight, and a thousand sights of</p>
<p>beauty.</p>
<p>“It was on one of these days, when my cot-</p>
<p>tagers periodically rested from labour — the old</p>
<p>198</p>
<p>man played on his guitar, and the children listened</p>
<p>to him — I observed that the countenance of Felix</p>
<p>was melancholy beyond expression: he sighed fre-</p>
<p>quently; and once his father paused in his music,</p>
<p>and I conjectured by his manner that he inquired the</p>
<p>cause of his son’s sorrow. Felix replied in a cheerful</p>
<p>accent, and the old man was recommencing his</p>
<p>music, when some one tapped at the door.</p>
<p>“It was a lady on horseback, accompanied by</p>
<p>a countryman as a guide. The lady was dressed</p>
<p>in a dark suit, and covered with a thick black veil.</p>
<p>Agatha asked a question; to which the stranger</p>
<p>only replied by pronouncing, in a sweet accent, the</p>
<p>name of Felix. Her voice was musical, but unlike</p>
<p>that of either of my friends. On hearing this word,</p>
<p>Felix came up hastily to the lady; who, when she</p>
<p>saw him, threw up her veil, and I beheld a counte-</p>
<p>nance of angelic beauty and expression. Her hair</p>
<p>of a shining raven black, and curiously braided; her</p>
<p>eyes were dark, but gentle, although animated; her</p>
<p>features of a regular proportion, and her complex-</p>
<p>ion wondrously fair, each cheek tinged with a lovely</p>
<p>pink.</p>
<p>“Felix seemed ravished with delight when he</p>
<p>saw her, every trait of sorrow vanished from his</p>
<p>face, and it instantly expressed a degree of ecstatic</p>
<p>joy, of which I could hardly have believed it capable;</p>
<p>his eyes sparkled, as his cheek ﬂushed with plea-</p>
<p>sure; and at that moment I thought him as beautiful</p>
<p>as the stranger. She appeared affected by different</p>
<p>feelings; wiping a few tears from her lovely eyes,</p>
<p>she held out her hand to Felix, who kissed it raptur-</p>
<p>ously, and called her, as well as I could distinguish,</p>
<p>his sweet Arabian. She did not appear to under-</p>
<p>stand him, but smiled. He assisted her to dismount,</p>
<p>and, dismissing her guide, conducted her into the</p>
<p>cottage. Some conversation took place between</p>
<p>him and his father; and the young stranger knelt at</p>
<p>the old man’s feet, and would have kissed his hand,</p>
<p>but he raised her, and embraced her affectionately.</p>
<p>“I soon perceived, that although the stranger</p>
<p>uttered articulate sounds, and appeared to have a</p>
<p>language of her own, she was neither understood</p>
<p>by, or herself understood, the cottagers. They made</p>
<p>many signs which I did not comprehend; but I saw</p>
<p>that her presence diffused gladness through the cot-</p>
<p>tage, dispelling their sorrow as the sun dissipates</p>
<p>the morning mists. Felix seemed peculiarly happy,</p>
<p>and with smiles of delight welcomed his Arabian.</p>
<p>Agatha, the ever-gentle Agatha, kissed the hands</p>
<p>of the lovely stranger; and, pointing to her brother,</p>
<p>made signs which appeared to me to mean that he</p>
<p>had been sorrowful until she came. Some hours</p>
<p>passed thus, while they, by their countenances,</p>
<p>expressed joy, the cause of which I did not com-</p>
<p>prehend. Presently I found, by the frequent recur-</p>
<p>rence of one sound which the stranger repeated</p>
<p>after them, that she was endeavouring to learn their</p>
<p>language; and the idea instantly occurred to me,</p>
<p>that I should make use of the same instructions to</p>
<p>the same end. The stranger learned about twenty</p>
<p>words at the ﬁrst lesson, most of them indeed were</p>
<p>those which I had before understood, but I proﬁted</p>
<p>by the others.</p>
<p>“As night came on, Agatha and the Arabian</p>
<p>retired early. When they separated, Felix kissed</p>
<p>the hand of the stranger, and said, ‘Good night,</p>
<p>sweet Saﬁe.’ He sat up much longer, conversing</p>
<p>with his father; and, by the frequent repetition of her</p>
<p>name, I conjectured that their lovely guest was the</p>
<p>subject of their conversation. I ardently desired to</p>
<p>understand them, and bent every faculty towards</p>
<p>that purpose, but found it utterly impossible.</p>
<p>“The next morning Felix went out to his work;</p>
<p>and, after the usual occupations of Agatha were</p>
<p>ﬁnished, the Arabian sat at the feet of the old man,</p>
<p>and, taking his guitar, played some airs so entranc-</p>
<p>ingly beautiful, that they at once drew tears of sor-</p>
<p>row and delight from my eyes. She sang, and her</p>
<p>voice ﬂowed in a rich cadence, swelling or dying</p>
<p>away, like a nightingale of the woods.</p>
<p>“When she had ﬁnished, she gave the guitar to</p>
<p>Agatha, who at ﬁrst declined it. She played a sim-</p>
<p>ple air, and her voice accompanied it in sweet ac-</p>
<p>cents, but unlike the wondrous strain of the stranger.</p>
<p>The old man appeared enraptured, and said some</p>
<p>words, which Agatha endeavoured to explain to</p>
<p>Saﬁe, and by which he appeared to wish to express</p>
<p>that she bestowed on him the greatest delight by</p>
<p>her music.</p>
<p>“The days now passed as peaceably as before,</p>
<p>with the sole alteration, that joy had taken place of</p>
<p>sadness in the countenances of my friends. Saﬁe</p>
<p>was always gay and happy; she and I improved</p>
<p>rapidly in the knowledge of language, so that in two</p>
<p>months I began to comprehend most of the words</p>
<p>uttered by my protectors.</p>
<p>“In the meanwhile also the black ground was</p>
<p>covered with herbage, and the green banks inter-</p>
<p>spersed with innumerable ﬂowers, sweet to the</p>
<p>scent and the eyes, stars of pale radiance among</p>
<p>the moonlight woods; the sun became warmer, the</p>
<p>nights clear and balmy; and my nocturnal rambles</p>
<p>were an extreme pleasure to me, although they</p>
<p>were considerably shortened by the late setting and</p>
<p>early rising of the sun; for I never ventured abroad</p>
<p>during daylight, fearful of meeting with the same</p>
<p>treatment as I had formerly endured in the ﬁrst vil-</p>
<p>lage which I entered.</p>
<p>“My days were spent in close attention, that I</p>
<p>might more speedily master the language; and I</p>
<p>may boast that I improved more rapidly than the</p>
<p>Arabian, who understood very little, and conversed</p>
<p>in broken accents, whilst I comprehended and could</p>
<p>imitate almost every word that was spoken.</p>
<p>“While I improved in speech, I also learned the</p>
<p>science of letters, as it was taught to the stranger;</p>
<p>and this opened before me a wide ﬁeld for wonder</p>
<p>and delight.</p>
<p>“The book from which Felix instructed Saﬁe was</p>
<p>Volney’s Ruins of Empires. I should not have un-</p>
<p>derstood the purport of this book, had not Felix, in</p>
<p>reading it, given very minute explanations. He had</p>
<p>chosen this work, he said, because the declama-</p>
<p>tory style was framed in imitation of the eastern</p>
<p>authors. Through this work I obtained a cursory</p>
<p>knowledge of history, and a view of the several em-</p>
<p>pires at present existing in the world; it gave me</p>
<p>an insight into the manners, governments, and reli-</p>
<p>gions of the different nations of the earth. I heard</p>
<p>of the slothful Asiatics; of the stupendous genius</p>
<p>and mental activity of the Grecians; of the wars and</p>
<p>wonderful virtue of the early Romans — of their</p>
<p>subsequent degeneration — of the decline of that</p>
<p>mighty empire; of chivalry, Christianity, and kings. I</p>
<p>heard of the discovery of the American hemisphere,</p>
<p>and wept with Saﬁe over the hapless fate of its</p>
<p>original inhabitants.</p>
<p>“These wonderful narrations inspired me with</p>
<p>strange feelings. Was man, indeed, at once so pow-</p>
<p>erful, so virtuous, and magniﬁcent, yet so vicious</p>
<p>and base? He appeared at one time a mere scion</p>
<p>of the evil principle, and at another as all that can</p>
<p>be conceived of noble and godlike. To be a great</p>
<p>and virtuous man appeared the highest honour that</p>
<p>can befall a sensitive being; to be base and vicious,</p>
<p>as many on record have been, appeared the lowest</p>
<p>degradation, a condition more abject than that of</p>
<p>the blind mole or harmless worm. For a long time I</p>
<p>could not conceive how one man could go forth to</p>
<p>murder his fellow, or even why there were laws and</p>
<p>governments; but when I heard details of vice and</p>
<p>bloodshed, my wonder ceased, and I turned away</p>
<p>with disgust and loathing.</p>
<p>“Every conversation of the cottagers now op-</p>
<p>ened new wonders to me. While I listened to the</p>
<p>instructions which Felix bestowed upon the Ara-</p>
<p>bian, the strange system of human society was</p>
<p>explained to me. I heard of the division of property,</p>
<p>of immense wealth and squalid poverty; of rank,</p>
<p>descent, and noble blood.</p>
<p>“The words induced me to turn towards my-</p>
<p>self. I learned that the possessions most esteemed</p>
<p>by your fellow-creatures were, high and unsullied</p>
<p>descent united with riches. A man might be re-</p>
<p>spected with only one of these acquisitions; but</p>
<p>without either he was considered, except in very</p>
<p>rare instances, as a vagabond and a slave, doomed</p>
<p>to waste his powers for the proﬁt of the chosen few.</p>
<p>And what was I? Of my creation and creator I was</p>
<p>absolutely ignorant; but I knew that I possessed</p>
<p>no money, no friends, no kind of property. I was,</p>
<p>besides, endowed with a ﬁgure hideously deformed</p>
<p>and loathsome; I was not even of the same nature</p>
<p>as man. I was more agile than they, and could sub-</p>
<p>sist upon coarser diet; I bore the extremes of heat</p>
<p>and cold with less injury to my frame; my stature far</p>
<p>exceeded their’s. When I looked around, I saw and</p>
<p>heard of none like me. Was I then a monster, a blot</p>
<p>upon the earth, from which all men ﬂed, and whom</p>
<p>all men disowned?</p>
<p>“I cannot describe to you the agony that these</p>
<p>reﬂections inﬂicted upon me; I tried to dispel them,</p>
<p>but sorrow only increased with knowledge. Oh,</p>
<p>that I had for ever remained in my native wood,</p>
<p>nor known or felt beyond the sensations of hunger,</p>
<p>thirst, and heat!</p>
<p>“Of what a strange nature is knowledge! It clings</p>
<p>to the mind, when it has once seized on it, like a</p>
<p>lichen on the rock. I wished sometimes to shake off</p>
<p>all thought and feeling; but I learned that there was</p>
<p>but one means to overcome the sensation of pain,</p>
<p>and that was death — a state which I feared yet did</p>
<p>not understand. I admired virtue and good feelings,</p>
<p>and loved the gentle manners and amiable qualities</p>
<p>of my cottagers; but I was shut out from intercourse</p>
<p>with them, except through means which I obtained</p>
<p>by stealth, when I was unseen and unknown, and</p>
<p>which rather increased than satisﬁed the desire</p>
<p>I had of becoming one among my fellows. The</p>
<p>gentle words of Agatha, and the animated smiles</p>
<p>of the charming Arabian, were not for me. The</p>
<p>mild exhortations of the old man, and the lively</p>
<p>conversation of the loved Felix, were not for me.</p>
<p>Miserable, unhappy wretch!</p>
<p>“Other lessons were impressed upon me even</p>
<p>more deeply. I heard of the difference of sexes;</p>
<p>of the birth and growth of children; how the father</p>
<p>doated on the smiles of the infant, and the lively</p>
<p>sallies of the older child; how all the life and cares</p>
<p>of the mother were wrapt up in the precious charge;</p>
<p>how the mind of youth expanded and gained knowl-</p>
<p>edge; of brother, sister, and all the various relation-</p>
<p>ships which bind one human being to another in</p>
<p>mutual bonds.</p>
<p>“But where were my friends and relations? No</p>
<p>father had watched my infant days, no mother had</p>
<p>blessed me with smiles and caresses; or if they</p>
<p>had, all my past life was now a blot, a blind vacancy</p>
<p>in which I distinguished nothing. From my earliest</p>
<p>remembrance I had been as I then was in height</p>
<p>and proportion. I had never yet seen a being re-</p>
<p>sembling me, or who claimed any intercourse with</p>
<p>me. What was I? The question again recurred, to</p>
<p>be answered only with groans.</p>
<p>“I will soon explain to what these feelings</p>
<p>tended; but allow me now to return to the cottagers,</p>
<p>whose story excited in me such various feelings</p>
<p>of indignation, delight, and wonder, but which all</p>
<p>terminated in additional love and reverence for my</p>
<p>protectors (for so I loved, in an innocent, half painful</p>
<p>self-deceit, to call them).”</p>
<p>Chapter VI</p>
<p>“SOME time elapsed before I learned the history</p>
<p>of my friends. It was one which could not fail to</p>
<p>impress itself deeply on my mind, unfolding as it did</p>
<p>a number of circumstances each interesting and</p>
<p>wonderful to one so utterly inexperienced as I was.</p>
<p>“The name of the old man was De Lacey. He</p>
<p>was descended from a good family in France, where</p>
<p>he had lived for many years in afﬂuence, respected</p>
<p>by his superiors, and beloved by his equals. His son</p>
<p>was bred in the service of his country; and Agatha</p>
<p>had ranked with ladies of the highest distinction. A</p>
<p>few months before my arrival, they had lived in a</p>
<p>210</p>
<p>large and luxurious city, called Paris, surrounded by</p>
<p>friends, and possessed of every enjoyment which</p>
<p>virtue, reﬁnement of intellect, or taste, accompanied</p>
<p>by a moderate fortune, could afford.</p>
<p>“The father of Saﬁe had been the cause of their</p>
<p>ruin. He was a Turkish merchant, and had inhab-</p>
<p>ited Paris for many years, when, for some reason</p>
<p>which I could not learn, he became obnoxious to the</p>
<p>government. He was seized and cast into prison</p>
<p>the very day that Saﬁe arrived from Constantino-</p>
<p>ple to join him. He was tried, and condemned to</p>
<p>death. The injustice of his sentence was very ﬂa-</p>
<p>grant; all Paris was indignant; and it was judged</p>
<p>that his religion and wealth, rather than the crime</p>
<p>alleged against him, had been the cause of his</p>
<p>condemnation.</p>
<p>“Felix had been present at the trial; his hor-</p>
<p>ror and indignation were uncontrollable, when he</p>
<p>heard the decision of the court. He made, at that</p>
<p>moment, a solemn vow to deliver him, and then</p>
<p>looked around for the means. After many fruitless</p>
<p>attempts to gain admittance to the prison, he found</p>
<p>a strongly grated window in an unguarded part of</p>
<p>the building, which lighted the dungeon of the unfor-</p>
<p>tunate Mahometan; who, loaded with chains, waited</p>
<p>in despair the execution of the barbarous sentence.</p>
<p>Felix visited the grate at night, and made known</p>
<p>to the prisoner his intentions in his favour. The</p>
<p>Turk, amazed and delighted, endeavoured to kindle</p>
<p>the zeal of his deliverer by promises of reward and</p>
<p>wealth. Felix rejected his offers with contempt; yet</p>
<p>when he saw the lovely Saﬁe, who was allowed</p>
<p>to visit her father, and who, by her gestures, ex-</p>
<p>pressed her lively gratitude, the youth could not</p>
<p>help owning to his own mind, that the captive pos-</p>
<p>sessed a treasure which would fully reward his toil</p>
<p>and hazard.</p>
<p>“The Turk quickly perceived the impression that</p>
<p>his daughter had made on the heart of Felix, and</p>
<p>endeavoured to secure him more entirely in his</p>
<p>interests by the promise of her hand in marriage, so</p>
<p>soon as he should be conveyed to a place of safety.</p>
<p>Felix was too delicate to accept this offer; yet he</p>
<p>looked forward to the probability of that event as to</p>
<p>the consummation of his happiness.</p>
<p>“During the ensuing days, while the prepara-</p>
<p>tions were going forward for the escape of the mer-</p>
<p>chant, the zeal of Felix was warmed by several let-</p>
<p>ters that he received from this lovely girl, who found</p>
<p>means to express her thoughts in the language of</p>
<p>her lover by the aid of an old man, a servant of her</p>
<p>father’s, who understood French. She thanked him</p>
<p>in the most ardent terms for his intended services</p>
<p>towards her father; and at the same time she gently</p>
<p>deplored her own fate.</p>
<p>“I have copies of these letters; for I found means,</p>
<p>during my residence in the hovel, to procure the</p>
<p>implements of writing; and the letters were often in</p>
<p>the hands of Felix or Agatha. Before I depart, I will</p>
<p>give them to you, they will prove the truth of my tale;</p>
<p>but at present, as the sun is already far declined,</p>
<p>I shall only have time to repeat the substance of</p>
<p>them to you.</p>
<p>“Saﬁe related, that her mother was a Christian</p>
<p>Arab, seized and made a slave by the Turks; rec-</p>
<p>ommended by her beauty, she had won the heart</p>
<p>of the father of Saﬁe, who married her. The young</p>
<p>girl spoke in high and enthusiastic terms of her</p>
<p>mother, who, born in freedom spurned the bondage</p>
<p>to which she was now reduced. She instructed her</p>
<p>daughter in the tenets of her religion, and taught</p>
<p>her to aspire to higher powers of intellect, and an</p>
<p>independence of spirit, forbidden to the female fol-</p>
<p>lowers of Mahomet. This lady died; but her lessons</p>
<p>were indelibly impressed on the mind of Saﬁe, who</p>
<p>sickened at the prospect of again returning to Asia,</p>
<p>and the being immured within the walls of a haram,</p>
<p>allowed only to occupy herself with puerile amuse-</p>
<p>ments, ill suited to the temper of her soul, now</p>
<p>accustomed to grand ideas and a noble emulation</p>
<p>for virtue. The prospect of marrying a Christian,</p>
<p>and remaining in a country where women were al-</p>
<p>lowed to take a rank in society, was enchanting to</p>
<p>her.</p>
<p>“The day for the execution of the Turk was ﬁxed;</p>
<p>but, on the night previous to it, he had quitted prison,</p>
<p>and before morning was distant many leagues from</p>
<p>Paris. Felix had procured passports in the name</p>
<p>of his father, sister, and himself. He had previously</p>
<p>communicated his plan to the former, who aided the</p>
<p>deceit by quitting his house, under the pretence of</p>
<p>a journey, and concealed himself, with his daughter,</p>
<p>in an obscure part of Paris.</p>
<p>“Felix conducted the fugitives through France to</p>
<p>Lyons, and across Mont Cenis to Leghorn, where</p>
<p>the merchant had decided to wait a favourable op-</p>
<p>portunity of passing into some part of the Turkish</p>
<p>dominions.</p>
<p>“Saﬁe resolved to remain with her father until</p>
<p>the moment of his departure, before which time</p>
<p>the Turk renewed his promise that she should be</p>
<p>united to his deliverer; and Felix remained with</p>
<p>them in expectation of that event; and in the mean</p>
<p>time he enjoyed the society of the Arabian, who</p>
<p>exhibited towards him the simplest and tenderest</p>
<p>affection. They conversed with one another through</p>
<p>the means of an interpreter, and sometimes with</p>
<p>the interpretation of looks; and Saﬁe sang to him</p>
<p>the divine airs of her native country.</p>
<p>“The Turk allowed this intimacy to take place,</p>
<p>and encouraged the hopes of the youthful lovers,</p>
<p>while in his heart he had formed far other plans. He</p>
<p>loathed the idea that his daughter should be united</p>
<p>to a Christian; but he feared the resentment of Felix</p>
<p>if he should appear lukewarm; for he knew that he</p>
<p>was still in the power of his deliverer, if he should</p>
<p>choose to betray him to the Italian state which they</p>
<p>inhabited. He revolved a thousand plans by which</p>
<p>he should be enabled to prolong the deceit until it</p>
<p>might be no longer necessary, and secretly to take</p>
<p>his daughter with him when he departed. His plans</p>
<p>were greatly facilitated by the news which arrived</p>
<p>from Paris.</p>
<p>“The government of France were greatly en-</p>
<p>raged at the escape of their victim, and spared no</p>
<p>pains to detect and punish his deliverer. The plot</p>
<p>of Felix was quickly discovered, and De Lacey and</p>
<p>Agatha were thrown into prison. The news reached</p>
<p>Felix, and roused him from his dream of pleasure.</p>
<p>His blind and aged father, and his gentle sister, lay</p>
<p>in a noisome dungeon, while he enjoyed the free</p>
<p>air, and the society of her whom he loved. This</p>
<p>idea was torture to him. He quickly arranged with</p>
<p>the Turk, that if the latter should ﬁnd a favourable</p>
<p>opportunity for escape before Felix could return to</p>
<p>Italy, Saﬁe should remain as a boarder at a convent</p>
<p>at Leghorn; and then, quitting the lovely Arabian,</p>
<p>he hastened to Paris, and delivered himself up to</p>
<p>the vengeance of the law, hoping to free De Lacey</p>
<p>and Agatha by this proceeding.</p>
<p>“He did not succeed. They remained conﬁned</p>
<p>for ﬁve months before the trial took place; the result</p>
<p>of which deprived them of their fortune, and con-</p>
<p>demned them to a perpetual exile from their native</p>
<p>country.</p>
<p>“They found a miserable asylum in the cottage</p>
<p>in Germany, where I discovered them. Felix soon</p>
<p>learned that the treacherous Turk, for whom he</p>
<p>and his family endured such unheard-of oppres-</p>
<p>sion, on discovering that his deliverer was thus re-</p>
<p>duced to poverty and impotence, became a traitor</p>
<p>to good feeling and honour, and had quitted Italy</p>
<p>with his daughter, insultingly sending Felix a pit-</p>
<p>tance of money to aid him, as he said, in some plan</p>
<p>of future maintenance.</p>
<p>“Such were the events that preyed on the heart</p>
<p>of Felix, and rendered him, when I ﬁrst saw him,</p>
<p>the most miserable of his family. He could have</p>
<p>endured poverty, and when this distress had been</p>
<p>the meed of his virtue, he would have gloried in it:</p>
<p>but the ingratitude of the Turk, and the loss of his</p>
<p>beloved Saﬁe, were misfortunes more bitter and</p>
<p>irreparable. The arrival of the Arabian now infused</p>
<p>new life into his soul.</p>
<p>“When the news reached Leghorn, that Felix</p>
<p>was deprived of his wealth and rank, the merchant</p>
<p>commanded his daughter to think no more of her</p>
<p>lover, but to prepare to return with him to her na-</p>
<p>tive country. The generous nature of Saﬁe was</p>
<p>outraged by this command; she attempted to ex-</p>
<p>postulate with her father, but he left her angrily,</p>
<p>reiterating his tyrannical mandate.</p>
<p>“A few days after, the Turk entered his daugh-</p>
<p>ter’s apartment, and told her hastily, that he had</p>
<p>reason to believe that his residence at Leghorn</p>
<p>had been divulged, and that he should speedily</p>
<p>be delivered up to the French government; he had,</p>
<p>consequently, hired a vessel to convey him to Con-</p>
<p>stantinople, for which city he should sail in a few</p>
<p>hours. He intended to leave his daughter under</p>
<p>the care of a conﬁdential servant, to follow at her</p>
<p>leisure with the greater part of his property, which</p>
<p>had not yet arrived at Leghorn.</p>
<p>“When alone, Saﬁe resolved in her own mind</p>
<p>the plan of conduct that it would become her to</p>
<p>pursue in this emergency. A residence in Turkey</p>
<p>was abhorrent to her; her religion and feelings were</p>
<p>alike adverse to it. By some papers of her father’s,</p>
<p>which fell into her hands, she heard of the exile of</p>
<p>her lover, and learnt the name of the spot where</p>
<p>he then resided. She hesitated some time, but at</p>
<p>length she formed her determination. Taking with</p>
<p>her some jewels that belonged to her, and a small</p>
<p>sum of money, she quitted Italy, with an attendant, a</p>
<p>native of Leghorn, but who understood the common</p>
<p>language of Turkey, and departed for Germany.</p>
<p>“She arrived in safety at a town about twenty</p>
<p>leagues from the cottage of De Lacey, when her</p>
<p>attendant fell dangerously ill. Saﬁe nursed her with</p>
<p>the most devoted affection; but the poor girl died,</p>
<p>and the Arabian was left alone, unacquainted with</p>
<p>the language of the country, and utterly ignorant of</p>
<p>the customs of the world. She fell, however, into</p>
<p>good hands. The Italian had mentioned the name</p>
<p>of the spot for which they were bound; and, after</p>
<p>her death, the woman of the house in which they</p>
<p>had lived took care that Saﬁe should arrive in safety</p>
<p>at the cottage of her lover.”</p>
<p>Chapter VII</p>
<p>“SUCH was the history of my beloved cottagers. It</p>
<p>impressed me deeply. I learned, from the views of</p>
<p>social life which it developed, to admire their virtues,</p>
<p>and to deprecate the vices of mankind.</p>
<p>“As yet I looked upon crime as a distant evil;</p>
<p>benevolence and generosity were ever present be-</p>
<p>fore me, inciting within me a desire to become an</p>
<p>actor in the busy scene where so many admirable</p>
<p>qualities were called forth and displayed. But, in</p>
<p>giving an account of the progress of my intellect, I</p>
<p>must not omit a circumstance which occurred in the</p>
<p>beginning of the month of August of the same year.</p>
<p>221</p>
<p>“One night, during my accustomed visit to the</p>
<p>neighbouring wood, where I collected my own food,</p>
<p>and brought home ﬁring for my protectors, I found</p>
<p>on the ground a leathern portmanteau, containing</p>
<p>several articles of dress and some books. I eagerly</p>
<p>seized the prize, and returned with it to my hovel.</p>
<p>Fortunately the books were written in the language</p>
<p>the elements of which I had acquired at the cot-</p>
<p>tage; they consisted of Paradise Lost, a volume of</p>
<p>Plutarch’s Lives, and the Sorrows of Werter. The</p>
<p>possession of these treasures gave me extreme</p>
<p>delight; I now continually studied and exercised my</p>
<p>mind upon these histories, whilst my friends were</p>
<p>employed in their ordinary occupations.</p>
<p>“I can hardly describe to you the effect of these</p>
<p>books. They produced in me an inﬁnity of new im-</p>
<p>ages and feelings, that sometimes raised me to</p>
<p>ecstacy, but more frequently sunk me into the low-</p>
<p>est dejection. In the Sorrows of Werter, besides the</p>
<p>interest of its simple and affecting story, so many</p>
<p>opinions are canvassed, and so many lights thrown</p>
<p>upon what had hitherto been to me obscure sub-</p>
<p>jects, that I found in it a never-ending source of</p>
<p>speculation and astonishment. The gentle and do-</p>
<p>mestic manners it described, combined with lofty</p>
<p>sentiments and feelings, which had for their object</p>
<p>something out of self, accorded well with my expe-</p>
<p>rience among my protectors, and with the wants</p>
<p>which were for ever alive in my own bosom. But I</p>
<p>thought Werter himself a more divine being than</p>
<p>I had ever beheld or imagined; his character con-</p>
<p>tained no pretension, but it sunk deep. The dis-</p>
<p>quisitions upon death and suicide were calculated</p>
<p>to ﬁll me with wonder. I did not pretend to enter</p>
<p>into the merits of the case, yet I inclined towards</p>
<p>the opinions of the hero, whose extinction I wept,</p>
<p>without precisely understanding it.</p>
<p>“As I read, however, I applied much personally</p>
<p>to my own feelings and condition. I found myself</p>
<p>similar, yet at the same time strangely unlike the</p>
<p>beings concerning whom I read, and to whose con-</p>
<p>versation I was a listener. I sympathized with, and</p>
<p>partly understood them, but I was unformed in mind;</p>
<p>I was dependent on none, and related to none. ‘The</p>
<p>path of my departure was free;’ and there was none</p>
<p>to lament my annihilation. My person was hideous,</p>
<p>and my stature gigantic: what did this mean? Who</p>
<p>was I? What was I? Whence did I come? What</p>
<p>was my destination? These questions continually</p>
<p>recurred, but I was unable to solve them.</p>
<p>“The volume of Plutarch’s Lives which I pos-</p>
<p>sessed, contained the histories of the ﬁrst founders</p>
<p>of the ancient republics. This book had a far differ-</p>
<p>ent effect upon me from the Sorrows of Werter. I</p>
<p>learned from Werter’s imaginations despondency</p>
<p>and gloom: but Plutarch taught me high thoughts;</p>
<p>he elevated me above the wretched sphere of my</p>
<p>own reﬂections, to admire and love the heroes of</p>
<p>past ages. Many things I read surpassed my un-</p>
<p>derstanding and experience. I had a very confused</p>
<p>knowledge of kingdoms, wide extents of country,</p>
<p>mighty rivers, and boundless seas. But I was per-</p>
<p>fectly unacquainted with towns, and large assem-</p>
<p>blages of men. The cottage of my protectors had</p>
<p>been the only school in which I had studied human</p>
<p>nature; but this book developed new and mightier</p>
<p>scenes of action. I read of men concerned in public</p>
<p>affairs governing or massacring their species. I felt</p>
<p>the greatest ardour for virtue rise within me, and</p>
<p>abhorrence for vice, as far as I understood the sig-</p>
<p>niﬁcation of those terms, relative as they were, as I</p>
<p>applied them, to pleasure and pain alone. Induced</p>
<p>by these feelings, I was of course led to admire</p>
<p>peaceable law-givers, Numa, Solon, and Lycurgus,</p>
<p>in preference to Romulus and Theseus. The patri-</p>
<p>archal lives of my protectors caused these impres-</p>
<p>sions to take a ﬁrm hold on my mind; perhaps, if</p>
<p>my ﬁrst introduction to humanity had been made by</p>
<p>a young soldier, burning for glory and slaughter, I</p>
<p>should have been imbued with different sensations.</p>
<p>“But Paradise Lost excited different and far</p>
<p>deeper emotions. I read it, as I had read the other</p>
<p>volumes which had fallen into my hands, as a</p>
<p>true history. It moved every feeling of wonder and</p>
<p>awe, that the picture of an omnipotent God warring</p>
<p>with his creatures was capable of exciting. I often</p>
<p>referred the several situations, as their similarity</p>
<p>struck me, to my own. Like Adam, I was created</p>
<p>apparently united by no link to any other being in</p>
<p>existence; but his state was far different from mine</p>
<p>in every other respect. He had come forth from</p>
<p>the hands of God a perfect creature, happy and</p>
<p>prosperous, guarded by the especial care of his</p>
<p>Creator; he was allowed to converse with, and ac-</p>
<p>quire knowledge from beings of a superior nature:</p>
<p>but I was wretched, helpless, and alone. Many</p>
<p>times I considered Satan as the ﬁtter emblem of</p>
<p>my condition; for often, like him, when I viewed the</p>
<p>bliss of my protectors, the bitter gall of envy rose</p>
<p>within me.</p>
<p>“Another circumstance strengthened and con-</p>
<p>ﬁrmed these feelings. Soon after my arrival in the</p>
<p>hovel, I discovered some papers in the pocket of</p>
<p>the dress which I had taken from your laboratory.</p>
<p>At ﬁrst I had neglected them; but now that I was</p>
<p>able to decypher the characters in which they were</p>
<p>written, I began to study them with diligence. It was</p>
<p>your journal of the four months that preceded my</p>
<p>creation. You minutely described in these papers</p>
<p>every step you took in the progress of your work;</p>
<p>this history was mingled with accounts of domes-</p>
<p>tic occurrences. You, doubtless, recollect these</p>
<p>papers. Here they are. Every thing is related in</p>
<p>them which bears reference to my accursed ori-</p>
<p>gin; the whole detail of that series of disgusting</p>
<p>circumstances which produced it is set in view; the</p>
<p>minutest description of my odious and loathsome</p>
<p>person is given, in language which painted your</p>
<p>own horrors, and rendered mine ineffaceable. I</p>
<p>sickened as I read. ‘Hateful day when I received</p>
<p>life!’ I exclaimed in agony. ‘Cursed creator! Why did</p>
<p>you form a monster so hideous that even you turned</p>
<p>from me in disgust? God in pity made man beautiful</p>
<p>and alluring, after his own image; but my form is a</p>
<p>ﬁlthy type of your’s, more horrid from its very resem-</p>
<p>blance. Satan had his companions, fellow-devils, to</p>
<p>admire and encourage him; but I am solitary and</p>
<p>detested.’</p>
<p>“These were the reﬂections of my hours of</p>
<p>despondency and solitude; but when I contem-</p>
<p>plated the virtues of the cottagers, their amiable</p>
<p>and benevolent dispositions, I persuaded myself</p>
<p>that when they should become acquainted with my</p>
<p>admiration of their virtues, they would compassion-</p>
<p>ate me, and overlook my personal deformity. Could</p>
<p>they turn from their door one, however monstrous,</p>
<p>who solicited their compassion and friendship? I</p>
<p>resolved, at least, not to despair, but in every way</p>
<p>to ﬁt myself for an interview with them which would</p>
<p>decide my fate. I postponed this attempt for some</p>
<p>months longer; for the importance attached to its</p>
<p>success inspired me with a dread lest I should fail.</p>
<p>Besides, I found that my understanding improved</p>
<p>so much with every day’s experience, that I was</p>
<p>unwilling to commence this undertaking until a few</p>
<p>more months should have added to my wisdom.</p>
<p>“Several changes, in the mean time, took place</p>
<p>in the cottage. The presence of Saﬁe diffused hap-</p>
<p>piness among its inhabitants; and I also found that</p>
<p>a greater degree of plenty reigned there. Felix and</p>
<p>Agatha spent more time in amusement and con-</p>
<p>versation, and were assisted in their labours by</p>
<p>servants. They did not appear rich, but they were</p>
<p>contented and happy; their feelings were serene</p>
<p>and peaceful, while mine became every day more</p>
<p>tumultuous. Increase of knowledge only discovered</p>
<p>to me more clearly what a wretched outcast I was.</p>
<p>I cherished hope, it is true; but it vanished, when I</p>
<p>beheld my person reﬂected in water, or my shadow</p>
<p>in the moon-shine, even as that frail image and that</p>
<p>inconstant shade.</p>
<p>“I endeavoured to crush these fears, and to</p>
<p>fortify myself for the trial which in a few months I</p>
<p>resolved to undergo; and sometimes I allowed my</p>
<p>thoughts, unchecked by reason, to ramble in the</p>
<p>ﬁelds of Paradise, and dared to fancy amiable and</p>
<p>lovely creatures sympathizing with my feelings and</p>
<p>cheering my gloom; their angelic countenances</p>
<p>breathed smiles of consolation. But it was all a</p>
<p>dream: no Eve soothed my sorrows, or shared</p>
<p>my thoughts; I was alone. I remembered Adam’s</p>
<p>supplication to his Creator; but where was mine?</p>
<p>he had abandoned me, and, in the bitterness of my</p>
<p>heart, I cursed him.</p>
<p>“Autumn passed thus. I saw, with surprise and</p>
<p>grief, the leaves decay and fall, and nature again</p>
<p>assume the barren and bleak appearance it had</p>
<p>worn when I ﬁrst beheld the woods and the lovely</p>
<p>moon. Yet I did not heed the bleakness of the</p>
<p>weather; I was better ﬁtted by my conformation for</p>
<p>the endurance of cold than heat. But my chief de-</p>
<p>lights were the sight of the ﬂowers, the birds, and all</p>
<p>the gay apparel of summer; when those deserted</p>
<p>me, I turned with more attention towards the cot-</p>
<p>tagers. Their happiness was not decreased by the</p>
<p>absence of summer. They loved, and sympathized</p>
<p>with one another; and their joys, depending on each</p>
<p>other, were not interrupted by the casualties that</p>
<p>took place around them. The more I saw of them,</p>
<p>the greater became my desire to claim their protec-</p>
<p>tion and kindness; my heart yearned to be known</p>
<p>and loved by these amiable creatures: to see their</p>
<p>sweet looks turned towards me with affection, was</p>
<p>the utmost limit of my ambition. I dared not think</p>
<p>that they would turn them from me with disdain and</p>
<p>horror. The poor that stopped at their door were</p>
<p>never driven away. I asked, it is true, for greater</p>
<p>treasures than a little food or rest; I required kind-</p>
<p>ness and sympathy; but I did not believe myself</p>
<p>utterly unworthy of it.</p>
<p>“The winter advanced, and an entire revolution</p>
<p>of the seasons had taken place since I awoke into</p>
<p>life. My attention, at this time, was solely directed</p>
<p>towards my plan of introducing myself into the cot-</p>
<p>tage of my protectors. I revolved many projects;</p>
<p>but that on which I ﬁnally ﬁxed was, to enter the</p>
<p>dwelling when the blind old man should be alone. I</p>
<p>had sagacity enough to discover, that the unnatu-</p>
<p>ral hideousness of my person was the chief object</p>
<p>of horror with those who had formerly beheld me.</p>
<p>My voice, although harsh, had nothing terrible in it;</p>
<p>I thought, therefore, that if, in the absence of his</p>
<p>children, I could gain the good-will and mediation of</p>
<p>the old De Lacy, I might, by his means, be tolerated</p>
<p>by my younger protectors.</p>
<p>“One day, when the sun shone on the red leaves</p>
<p>that strewed the ground, and diffused cheerfulness,</p>
<p>although it denied warmth, Saﬁe, Agatha, and Felix,</p>
<p>departed on a long country walk, and the old man,</p>
<p>at his own desire, was left alone in the cottage.</p>
<p>When his children had departed, he took up his</p>
<p>guitar, and played several mournful, but sweet airs,</p>
<p>more sweet and mournful than I had ever heard him</p>
<p>play before. At ﬁrst his countenance was illuminated</p>
<p>with pleasure, but, as he continued, thoughtfulness</p>
<p>and sadness succeeded; at length, laying aside the</p>
<p>instrument, he sat absorbed in reﬂection.</p>
<p>“My heart beat quick; this was the hour and</p>
<p>moment of trial, which would decide my hopes, or</p>
<p>realize my fears. The servants were gone to a</p>
<p>neighbouring fair. All was silent in and around the</p>
<p>cottage: it was an excellent opportunity; yet, when</p>
<p>I proceeded to execute my plan, my limbs failed</p>
<p>me, and I sunk to the ground. Again I rose; and,</p>
<p>exerting all the ﬁrmness of which I was master,</p>
<p>removed the planks which I had placed before my</p>
<p>hovel to conceal my retreat. The fresh air revived</p>
<p>me, and, with renewed determination, I approached</p>
<p>the door of their cottage.</p>
<p>“I knocked. ‘Who is there?’ said the old man —</p>
<p>‘Come in.’</p>
<p>“I entered; ‘Pardon this intrusion,’ said I, ‘I am</p>
<p>a traveller in want of a little rest; you would greatly</p>
<p>oblige me, if you would allow me to remain a few</p>
<p>minutes before the ﬁre.’</p>
<p>“ ‘Enter,’ said De Lacy; ‘and I will try in what</p>
<p>manner I can relieve your wants; but, unfortunately,</p>
<p>my children are from home, and, as I am blind, I am</p>
<p>afraid I shall ﬁnd it difﬁcult to procure food for you.’</p>
<p>“ ‘Do not trouble yourself, my kind host, I have</p>
<p>food; it is warmth and rest only that I need.’</p>
<p>“I sat down, and a silence ensued. I knew that</p>
<p>every minute was precious to me, yet I remained</p>
<p>irresolute in what manner to commence the inter-</p>
<p>view; when the old man addressed me —</p>
<p>“ ‘By your language, stranger, I suppose you are</p>
<p>my countryman; — are you French?’</p>
<p>“ ‘No; but I was educated by a French family,</p>
<p>and understand that language only. I am now going</p>
<p>to claim the protection of some friends, whom I</p>
<p>sincerely love, and of whose favour I have some</p>
<p>hopes.’</p>
<p>“ ‘Are these Germans?’</p>
<p>“ ‘No, they are French. But let us change the</p>
<p>subject. I am an unfortunate and deserted creature;</p>
<p>I look around, and I have no relation or friend upon</p>
<p>earth. These amiable people to whom I go have</p>
<p>never seen me, and know little of me. I am full of</p>
<p>fears; for if I fail there, I am an outcast in the world</p>
<p>for ever.’</p>
<p>“ ‘Do not despair. To be friendless is indeed</p>
<p>to be unfortunate; but the hearts of men, when</p>
<p>unprejudiced by any obvious self-interest, are full of</p>
<p>brotherly love and charity. Rely, therefore, on your</p>
<p>hopes; and if these friends are good and amiable,</p>
<p>do not despair.’</p>
<p>“ ‘They are kind — they are the most excellent</p>
<p>creatures in the world; but, unfortunately, they are</p>
<p>prejudiced against me. I have good dispositions;</p>
<p>my life has been hitherto harmless, and, in some</p>
<p>degree, beneﬁcial; but a fatal prejudice clouds their</p>
<p>eyes, and where they ought to see a feeling and</p>
<p>kind friend, they behold only a detestable monster.’</p>
<p>“ ‘That is indeed unfortunate; but if you are really</p>
<p>blameless, cannot you undeceive them?’</p>
<p>“ ‘I am about to undertake that task; and it is on</p>
<p>that account that I feel so many overwhelming ter-</p>
<p>rors. I tenderly love these friends; I have, unknown</p>
<p>to them, been for many months in the habits of daily</p>
<p>kindness towards them; but they believe that I wish</p>
<p>to injure them, and it is that prejudice which I wish</p>
<p>to overcome.’</p>
<p>“ ‘Where do these friends reside?’</p>
<p>“ ‘Near this spot.’</p>
<p>“The old man paused, and then continued, ‘If</p>
<p>you will unreservedly conﬁde to me the particulars</p>
<p>of your tale, I perhaps may be of use in undeceiving</p>
<p>them. I am blind, and cannot judge of your counte-</p>
<p>nance, but there is something in your words which</p>
<p>persuades me that you are sincere. I am poor, and</p>
<p>an exile; but it will afford me true pleasure to be in</p>
<p>any way serviceable to a human creature.’</p>
<p>“ ‘Excellent man! I thank you, and accept your</p>
<p>generous offer. You raise me from the dust by this</p>
<p>kindness; and I trust that, by your aid, I shall not</p>
<p>be driven from the society and sympathy of your</p>
<p>fellow-creatures.’</p>
<p>“ ‘Heaven forbid! even if you were really criminal;</p>
<p>for that can only drive you to desperation, and not</p>
<p>instigate you to virtue. I also am unfortunate; I</p>
<p>and my family have been condemned, although</p>
<p>innocent: judge, therefore, if I do not feel for your</p>
<p>misfortunes.’</p>
<p>“ ‘How can I thank you, my best and only bene-</p>
<p>factor? from your lips ﬁrst have I heard the voice</p>
<p>of kindness directed towards me; I shall be for ever</p>
<p>grateful; and your present humanity assures me of</p>
<p>success with those friends whom I am on the point</p>
<p>of meeting.’</p>
<p>“ ‘May I know the names and residence of those</p>
<p>friends?’</p>
<p>“I paused. This, I thought, was the moment</p>
<p>of decision, which was to rob me of, or bestow</p>
<p>happiness on me for ever. I struggled vainly for</p>
<p>ﬁrmness sufﬁcient to answer him, but the effort</p>
<p>destroyed all my remaining strength; I sank on the</p>
<p>chair, and sobbed aloud. At that moment I heard</p>
<p>the steps of my younger protectors. I had not a</p>
<p>moment to lose; but, seizing the hand of the old</p>
<p>man, I cried, ‘Now is the time! — save and protect</p>
<p>me! You and your family are the friends whom I</p>
<p>seek. Do not you desert me in the hour of trial!’</p>
<p>“ ‘Great God!’ exclaimed the old man, ‘who are</p>
<p>you?’</p>
<p>“At that instant the cottage door was opened,</p>
<p>and Felix, Saﬁe, and Agatha entered. Who can de-</p>
<p>scribe their horror and consternation on beholding</p>
<p>me? Agatha fainted; and Saﬁe, unable to attend to</p>
<p>her friend, rushed out of the cottage. Felix darted</p>
<p>forward, and with supernatural force tore me from</p>
<p>his father, to whose knees I clung: in a transport</p>
<p>of fury, he dashed me to the ground, and struck</p>
<p>me violently with a stick. I could have torn him limb</p>
<p>from limb, as the lion rends the antelope. But my</p>
<p>heart sunk within me as with bitter sickness, and I</p>
<p>refrained. I saw him on the point of repeating his</p>
<p>blow, when, overcome by pain and anguish, I quit-</p>
<p>ted the cottage, and in the general tumult escaped</p>
<p>unperceived to my hovel.”</p>
<p>Chapter VIII</p>
<p>“CURSED, cursed creator! Why did I live? Why,</p>
<p>in that instant, did I not extinguish the spark of</p>
<p>existence which you had so wantonly bestowed? I</p>
<p>know not; despair had not yet taken possession of</p>
<p>me; my feelings were those of rage and revenge. I</p>
<p>could with pleasure have destroyed the cottage and</p>
<p>its inhabitants, and have glutted myself with their</p>
<p>shrieks and misery.</p>
<p>“When night came, I quitted my retreat, and</p>
<p>wandered in the wood; and now, no longer re-</p>
<p>strained by the fear of discovery, I gave vent to my</p>
<p>anguish in fearful howlings. I was like a wild beast</p>
<p>238</p>
<p>that had broken the toils; destroying the objects</p>
<p>that obstructed me, and ranging through the wood</p>
<p>with a stag-like swiftness. Oh! what a miserable</p>
<p>night I passed! the cold stars shone in mockery,</p>
<p>and the bare trees waved their branches above me:</p>
<p>now and then the sweet voice of a bird burst forth</p>
<p>amidst the universal stillness. All, save I, were at</p>
<p>rest or in enjoyment: I, like the arch ﬁend, bore a</p>
<p>hell within me; and, ﬁnding myself unsympathized</p>
<p>with, wished to tear up the trees, spread havoc and</p>
<p>destruction around me, and then to have sat down</p>
<p>and enjoyed the ruin.</p>
<p>“But this was a luxury of sensation that could</p>
<p>not endure; I became fatigued with excess of bod-</p>
<p>ily exertion, and sank on the damp grass in the</p>
<p>sick impotence of despair. There was none among</p>
<p>the myriads of men that existed who would pity or</p>
<p>assist me; and should I feel kindness towards my</p>
<p>enemies? No: from that moment I declared ever-</p>
<p>lasting war against the species, and, more than all,</p>
<p>against him who had formed me, and sent me forth</p>
<p>to this insupportable misery.</p>
<p>“The sun rose; I heard the voices of men, and</p>
<p>knew that it was impossible to return to my retreat</p>
<p>during that day. Accordingly I hid myself in some</p>
<p>thick underwood, determining to devote the ensuing</p>
<p>hours to reﬂection on my situation.</p>
<p>“The pleasant sunshine, and the pure air of day,</p>
<p>restored me to some degree of tranquillity; and</p>
<p>when I considered what had passed at the cottage,</p>
<p>I could not help believing that I had been too hasty</p>
<p>in my conclusions. I had certainly acted imprudently.</p>
<p>It was apparent that my conversation had interested</p>
<p>the father in my behalf, and I was a fool in having</p>
<p>exposed my person to the horror of his children. I</p>
<p>ought to have familiarized the old De Lacy to me,</p>
<p>and by degrees have discovered myself to the rest</p>
<p>of his family, when they should have been prepared</p>
<p>for my approach. But I did not believe my errors</p>
<p>to be irretrievable; and, after much consideration, I</p>
<p>resolved to return to the cottage, seek the old man,</p>
<p>and by my representations win him to my party.</p>
<p>“These thoughts calmed me, and in the after-</p>
<p>noon I sank into a profound sleep; but the fever of</p>
<p>my blood did not allow me to be visited by peace-</p>
<p>ful dreams. The horrible scene of the preceding</p>
<p>day was for ever acting before my eyes; the fe-</p>
<p>males were ﬂying, and the enraged Felix tearing</p>
<p>me from his father’s feet. I awoke exhausted; and,</p>
<p>ﬁnding that it was already night, I crept forth from</p>
<p>my hiding-place, and went in search of food.</p>
<p>“When my hunger was appeased, I directed my</p>
<p>steps towards the well-known path that conducted</p>
<p>to the cottage. All there was at peace. I crept into</p>
<p>my hovel, and remained in silent expectation of the</p>
<p>accustomed hour when the family arose. That hour</p>
<p>past, the sun mounted high in the heavens, but the</p>
<p>cottagers did not appear. I trembled violently, ap-</p>
<p>prehending some dreadful misfortune. The inside</p>
<p>of the cottage was dark, and I heard no motion; I</p>
<p>cannot describe the agony of this suspence.</p>
<p>“Presently two countrymen passed by; but,</p>
<p>pausing near the cottage, they entered into con-</p>
<p>versation, using violent gesticulations; but I did not</p>
<p>understand what they said, as they spoke the lan-</p>
<p>guage of the country, which differed from that of my</p>
<p>protectors. Soon after, however, Felix approached</p>
<p>with another man: I was surprised, as I knew that</p>
<p>he had not quitted the cottage that morning, and</p>
<p>waited anxiously to discover, from his discourse,</p>
<p>the meaning of these unusual appearances.</p>
<p>“ ‘Do you consider,’ said his companion to him,</p>
<p>‘that you will be obliged to pay three months’ rent,</p>
<p>and to lose the produce of your garden? I do not</p>
<p>wish to take any unfair advantage, and I beg there-</p>
<p>fore that you will take some days to consider of your</p>
<p>determination.’</p>
<p>“ ‘It is utterly useless,’ replied Felix, ‘we can</p>
<p>never again inhabit your cottage. The life of my</p>
<p>father is in the greatest danger, owing to the dread-</p>
<p>ful circumstance that I have related. My wife and my</p>
<p>sister will never recover their horror. I entreat you</p>
<p>not to reason with me any more. Take possession</p>
<p>of your tenement, and let me ﬂy from this place.’</p>
<p>“Felix trembled violently as he said this. He and</p>
<p>his companion entered the cottage, in which they</p>
<p>remained for a few minutes, and then departed. I</p>
<p>never saw any of the family of De Lacy more.</p>
<p>“I continued for the remainder of the day in my</p>
<p>hovel in a state of utter and stupid despair. My</p>
<p>protectors had departed, and had broken the only</p>
<p>link that held me to the world. For the ﬁrst time</p>
<p>the feelings of revenge and hatred ﬁlled my bosom,</p>
<p>and I did not strive to controul them; but, allowing</p>
<p>myself to be borne away by the stream, I bent my</p>
<p>mind towards injury and death. When I thought of</p>
<p>my friends, of the mild voice of De Lacy, the gentle</p>
<p>eyes of Agatha, and the exquisite beauty of the</p>
<p>Arabian, these thoughts vanished, and a gush of</p>
<p>tears somewhat soothed me. But again, when I</p>
<p>reﬂected that they had spurned and deserted me,</p>
<p>anger returned, a rage of anger; and, unable to</p>
<p>injure any thing human, I turned my fury towards</p>
<p>inanimate objects. As night advanced, I placed a</p>
<p>variety of combustibles around the cottage; and,</p>
<p>after having destroyed every vestige of cultivation</p>
<p>in the garden, I waited with forced impatience until</p>
<p>the moon had sunk to commence my operations.</p>
<p>“As the night advanced, a ﬁerce wind arose</p>
<p>from the woods, and quickly dispersed the clouds</p>
<p>that had loitered in the heavens: the blast tore along</p>
<p>like a mighty avalanche, and produced a kind of</p>
<p>insanity in my spirits, that burst all bounds of reason</p>
<p>and reﬂection. I lighted the dry branch of a tree,</p>
<p>and danced with fury around the devoted cottage,</p>
<p>my eyes still ﬁxed on the western horizon, the edge</p>
<p>of which the moon nearly touched. A part of its orb</p>
<p>was at length hid, and I waved my brand; it sunk,</p>
<p>and, with a loud scream, I ﬁred the straw, and heath,</p>
<p>and bushes, which I had collected. The wind fanned</p>
<p>the ﬁre, and the cottage was quickly enveloped by</p>
<p>the ﬂames, which clung to it, and licked it with their</p>
<p>forked and destroying tongues.</p>
<p>“As soon as I was convinced that no assistance</p>
<p>could save any part of the habitation, I quitted the</p>
<p>scene, and sought for refuge in the woods.</p>
<p>“And now, with the world before me, whither</p>
<p>should I bend my steps? I resolved to ﬂy far from</p>
<p>the scene of my misfortunes; but to me, hated and</p>
<p>despised, every country must be equally horrible.</p>
<p>At length the thought of you crossed my mind. I</p>
<p>learned from your papers that you were my father,</p>
<p>my creator; and to whom could I apply with more</p>
<p>ﬁtness than to him who had given me life? Among</p>
<p>the lessons that Felix had bestowed upon Saﬁe</p>
<p>geography had not been omitted: I had learned</p>
<p>from these the relative situations of the different</p>
<p>countries of the earth. You had mentioned Geneva</p>
<p>as the name of your native town; and towards this</p>
<p>place I resolved to proceed.</p>
<p>“But how was I to direct myself? I knew that I</p>
<p>must travel in a south-westerly direction to reach</p>
<p>my destination; but the sun was my only guide. I did</p>
<p>not know the names of the towns that I was to pass</p>
<p>through, nor could I ask information from a single</p>
<p>human being; but I did not despair. From you only</p>
<p>could I hope for succour, although towards you I felt</p>
<p>no sentiment but that of hatred. Unfeeling, heart-</p>
<p>less creator! you had endowed me with perceptions</p>
<p>and passions, and then cast me abroad an object</p>
<p>for the scorn and horror of mankind. But on you</p>
<p>only had I any claim for pity and redress, and from</p>
<p>you I determined to seek that justice which I vainly</p>
<p>attempted to gain from any other being that wore</p>
<p>the human form.</p>
<p>“My travels were long, and the sufferings I en-</p>
<p>dured intense. It was late in autumn when I quitted</p>
<p>the district where I had so long resided. I travelled</p>
<p>only at night, fearful of encountering the visage of a</p>
<p>human being. Nature decayed around me, and the</p>
<p>sun became heatless; rain and snow poured around</p>
<p>me; mighty rivers were frozen; the surface of the</p>
<p>earth was hard, and chill, and bare, and I found</p>
<p>no shelter. Oh, earth! how often did I imprecate</p>
<p>curses on the cause of my being! The mildness of</p>
<p>my nature had ﬂed, and all within me was turned</p>
<p>to gall and bitterness. The nearer I approached</p>
<p>to your habitation, the more deeply did I feel the</p>
<p>spirit of revenge enkindled in my heart. Snow fell,</p>
<p>and the waters were hardened, but I rested not.</p>
<p>A few incidents now and then directed me, and I</p>
<p>possessed a map of the country; but I often wan-</p>
<p>dered wide from my path. The agony of my feelings</p>
<p>allowed me no respite: no incident occurred from</p>
<p>which my rage and misery could not extract its food;</p>
<p>but a circumstance that happened when I arrived</p>
<p>on the conﬁnes of Switzerland, when the sun had</p>
<p>recovered its warmth, and the earth again began</p>
<p>to look green, conﬁrmed in an especial manner the</p>
<p>bitterness and horror of my feelings.</p>
<p>“I generally rested during the day, and travelled</p>
<p>only when I was secured by night from the view of</p>
<p>man. One morning, however, ﬁnding that my path</p>
<p>lay through a deep wood, I ventured to continue</p>
<p>my journey after the sun had risen; the day, which</p>
<p>was one of the ﬁrst of spring, cheered even me by</p>
<p>the loveliness of its sunshine and the balminess</p>
<p>of the air. I felt emotions of gentleness and plea-</p>
<p>sure, that had long appeared dead, revive within</p>
<p>me. Half surprised by the novelty of these sensa-</p>
<p>tions, I allowed myself to be borne away by them;</p>
<p>and, forgetting my solitude and deformity, dared to</p>
<p>be happy. Soft tears again bedewed my cheeks,</p>
<p>and I even raised my humid eyes with thankfulness</p>
<p>towards the blessed sun which bestowed such joy</p>
<p>upon me.</p>
<p>“I continued to wind among the paths of the</p>
<p>wood, until I came to its boundary, which was</p>
<p>skirted by a deep and rapid river, into which many</p>
<p>of the trees bent their branches, now budding</p>
<p>with the fresh spring. Here I paused, not exactly</p>
<p>knowing what path to pursue, when I heard the</p>
<p>sound of voices, that induced me to conceal myself</p>
<p>under the shade of a cypress. I was scarcely hid,</p>
<p>when a young girl came running towards the spot</p>
<p>where I was concealed, laughing as if she ran from</p>
<p>some one in sport. She continued her course along</p>
<p>the precipitous sides of the river, when suddenly</p>
<p>her foot slipt, and she fell into the rapid stream. I</p>
<p>rushed from my hiding place, and, with extreme</p>
<p>labour from the force of the current, saved her, and</p>
<p>dragged her to shore. She was senseless; and</p>
<p>I endeavoured, by every means in my power, to</p>
<p>restore animation, when I was suddenly interrupted</p>
<p>by the approach of a rustic, who was probably</p>
<p>the person from whom she had playfully ﬂed. On</p>
<p>seeing me, he darted towards me, and, tearing the</p>
<p>girl from my arms, hastened towards the deeper</p>
<p>parts of the wood. I followed speedily, I hardly</p>
<p>knew why; but when the man saw me draw near,</p>
<p>he aimed a gun, which he carried, at my body, and</p>
<p>ﬁred. I sunk to the ground, and my injurer, with</p>
<p>increased swiftness, escaped into the wood.</p>
<p>“This was then the reward of my benevolence!</p>
<p>I had saved a human being from destruction, and,</p>
<p>as a recompence, I now writhed under the miser-</p>
<p>able pain of a wound, which shattered the ﬂesh</p>
<p>and bone. The feelings of kindness and gentle-</p>
<p>ness, which I had entertained but a few moments</p>
<p>before, gave place to hellish rage and gnashing</p>
<p>of teeth. Inﬂamed by pain, I vowed eternal hatred</p>
<p>and vengeance to all mankind. But the agony of</p>
<p>my wound overcame me; my pulses paused, and I</p>
<p>fainted.</p>
<p>“For some weeks I led a miserable life in the</p>
<p>woods, endeavouring to cure the wound which I</p>
<p>had received. The ball had entered my shoulder,</p>
<p>and I knew not whether it had remained there or</p>
<p>passed through; at any rate I had no means of</p>
<p>extracting it. My sufferings were augmented also by</p>
<p>the oppressive sense of the injustice and ingratitude</p>
<p>of their inﬂiction. My daily vows rose for revenge —</p>
<p>a deep and deadly revenge, such as would alone</p>
<p>compensate for the outrages and anguish I had</p>
<p>endured.</p>
<p>“After some weeks my wound healed, and I</p>
<p>continued my journey. The labours I endured were</p>
<p>no longer to be alleviated by the bright sun or gentle</p>
<p>breezes of spring; all joy was but a mockery, which</p>
<p>insulted my desolate state, and made me feel more</p>
<p>painfully that I was not made for the enjoyment of</p>
<p>pleasure.</p>
<p>“But my toils now drew near a close; and, two</p>
<p>months from this time, I reached the environs of</p>
<p>Geneva.</p>
<p>“It was evening when I arrived, and I retired to</p>
<p>a hiding-place among the ﬁelds that surround it, to</p>
<p>meditate in what manner I should apply to you. I</p>
<p>was oppressed by fatigue and hunger, and far too</p>
<p>unhappy to enjoy the gentle breezes of evening, or</p>
<p>the prospect of the sun setting behind the stupen-</p>
<p>dous mountains of Jura.</p>
<p>“At this time a slight sleep relieved me from the</p>
<p>pain of reﬂection, which was disturbed by the ap-</p>
<p>proach of a beautiful child, who came running into</p>
<p>the recess I had chosen with all the sportiveness</p>
<p>of infancy. Suddenly, as I gazed on him, an idea</p>
<p>seized me, that this little creature was unprejudiced,</p>
<p>and had lived too short a time to have imbibed a</p>
<p>horror of deformity. If, therefore, I could seize him,</p>
<p>and educate him as my companion and friend, I</p>
<p>should not be so desolate in this peopled earth.</p>
<p>“Urged by this impulse, I seized on the boy as</p>
<p>he passed, and drew him towards me. As soon as</p>
<p>he beheld my form, he placed his hands before his</p>
<p>eyes, and uttered a shrill scream: I drew his hand</p>
<p>forcibly from his face, and said, ‘Child, what is the</p>
<p>meaning of this? I do not intend to hurt you; listen</p>
<p>to me.’</p>
<p>“He struggled violently; ‘Let me go,’ he cried;</p>
<p>‘monster! ugly wretch! you wish to eat me, and tear</p>
<p>me to pieces — You are an ogre — Let me go, or I</p>
<p>will tell my papa.’</p>
<p>“ ‘Boy, you will never see your father again; you</p>
<p>must come with me.’</p>
<p>“ ‘Hideous monster! let me go; My papa is a</p>
<p>Syndic — he is M. Frankenstein — he would punish</p>
<p>you. You dare not keep me.’</p>
<p>“ ‘Frankenstein! you belong then to my enemy —</p>
<p>to him towards whom I have sworn eternal revenge;</p>
<p>you shall be my ﬁrst victim.’</p>
<p>“The child still struggled, and loaded me with ep-</p>
<p>ithets which carried despair to my heart: I grasped</p>
<p>his throat to silence him, and in a moment he lay</p>
<p>dead at my feet.</p>
<p>“I gazed on my victim, and my heart swelled</p>
<p>with exultation and hellish triumph: clapping my</p>
<p>hands, I exclaimed, ‘I, too, can create desolation;</p>
<p>my enemy is not impregnable; this death will carry</p>
<p>despair to him, and a thousand other miseries shall</p>
<p>torment and destroy him.’</p>
<p>“As I ﬁxed my eyes on the child, I saw something</p>
<p>glittering on his breast. I took it; it was a portrait</p>
<p>of a most lovely woman. In spite of my malignity, it</p>
<p>softened and attracted me. For a few moments I</p>
<p>gazed with delight on her dark eyes, fringed by deep</p>
<p>lashes, and her lovely lips; but presently my rage</p>
<p>returned: I remembered that I was for ever deprived</p>
<p>of the delights that such beautiful creatures could</p>
<p>bestow; and that she whose resemblance I contem-</p>
<p>plated would, in regarding me, have changed that</p>
<p>air of divine benignity to one expressive of disgust</p>
<p>and affright.</p>
<p>“Can you wonder that such thoughts trans-</p>
<p>ported me with rage? I only wonder that at that</p>
<p>moment, instead of venting my sensations in excla-</p>
<p>mations and agony, I did not rush among mankind,</p>
<p>and perish in the attempt to destroy them.</p>
<p>“While I was overcome by these feelings, I left</p>
<p>the spot where I had committed the murder, and</p>
<p>was seeking a more secluded hiding-place, when</p>
<p>I perceived a woman passing near me. She was</p>
<p>young, not indeed so beautiful as her whose portrait</p>
<p>I held, but of an agreeable aspect, and blooming in</p>
<p>the loveliness of youth and health. Here, I thought,</p>
<p>is one of those whose smiles are bestowed on all</p>
<p>but me; she shall not escape: thanks to the lessons</p>
<p>of Felix, and the sanguinary laws of man, I have</p>
<p>learned how to work mischief. I approached her</p>
<p>unperceived, and placed the portrait securely in</p>
<p>one of the folds of her dress.</p>
<p>“For some days I haunted the spot where these</p>
<p>scenes had taken place; sometimes wishing to see</p>
<p>you, sometimes resolved to quit the world and its</p>
<p>miseries for ever. At length I wandered towards</p>
<p>these mountains, and have ranged through their im-</p>
<p>mense recesses, consumed by a burning passion</p>
<p>which you alone can gratify. We may not part until</p>
<p>you have promised to comply with my requisition.</p>
<p>I am alone, and miserable; man will not associate</p>
<p>with me; but one as deformed and horrible as my-</p>
<p>self would not deny herself to me. My companion</p>
<p>must be of the same species, and have the same</p>
<p>defects. This being you must create.”</p>
<p>Chapter IX</p>
<p>THE being ﬁnished speaking, and ﬁxed his looks</p>
<p>upon me in expectation of a reply. But I was bewil-</p>
<p>dered, perplexed, and unable to arrange my ideas</p>
<p>sufﬁciently to understand the full extent of his propo-</p>
<p>sition. He continued —</p>
<p>“You must create a female for me, with whom</p>
<p>I can live in the interchange of those sympathies</p>
<p>necessary for my being. This you alone can do;</p>
<p>and I demand it of you as a right which you must</p>
<p>not refuse.”</p>
<p>The latter part of his tale had kindled anew in</p>
<p>me the anger that had died away while he narrated</p>
<p>255</p>
<p>his peaceful life among the cottagers, and, as he</p>
<p>said this, I could no longer suppress the rage that</p>
<p>burned within me.</p>
<p>“I do refuse it,” I replied; “and no torture shall</p>
<p>ever extort a consent from me. You may render</p>
<p>me the most miserable of men, but you shall never</p>
<p>make me base in my own eyes. Shall I create</p>
<p>another like yourself, whose joint wickedness might</p>
<p>desolate the world. Begone! I have answered you;</p>
<p>you may torture me, but I will never consent.”</p>
<p>“You are in the wrong,” replied the ﬁend; “and,</p>
<p>instead of threatening, I am content to reason with</p>
<p>you. I am malicious because I am miserable; am</p>
<p>I not shunned and hated by all mankind? You, my</p>
<p>creator, would tear me to pieces, and triumph; re-</p>
<p>member that, and tell me why I should pity man</p>
<p>more than he pities me? You would not call it mur-</p>
<p>der, if you could precipitate me into one of those</p>
<p>ice-rifts, and destroy my frame, the work of your</p>
<p>own hands. Shall I respect man, when he con-</p>
<p>temns me? Let him live with me in the interchange</p>
<p>of kindness, and, instead of injury, I would bestow</p>
<p>every beneﬁt upon him with tears of gratitude at his</p>
<p>acceptance. But that cannot be; the human senses</p>
<p>are insurmountable barriers to our union. Yet mine</p>
<p>shall not be the submission of abject slavery. I will</p>
<p>revenge my injuries: if I cannot inspire love, I will</p>
<p>cause fear; and chieﬂy towards you my arch-enemy,</p>
<p>because my creator, do I swear inextinguishable ha-</p>
<p>tred. Have a care: I will work at your destruction,</p>
<p>nor ﬁnish until I desolate your heart, so that you</p>
<p>curse the hour of your birth.”</p>
<p>A ﬁendish rage animated him as he said this;</p>
<p>his face was wrinkled into contortions too horrible</p>
<p>for human eyes to behold; but presently he calmed</p>
<p>himself, and proceeded —</p>
<p>“I intended to reason. This passion is detrimen-</p>
<p>tal to me; for you do not reﬂect that you are the</p>
<p>cause of its excess. If any being felt emotions of</p>
<p>benevolence towards me, I should return them an</p>
<p>hundred and an hundred fold; for that one crea-</p>
<p>ture’s sake, I would make peace with the whole</p>
<p>kind! But I now indulge in dreams of bliss that can-</p>
<p>not be realized. What I ask of you is reasonable</p>
<p>and moderate; I demand a creature of another sex,</p>
<p>but as hideous as myself: the gratiﬁcation is small,</p>
<p>but it is all that I can receive, and it shall content</p>
<p>me. It is true, we shall be monsters, cut off from</p>
<p>all the world; but on that account we shall be more</p>
<p>attached to one another. Our lives will not be happy,</p>
<p>but they will be harmless, and free from the misery</p>
<p>I now feel. Oh! my creator, make me happy; let</p>
<p>me feel gratitude towards you for one beneﬁt! Let</p>
<p>me see that I excite the sympathy of some existing</p>
<p>thing; do not deny me my request!”</p>
<p>I was moved. I shuddered when I thought of the</p>
<p>possible consequences of my consent; but I felt that</p>
<p>there was some justice in his argument. His tale,</p>
<p>and the feelings he now expressed, proved him to</p>
<p>be a creature of ﬁne sensations; and did I not, as</p>
<p>his maker, owe him all the portion of happiness that</p>
<p>it was in my power to bestow? He saw my change</p>
<p>of feeling, and continued —</p>
<p>“If you consent, neither you nor any other hu-</p>
<p>man being shall ever see us again: I will go to the</p>
<p>vast wilds of South America. My food is not that of</p>
<p>man; I do not destroy the lamb and the kid, to glut</p>
<p>my appetite; acorns and berries afford me sufﬁcient</p>
<p>nourishment. My companion will be of the same</p>
<p>nature as myself, and will be content with the same</p>
<p>fare. We shall make our bed of dried leaves; the sun</p>
<p>will shine on us as on man, and will ripen our food.</p>
<p>The picture I present to you is peaceful and human,</p>
<p>and you must feel that you could deny it only in the</p>
<p>wantonness of power and cruelty. Pitiless as you</p>
<p>have been towards me, I now see compassion in</p>
<p>your eyes: let me seize the favourable moment, and</p>
<p>persuade you to promise what I so ardently desire.”</p>
<p>“You propose,” replied I, “to ﬂy from the habi-</p>
<p>tations of man, to dwell in those wilds where the</p>
<p>beasts of the ﬁeld will be your only companions.</p>
<p>How can you, who long for the love and sympathy</p>
<p>of man, persevere in this exile? You will return, and</p>
<p>again seek their kindness, and you will meet with</p>
<p>their detestation; your evil passions will be renewed,</p>
<p>and you will then have a companion to aid you in</p>
<p>the task of destruction. This may not be; cease to</p>
<p>argue the point, for I cannot consent.”</p>
<p>“How inconstant are your feelings! but a mo-</p>
<p>ment ago you were moved by my representations,</p>
<p>and why do you again harden yourself to my com-</p>
<p>plaints? I swear to you, by the earth which I inhabit,</p>
<p>and by you that made me, that, with the companion</p>
<p>you bestow, I will quit the neighbourhood of man,</p>
<p>and dwell, as it may chance, in the most savage of</p>
<p>places. My evil passions will have ﬂed, for I shall</p>
<p>meet with sympathy; my life will ﬂow quietly away,</p>
<p>and, in my dying moments, I shall not curse my</p>
<p>maker.”</p>
<p>His words had a strange effect upon me. I com-</p>
<p>passionated him, and sometimes felt a wish to con-</p>
<p>sole him; but when I looked upon him, when I saw</p>
<p>the ﬁlthy mass that moved and talked, my heart</p>
<p>sickened, and my feelings were altered to those of</p>
<p>horror and hatred. I tried to stiﬂe these sensations;</p>
<p>I thought, that as I could not sympathize with him, I</p>
<p>had no right to withhold from him the small portion</p>
<p>of happiness which was yet in my power to bestow.</p>
<p>“You swear,” I said, “to be harmless; but have</p>
<p>you not already shewn a degree of malice that</p>
<p>should reasonably make me distrust you? May not</p>
<p>even this be a feint that will increase your triumph</p>
<p>by affording a wider scope for your revenge?”</p>
<p>“How is this? I thought I had moved your com-</p>
<p>passion, and yet you still refuse to bestow on me</p>
<p>the only beneﬁt that can soften my heart, and ren-</p>
<p>der me harmless. If I have no ties and no affections,</p>
<p>hatred and vice must be my portion; the love of</p>
<p>another will destroy the cause of my crimes, and</p>
<p>I shall become a thing, of whose existence every</p>
<p>one will be ignorant. My vices are the children of</p>
<p>a forced solitude that I abhor; and my virtues will</p>
<p>necessarily arise when I live in communion with an</p>
<p>equal. I shall feel the affections of a sensitive being,</p>
<p>and become linked to the chain of existence and</p>
<p>events, from which I am now excluded.”</p>
<p>I paused some time to reﬂect on all he had</p>
<p>related, and the various arguments which he had</p>
<p>employed. I thought of the promise of virtues which</p>
<p>he had displayed on the opening of his existence,</p>
<p>and the subsequent blight of all kindly feeling by</p>
<p>the loathing and scorn which his protectors had</p>
<p>manifested towards him. His power and threats</p>
<p>were not omitted in my calculations: a creature</p>
<p>who could exist in the ice caves of the glaciers,</p>
<p>and hide himself from pursuit among the ridges of</p>
<p>inaccessible precipices, was a being possessing</p>
<p>faculties it would be vain to cope with. After a long</p>
<p>pause of reﬂection, I concluded, that the justice due</p>
<p>both to him and my fellow-creatures demanded of</p>
<p>me that I should comply with his request. Turning</p>
<p>to him, therefore, I said —</p>
<p>“I consent to your demand, on your solemn oath</p>
<p>to quit Europe for ever, and every other place in the</p>
<p>neighbourhood of man, as soon as I shall deliver</p>
<p>into your hands a female who will accompany you</p>
<p>in your exile.”</p>
<p>“I swear,” he cried, “by the sun, and by the blue</p>
<p>sky of heaven, that if you grant my prayer, while</p>
<p>they exist you shall never behold me again. Depart</p>
<p>to your home, and commence your labours: I shall</p>
<p>watch their progress with unutterable anxiety; and</p>
<p>fear not but that when you are ready I shall appear.”</p>
<p>Saying this, he suddenly quitted me, fearful,</p>
<p>perhaps, of any change in my sentiments. I saw</p>
<p>him descend the mountain with greater speed than</p>
<p>the ﬂight of an eagle, and quickly lost him among</p>
<p>the undulations of the sea of ice.</p>
<p>His tale had occupied the whole day; and the</p>
<p>sun was upon the verge of the horizon when he</p>
<p>departed. I knew that I ought to hasten my descent</p>
<p>towards the valley, as I should soon be encom-</p>
<p>passed in darkness; but my heart was heavy, and</p>
<p>my steps slow. The labour of winding among the lit-</p>
<p>tle paths of the mountains, and ﬁxing my feet ﬁrmly</p>
<p>as I advanced, perplexed me, occupied as I was by</p>
<p>the emotions which the occurrences of the day had</p>
<p>produced. Night was far advanced, when I came to</p>
<p>the half-way resting-place, and seated myself be-</p>
<p>side the fountain. The stars shone at intervals, as</p>
<p>the clouds passed from over them; the dark pines</p>
<p>rose before me, and every here and there a broken</p>
<p>tree lay on the ground: it was a scene of wonderful</p>
<p>solemnity, and stirred strange thoughts within me.</p>
<p>I wept bitterly; and, clasping my hands in agony, I</p>
<p>exclaimed, “Oh! stars, and clouds, and winds, ye</p>
<p>are all about to mock me: if ye really pity me, crush</p>
<p>sensation and memory; let me become as nought;</p>
<p>but if not, depart, depart and leave me in darkness.”</p>
<p>These were wild and miserable thoughts; but I</p>
<p>cannot describe to you how the eternal twinkling of</p>
<p>the stars weighed upon me, and how I listened to</p>
<p>every blast of wind, as if it were a dull ugly siroc on</p>
<p>its way to consume me.</p>
<p>Morning dawned before I arrived at the village</p>
<p>of Chamounix; but my presence, so haggard and</p>
<p>strange, hardly calmed the fears of my family, who</p>
<p>had waited the whole night in anxious expectation</p>
<p>of my return.</p>
<p>The following day we returned to Geneva. The</p>
<p>intention of my father in coming had been to divert</p>
<p>my mind, and to restore me to my lost tranquillity;</p>
<p>but the medicine had been fatal. And, unable to</p>
<p>account for the excess of misery I appeared to</p>
<p>suffer, he hastened to return home, hoping the quiet</p>
<p>and monotony of a domestic life would by degrees</p>
<p>alleviate my sufferings from whatsoever cause they</p>
<p>might spring.</p>
<p>For myself, I was passive in all their arrange-</p>
<p>ments; and the gentle affection of my beloved Eliza-</p>
<p>beth was inadequate to draw me from the depth of</p>
<p>my despair. The promise I had made to the dæmon</p>
<p>weighed upon my mind, like Dante’s iron cowl on</p>
<p>the heads of the hellish hypocrites. All pleasures</p>
<p>of earth and sky passed before me like a dream,</p>
<p>and that thought only had to me the reality of life.</p>
<p>Can you wonder, that sometimes a kind of insanity</p>
<p>possessed me, or that I saw continually about me</p>
<p>a multitude of ﬁlthy animals inﬂicting on me inces-</p>
<p>sant torture, that often extorted screams and bitter</p>
<p>groans?</p>
<p>By degrees, however, these feelings became</p>
<p>calmed. I entered again into the every-day scene</p>
<p>of life, if not with interest, at least with some degree</p>
<p>of tranquillity.</p>
<p>Volume III</p>
<p>266</p>
<p>Chapter I</p>
<p>DAY after day, week after week, passed away on</p>
