<p>my return to Geneva; and I could not collect the</p>
<p>courage to recommence my work. I feared the</p>
<p>vengeance of the disappointed ﬁend, yet I was un-</p>
<p>able to overcome my repugnance to the task which</p>
<p>was enjoined me. I found that I could not compose</p>
<p>a female without again devoting several months</p>
<p>to profound study and laborious disquisition. I had</p>
<p>heard of some discoveries having been made by an</p>
<p>English philosopher, the knowledge of which was</p>
<p>material to my success, and I sometimes thought</p>
<p>of obtaining my father’s consent to visit England</p>
<p>267</p>
<p>for this purpose; but I clung to every pretence of</p>
<p>delay, and could not resolve to interrupt my return-</p>
<p>ing tranquillity. My health, which had hitherto de-</p>
<p>clined, was now much restored; and my spirits,</p>
<p>when unchecked by the memory of my unhappy</p>
<p>promise, rose proportionably. My father saw this</p>
<p>change with pleasure, and he turned his thoughts</p>
<p>towards the best method of eradicating the remains</p>
<p>of my melancholy, which every now and then would</p>
<p>return by ﬁts, and with a devouring blackness over-</p>
<p>cast the approaching sunshine. At these moments</p>
<p>I took refuge in the most perfect solitude. I passed</p>
<p>whole days on the lake alone in a little boat, watch-</p>
<p>ing the clouds, and listening to the rippling of the</p>
<p>waves, silent and listless. But the fresh air and</p>
<p>bright sun seldom failed to restore me to some de-</p>
<p>gree of composure; and, on my return, I met the</p>
<p>salutations of my friends with a readier smile and a</p>
<p>more cheerful heart.</p>
<p>It was after my return from one of these rambles</p>
<p>that my father, calling me aside, thus addressed</p>
<p>me:—</p>
<p>“I am happy to remark, my dear son, that you</p>
<p>have resumed your former pleasures, and seem</p>
<p>to be returning to yourself. And yet you are still</p>
<p>unhappy, and still avoid our society. For some time</p>
<p>I was lost in conjecture as to the cause of this;</p>
<p>but yesterday an idea struck me, and if it is well</p>
<p>founded, I conjure you to avow it. Reserve on such</p>
<p>a point would be not only useless, but draw down</p>
<p>treble misery on us all.”</p>
<p>I trembled violently at this exordium, and my</p>
<p>father continued —</p>
<p>“I confess, my son, that I have always looked</p>
<p>forward to your marriage with your cousin as the</p>
<p>tie of our domestic comfort, and the stay of my</p>
<p>declining years. You were attached to each other</p>
<p>from your earliest infancy; you studied together, and</p>
<p>appeared, in dispositions and tastes, entirely suited</p>
<p>to one another. But so blind is the experience of</p>
<p>man, that what I conceived to be the best assistants</p>
<p>to my plan may have entirely destroyed it. You,</p>
<p>perhaps, regard her as your sister, without any wish</p>
<p>that she might become your wife. Nay, you may</p>
<p>have met with another whom you may love; and,</p>
<p>considering yourself as bound in honour to your</p>
<p>cousin, this struggle may occasion the poignant</p>
<p>misery which you appear to feel.”</p>
<p>“My dear father, re-assure yourself. I love</p>
<p>my cousin tenderly and sincerely. I never saw</p>
<p>any woman who excited, as Elizabeth does, my</p>
<p>warmest admiration and affection. My future hopes</p>
<p>and prospects are entirely bound up in the expecta-</p>
<p>tion of our union.”</p>
<p>“The expression of your sentiments on this sub-</p>
<p>ject, my dear Victor, gives me more pleasure than I</p>
<p>have for some time experienced. If you feel thus, we</p>
<p>shall assuredly be happy, however present events</p>
<p>may cast a gloom over us. But it is this gloom, which</p>
<p>appears to have taken so strong a hold of your</p>
<p>mind, that I wish to dissipate. Tell me, therefore,</p>
<p>whether you object to an immediate solemnization</p>
<p>of the marriage. We have been unfortunate, and</p>
<p>recent events have drawn us from that every-day</p>
<p>tranquillity beﬁtting my years and inﬁrmities. You</p>
<p>are younger; yet I do not suppose, possessed as</p>
<p>you are of a competent fortune, that an early mar-</p>
<p>riage would at all interfere with any future plans of</p>
<p>honour and utility that you may have formed. Do not</p>
<p>suppose, however, that I wish to dictate happiness</p>
<p>to you, or that a delay on your part would cause</p>
<p>me any serious uneasiness. Interpret my words</p>
<p>with candour, and answer me, I conjure you, with</p>
<p>conﬁdence and sincerity.”</p>
<p>I listened to my father in silence, and remained</p>
<p>for some time incapable of offering any reply. I re-</p>
<p>volved rapidly in my mind a multitude of thoughts,</p>
<p>and endeavoured to arrive at some conclusion.</p>
<p>Alas! to me the idea of an immediate union with my</p>
<p>cousin was one of horror and dismay. I was bound</p>
<p>by a solemn promise, which I had not yet fulﬁlled,</p>
<p>and dared not break; or, if I did, what manifold mis-</p>
<p>eries might not impend over me and my devoted</p>
<p>family! Could I enter into a festival with this deadly</p>
<p>weight yet hanging round my neck, and bowing me</p>
<p>to the ground. I must perform my engagement, and</p>
<p>let the monster depart with his mate, before I al-</p>
<p>lowed myself to enjoy the delight of an union from</p>
<p>which I expected peace.</p>
<p>I remembered also the necessity imposed upon</p>
<p>me of either journeying to England, or entering</p>
<p>into a long correspondence with those philosophers</p>
<p>of that country, whose knowledge and discoveries</p>
<p>were of indispensable use to me in my present</p>
<p>undertaking. The latter method of obtaining the</p>
<p>desired intelligence was dilatory and unsatisfactory:</p>
<p>besides, any variation was agreeable to me, and</p>
<p>I was delighted with the idea of spending a year</p>
<p>or two in change of scene and variety of occupa-</p>
<p>tion, in absence from my family; during which pe-</p>
<p>riod some event might happen which would restore</p>
<p>me to them in peace and happiness: my promise</p>
<p>might be fulﬁlled, and the monster have departed;</p>
<p>or some accident might occur to destroy him, and</p>
<p>put an end to my slavery for ever.</p>
<p>These feelings dictated my answer to my father.</p>
<p>I expressed a wish to visit England; but, concealing</p>
<p>the true reasons of this request, I clothed my de-</p>
<p>sires under the guise of wishing to travel and see</p>
<p>the world before I sat down for life within the walls</p>
<p>of my native town.</p>
<p>I urged my entreaty with earnestness, and my</p>
<p>father was easily induced to comply; for a more</p>
<p>indulgent and less dictatorial parent did not exist</p>
<p>upon earth. Our plan was soon arranged. I should</p>
<p>travel to Strasburgh, where Clerval would join me.</p>
<p>Some short time would be spent in the towns of</p>
<p>Holland, and our principal stay would be in England.</p>
<p>We should return by France; and it was agreed that</p>
<p>the tour should occupy the space of two years.</p>
<p>My father pleased himself with the reﬂection,</p>
<p>that my union with Elizabeth should take place im-</p>
<p>mediately on my return to Geneva. “These two</p>
<p>years,” said be, “will pass swiftly, and it will be the</p>
<p>last delay that will oppose itself to your happiness.</p>
<p>And, indeed, I earnestly desire that period to arrive,</p>
<p>when we shall all be united, and neither hopes or</p>
<p>fears arise to disturb our domestic calm.”</p>
<p>“I am content,” I replied, “with your arrangement.</p>
<p>By that time we shall both have become wiser, and</p>
<p>I hope happier, than we at present are.” I sighed;</p>
<p>but my father kindly forbore to question me further</p>
<p>concerning the cause of my dejection. He hoped</p>
<p>that new scenes, and the amusement of travelling,</p>
<p>would restore my tranquillity.</p>
<p>I now made arrangements for my journey; but</p>
<p>one feeling haunted me, which ﬁlled me with fear</p>
<p>and agitation. During my absence I should leave my</p>
<p>friends unconscious of the existence of their enemy,</p>
<p>and unprotected from his attacks, exasperated as</p>
<p>he might be by my departure. But he had promised</p>
<p>to follow me wherever I might go; and would he not</p>
<p>accompany me to England? This imagination was</p>
<p>dreadful in itself, but soothing, inasmuch as it sup-</p>
<p>posed the safety of my friends. I was agonized with</p>
<p>the idea of the possibility that the reverse of this</p>
<p>might happen. But through the whole period during</p>
<p>which I was the slave of my creature, I allowed my-</p>
<p>self to be governed by the impulses of the moment;</p>
<p>and my present sensations strongly intimated that</p>
<p>the ﬁend would follow me, and exempt my family</p>
<p>from the danger of his machinations.</p>
<p>It was in the latter end of August that I departed,</p>
<p>to pass two years of exile. Elizabeth approved of the</p>
<p>reasons of my departure, and only regretted that</p>
<p>she had not the same opportunities of enlarging</p>
<p>her experience, and cultivating her understanding.</p>
<p>She wept, however, as she bade me farewell, and</p>
<p>entreated me to return happy and tranquil. “We</p>
<p>all,” said she, “depend upon you; and if you are</p>
<p>miserable, what must be our feelings?”</p>
<p>I threw myself into the carriage that was to con-</p>
<p>vey me away, hardly knowing whither I was going,</p>
<p>and careless of what was passing around. I re-</p>
<p>membered only, and it was with a bitter anguish</p>
<p>that I reﬂected on it, to order that my chemical in-</p>
<p>struments should be packed to go with me: for</p>
<p>I resolved to fulﬁl my promise while abroad, and</p>
<p>return, if possible, a free man. Filled with dreary</p>
<p>imaginations, I passed through many beautiful and</p>
<p>majestic scenes; but my eyes were ﬁxed and un-</p>
<p>observing. I could only think of the bourne of my</p>
<p>travels, and the work which was to occupy me whilst</p>
<p>they endured.</p>
<p>After some days spent in listless indolence, dur-</p>
<p>ing which I traversed many leagues, I arrived at</p>
<p>Strasburgh, where I waited two days for Clerval. He</p>
<p>came. Alas, how great was the contrast between</p>
<p>us! He was alive to every new scene; joyful when</p>
<p>he saw the beauties of the setting sun, and more</p>
<p>happy when he beheld it rise, and recommence a</p>
<p>new day. He pointed out to me the shifting colours</p>
<p>of the landscape, and the appearances of the sky.</p>
<p>“This is what it is to live;” he cried, “now I enjoy exis-</p>
<p>tence! But you, my dear Frankenstein, wherefore</p>
<p>are you desponding and sorrowful?” In truth, I was</p>
<p>occupied by gloomy thoughts, and neither saw the</p>
<p>descent of the evening star, nor the golden sun-rise</p>
<p>reﬂected in the Rhine. — And you, my friend, would</p>
<p>be far more amused with the journal of Clerval, who</p>
<p>observed the scenery with an eye of feeling and</p>
<p>delight, than to listen to my reﬂections. I, a miser-</p>
<p>able wretch, haunted by a curse that shut up every</p>
<p>avenue to enjoyment.</p>
<p>We had agreed to descend the Rhine in a boat</p>
<p>from Strasburgh to Rotterdam, whence we might</p>
<p>take shipping for London. During this voyage, we</p>
<p>passed by many willowy islands, and saw several</p>
<p>beautiful towns. We staid a day at Manheim, and,</p>
<p>on the ﬁfth from our departure from Strasburgh, ar-</p>
<p>rived at Mayence. The course of the Rhine below</p>
<p>Mayence becomes much more picturesque. The</p>
<p>river descends rapidly, and winds between hills,</p>
<p>not high, but steep, and of beautiful forms. We</p>
<p>saw many ruined castles standing on the edges of</p>
<p>precipices, surrounded by black woods, high and in-</p>
<p>accessible. This part of the Rhine, indeed, presents</p>
<p>a singularly variegated landscape. In one spot</p>
<p>you view rugged hills, ruined castles overlooking</p>
<p>tremendous precipices, with the dark Rhine rushing</p>
<p>beneath; and, on the sudden turn of a promontory,</p>
<p>ﬂourishing vineyards, with green sloping banks, and</p>
<p>a meandering river, and populous towns, occupy</p>
<p>the scene.</p>
<p>We travelled at the time of the vintage, and</p>
<p>heard the song of the labourers, as we glided down</p>
<p>the stream. Even I, depressed in mind, and my</p>
<p>spirits continually agitated by gloomy feelings, even</p>
<p>I was pleased. I lay at the bottom of the boat, and,</p>
<p>as I gazed on the cloudless blue sky, I seemed to</p>
<p>drink in a tranquillity to which I had long been a</p>
<p>stranger. And if these were my sensations, who</p>
<p>can describe those of Henry? He felt as if he had</p>
<p>been transported to Fairy-land, and enjoyed a hap-</p>
<p>piness seldom tasted by man. “I have seen,” he</p>
<p>said, “the most beautiful scenes of my own country;</p>
<p>I have visited the lakes of Lucerne and Uri, where</p>
<p>the snowy mountains descend almost perpendicu-</p>
<p>larly to the water, casting black and impenetrable</p>
<p>shades, which would cause a gloomy and mournful</p>
<p>appearance, were it not for the most verdant is-</p>
<p>lands that relieve the eye by their gay appearance; I</p>
<p>have seen this lake agitated by a tempest, when the</p>
<p>wind tore up whirlwinds of water, and gave you an</p>
<p>idea of what the water-spout must be on the great</p>
<p>ocean, and the waves dash with fury the base of the</p>
<p>mountain, where the priest and his mistress were</p>
<p>overwhelmed by an avalanche, and where their dy-</p>
<p>ing voices are still said to be heard amid the pauses</p>
<p>of the nightly wind; I have seen the mountains of La</p>
<p>Valais, and the Pays de Vaud: but this country, Vic-</p>
<p>tor, pleases me more than all those wonders. The</p>
<p>mountains of Switzerland are more majestic and</p>
<p>strange; but there is a charm in the banks of this di-</p>
<p>vine river, that I never before saw equalled. Look at</p>
<p>that castle which overhangs yon precipice; and that</p>
<p>also on the island, almost concealed amongst the</p>
<p>foliage of those lovely trees; and now that group of</p>
<p>labourers coming from among their vines; and that</p>
<p>village half-hid in the recess of the mountain. Oh,</p>
<p>surely, the spirit that inhabits and guards this place</p>
<p>has a soul more in harmony with man, than those</p>
<p>who pile the glacier, or retire to the inaccessible</p>
<p>peaks of the mountains of our own country.”</p>
<p>Clerval! beloved friend! even now it delights</p>
<p>me to record your words, and to dwell on the praise</p>
<p>of which you are so eminently deserving. He was</p>
<p>a being formed in the “very poetry of nature.” His</p>
<p>wild and enthusiastic imagination was chastened</p>
<p>by the sensibility of his heart. His soul overﬂowed</p>
<p>with ardent affections, and his friendship was of</p>
<p>that devoted and wondrous nature that the worldly-</p>
<p>minded teach us to look for only in the imagination.</p>
<p>But even human sympathies were not sufﬁcient to</p>
<p>satisfy his eager mind. The scenery of external</p>
<p>nature, which others regard only with admiration,</p>
<p>he loved with ardour:</p>
<p>—— —— “The sounding cataract</p>
<p>Haunted him like a passion: the tall rock,</p>
<p>The mountain, and the deep and gloomy wood,</p>
<p>Their colours and their forms, were then to him</p>
<p>An appetite; a feeling, and a love,</p>
<p>That had no need of a remoter charm,</p>
<p>By thought supplied, or any interest</p>
<p>Unborrowed from the eye.”</p>
<p>And where does he now exist? Is this gentle</p>
<p>and lovely being lost for ever? Has this mind so</p>
<p>replete with ideas, imaginations fanciful and mag-</p>
<p>niﬁcent, which formed a world, whose existence</p>
<p>depended on the life of its creator; has this mind</p>
<p>perished? Does it now only exist in my memory?</p>
<p>No, it is not thus; your form so divinely wrought, and</p>
<p>beaming with beauty, has decayed, but your spirit</p>
<p>still visits and consoles your unhappy friend.</p>
<p>Pardon this gush of sorrow; these ineffectual</p>
<p>words are but a slight tribute to the unexampled</p>
<p>worth of Henry, but they soothe my heart, over-</p>
<p>ﬂowing with the anguish which his remembrance</p>
<p>creates. I will proceed with my tale.</p>
<p>Beyond Cologne we descended to the plains of</p>
<p>Holland; and we resolved to post the remainder of</p>
<p>our way; for the wind was contrary, and the stream</p>
<p>of the river was too gentle to aid us.</p>
<p>Our journey here lost the interest arising from</p>
<p>beautiful scenery; but we arrived in a few days</p>
<p>at Rotterdam, whence we proceeded by sea to</p>
<p>England. It was on a clear morning, in the latter</p>
<p>days of December, that I ﬁrst saw the white cliffs</p>
<p>of Britain. The banks of the Thames presented a</p>
<p>new scene; they were ﬂat, but fertile, and almost</p>
<p>every town was marked by the remembrance of</p>
<p>some story. We saw Tilbury Fort, and remembered</p>
<p>the Spanish armada; Gravesend, Woolwich, and</p>
<p>Greenwich, places which I had heard of even in my</p>
<p>country.</p>
<p>At length we saw the numerous steeples of Lon-</p>
<p>don, St. Paul’s towering above all, and the Tower</p>
<p>famed in English history.</p>
<p>Chapter II</p>
<p>LONDON was our present point of rest; we deter-</p>
<p>mined to remain several months in this wonderful</p>
<p>and celebrated city. Clerval desired the intercourse</p>
<p>of the men of genius and talent who ﬂourished at</p>
<p>this time; but this was with me a secondary ob-</p>
<p>ject; I was principally occupied with the means of</p>
<p>obtaining the information necessary for the comple-</p>
<p>tion of my promise, and quickly availed myself of</p>
<p>the letters of introduction that I had brought with</p>
<p>me, addressed to the most distinguished natural</p>
<p>philosophers.</p>
<p>283</p>
<p>If this journey had taken place during my days</p>
<p>of study and happiness, it would have afforded me</p>
<p>inexpressible pleasure. But a blight had come over</p>
<p>my existence, and I only visited these people for</p>
<p>the sake of the information they might give me on</p>
<p>the subject in which my interest was so terribly pro-</p>
<p>found. Company was irksome to me; when alone,</p>
<p>I could ﬁll my mind with the sights of heaven and</p>
<p>earth; the voice of Henry soothed me, and I could</p>
<p>thus cheat myself into a transitory peace. But busy</p>
<p>uninteresting joyous faces brought back despair to</p>
<p>my heart. I saw an insurmountable barrier placed</p>
<p>between me and my fellow-men; this barrier was</p>
<p>sealed with the blood of William and Justine; and to</p>
<p>reﬂect on the events connected with those names</p>
<p>ﬁlled my soul with anguish.</p>
<p>But in Clerval I saw the image of my former self;</p>
<p>he was inquisitive, and anxious to gain experience</p>
<p>and instruction. The difference of manners which</p>
<p>he observed was to him an inexhaustible source</p>
<p>of instruction and amusement. He was for ever</p>
<p>busy; and the only check to his enjoyments was</p>
<p>my sorrowful and dejected mien. I tried to conceal</p>
<p>this as much as possible, that I might not debar</p>
<p>him from the pleasures natural to one who was</p>
<p>entering on a new scene of life, undisturbed by</p>
<p>any care or bitter recollection. I often refused to</p>
<p>accompany him, alleging another engagement, that</p>
<p>I might remain alone. I now also began to collect</p>
<p>the materials necessary for my new creation, and</p>
<p>this was to me like the torture of single drops of</p>
<p>water continually falling on the head. Every thought</p>
<p>that was devoted to it was an extreme anguish, and</p>
<p>every word that I spoke in allusion to it caused my</p>
<p>lips to quiver, and my heart to palpitate.</p>
<p>After passing some months in London, we re-</p>
<p>ceived a letter from a person in Scotland, who had</p>
<p>formerly been our visitor at Geneva. He mentioned</p>
<p>the beauties of his native country, and asked us if</p>
<p>those were not sufﬁcient allurements to induce us to</p>
<p>prolong our journey as far north as Perth, where he</p>
<p>resided. Clerval eagerly desired to accept this invi-</p>
<p>tation; and I, although I abhorred society, wished to</p>
<p>view again mountains and streams, and all the won-</p>
<p>drous works with which Nature adorns her chosen</p>
<p>dwelling-places.</p>
<p>We had arrived in England at the beginning of</p>
<p>October, and it was now February. We accordingly</p>
<p>determined to commence our journey towards the</p>
<p>north at the expiration of another month. In this</p>
<p>expedition we did not intend to follow the great road</p>
<p>to Edinburgh, but to visit Windsor, Oxford, Matlock,</p>
<p>and the Cumberland lakes, resolving to arrive at</p>
<p>the completion of this tour about the end of July. I</p>
<p>packed my chemical instruments, and the materials</p>
<p>I had collected, resolving to ﬁnish my labours in</p>
<p>some obscure nook in the northern highlands of</p>
<p>Scotland.</p>
<p>We quitted London on the 27th of March, and</p>
<p>remained a few days at Windsor, rambling in its</p>
<p>beautiful forest. This was a new scene to us moun-</p>
<p>taineers; the majestic oaks, the quantity of game,</p>
<p>and the herds of stately deer, were all novelties to</p>
<p>us.</p>
<p>From thence we proceeded to Oxford. As we</p>
<p>entered this city, our minds were ﬁlled with the re-</p>
<p>membrance of the events that had been transacted</p>
<p>there more than a century and a half before. It was</p>
<p>here that Charles I. had collected his forces. This</p>
<p>city had remained faithful to him, after the whole</p>
<p>nation had forsaken his cause to join the standard</p>
<p>of parliament and liberty. The memory of that un-</p>
<p>fortunate king, and his companions, the amiable</p>
<p>Falkland, the insolent Gower, his queen, and son,</p>
<p>gave a peculiar interest to every part of the city,</p>
<p>which they might be supposed to have inhabited.</p>
<p>The spirit of elder days found a dwelling here, and</p>
<p>we delighted to trace its footsteps. If these feel-</p>
<p>ings had not found an imaginary gratiﬁcation, the</p>
<p>appearance of the city had yet in itself sufﬁcient</p>
<p>beauty to obtain our admiration. The colleges are</p>
<p>ancient and picturesque; the streets are almost</p>
<p>magniﬁcent; and the lovely Isis, which ﬂows beside</p>
<p>it through meadows of exquisite verdure, is spread</p>
<p>forth into a placid expanse of waters, which reﬂects</p>
<p>its majestic assemblage of towers, and spires, and</p>
<p>domes, embosomed among aged trees.</p>
<p>I enjoyed this scene; and yet my enjoyment was</p>
<p>embittered both by the memory of the past, and the</p>
<p>anticipation of the future. I was formed for peaceful</p>
<p>happiness. During my youthful days discontent</p>
<p>never visited my mind; and if I was ever overcome</p>
<p>by ennui, the sight of what is beautiful in nature, or</p>
<p>the study of what is excellent and sublime in the</p>
<p>productions of man, could always interest my heart,</p>
<p>and communicate elasticity to my spirits. But I am</p>
<p>a blasted tree; the bolt has entered my soul; and</p>
<p>I felt then that I should survive to exhibit, what I</p>
<p>shall soon cease to be — a miserable spectacle of</p>
<p>wrecked humanity, pitiable to others, and abhorrent</p>
<p>to myself.</p>
<p>We passed a considerable period at Oxford,</p>
<p>rambling among its environs, and endeavouring to</p>
<p>identify every spot which might relate to the most</p>
<p>animating epoch of English history. Our little voy-</p>
<p>ages of discovery were often prolonged by the suc-</p>
<p>cessive objects that presented themselves. We</p>
<p>visited the tomb of the illustrious Hampden, and the</p>
<p>ﬁeld on which that patriot fell. For a moment my</p>
<p>soul was elevated from its debasing and miserable</p>
<p>fears to contemplate the divine ideas of liberty and</p>
<p>self-sacriﬁce, of which these sights were the mon-</p>
<p>uments and the remembrancers. For an instant I</p>
<p>dared to shake off my chains, and look around me</p>
<p>with a free and lofty spirit; but the iron had eaten into</p>
<p>my ﬂesh, and I sank again, trembling and hopeless,</p>
<p>into my miserable self.</p>
<p>We left Oxford with regret, and proceeded to</p>
<p>Matlock, which was our next place of rest. The</p>
<p>country in the neighbourhood of this village resem-</p>
<p>bled, to a greater degree, the scenery of Switzer-</p>
<p>land; but every thing is on a lower scale, and the</p>
<p>green hills want the crown of distant white Alps,</p>
<p>which always attend on the piny mountains of my</p>
<p>native country. We visited the wondrous cave, and</p>
<p>the little cabinets of natural history, where the cu-</p>
<p>riosities are disposed in the same manner as in</p>
<p>the collections at Servox and Chamounix. The lat-</p>
<p>ter name made me tremble, when pronounced by</p>
<p>Henry; and I hastened to quit Matlock, with which</p>
<p>that terrible scene was thus associated.</p>
<p>From Derby still journeying northward, we</p>
<p>passed two months in Cumberland and Westmore-</p>
<p>land. I could now almost fancy myself among the</p>
<p>Swiss mountains. The little patches of snow which</p>
<p>yet lingered on the northern sides of the mountains,</p>
<p>the lakes, and the dashing of the rocky streams,</p>
<p>were all familiar and dear sights to me. Here also</p>
<p>we made some acquaintances, who almost con-</p>
<p>trived to cheat me into happiness. The delight of</p>
<p>Clerval was proportionably greater than mine; his</p>
<p>mind expanded in the company of men of talent,</p>
<p>and he found in his own nature greater capacities</p>
<p>and resources than he could have imagined himself</p>
<p>to have possessed while he associated with his</p>
<p>inferiors. “I could pass my life here,” said he to</p>
<p>me; “and among these mountains I should scarcely</p>
<p>regret Switzerland and the Rhine.”</p>
<p>But he found that a traveller’s life is one that</p>
<p>includes much pain amidst its enjoyments. His feel-</p>
<p>ings are for ever on the stretch; and when he begins</p>
<p>to sink into repose, he ﬁnds himself obliged to quit</p>
<p>that on which he rests in pleasure for something</p>
<p>new, which again engages his attention, and which</p>
<p>also he forsakes for other novelties.</p>
<p>We had scarcely visited the various lakes of</p>
<p>Cumberland and Westmoreland, and conceived</p>
<p>an affection for some of the inhabitants, when the</p>
<p>period of our appointment with our Scotch friend</p>
<p>approached, and we left them to travel on. For my</p>
<p>own part I was not sorry. I had now neglected my</p>
<p>promise for some time, and I feared the effects of</p>
<p>the dæmon’s disappointment. He might remain in</p>
<p>Switzerland, and wreak his vengeance on my rela-</p>
<p>tives. This idea pursued me, and tormented me at</p>
<p>every moment from which I might otherwise have</p>
<p>snatched repose and peace. I waited for my letters</p>
<p>with feverish impatience: if they were delayed, I was</p>
<p>miserable, and overcome by a thousand fears; and</p>
<p>when they arrived, and I saw the superscription of</p>
<p>Elizabeth or my father, I hardly dared to read and</p>
<p>ascertain my fate. Sometimes I thought that the</p>
<p>ﬁend followed me, and might expedite my remiss-</p>
<p>ness by murdering my companion. When these</p>
<p>thoughts possessed me, I would not quit Henry for</p>
<p>a moment, but followed him as his shadow, to pro-</p>
<p>tect him from the fancied rage of his destroyer. I felt</p>
<p>as if I had committed some great crime, the con-</p>
<p>sciousness of which haunted me. I was guiltless,</p>
<p>but I had indeed drawn down a horrible curse upon</p>
<p>my head, as mortal as that of crime.</p>
<p>I visited Edinburgh with languid eyes and mind;</p>
<p>and yet that city might have interested the most</p>
<p>unfortunate being. Clerval did not like it so well</p>
<p>as Oxford; for the antiquity of the latter city was</p>
<p>more pleasing to him. But the beauty and regularity</p>
<p>of the new town of Edinburgh, its romantic castle,</p>
<p>and its environs, the most delightful in the world,</p>
<p>Arthur’s Seat, St. Bernard’s Well, and the Pentland</p>
<p>Hills, compensated him for the change, and ﬁlled</p>
<p>him with cheerfulness and admiration. But I was</p>
<p>impatient to arrive at the termination of my journey.</p>
<p>We left Edinburgh in a week, passing through</p>
<p>Coupar, St. Andrews, and along the banks of the</p>
<p>Tay, to Perth, where our friend expected us. But I</p>
<p>was in no mood to laugh and talk with strangers, or</p>
<p>enter into their feelings or plans with the good hu-</p>
<p>mour expected from a guest; and accordingly I told</p>
<p>Clerval that I wished to make the tour of Scotland</p>
<p>alone. “Do you,” said I, “enjoy yourself, and let this</p>
<p>be our rendezvous. I may be absent a month or two;</p>
<p>but do not interfere with my motions, I entreat you:</p>
<p>leave me to peace and solitude for a short time;</p>
<p>and when I return, I hope it will be with a lighter</p>
<p>heart, more congenial to your own temper.”</p>
<p>Henry wished to dissuade me; but, seeing me</p>
<p>bent on this plan, ceased to remonstrate. He en-</p>
<p>treated me to write often. “I had rather be with you,”</p>
<p>he said, “in your solitary rambles, than with these</p>
<p>Scotch people, whom I do not know: hasten then,</p>
<p>my dear friend, to return, that I may again feel my-</p>
<p>self somewhat at home, which I cannot do in your</p>
<p>absence.”</p>
<p>Having parted from my friend, I determined to</p>
<p>visit some remote spot of Scotland, and ﬁnish my</p>
<p>work in solitude. I did not doubt but that the mon-</p>
<p>ster followed me, and would discover himself to me</p>
<p>when I should have ﬁnished, that he might receive</p>
<p>his companion.</p>
<p>With this resolution I traversed the northern</p>
<p>highlands, and ﬁxed on one of the remotest of the</p>
<p>Orkneys as the scene labours. It was a place ﬁtted</p>
<p>for such a work, being hardly more than a rock,</p>
<p>whose high sides were continually beaten upon by</p>
<p>the waves. The soil was barren, scarcely afford-</p>
<p>ing pasture for a few miserable cows, and oatmeal</p>
<p>for its inhabitants, which consisted of ﬁve persons,</p>
<p>whose gaunt and scraggy limbs gave tokens of their</p>
<p>miserable fare. Vegetables and bread, when they</p>
<p>indulged in such luxuries, and even fresh water,</p>
<p>was to be procured from the main land, which was</p>
<p>about ﬁve miles distant.</p>
<p>On the whole island there were but three mis-</p>
<p>erable huts, and one of these was vacant when I</p>
<p>arrived. This I hired. It contained but two rooms,</p>
<p>and these exhibited all the squalidness of the most</p>
<p>miserable penury. The thatch had fallen in, the</p>
<p>walls were unplastered, and the door was off its</p>
<p>hinges. I ordered it to be repaired, bought some</p>
<p>furniture, and took possession; an incident which</p>
<p>would, doubtless, have occasioned some surprise,</p>
<p>had not all the senses of the cottagers been be-</p>
<p>numbed by want and squalid poverty. As it was, I</p>
<p>lived ungazed at and unmolested, hardly thanked</p>
<p>for the pittance of food and clothes which I gave;</p>
<p>so much does suffering blunt even the coarsest</p>
<p>sensations of men.</p>
<p>In this retreat I devoted the morning to labour;</p>
<p>but in the evening, when the weather permitted, I</p>
<p>walked on the stony beach of the sea, to listen to the</p>
<p>waves as they roared, and dashed at my feet. It was</p>
<p>a monotonous, yet ever-changing scene. I thought</p>
<p>of Switzerland; it was far different from this desolate</p>
<p>and appalling landscape. Its hills are covered with</p>
<p>vines, and its cottages are scattered thickly in the</p>
<p>plains. Its fair lakes reﬂect a blue and gentle sky;</p>
<p>and, when troubled by the winds, their tumult is but</p>
<p>as the play of a lively infant, when compared to the</p>
<p>roarings of the giant ocean.</p>
<p>In this manner I distributed my occupations</p>
<p>when I ﬁrst arrived; but, as I proceeded in my labour,</p>
<p>it became every day more horrible and irksome to</p>
<p>me. Sometimes I could not prevail on myself to</p>
<p>enter my laboratory for several days; and at other</p>
<p>times I toiled day and night in order to complete</p>
<p>my work. It was indeed a ﬁlthy process in which I</p>
<p>was engaged. During my ﬁrst experiment, a kind</p>
<p>of enthusiastic frenzy had blinded me to the horror</p>
<p>of my employment; my mind was intently ﬁxed on</p>
<p>the sequel of my labour, and my eyes were shut to</p>
<p>the horror of my proceedings. But now I went to it</p>
<p>in cold blood, and my heart often sickened at the</p>
<p>work of my hands.</p>
<p>Thus situated, employed in the most detestable</p>
<p>occupation, immersed in a solitude where nothing</p>
<p>could for an instant call my attention from the actual</p>
<p>scene in which I was engaged, my spirits became</p>
<p>unequal; I grew restless and nervous. Every mo-</p>
<p>ment I feared to meet my persecutor. Sometimes</p>
<p>I sat with my eyes ﬁxed on the ground, fearing to</p>
<p>raise them lest they should encounter the object</p>
<p>which I so much dreaded to behold. I feared to wan-</p>
<p>der from the sight of my fellow-creatures, lest when</p>
<p>alone he should come to claim his companion.</p>
<p>In the mean time I worked on, and my labour</p>
<p>was already considerably advanced. I looked to-</p>
<p>wards its completion with a tremulous and eager</p>
<p>hope, which I dared not trust myself to question,</p>
<p>but which was intermixed with obscure forebodings</p>
<p>of evil, that made my heart sicken in my bosom.</p>
<p>Chapter III</p>
<p>I SAT one evening in my laboratory; the sun had</p>
<p>set, and the moon was just rising from the sea; I</p>
<p>had not sufﬁcient light for my employment, and I re-</p>
<p>mained idle, in a pause of consideration of whether</p>
<p>I should leave my labour for the night, or hasten its</p>
<p>conclusion by an unremitting attention to it. As I sat,</p>
<p>a train of reﬂection occurred to me, which led me to</p>
<p>consider the effects of what I was now doing. Three</p>
<p>years before I was engaged in the same manner,</p>
<p>and had created a ﬁend whose unparalleled bar-</p>
<p>barity had desolated my heart, and ﬁlled it for ever</p>
<p>with the bitterest remorse. I was now about to form</p>
<p>298</p>
<p>another being, of whose dispositions I was alike</p>
<p>ignorant; she might become ten thousand times</p>
<p>more malignant than her mate, and delight, for its</p>
<p>own sake, in murder and wretchedness. He had</p>
<p>sworn to quit the neighbourhood of man, and hide</p>
<p>himself in deserts; but she had not; and she, who in</p>
<p>all probability was to become a thinking and reason-</p>
<p>ing animal, might refuse to comply with a compact</p>
<p>made before her creation. They might even hate</p>
<p>each other; the creature who already lived loathed</p>
<p>his own deformity, and might he not conceive a</p>
<p>greater abhorence for it when it came before his</p>
<p>eyes in the female form? She also might turn with</p>
<p>disgust from him to the superior beauty of man; she</p>
<p>might quit him, and he be again alone, exasperated</p>
<p>by the fresh provocation of being deserted by one</p>
<p>of his own species.</p>
<p>Even if they were to leave Europe, and inhabit</p>
<p>the deserts of the new world, yet one of the ﬁrst</p>
<p>results of those sympathies for which the dæmon</p>
<p>thirsted would be children, and a race of devils</p>
<p>would be propagated upon the earth, who might</p>
<p>make the very existence of the species of man</p>
<p>a condition precarious and full of terror. Had I a</p>
<p>right, for my own beneﬁt, to inﬂict this curse upon</p>
<p>everlasting generations? I had before been moved</p>
<p>by the sophisms of the being I had created; I had</p>
<p>been struck senseless by his ﬁendish threats: but</p>
<p>now, for the ﬁrst time, the wickedness of my promise</p>
<p>burst upon me; I shuddered to think that future ages</p>
<p>might curse me as their pest, whose selﬁshness</p>
<p>had not hesitated to buy its own peace at the price</p>
<p>perhaps of the existence of the whole human race.</p>
<p>I trembled, and my heart failed within me; when,</p>
<p>on looking up, I saw, by the light of the moon, the</p>
<p>dæmon at the casement. A ghastly grin wrinkled his</p>
<p>lips as he gazed on me, where I sat fulﬁlling the task</p>
<p>which he had allotted to me. Yes, he had followed</p>
<p>me in my travels; he had loitered in forests, hid</p>
<p>himself in caves, or taken refuge in wide and desert</p>
<p>heaths; and he now came to mark my progress,</p>
<p>and claim the fulﬁlment of my promise.</p>
<p>As I looked on him, his countenance expressed</p>
<p>the utmost extent of malice and treachery. I thought</p>
<p>with a sensation of madness on my promise of</p>
<p>creating another like to him, and, trembling with</p>
<p>passion, tore to pieces the thing on which I was en-</p>
<p>gaged. The wretch saw me destroy the creature on</p>
<p>whose future existence he depended for happiness,</p>
<p>and, with a howl of devilish despair and revenge,</p>
<p>withdrew.</p>
<p>I left the room, and, locking the door, made a</p>
<p>solemn vow in my own heart never to resume my</p>
<p>labours; and then, with trembling steps, I sought</p>
<p>my own apartment. I was alone; none were near</p>
<p>me to dissipate the gloom, and relieve me from the</p>
<p>sickening oppression of the most terrible reveries.</p>
<p>Several hours past, and I remained near my</p>
<p>window gazing on the sea; it was almost motion-</p>
<p>less, for the winds were hushed, and all nature</p>
<p>reposed under the eye of the quiet moon. A few</p>
<p>ﬁshing vessels alone specked the water, and now</p>
<p>and then the gentle breeze wafted the sound of</p>
<p>voices, as the ﬁshermen called to one another. I</p>
<p>felt the silence, although I was hardly conscious of</p>
<p>its extreme profundity until my ear was suddenly</p>
<p>arrested by the paddling of oars near the shore,</p>
<p>and a person landed close to my house.</p>
<p>In a few minutes after, I heard the creaking of my</p>
<p>door, as if some one endeavoured to open it softly.</p>
<p>I trembled from head to foot; I felt a presentiment of</p>
<p>who it was, and wished to rouse one of the peasants</p>
<p>who dwelt in a cottage not far from mine; but I</p>
<p>was overcome by the sensation of helplessness,</p>
<p>so often felt in frightful dreams, when you in vain</p>
<p>endeavour to ﬂy from an impending danger, and</p>
<p>was rooted to the spot.</p>
<p>Presently I heard the sound of footsteps along</p>
<p>the passage; the door opened, and the wretch</p>
<p>whom I dreaded appeared. Shutting the door, he</p>
<p>approached me, and said, in a smothered voice —</p>
<p>“You have destroyed the work which you began;</p>
<p>what is it that you intend? Do you dare to break</p>
<p>your promise? I have endured toil and misery: I left</p>
<p>Switzerland with you; I crept along the shores of</p>
<p>the Rhine, among its willow islands, and over the</p>
<p>summits of its hills. I have dwelt many months in</p>
<p>the heaths of England, and among the deserts of</p>
<p>Scotland. I have endured incalculable fatigue, and</p>
<p>cold, and hunger; do you dare destroy my hopes?”</p>
<p>“Begone! I do break my promise; never will I</p>
<p>create another like yourself, equal in deformity and</p>
<p>wickedness.”</p>
<p>“Slave, I before reasoned with you, but you have</p>
<p>proved yourself unworthy of my condescension. Re-</p>
<p>member that I have power; you believe yourself</p>
<p>miserable, but I can make you so wretched that</p>
<p>the light of day will be hateful to you. You are my</p>
<p>creator, but I am your master; — obey!”</p>
<p>“The hour of my weakness is past, and the pe-</p>
<p>riod of your power is arrived. Your threats cannot</p>
<p>move me to do an act of wickedness; but they con-</p>
<p>ﬁrm me in a resolution of not creating you a com-</p>
<p>panion in vice. Shall I, in cool blood, set loose upon</p>
<p>the earth a dæmon, whose delight is in death and</p>
<p>wretchedness. Begone! I am ﬁrm, and your words</p>
<p>will only exasperate my rage.”</p>
<p>The monster saw my determination in my face,</p>
<p>and gnashed his teeth in the impotence of anger.</p>
<p>“Shall each man,” cried he, “ﬁnd a wife for his bos-</p>
<p>om, and each beast have his mate, and I be alone?</p>
<p>I had feelings of affection, and they were requited</p>
<p>by detestation and scorn. Man, you may hate; but</p>
<p>beware! Your hours will pass in dread and misery,</p>
<p>and soon the bolt will fall which must ravish from you</p>
<p>your happiness for ever. Are you to be happy, while</p>
<p>I grovel in the intensity of my wretchedness? You</p>
<p>can blast my other passions; but revenge remains</p>
<p>— revenge, henceforth dearer than light or food! I</p>
<p>may die; but ﬁrst you, my tyrant and tormentor, shall</p>
<p>curse the sun that gazes on your misery. Beware;</p>
<p>for I am fearless, and therefore powerful. I will watch</p>
<p>with the wiliness of a snake, that I may sting with</p>
<p>its venom. Man, you shall repent of the injuries you</p>
<p>inﬂict.”</p>
<p>“Devil, cease; and do not poison the air with</p>
<p>these sounds of malice. I have declared my reso-</p>
<p>lution to you, and I am no coward to bend beneath</p>
<p>words. Leave me; I am inexorable.”</p>
<p>“It is well. I go; but remember, I shall be with</p>
<p>you on your wedding-night.”</p>
<p>I started forward, and exclaimed, “Villain! before</p>
<p>you sign my death-warrant, be sure that you are</p>
<p>yourself safe.”</p>
<p>I would have seized him; but he eluded me,</p>
<p>and quitted the house with precipitation: in a few</p>
<p>moments I saw him in his boat, which shot across</p>
<p>the waters with an arrowy swiftness, and was soon</p>
<p>lost amidst the waves.</p>
<p>All was again silent; but his words rung in my</p>
<p>ears. I burned with rage to pursue the murderer</p>
<p>of my peace, and precipitate him into the ocean. I</p>
<p>walked up and down my room hastily and perturbed,</p>
<p>while my imagination conjured up a thousand im-</p>
<p>ages to torment and sting me. Why had I not fol-</p>
<p>lowed him, and closed with him in mortal strife? But</p>
<p>I had suffered him to depart, and he had directed</p>
<p>his course towards the main land. I shuddered to</p>
<p>think who might be the next victim sacriﬁced to his</p>
<p>insatiate revenge. And then I thought again of his</p>
<p>words — “I will be with you on your wedding-night.”</p>
<p>That then was the period ﬁxed for the fulﬁlment of</p>
<p>my destiny. In that hour I should die, and at once</p>
<p>satisfy and extinguish his malice. The prospect</p>
<p>did not move me to fear; yet when I thought of my</p>
<p>beloved Elizabeth, — of her tears and endless sor-</p>
<p>row, when she should ﬁnd her lover so barbarously</p>
<p>snatched from her, — tears, the ﬁrst I had shed</p>
<p>for many months, streamed from my eyes, and I</p>
<p>resolved not to fall before my enemy without a bitter</p>
<p>struggle.</p>
<p>The night passed away, and the sun rose from</p>
<p>the ocean; my feelings became calmer, if it may be</p>
<p>called calmness, when the violence of rage sinks</p>
<p>into the depths of despair. I left the house, the horrid</p>
<p>scene of the last night’s contention, and walked on</p>
<p>the beach of the sea, which I almost regarded as</p>
<p>an insuperable barrier between me and my fellow-</p>
<p>creatures; nay, a wish that such should prove the</p>
<p>fact stole across me. I desired that I might pass</p>
<p>my life on that barren rock, wearily it is true, but</p>
<p>uninterrupted by any sudden shock of misery. If I</p>
<p>returned, it was to be sacriﬁced, or to see those</p>
<p>whom I most loved die under the grasp of a dæmon</p>
<p>whom I had myself created.</p>
<p>I walked about the isle like a restless spectre,</p>
<p>separated from all it loved, and miserable in the sep-</p>
<p>aration. When it became noon, and the sun rose</p>
<p>higher, I lay down on the grass, and was overpow-</p>
<p>ered by a deep sleep. I had been awake the whole</p>
<p>of the preceding night, my nerves were agitated,</p>
<p>and my eyes inﬂamed by watching and misery. The</p>
<p>sleep into which I now sunk refreshed me; and</p>
<p>when I awoke, I again felt as if I belonged to a race</p>
<p>of human beings like myself, and I began to reﬂect</p>
<p>upon what had passed with greater composure; yet</p>
<p>still the words of the ﬁend rung in my ears like a</p>
<p>death-knell, they appeared like a dream, yet distinct</p>
<p>and oppressive as a reality.</p>
<p>The sun had far descended, and I still sat on the</p>
<p>shore, satisfying my appetite, which had become</p>
<p>ravenous, with an oaten cake, when I saw a ﬁshing-</p>
<p>boat land close to me, and one of the men brought</p>
<p>me a packet; it contained letters from Geneva, and</p>
<p>one from Clerval, entreating me to join him. He</p>
<p>said that nearly a year had elapsed since we had</p>
<p>quitted Switzerland, and France was yet unvisited.</p>
<p>He entreated me, therefore, to leave my solitary</p>
<p>isle, and meet him at Perth, in a week from that</p>
<p>time, when we might arrange the plan of our future</p>
<p>proceedings. This letter in a degree recalled me</p>
<p>to life, and I determined to quit my island at the</p>
<p>expiration of two days.</p>
<p>Yet, before I departed, there was a task to per-</p>
<p>form, on which I shuddered to reﬂect: I must pack</p>
<p>my chemical instruments; and for that purpose I</p>
<p>must enter the room which had been the scene</p>
<p>of my odious work, and I must handle those uten-</p>
<p>sils, the sight of which was sickening to me. The</p>
<p>next morning, at day-break, I summoned sufﬁcient</p>
<p>courage, and unlocked the door of my laboratory.</p>
<p>The remains of the half-ﬁnished creature, whom</p>
<p>I had destroyed, lay scattered on the ﬂoor, and I</p>
<p>almost felt as if I had mangled the living ﬂesh of</p>
<p>a human being. I paused to collect myself, and</p>
<p>then entered the chamber. With trembling hand</p>
<p>I conveyed the instruments out of the room; but</p>
<p>I reﬂected that I ought not to leave the relics of</p>
<p>my work to excite the horror and suspicion of the</p>
<p>peasants, and I accordingly put them into a basket,</p>
<p>with a great quantity of stones, and laying them up,</p>
<p>determined to throw them into the sea that very</p>
<p>night; and in the mean time I sat upon the beach,</p>
<p>employed in cleaning and arranging my chemical</p>
<p>apparatus.</p>
<p>Nothing could be more complete than the al-</p>
<p>teration that had taken place in my feelings since</p>
<p>the night of the appearance of the dæmon. I had</p>
<p>before regarded my promise with a gloomy despair,</p>
<p>as a thing that, with whatever consequences, must</p>
<p>be fulﬁlled; but I now felt as if a ﬁlm had been taken</p>
<p>from before my eyes, and that I, for the ﬁrst time,</p>
<p>saw clearly. The idea of renewing my labours did</p>
<p>not for one instant occur to me; the threat I had</p>
<p>heard weighed on my thoughts, but I did not reﬂect</p>
<p>that a voluntary act of mine could avert it. I had</p>
<p>resolved in my own mind, that to create another</p>
<p>like the ﬁend I had ﬁrst made would be an act of</p>
<p>the basest and most atrocious selﬁshness; and I</p>
<p>banished from my mind every thought that could</p>
<p>lead to a different conclusion.</p>
<p>Between two and three in the morning the moon</p>
<p>rose; and I then, putting my basket aboard a little</p>
<p>skiff, sailed out about four miles from the shore.</p>
<p>The scene was perfectly solitary: a few boats were</p>
<p>returning towards land, but I sailed away from them.</p>
<p>I felt as if I was about the commission of a dreadful</p>
<p>crime, and avoided with shuddering anxiety any en-</p>
<p>counter with my fellow-creatures. At one time the</p>
<p>moon, which had before been clear, was suddenly</p>
<p>overspread by a thick cloud, and I took advantage</p>
<p>of the moment of darkness, and cast my basket</p>
<p>into the sea; I listened to the gurgling sound as it</p>
<p>sunk, and then sailed away from the spot. The sky</p>
<p>became clouded; but the air was pure, although</p>
<p>chilled by the north-east breeze that was then ris-</p>
<p>ing. But it refreshed me, and ﬁlled me with such</p>
<p>agreeable sensations, that I resolved to prolong my</p>
<p>stay on the water, and ﬁxing the rudder in a direct</p>
<p>position, stretched myself at the bottom of the boat.</p>
<p>Clouds hid the moon, every thing was obscure, and</p>
<p>I heard only the sound of the boat, as its keel cut</p>
<p>through the waves; the murmur lulled me, and in a</p>
<p>short time I slept soundly.</p>
<p>I do not know how long I remained in this situ-</p>
<p>ation, but when I awoke I found that the sun had</p>
<p>already mounted considerably. The wind was high,</p>
<p>and the waves continually threatened the safety of</p>
<p>my little skiff. I found that the wind was north-east,</p>
<p>and must have driven me far from the coast from</p>
<p>which I had embarked. I endeavoured to change</p>
<p>my course, but quickly found that if I again made</p>
<p>the attempt the boat would be instantly ﬁlled with</p>
<p>water. Thus situated, my only resource was to drive</p>
<p>before the wind. I confess that I felt a few sensa-</p>
<p>tions of terror. I had no compass with me, and was</p>
<p>so little acquainted with the geography of this part</p>
<p>of the world that the sun was of little beneﬁt to me.</p>
<p>I might be driven into the wide Atlantic, and feel</p>
<p>all the tortures of starvation, or be swallowed up in</p>
<p>the immeasurable waters that roared and buffeted</p>
<p>around me. I had already been out many hours,</p>
<p>and felt the torment of a burning thirst, a prelude to</p>
<p>my other sufferings. I looked on the heavens, which</p>
<p>were covered by clouds that ﬂew before the wind</p>
<p>only to be replaced by others: I looked upon the</p>
<p>sea, it was to be my grave. “Fiend,” I exclaimed,</p>
<p>“your task is already fulﬁlled!” I thought of Elizabeth,</p>
<p>of my father, and of Clerval; and sunk into a reverie,</p>
<p>so despairing and frightful, that even now, when the</p>
<p>scene is on the point of closing before me for ever,</p>
<p>I shudder to reﬂect on it.</p>
<p>Some hours passed thus; but by degrees, as</p>
<p>the sun declined towards the horizon, the wind died</p>
<p>away into a gentle breeze, and the sea became free</p>
<p>from breakers. But these gave place to a heavy</p>
<p>swell; I felt sick, and hardly able to hold the rudder,</p>
<p>when suddenly I saw a line of high land towards the</p>
<p>south.</p>
<p>Almost spent, as I was, by fatigue, and the</p>
<p>dreadful suspense I endured for several hours, this</p>
<p>sudden certainty of life rushed like a ﬂood of warm</p>
<p>joy to my heart, and tears gushed from my eyes.</p>
<p>How mutable are our feelings, and how strange</p>
<p>is that clinging love we have of life even in the ex-</p>
<p>cess of misery! I constructed another sail with a</p>
<p>part of my dress, and eagerly steered my course to-</p>
<p>wards the land. It had a wild and rocky appearance;</p>
<p>but as I approached nearer, I easily perceived the</p>
<p>traces of cultivation. I saw vessels near the shore,</p>
<p>and found myself suddenly transported back to the</p>
<p>neighbourhood of civilized man. I eagerly traced the</p>
<p>windings of the land, and hailed a steeple which I at</p>
<p>length saw issuing from behind a small promontory.</p>
<p>As I was in a state of extreme debility, I resolved</p>
<p>to sail directly towards the town as a place where I</p>
<p>could most easily procure nourishment. Fortunately</p>
<p>I had money with me. As I turned the promontory,</p>
<p>I perceived a small neat town and a good harbour,</p>
<p>which I entered, my heart bounding with joy at my</p>
<p>unexpected escape.</p>
<p>As I was occupied in ﬁxing the boat and arrang-</p>
<p>ing the sails, several people crowded towards the</p>
<p>spot. They seemed very much surprised at my</p>
<p>appearance; but, instead of offering me any assis-</p>
<p>tance, whispered together with gestures that at any</p>
<p>other time might have produced in me a slight sen-</p>
<p>sation of alarm. As it was, I merely remarked that</p>
<p>they spoke English; and I therefore addressed them</p>
<p>in that language: “My good friends,” said I, “will you</p>
<p>be so kind as to tell me the name of this town, and</p>
<p>inform me where I am?”</p>
<p>“You will know that soon enough,” replied a man</p>
<p>with a gruff voice. “May be you are come to a place</p>
<p>that will not prove much to your taste; but you will</p>
<p>not be consulted as to your quarters, I promise you.”</p>
<p>I was exceedingly surprised on receiving so</p>
<p>rude an answer from a stranger; and I was also</p>
<p>disconcerted on perceiving the frowning and angry</p>
<p>countenances of his companions. “Why do you</p>
<p>answer me so roughly?” I replied: “surely it is not</p>
<p>the custom of Englishmen to receive strangers so</p>
<p>inhospitably.”</p>
<p>“I do not know,” said the man, “what the custom</p>
<p>of the English may be; but it is the custom of the</p>
<p>Irish to hate villains.”</p>
<p>While this strange dialogue continued, I per-</p>
<p>ceived the crowd rapidly increase. Their faces ex-</p>
<p>pressed a mixture of curiosity and anger, which an-</p>
<p>noyed, and in some degree alarmed me. I inquired</p>
<p>the way to the inn; but no one replied. I then moved</p>
<p>forward, and a murmuring sound arose from the</p>
<p>crowd as they followed and surrounded me; when</p>
<p>an ill-looking man approaching, tapped me on the</p>
<p>shoulder, and said, “Come, Sir, you must follow me</p>
<p>to Mr. Kirwin’s, to give an account of yourself.”</p>
<p>“Who is Mr. Kirwin? Why am I to give an ac-</p>
<p>count of myself? Is not this a free country?”</p>
<p>“Aye, Sir, free enough for honest folks. Mr. Kir-</p>
<p>win is a magistrate; and you are to give an account</p>
<p>of the death of a gentleman who was found mur-</p>
<p>dered here last night.”</p>
<p>This answer startled me; but I presently recov-</p>
<p>ered myself. I was innocent; that could easily be</p>
<p>proved: accordingly I followed my conductor in si-</p>
<p>lence, and was led to one of the best houses in the</p>
<p>town. I was ready to sink from fatigue and hunger;</p>
<p>but, being surrounded by a crowd, I thought it politic</p>
<p>to rouse all my strength, that no physical debility</p>
<p>might be construed into apprehension or conscious</p>
<p>guilt. Little did I then expect the calamity that was</p>
<p>in a few moments to overwhelm me, and extinguish</p>
<p>in horror and despair all fear of ignominy or death.</p>
<p>I must pause here; for it requires all my forti-</p>
<p>tude to recall the memory of the frightful events</p>
<p>which I am about to relate, in proper detail, to my</p>
<p>recollection.</p>
<p>Chapter IV</p>
<p>I WAS soon introduced into the presence of the mag-</p>
<p>istrate, an old benevolent man, with calm and mild</p>
<p>manners. He looked upon me, however, with some</p>
<p>degree of severity; and then, turning towards my</p>
<p>conductors, he asked who appeared as witnesses</p>
<p>on this occasion.</p>
<p>About half a dozen men came forward; and one</p>
<p>being selected by the magistrate, he deposed, that</p>
<p>he had been out ﬁshing the night before with his</p>
<p>son and brother-in-law, Daniel Nugent, when, about</p>
<p>ten o’clock, they observed a strong northerly blast</p>
<p>rising, and they accordingly put in for port. It was a</p>
<p>317</p>
<p>very dark night, as the moon had not yet risen; they</p>
<p>did not land at the harbour, but, as they had been</p>
<p>accustomed, at a creek about two miles below. He</p>
<p>walked on ﬁrst, carrying a part of the ﬁshing tackle,</p>
<p>and his companions followed him at some distance.</p>
<p>As he was proceeding along the sands, he struck</p>
<p>his foot against something, and fell all his length on</p>
<p>the ground. His companions came up to assist him;</p>
<p>and, by the light of their lantern, they found that he</p>
<p>had fallen on the body of a man, who was to all</p>
<p>appearance dead. Their ﬁrst supposition was, that</p>
<p>it was the corpse of some person who had been</p>
<p>drowned, and was thrown on shore by the waves;</p>
<p>but, upon examination, they found that the clothes</p>
<p>were not wet, and even that the body was not then</p>
<p>cold. They instantly carried it to the cottage of an</p>
<p>old woman near the spot, and endeavoured, but</p>
<p>in vain, to restore it to life. He appeared to be a</p>
<p>handsome young man, about ﬁve and twenty years</p>
<p>of age. He had apparently been strangled; for there</p>
<p>was no sign of any violence, except the black mark</p>
<p>of ﬁngers on his neck.</p>
<p>The ﬁrst part of this deposition did not in the</p>
<p>least interest me; but when the mark of the ﬁn-</p>
<p>gers was mentioned, I remembered the murder of</p>
<p>my brother, and felt myself extremely agitated; my</p>
<p>limbs trembled, and a mist came over my eyes,</p>
<p>which obliged me to lean on a chair for support.</p>
<p>The magistrate observed me with a keen eye, and</p>
<p>of course drew an unfavourable augury from my</p>
<p>manner.</p>
<p>The son conﬁrmed his father’s account: but</p>
<p>when Daniel Nugent was called, he swore positively</p>
<p>that, just before the fall of his companion, he saw</p>
<p>a boat, with a single man in it, at a short distance</p>
<p>from the shore; and, as far as he could judge by the</p>
<p>light of a few stars, it was the same boat in which I</p>
<p>had just landed.</p>
<p>A woman deposed, that she lived near the</p>
<p>beach, and was standing at the door of her cottage,</p>
<p>waiting for the return of the ﬁshermen, about an</p>
<p>hour before she heard of the discovery of the body,</p>
<p>when she saw a boat, with only one man in it, push</p>
<p>off from that part of the shore where the corpse</p>
<p>was afterwards found.</p>
<p>Another woman conﬁrmed the account of the</p>
<p>ﬁshermen having brought the body into her house;</p>
<p>it was not cold. They put it into a bed, and rubbed</p>
<p>it; and Daniel went to the town for an apothecary,</p>
<p>but life was quite gone.</p>
<p>Several other men were examined concerning</p>
<p>my landing; and they agreed, that, with the strong</p>
<p>north wind that had arisen during the night, it was</p>
<p>very probable that I had beaten about for many</p>
<p>hours, and had been obliged to return nearly to the</p>
<p>same spot from which I had departed. Besides,</p>
<p>they observed that it appeared that I had brought</p>
<p>the body from another place, and it was likely, that</p>
<p>as I did not appear to know the shore, I might have</p>
<p>put into the harbour ignorant of the distance of the</p>
<p>town of —— from the place where I had deposited</p>
<p>the corpse.</p>
<p>Mr. Kirwin, on hearing this evidence, desired</p>
<p>that I should be taken into the room where the body</p>
<p>lay for interment that it might be observed what ef-</p>
<p>fect the sight of it would produce upon me. This</p>
<p>idea was probably suggested by the extreme agita-</p>
<p>tion I had exhibited when the mode of the murder</p>
<p>had been described. I was accordingly conducted,</p>
<p>by the magistrate and several other persons, to the</p>
<p>inn. I could not help being struck by the strange</p>
<p>coincidences that had taken place during this event-</p>
<p>ful night; but, knowing that I had been conversing</p>
<p>with several persons in the island I had inhabited</p>
<p>about the time that the body had been found, I was</p>
<p>perfectly tranquil as to the consequences of the</p>
<p>affair.</p>
<p>I entered the room where the corpse lay, and</p>
<p>was led up to the cofﬁn. How can I describe my</p>
<p>sensations on beholding it? I feel yet parched with</p>
<p>horror, nor can I reﬂect on that terrible moment</p>
<p>without shuddering and agony, that faintly reminds</p>
<p>me of the anguish of the recognition. The trial, the</p>
<p>presence of the magistrate and witnesses, passed</p>
<p>like a dream from my memory, when I saw the</p>
<p>lifeless form of Henry Clerval stretched before me. I</p>
<p>gasped for breath; and, throwing myself on the body,</p>
<p>I exclaimed, “Have my murderous machinations</p>
<p>deprived you also, my dearest Henry, of life? Two</p>
<p>I have already destroyed; other victims await their</p>
<p>destiny: but you, Clerval, my friend, my benefactor”</p>
<p>——</p>
<p>The human frame could no longer support the</p>
<p>agonizing suffering that I endured, and I was carried</p>
<p>out of the room in strong convulsions.</p>
<p>A fever succeeded to this. I lay for two months</p>
<p>on the point of death: my ravings, as I afterwards</p>
<p>heard, were frightful; I called myself the murderer</p>
<p>of William, of Justine, and of Clerval. Sometimes</p>
<p>I entreated my attendants to assist me in the de-</p>
<p>struction of the ﬁend by whom I was tormented;</p>
<p>and, at others, I felt the ﬁngers of the monster al-</p>
<p>ready grasping my neck, and screamed aloud with</p>
<p>agony and terror. Fortunately, as I spoke my native</p>
<p>language, Mr. Kirwin alone understood me; but my</p>
<p>gestures and bitter cries were sufﬁcient to affright</p>
<p>the other witnesses.</p>
<p>Why did I not die? More miserable than man</p>
<p>ever was before, why did I not sink into forgetfulness</p>
<p>and rest? Death snatches away many blooming</p>
<p>children, the only hopes of their doating parents:</p>
<p>how many brides and youthful lovers have been</p>
<p>one day in the bloom of health and hope, and the</p>
<p>next a prey for worms and the decay of the tomb!</p>
<p>Of what materials was I made, that I could thus</p>
<p>resist so many shocks, which, like the turning of the</p>
<p>wheel, continually renewed the torture.</p>
<p>But I was doomed to live; and, in two months,</p>
<p>found myself as awaking from a dream, in a prison,</p>
<p>stretched on a wretched bed, surrounded by gaol-</p>
<p>ers, turnkeys, bolts, and all the miserable apparatus</p>
<p>of a dungeon. It was morning, I remember, when</p>
<p>I thus awoke to understanding: I had forgotten the</p>
<p>particulars of what had happened, and only felt as if</p>
<p>some great misfortune had suddenly overwhelmed</p>
<p>me; but when I looked around, and saw the barred</p>
<p>windows, and the squalidness of the room in which</p>
<p>I was, all ﬂashed across my memory, and I groaned</p>
<p>bitterly.</p>
<p>This sound disturbed an old woman who was</p>
<p>sleeping in a chair beside me. She was a hired</p>
<p>nurse, the wife of one of the turnkeys, and her coun-</p>
<p>tenance expressed all those bad qualities which</p>
<p>often characterize that class. The lines of her face</p>
<p>were hard and rude, like that of persons accus-</p>
<p>tomed to see without sympathizing in sights of mis-</p>
<p>ery. Her tone expressed her entire indifference; she</p>
<p>addressed me in English, and the voice struck me</p>
<p>as one that I had heard during my sufferings:</p>
<p>“Are you better now, Sir?” said she.</p>
<p>I replied in the same language, with a feeble</p>
<p>voice, “I believe I am; but if it be all true, if indeed I</p>
<p>did not dream, I am sorry that I am still alive to feel</p>
<p>this misery and horror.”</p>
<p>“For that matter,” replied the old woman, “if you</p>
<p>mean about the gentleman you murdered, I believe</p>
<p>that it were better for you if you were dead, for I</p>
<p>fancy it will go hard with you; but you will be hung</p>
<p>when the next sessions come on. However, that’s</p>
<p>none of my business, I am sent to nurse you, and</p>
<p>get you well; I do my duty with a safe conscience, it</p>
<p>were well if every body did the same.”</p>
<p>I turned with loathing from the woman who</p>
<p>could utter so unfeeling a speech to a person just</p>
<p>saved, on the very edge of death; but I felt languid,</p>
<p>and unable to reﬂect on all that had passed. The</p>
<p>whole series of my life appeared to me as a dream;</p>
<p>I sometimes doubted if indeed it were all true, for it</p>
<p>never presented itself to my mind with the force of</p>
<p>reality.</p>
<p>As the images that ﬂoated before me became</p>
<p>more distinct, I grew feverish; a darkness pressed</p>
<p>around me; no one was near me who soothed me</p>
<p>with the gentle voice of love; no dear hand sup-</p>
<p>ported me. The physician came and prescribed</p>
<p>medicines, and the old woman prepared them for</p>
<p>me; but utter carelessness was visible in the ﬁrst,</p>
<p>and the expression of brutality was strongly marked</p>
<p>in the visage of the second. Who could be inter-</p>
<p>ested in the fate of a murderer, but the hangman</p>
<p>who would gain his fee?</p>
<p>These were my ﬁrst reﬂections; but I soon</p>
<p>learned that Mr. Kirwin had shewn me extreme</p>
<p>kindness. He had caused the best room in the</p>
<p>prison to be prepared for me (wretched indeed was</p>
<p>the best); and it was he who had provided a physi-</p>
<p>cian and a nurse. It is true, he seldom came to see</p>
<p>me; for, although he ardently desired to relieve the</p>
<p>sufferings of every human creature, he did not wish</p>
<p>to be present at the agonies and miserable ravings</p>
<p>of a murderer. He came, therefore, sometimes to</p>
<p>see that I was not neglected; but his visits were</p>
<p>short, and at long intervals.</p>
<p>One day, when I was gradually recovering, I</p>
<p>was seated in a chair, my eyes half open, and my</p>
<p>cheeks livid like those in death, I was overcome by</p>
<p>gloom and misery, and often reﬂected I had better</p>
<p>seek death than remain miserably pent up only to</p>
<p>be let loose in a world replete with wretchedness. At</p>
<p>one time I considered whether I should not declare</p>
<p>myself guilty, and suffer the penalty of the law, less</p>
<p>innocent than poor Justine had been. Such were</p>
<p>my thoughts, when the door of my apartment was</p>
<p>opened, and Mr. Kirwin entered. His countenance</p>
<p>expressed sympathy and compassion; he drew a</p>
<p>chair close to mine, and addressed me in French —</p>
<p>“I fear that this place is very shocking to you;</p>
<p>can I do any thing to make you more comfortable?”</p>
<p>“I thank you; but all that you mention is nothing</p>
<p>to me: on the whole earth there is no comfort which</p>
<p>I am capable of receiving.”</p>
<p>“I know that the sympathy of a stranger can be</p>
<p>but of little relief to one borne down as you are by</p>
<p>so strange a misfortune. But you will, I hope, soon</p>
<p>quit this melancholy abode; for, doubtless, evidence</p>
<p>can easily be brought to free you from the criminal</p>
<p>charge.”</p>
<p>“That is my least concern: I am, by a course</p>
<p>of strange events, become the most miserable of</p>
<p>mortals. Persecuted and tortured as I am and have</p>
<p>been, can death be any evil to me?”</p>
<p>“Nothing indeed could be more unfortunate and</p>
<p>agonizing than the strange chances that have lately</p>
<p>occurred. You were thrown, by some surprising</p>
<p>accident, on this shore, renowned for its hospitality:</p>
<p>seized immediately, and charged with murder. The</p>
<p>ﬁrst sight that was presented to your eyes was the</p>
<p>body of your friend, murdered in so unaccountable</p>
<p>a manner, and placed, as it were, by some ﬁend</p>
<p>across your path.”</p>
<p>As Mr. Kirwin said this, notwithstanding the agi-</p>
<p>tation I endured on this retrospect of my sufferings,</p>
<p>I also felt considerable surprise at the knowledge</p>
<p>he seemed to possess concerning me. I suppose</p>
<p>some astonishment was exhibited in my counte-</p>
<p>nance; for Mr. Kirwin hastened to say —</p>
<p>“It was not until a day or two after your illness</p>
<p>that I thought of examining your dress, that I might</p>
<p>discover some trace by which I could send to your</p>
<p>relations an account of your misfortune and illness.</p>
<p>I found several letters, and, among others, one</p>
<p>which I discovered from its commencement to be</p>
<p>from your father. I instantly wrote to Geneva: nearly</p>
<p>two months have elapsed since the departure of</p>
<p>my letter. — But you are ill; even now you tremble:</p>
<p>you are unﬁt for agitation of any kind.”</p>
<p>“This suspense is a thousand times worse than</p>
<p>the most horrible event: tell me what new scene of</p>
<p>death has been acted, and whose murder I am now</p>
<p>to lament.”</p>
<p>“Your family is perfectly well,” said Mr. Kirwin,</p>
<p>with gentleness; “and some one, a friend, is come</p>
<p>to visit you.”</p>
<p>I know not by what chain of thought the idea</p>
<p>presented itself, but it instantly darted into my mind</p>
<p>that the murderer had come to mock at my misery,</p>
<p>and taunt me with the death of Clerval, as a new</p>
<p>incitement for me to comply with his hellish desires.</p>
<p>I put my hand before my eyes, and cried out in</p>
<p>agony —</p>
<p>“Oh! take him away! I cannot see him; for God’s</p>
<p>sake, do not let him enter!”</p>
<p>Mr. Kirwin regarded me with a troubled counte-</p>
<p>nance. He could not help regarding my exclamation</p>
<p>as a presumption of my guilt, and said, in rather a</p>
<p>severe tone —</p>
<p>“I should have thought, young man, that the</p>
<p>presence of your father would have been welcome,</p>
<p>instead of inspiring such violent repugnance.”</p>
<p>“My father!” cried I, while every feature and</p>
<p>every muscle was relaxed from anguish to pleasure.</p>
<p>“Is my father, indeed, come? How kind, how very</p>
<p>kind. But where is he, why does he not hasten to</p>
<p>me?”</p>
<p>My change of manner surprised and pleased</p>
<p>the magistrate; perhaps he thought that my former</p>
<p>exclamation was a momentary return of delirium,</p>
<p>and now he instantly resumed his former benevo-</p>
<p>lence. He rose, and quitted the room with my nurse,</p>
<p>and in a moment my father entered it.</p>
<p>Nothing, at this moment, could have given me</p>
<p>greater pleasure than the arrival of my father. I</p>
<p>stretched out my hand to him, and cried —</p>
<p>“Are you then safe — and Elizabeth — and</p>
<p>Ernest?”</p>
<p>My father calmed me with assurances of their</p>
<p>welfare, and endeavoured, by dwelling on these</p>
<p>subjects so interesting to my heart, to raise my</p>
<p>desponding spirits; but he soon felt that a prison</p>
<p>cannot be the abode of cheerfulness. “What a place</p>
<p>is this that you inhabit, my son!” said he, looking</p>
<p>mournfully at the barred windows, and wretched</p>
<p>appearance of the room. “You travelled to seek</p>
<p>happiness, but a fatality seems to pursue you. And</p>
<p>poor Clerval — ”</p>
<p>The name of my unfortunate and murdered</p>
<p>friend was an agitation too great to be endured</p>
<p>in my weak state; I shed tears.</p>
<p>“Alas! yes, my father,” replied I; “some destiny</p>
<p>of the most horrible kind hangs over me, and I must</p>
<p>live to fulﬁl it, or surely I should have died on the</p>
<p>cofﬁn of Henry.”</p>
<p>We were not allowed to converse for any length</p>
<p>of time, for the precarious state of my health ren-</p>
<p>dered every precaution necessary that could insure</p>
<p>tranquillity. Mr. Kirwin came in, and insisted that my</p>
<p>strength should not be exhausted by too much exer-</p>
<p>tion. But the appearance of my father was to me like</p>
<p>that of my good angel, and I gradually recovered</p>
<p>my health.</p>
<p>As my sickness quitted me, I was absorbed by</p>
<p>a gloomy and black melancholy, that nothing could</p>
<p>dissipate. The image of Clerval was for ever be-</p>
<p>fore me, ghastly and murdered. More than once</p>
<p>the agitation into which these reﬂections threw me</p>
<p>made my friends dread a dangerous relapse. Alas!</p>
<p>why did they preserve so miserable and detested</p>
<p>a life? It was surely that I might fulﬁl my destiny,</p>
<p>which is now drawing to a close. Soon, oh, very</p>
<p>soon, will death extinguish these throbbings, and</p>
<p>relieve me from the mighty weight of anguish that</p>
<p>bears me to the dust; and, in executing the award</p>
<p>of justice, I shall also sink to rest. Then the ap-</p>
<p>pearance of death was distant, although the wish</p>
<p>was ever present to my thoughts; and I often sat</p>
<p>for hours motionless and speechless, wishing for</p>
<p>some mighty revolution that might bury me and my</p>
<p>destroyer in its ruins.</p>
<p>The season of the assizes approached. I had</p>
<p>already been three months in prison; and although I</p>
<p>was still weak, and in continual danger of a relapse,</p>
<p>I was obliged to travel nearly a hundred miles to the</p>
<p>county-town, where the court was held. Mr. Kirwin</p>
<p>charged himself with every care of collecting wit-</p>
<p>nesses, and arranging my defence. I was spared</p>
<p>the disgrace of appearing publicly as a criminal, as</p>
<p>the case was not brought before the court that de-</p>
<p>cides on life and death. The grand jury rejected the</p>
<p>bill, on its being proved that I was on the Orkney</p>
<p>Islands at the hour the body of my friend was found,</p>
<p>and a fortnight after my removal I was liberated</p>
<p>from prison.</p>
<p>My father was enraptured on ﬁnding me freed</p>
<p>from the vexations of a criminal charge, that I was</p>
<p>again allowed to breathe the fresh atmosphere, and</p>
<p>allowed to return to my native country. I did not par-</p>
<p>ticipate in these feelings; for to me the walls of a</p>
<p>dungeon or a palace were alike hateful. The cup</p>
<p>of life was poisoned for ever; and although the sun</p>
<p>shone upon me, as upon the happy and gay of</p>
<p>heart, I saw around me nothing but a dense and</p>
<p>frightful darkness, penetrated by no light but the</p>
<p>glimmer of two eyes that glared upon me. Some-</p>
<p>times they were the expressive eyes of Henry, lan-</p>
<p>guishing in death, the dark orbs nearly covered</p>
<p>by the lids, and the long black lashes that fringed</p>
<p>them; sometimes it was the watery clouded eyes of</p>
<p>the monster, as I ﬁrst saw them in my chamber at</p>
<p>Ingolstadt.</p>
<p>My father tried to awaken in me the feelings</p>
<p>of affection. He talked of Geneva, which I should</p>
<p>soon visit — of Elizabeth, and Ernest; but these</p>
<p>words only drew deep groans from me. Sometimes,</p>
<p>indeed, I felt a wish for happiness; and thought,</p>
<p>with melancholy delight, of my beloved cousin; or</p>
<p>longed, with a devouring maladie du pays, to see</p>
<p>once more the blue lake and rapid Rhone, that had</p>
<p>been so dear to me in early childhood: but my gen-</p>
<p>eral state of feeling was a torpor, in which a prison</p>
<p>was as welcome a residence as the divinest scene</p>
<p>in nature; and these ﬁts were seldom interrupted,</p>
<p>but by paroxysms of anguish and despair. At these</p>
<p>moments I often endeavoured to put an end to the</p>
<p>existence I loathed; and it required unceasing atten-</p>
<p>dance and vigilance to restrain me from committing</p>
<p>some dreadful act of violence.</p>
<p>I remember, as I quitted the prison, I heard</p>
<p>one of the men say, “He may be innocent of the</p>
<p>murder, but he has certainly a bad conscience.”</p>
<p>These words struck me. A bad conscience! yes,</p>
<p>surely I had one. William, Justine, and Clerval, had</p>
<p>died through my infernal machinations; “And whose</p>
<p>death,” cried I, “is to ﬁnish the tragedy? Ah! my</p>
<p>father, do not remain in this wretched country; take</p>
<p>me where I may forget myself, my existence, and</p>
<p>all the world.”</p>
<p>My father easily acceded to my desire; and,</p>
<p>after having taken leave of Mr. Kirwin, we hastened</p>
<p>to Dublin. I felt as if I was relieved from a heavy</p>
<p>weight, when the packet sailed with a fair wind from</p>
<p>Ireland, and I had quitted for ever the country which</p>
<p>had been to me the scene of so much misery.</p>
<p>It was midnight. My father slept in the cabin; and</p>
<p>I lay on the deck, looking at the stars, and listening</p>
<p>to the dashing of the waves. I hailed the darkness</p>
<p>that shut Ireland from my sight, and my pulse beat</p>
<p>with a feverish joy, when I reﬂected that I should</p>
<p>soon see Geneva. The past appeared to me in the</p>
<p>light of a frightful dream; yet the vessel in which I</p>
<p>was, the wind that blew me from the detested shore</p>
<p>of Ireland, and the sea which surrounded me, told</p>
<p>me too forcibly that I was deceived by no vision,</p>
<p>and that Clerval, my friend and dearest companion,</p>
<p>had fallen a victim to me and the monster of my</p>
<p>creation. I repassed, in my memory, my whole life;</p>
<p>my quiet happiness while residing with my family in</p>
<p>Geneva, the death of my mother, and my departure</p>
<p>for Ingolstadt. I remembered shuddering at the mad</p>
<p>enthusiasm that hurried me on to the creation of</p>
<p>my hideous enemy, and I called to mind the night</p>
<p>during which he ﬁrst lived. I was unable to pursue</p>
<p>the train of thought; a thousand feelings pressed</p>
<p>upon me, and I wept bitterly.</p>
<p>Ever since my recovery from the fever I had</p>
<p>been in the custom of taking every night a small</p>
<p>quantity of laudanum; for it was by means of this</p>
<p>drug only that I was enabled to gain the rest nec-</p>
<p>essary for the preservation of life. Oppressed by</p>
<p>the recollection of my various misfortunes, I now</p>
<p>took a double dose, and soon slept profoundly. But</p>
<p>sleep did not afford me respite from thought and</p>
<p>misery; my dreams presented a thousand objects</p>
<p>that scared me. Towards morning I was possessed</p>
<p>by a kind of night-mare; I felt the ﬁend’s grasp in my</p>
<p>neck, and could not free myself from it; groans and</p>
<p>cries rung in my ears. My father, who was watching</p>
<p>over me, perceiving my restlessness, awoke me,</p>
<p>and pointed to the port of Holyhead, which we were</p>
<p>now entering.</p>
<p>Chapter V</p>
<p>WE had resolved not to go to London, but to cross</p>
<p>the country to Portsmouth, and thence to embark</p>
<p>for Havre. I preferred this plan principally because</p>
<p>I dreaded to see again those places in which I</p>
<p>had enjoyed a few moments of tranquillity with my</p>
<p>beloved Clerval. I thought with horror of seeing</p>
<p>again those persons whom we had been accus-</p>
<p>tomed to visit together, and who might make in-</p>
<p>quiries concerning an event, the very remembrance</p>
<p>of which made me again feel the pang I endured</p>
<p>when I gazed on his lifeless form in the inn at ——.</p>
<p>338</p>
<p>As for my father, his desires and exertions were</p>
<p>bounded to the again seeing me restored to health</p>
<p>and peace of mind. His tenderness and attentions</p>
<p>were unremitting; my grief and gloom was obstinate,</p>
<p>but he would not despair. Sometimes he thought</p>
<p>that I felt deeply the degradation of being obliged</p>
<p>to answer a charge of murder, and he endeavoured</p>
<p>to prove to me the futility of pride.</p>
<p>“Alas! my father,” said I, “how little do you know</p>
<p>me. Human beings, their feelings and passions,</p>
<p>would indeed be degraded, if such a wretch as I</p>
