<p>felt pride. Justine, poor unhappy Justine, was as</p>
<p>innocent as I, and she suffered the same charge;</p>
<p>she died for it; and I am the cause of this — I</p>
<p>murdered her. William, Justine, and Henry — they</p>
<p>all died by my hands.”</p>
<p>My father had often, during my imprisonment,</p>
<p>heard me make the same assertion; when I thus</p>
<p>accused myself, he sometimes seemed to desire</p>
<p>an explanation, and at others he appeared to con-</p>
<p>sider it as caused by delirium, and that, during my</p>
<p>illness, some idea of this kind had presented itself</p>
<p>to my imagination, the remembrance of which I pre-</p>
<p>served in my convalescence. I avoided explanation,</p>
<p>and maintained a continual silence concerning the</p>
<p>wretch I had created. I had a feeling that I should</p>
<p>be supposed mad, and this for ever chained my</p>
<p>tongue, when I would have given the whole world</p>
<p>to have conﬁded the fatal secret.</p>
<p>Upon this occasion my father said, with an</p>
<p>expression of unbounded wonder, “What do you</p>
<p>mean, Victor? are you mad? My dear son, I entreat</p>
<p>you never to make such an assertion again.”</p>
<p>“I am not mad,” I cried energetically; “the sun</p>
<p>and the heavens, who have viewed my operations,</p>
<p>can bear witness of my truth. I am the assassin</p>
<p>of those most innocent victims; they died by my</p>
<p>machinations. A thousand times would I have shed</p>
<p>my own blood, drop by drop, to have saved their</p>
<p>lives; but I could not, my father, indeed I could not</p>
<p>sacriﬁce the whole human race.”</p>
<p>The conclusion of this speech convinced my fa-</p>
<p>ther that my ideas were deranged, and he instantly</p>
<p>changed the subject of our conversation, and en-</p>
<p>deavoured to alter the course of my thoughts. He</p>
<p>wished as much as possible to obliterate the mem-</p>
<p>ory of the scenes that had taken place in Ireland,</p>
<p>and never alluded to them, or suffered me to speak</p>
<p>of my misfortunes.</p>
<p>As time passed away I became more calm: mis-</p>
<p>ery had her dwelling in my heart, but I no longer</p>
<p>talked in the same incoherent manner of my own</p>
<p>crimes; sufﬁcient for me was the consciousness</p>
<p>of them. By the utmost self-violence, I curbed the</p>
<p>imperious voice of wretchedness, which sometimes</p>
<p>desired to declare itself to the whole world; and my</p>
<p>manners were calmer and more composed than</p>
<p>they had ever been since my journey to the sea of</p>
<p>ice.</p>
<p>We arrived at Havre on the 8th of May, and</p>
<p>instantly proceeded to Paris, where my father had</p>
<p>some business which detained us a few weeks.</p>
<p>In this city, I received the following letter from</p>
<p>Elizabeth:—</p>
<p>“To VICTOR FRANKENSTEIN.</p>
<p>“MY DEAREST FRIEND,</p>
<p>“It gave me the greatest pleasure to receive</p>
<p>a letter from my uncle dated at Paris; you are no</p>
<p>longer at a formidable distance, and I may hope to</p>
<p>see you in less than a fortnight. My poor cousin,</p>
<p>how much you must have suffered! I expect to see</p>
<p>you looking even more ill than when you quitted</p>
<p>Geneva. This winter has been passed most miser-</p>
<p>ably, tortured as I have been by anxious suspense;</p>
<p>yet I hope to see peace in your countenance, and</p>
<p>to ﬁnd that your heart is not totally devoid of comfort</p>
<p>and tranquillity.</p>
<p>“Yet I fear that the same feelings now exist that</p>
<p>made you so miserable a year ago, even perhaps</p>
<p>augmented by time. I would not disturb you at</p>
<p>this period, when so many misfortunes weigh upon</p>
<p>you; but a conversation that I had with my uncle</p>
<p>previous to his departure renders some explanation</p>
<p>necessary before we meet.</p>
<p>“Explanation! you may possibly say; what can</p>
<p>Elizabeth have to explain? If you really say this, my</p>
<p>questions are answered, and I have no more to do</p>
<p>than to sign myself your affectionate cousin. But</p>
<p>you are distant from me, and it is possible that you</p>
<p>may dread, and yet be pleased with this explana-</p>
<p>tion; and, in a probability of this being the case, I</p>
<p>dare not any longer postpone writing what, during</p>
<p>your absence, I have often wished to express to</p>
<p>you, but have never had the courage to begin.</p>
<p>“You well know, Victor, that our union had been</p>
<p>the favourite plan of your parents ever since our</p>
<p>infancy. We were told this when young, and taught</p>
<p>to look forward to it as an event that would certainly</p>
<p>take place. We were affectionate playfellows during</p>
<p>childhood, and, I believe, dear and valued friends to</p>
<p>one another as we grew older. But as brother and</p>
<p>sister often entertain a lively affection towards each</p>
<p>other, without desiring a more intimate union, may</p>
<p>not such also be our case? Tell me, dearest Victor.</p>
<p>Answer me, I conjure you, by our mutual happiness,</p>
<p>with simple truth — Do you not love another?</p>
<p>“You have travelled; you have spent several</p>
<p>years of your life at Ingolstadt; and I confess to</p>
<p>you, my friend, that when I saw you last autumn so</p>
<p>unhappy, ﬂying to solitude, from the society of every</p>
<p>creature, I could not help supposing that you might</p>
<p>regret our connexion, and believe yourself bound in</p>
<p>honour to fulﬁl the wishes of your parents, although</p>
<p>they opposed themselves to your inclinations. But</p>
<p>this is false reasoning. I confess to you, my cousin,</p>
<p>that I love you, and that in my airy dreams of futurity</p>
<p>you have been my constant friend and compan-</p>
<p>ion. But it is your happiness I desire as well as</p>
<p>my own, when I declare to you, that our marriage</p>
<p>would render me eternally miserable, unless it were</p>
<p>the dictate of your own free choice. Even now I</p>
<p>weep to think, that, borne down as you are by the</p>
<p>cruelest misfortunes, you may stiﬂe, by the word</p>
<p>honour, all hope of that love and happiness which</p>
<p>would alone restore you to yourself. I, who have</p>
<p>so interested an affection for you, may increase</p>
<p>your miseries ten-fold, by being an obstacle to your</p>
<p>wishes. Ah, Victor, be assured that your cousin</p>
<p>and playmate has too sincere a love for you not to</p>
<p>be made miserable by this supposition. Be happy,</p>
<p>my friend; and if you obey me in this one request,</p>
<p>remain satisﬁed that nothing on earth will have the</p>
<p>power to interrupt my tranquillity.</p>
<p>“Do not let this letter disturb you; do not answer</p>
<p>it to-morrow, or the next day, or even until you come,</p>
<p>if it will give you pain. My uncle will send me news</p>
<p>of your health; and if I see but one smile on your</p>
<p>lips when we meet, occasioned by this or any other</p>
<p>exertion of mine, I shall need no other happiness.</p>
<p>“ELIZABETH LAVENZA.</p>
<p>“Geneva, May 18th, 17— .”</p>
<p>This letter revived in my memory what I had</p>
<p>before forgotten, the threat of the ﬁend — “I will</p>
<p>be with you on your wedding-night!” Such was my</p>
<p>sentence, and on that night would the dæmon em-</p>
<p>ploy every art to destroy me, and tear me from the</p>
<p>glimpse of happiness which promised partly to con-</p>
<p>sole my sufferings. On that night he had determined</p>
<p>to consummate his crimes by my death. Well, be</p>
<p>it so; a deadly struggle would then assuredly take</p>
<p>place, in which if he was victorious, I should be at</p>
<p>peace, and his power over me be at an end. If he</p>
<p>were vanquished, I should be a free man. Alas!</p>
<p>what freedom? such as the peasant enjoys when</p>
<p>his family have been massacred before his eyes,</p>
<p>his cottage burnt, his lands laid waste, and he is</p>
<p>turned adrift, homeless, pennyless, and alone, but</p>
<p>free. Such would be my liberty, except that in my</p>
<p>Elizabeth I possessed a treasure; alas! balanced</p>
<p>by those horrors of remorse and guilt, which would</p>
<p>pursue me until death.</p>
<p>Sweet and beloved Elizabeth! I read and re-</p>
<p>read her letter, and some softened feelings stole</p>
<p>into my heart, and dared to whisper paradisaical</p>
<p>dreams of love and joy; but the apple was already</p>
<p>eaten, and the angel’s arm bared to drive me from</p>
<p>all hope. Yet I would die to make her happy. If the</p>
<p>monster executed his threat, death was inevitable;</p>
<p>yet, again, I considered whether my marriage would</p>
<p>hasten my fate. My destruction might indeed arrive</p>
<p>a few months sooner; but if my torturer should sus-</p>
<p>pect that I postponed it, inﬂuenced by his menaces,</p>
<p>he would surely ﬁnd other, and perhaps more dread-</p>
<p>ful means of revenge. He had vowed to be with me</p>
<p>on my wedding-night, yet he did not consider that</p>
<p>threat as binding him to peace in the mean time; for,</p>
<p>as if to shew me that he was not yet satiated with</p>
<p>blood, he had murdered Clerval immediately after</p>
<p>the enunciation of his threats. I resolved, therefore,</p>
<p>that if my immediate union with my cousin would</p>
<p>conduce either to her’s or my father’s happiness,</p>
<p>my adversary’s designs against my life should not</p>
<p>retard it a single hour.</p>
<p>In this state of mind I wrote to Elizabeth. My</p>
<p>letter was calm and affectionate. “I fear, my beloved</p>
<p>girl,” I said, “little happiness remains for us on earth;</p>
<p>yet all that I may one day enjoy is concentered in</p>
<p>you. Chase away your idle fears; to you alone do</p>
<p>I consecrate my life, and my endeavours for con-</p>
<p>tentment. I have one secret, Elizabeth, a dreadful</p>
<p>one; when revealed to you, it will chill your frame</p>
<p>with horror, and then, far from being surprised at</p>
<p>my misery, you will only wonder that I survive what</p>
<p>I have endured. I will conﬁde this tale of misery and</p>
<p>terror to you the day after our marriage shall take</p>
<p>place; for, my sweet cousin, there must be perfect</p>
<p>conﬁdence between us. But until then, I conjure</p>
<p>you, do not mention or allude to it. This I most</p>
<p>earnestly entreat, and I know you will comply.”</p>
<p>In about a week after the arrival of Elizabeth’s</p>
<p>letter, we returned to Geneva. My cousin welcomed</p>
<p>me with warm affection; yet tears were in her eyes,</p>
<p>as she beheld my emaciated frame and feverish</p>
<p>cheeks. I saw a change in her also. She was</p>
<p>thinner, and had lost much of that heavenly vivacity</p>
<p>that had before charmed me; but her gentleness,</p>
<p>and soft looks of compassion, made her a more ﬁt</p>
<p>companion for one blasted and miserable as I was.</p>
<p>The tranquillity which I now enjoyed did not en-</p>
<p>dure. Memory brought madness with it; and when</p>
<p>I thought on what had passed, a real insanity pos-</p>
<p>sessed me; sometimes I was furious, and burnt</p>
<p>with rage, sometimes low and despondent. I nei-</p>
<p>ther spoke or looked, but sat motionless, bewildered</p>
<p>by the multitude of miseries that overcame me.</p>
<p>Elizabeth alone had the power to draw me from</p>
<p>these ﬁts; her gentle voice would soothe me when</p>
<p>transported by passion, and inspire me with hu-</p>
<p>man feelings when sunk in torpor. She wept with</p>
<p>me, and for me. When reason returned, she would</p>
<p>remonstrate, and endeavour to inspire me with res-</p>
<p>ignation. Ah! it is well for the unfortunate to be</p>
<p>resigned, but for the guilty there is no peace. The</p>
<p>agonies of remorse poison the luxury there is oth-</p>
<p>erwise sometimes found in indulging the excess of</p>
<p>grief.</p>
<p>Soon after my arrival my father spoke of my</p>
<p>immediate marriage with my cousin. I remained</p>
<p>silent.</p>
<p>“Have you, then, some other attachment?”</p>
<p>“None on earth. I love Elizabeth, and look for-</p>
<p>ward to our union with delight. Let the day therefore</p>
<p>be ﬁxed; and on it I will consecrate myself, in life or</p>
<p>death, to the happiness of my cousin.”</p>
<p>“My dear Victor, do not speak thus. Heavy mis-</p>
<p>fortunes have befallen us; but let us only cling closer</p>
<p>to what remains, and transfer our love for those</p>
<p>whom we have lost to those who yet live. Our circle</p>
<p>will be small, but bound close by the ties of affec-</p>
<p>tion and mutual misfortune. And when time shall</p>
<p>have softened your despair, new and dear objects</p>
<p>of care will be born to replace those of whom we</p>
<p>have been so cruelly deprived.”</p>
<p>Such were the lessons of my father. But to</p>
<p>me the remembrance of the threat returned: nor</p>
<p>can you wonder, that, omnipotent as the ﬁend had</p>
<p>yet been in his deeds of blood, I should almost</p>
<p>regard him as invincible; and that when he had pro-</p>
<p>nounced the words, “I shall be with you on your</p>
<p>wedding-night,” I should regard the threatened fate</p>
<p>as unavoidable. But death was no evil to me, if</p>
<p>the loss of Elizabeth were balanced with it; and I</p>
<p>therefore, with a contented and even cheerful coun-</p>
<p>tenance, agreed with my father, that if my cousin</p>
<p>would consent, the ceremony should take place in</p>
<p>ten days, and thus put, as I imagined, the seal to</p>
<p>my fate.</p>
<p>Great God! if for one instant I had thought what</p>
<p>might be the hellish intention of my ﬁendish adver-</p>
<p>sary, I would rather have banished myself for ever</p>
<p>from my native country, and wandered a friendless</p>
<p>outcast over the earth, than have consented to this</p>
<p>miserable marriage. But, as if possessed of magic</p>
<p>powers, the monster had blinded me to his real in-</p>
<p>tentions; and when I thought that I prepared only</p>
<p>my own death, I hastened that of a far dearer victim.</p>
<p>As the period ﬁxed for our marriage drew nearer,</p>
<p>whether from cowardice or a prophetic feeling, I</p>
<p>felt my heart sink within me. But I concealed my</p>
<p>feelings by an appearance of hilarity, that brought</p>
<p>smiles and joy to the countenance of my father,</p>
<p>but hardly deceived the ever-watchful and nicer</p>
<p>eye of Elizabeth. She looked forward to our union</p>
<p>with placid contentment, not unmingled with a little</p>
<p>fear, which past misfortunes had impressed, that</p>
<p>what now appeared certain and tangible happiness,</p>
<p>might soon dissipate into an airy dream, and leave</p>
<p>no trace but deep and everlasting regret.</p>
<p>Preparations were made for the event; congrat-</p>
<p>ulatory visits were received; and all wore a smiling</p>
<p>appearance. I shut up, as well as I could, in my</p>
<p>own heart the anxiety that preyed there, and en-</p>
<p>tered with seeming earnestness into the plans of</p>
<p>my father, although they might only serve as the</p>
<p>decorations of my tragedy. A house was purchased</p>
<p>for us near Cologny, by which we should enjoy the</p>
<p>pleasures of the country, and yet be so near Geneva</p>
<p>as to see my father every day; who would still reside</p>
<p>within the walls, for the beneﬁt of Ernest, that he</p>
<p>might follow his studies at the schools.</p>
<p>In the mean time I took every precaution to de-</p>
<p>fend my person, in case the ﬁend should openly</p>
<p>attack me. I carried pistols and a dagger constantly</p>
<p>about me, and was ever on the watch to prevent ar-</p>
<p>tiﬁce; and by these means gained a greater degree</p>
<p>of tranquillity. Indeed, as the period approached,</p>
<p>the threat appeared more as a delusion, not to</p>
<p>be regarded as worthy to disturb my peace, while</p>
<p>the happiness I hoped for in my marriage wore a</p>
<p>greater appearance of certainty, as the day ﬁxed for</p>
<p>its solemnization drew nearer, and I heard it contin-</p>
<p>ually spoken of as an occurrence which no accident</p>
<p>could possibly prevent.</p>
<p>Elizabeth seemed happy; my tranquil dem-</p>
<p>eanour contributed greatly to calm her mind. But on</p>
<p>the day that was to fulﬁl my wishes and my destiny,</p>
<p>she was melancholy, and a presentiment of evil</p>
<p>pervaded her; and perhaps also she thought of the</p>
<p>dreadful secret, which I had promised to reveal to</p>
<p>her the following day. My father was in the mean</p>
<p>time overjoyed, and, in the bustle of preparation,</p>
<p>only observed in the melancholy of his niece the</p>
<p>difﬁdence of a bride.</p>
<p>After the ceremony was performed, a large</p>
<p>party assembled at my father’s; but it was agreed</p>
<p>that Elizabeth and I should pass the afternoon and</p>
<p>night at Evian, and return to Cologny the next morn-</p>
<p>ing. As the day was fair, and the wind favourable,</p>
<p>we resolved to go by water.</p>
<p>Those were the last moments of my life dur-</p>
<p>ing which I enjoyed the feeling of happiness. We</p>
<p>passed rapidly along: the sun was hot, but we were</p>
<p>sheltered from its rays by a kind of canopy, while we</p>
<p>enjoyed the beauty of the scene, sometimes on one</p>
<p>side of the lake, where we saw Mont Salêve, the</p>
<p>pleasant banks of Montalêgre, and at a distance,</p>
<p>surmounting all, the beautiful Mont Blânc, and the</p>
<p>assemblage of snowy mountains that in vain en-</p>
<p>deavour to emulate her; sometimes coasting the</p>
<p>opposite banks, we saw the mighty Jura opposing</p>
<p>its dark side to the ambition that would quit its na-</p>
<p>tive country, and an almost insurmountable barrier</p>
<p>to the invader who should wish to enslave it.</p>
<p>I took the hand of Elizabeth: “You are sorrowful,</p>
<p>my love. Ah! if you knew what I have suffered, and</p>
<p>what I may yet endure, you would endeavour to let</p>
<p>me taste the quiet, and freedom from despair, that</p>
<p>this one day at least permits me to enjoy.”</p>
<p>“Be happy, my dear Victor,” replied Elizabeth;</p>
<p>“there is, I hope, nothing to distress you; and be</p>
<p>assured that if a lively joy is not painted in my face,</p>
<p>my heart is contented. Something whispers to me</p>
<p>not to depend too much on the prospect that is</p>
<p>opened before us; but I will not listen to such a</p>
<p>sinister voice. Observe how fast we move along,</p>
<p>and how the clouds which sometimes obscure, and</p>
<p>sometimes rise above the dome of Mont Blânc,</p>
<p>render this scene of beauty still more interesting.</p>
<p>Look also at the innumerable ﬁsh that are swimming</p>
<p>in the clear waters, where we can distinguish every</p>
<p>pebble that lies at the bottom. What a divine day!</p>
<p>how happy and serene all nature appears!”</p>
<p>Thus Elizabeth endeavoured to divert her th-</p>
<p>oughts and mine from all reﬂection upon melan-</p>
<p>choly subjects. But her temper was ﬂuctuating; joy</p>
<p>for a few instants shone in her eyes, but it continu-</p>
<p>ally gave place to distraction and reverie.</p>
<p>The sun sunk lower in the heavens; we passed</p>
<p>the river Drance, and observed its path through the</p>
<p>chasms of the higher, and the glens of the lower</p>
<p>hills. The Alps here come closer to the lake, and we</p>
<p>approached the amphitheatre of mountains which</p>
<p>forms its eastern boundary. The spire of Evian</p>
<p>shone under the woods that surrounded it, and the</p>
<p>range of mountain above mountain by which it was</p>
<p>overhung.</p>
<p>The wind, which had hitherto carried us along</p>
<p>with amazing rapidity, sunk at sunset to a light</p>
<p>breeze; the soft air just rufﬂed the water, and</p>
<p>caused a pleasant motion among the trees as we</p>
<p>approached the shore, from which it wafted the</p>
<p>most delightful scent of ﬂowers and hay. The sun</p>
<p>sunk beneath the horizon as we landed; and as</p>
<p>I touched the shore, I felt those cares and fears</p>
<p>revive, which soon were to clasp me, and cling to</p>
<p>me for ever.</p>
<p>Chapter VI</p>
<p>IT was eight o’clock when we landed; we walked for</p>
<p>a short time on the shore, enjoying the transitory</p>
<p>light, and then retired to the inn, and contemplated</p>
<p>the lovely scene of waters, woods, and mountains,</p>
<p>obscured in darkness, yet still displaying their black</p>
<p>outlines.</p>
<p>The wind, which had fallen in the south, now</p>
<p>rose with great violence in the west. The moon</p>
<p>had reached her summit in the heavens, and was</p>
<p>beginning to descend; the clouds swept across it</p>
<p>swifter than the ﬂight of the vulture, and dimmed</p>
<p>her rays, while the lake reﬂected the scene of the</p>
<p>357</p>
<p>busy heavens, rendered still busier by the restless</p>
<p>waves that were beginning to rise. Suddenly a</p>
<p>heavy storm of rain descended.</p>
<p>I had been calm during the day; but so soon as</p>
<p>night obscured the shapes of objects, a thousand</p>
<p>fears arose in my mind. I was anxious and watchful,</p>
<p>while my right hand grasped a pistol which was</p>
<p>hidden in my bosom; every sound terriﬁed me; but</p>
<p>I resolved that I would sell my life dearly, and not</p>
<p>relax the impending conﬂict until my own life, or that</p>
<p>of my adversary, were extinguished.</p>
<p>Elizabeth observed my agitation for some time</p>
<p>in timid and fearful silence; at length she said,</p>
<p>“What is it that agitates you, my dear Victor? What</p>
<p>is it you fear?”</p>
<p>“Oh! peace, peace, my love,” replied I, “this</p>
<p>night, and all will be safe: but this night is dreadful,</p>
<p>very dreadful.”</p>
<p>I passed an hour in this state of mind, when</p>
<p>suddenly I reﬂected how dreadful the combat which</p>
<p>I momentarily expected would be to my wife, and</p>
<p>I earnestly entreated her to retire, resolving not to</p>
<p>join her until I had obtained some knowledge as to</p>
<p>the situation of my enemy.</p>
<p>She left me, and I continued some time walking</p>
<p>up and down the passages of the house, and in-</p>
<p>specting every corner that might afford a retreat to</p>
<p>my adversary. But I discovered no trace of him, and</p>
<p>was beginning to conjecture that some fortunate</p>
<p>chance had intervened to prevent the execution of</p>
<p>his menaces; when suddenly I heard a shrill and</p>
<p>dreadful scream. It came from the room into which</p>
<p>Elizabeth had retired. As I heard it, the whole truth</p>
<p>rushed into my mind, my arms dropped, the motion</p>
<p>of every muscle and ﬁbre was suspended; I could</p>
<p>feel the blood trickling in my veins, and tingling in</p>
<p>the extremities of my limbs. This state lasted but for</p>
<p>an instant; the scream was repeated, and I rushed</p>
<p>into the room.</p>
<p>Great God! why did I not then expire! Why am I</p>
<p>here to relate the destruction of the best hope, and</p>
<p>the purest creature of earth. She was there, lifeless</p>
<p>and inanimate, thrown across the bed, her head</p>
<p>hanging down, and her pale and distorted features</p>
<p>half covered by her hair. Every where I turn I see</p>
<p>the same ﬁgure — her bloodless arms and relaxed</p>
<p>form ﬂung by the murderer on its bridal bier. Could</p>
<p>I behold this, and live? Alas! life is obstinate, and</p>
<p>clings closest where it is most hated. For a moment</p>
<p>only did I lose recollection; I fainted.</p>
<p>When I recovered, I found myself surrounded</p>
<p>by the people of the inn; their countenances ex-</p>
<p>pressed a breathless terror: but the horror of others</p>
<p>appeared only as a mockery, a shadow of the feel-</p>
<p>ings that oppressed me. I escaped from them to the</p>
<p>room where lay the body of Elizabeth, my love, my</p>
<p>wife, so lately living, so dear, so worthy. She had</p>
<p>been moved from the posture in which I had ﬁrst</p>
<p>beheld her; and now, as she lay, her head upon her</p>
<p>arm, and a handkerchief thrown across her face and</p>
<p>neck, I might have supposed her asleep. I rushed</p>
<p>towards her, and embraced her with ardour; but the</p>
<p>deathly languor and coldness of the limbs told me,</p>
<p>that what I now held in my arms had ceased to be</p>
<p>the Elizabeth whom I had loved and cherished. The</p>
<p>murderous mark of the ﬁend’s grasp was on her</p>
<p>neck, and the breath had ceased to issue from her</p>
<p>lips.</p>
<p>While I still hung over her in the agony of de-</p>
<p>spair, I happened to look up. The windows of the</p>
<p>room had before been darkened; and I felt a kind</p>
<p>of panic on seeing the pale yellow light of the moon</p>
<p>illuminate the chamber. The shutters had been</p>
<p>thrown back; and, with a sensation of horror not to</p>
<p>be described, I saw at the open window a ﬁgure</p>
<p>the most hideous and abhorred. A grin was on the</p>
<p>face of the monster; he seemed to jeer, as with</p>
<p>his ﬁendish ﬁnger he pointed towards the corpse of</p>
<p>my wife. I rushed towards the window, and draw-</p>
<p>ing a pistol from my bosom, shot; but he eluded</p>
<p>me, leaped from his station, and, running with the</p>
<p>swiftness of lightning, plunged into the lake.</p>
<p>The report of the pistol brought a crowd into the</p>
<p>room. I pointed to the spot where he had disap-</p>
<p>peared, and we followed the track with boats; nets</p>
<p>were cast, but in vain. After passing several hours,</p>
<p>we returned hopeless, most of my companions be-</p>
<p>lieving it to have been a form conjured by my fancy.</p>
<p>After having landed, they proceeded to search the</p>
<p>country, parties going in different directions among</p>
<p>the woods and vines.</p>
<p>I did not accompany them; I was exhausted: a</p>
<p>ﬁlm covered my eyes, and my skin was parched</p>
<p>with the heat of fever. In this state I lay on a bed,</p>
<p>hardly conscious of what had happened; my eyes</p>
<p>wandered round the room, as if to seek something</p>
<p>that I had lost.</p>
<p>At length I remembered that my father would</p>
<p>anxiously expect the return of Elizabeth and myself,</p>
<p>and that I must return alone. This reﬂection brought</p>
<p>tears into my eyes, and I wept for a long time; but my</p>
<p>thoughts rambled to various subjects, reﬂecting on</p>
<p>my misfortunes, and their cause. I was bewildered</p>
<p>in a cloud of wonder and horror. The death of</p>
<p>William, the execution of Justine, the murder of</p>
<p>Clerval, and lastly of my wife; even at that moment</p>
<p>I knew not that my only remaining friends were safe</p>
<p>from the malignity of the ﬁend; my father even now</p>
<p>might be writhing under his grasp, and Ernest might</p>
<p>be dead at his feet. This idea made me shudder,</p>
<p>and recalled me to action. I started up, and resolved</p>
<p>to return to Geneva with all possible speed.</p>
<p>There were no horses to be procured, and</p>
<p>I must return by the lake; but the wind was un-</p>
<p>favourable, and the rain fell in torrents. However, it</p>
<p>was hardly morning, and I might reasonably hope</p>
<p>to arrive by night. I hired men to row, and took</p>
<p>an oar myself, for I had always experienced relief</p>
<p>from mental torment in bodily exercise. But the</p>
<p>overﬂowing misery I now felt, and the excess of</p>
<p>agitation that I endured, rendered me incapable of</p>
<p>any exertion. I threw down the oar; and, leaning my</p>
<p>head upon my hands, gave way to every gloomy</p>
<p>idea that arose. If I looked up, I saw the scenes</p>
<p>which were familiar to me in my happier time, and</p>
<p>which I had contemplated but the day before in the</p>
<p>company of her who was now but a shadow and a</p>
<p>recollection. Tears streamed from my eyes. The</p>
<p>rain had ceased for a moment, and I saw the ﬁsh</p>
<p>play in the waters as they had done a few hours</p>
<p>before; they had then been observed by Elizabeth.</p>
<p>Nothing is so painful to the human mind as a great</p>
<p>and sudden change. The sun might shine, or the</p>
<p>clouds might lour; but nothing could appear to</p>
<p>me as it had done the day before. A ﬁend had</p>
<p>snatched from me every hope of future happiness:</p>
<p>no creature had ever been so miserable as I was;</p>
<p>so frightful an event is single in the history of man.</p>
<p>But why should I dwell upon the incidents</p>
<p>that followed this last overwhelming event. Mine</p>
<p>has been a tale of horrors; I have reached their</p>
<p>acme, and what I must now relate can but be te-</p>
<p>dious to you. Know that, one by one, my friends</p>
<p>were snatched away; I was left desolate. My own</p>
<p>strength is exhausted; and I must tell, in a few</p>
<p>words, what remains of my hideous narration.</p>
<p>I arrived at Geneva. My father and Ernest yet</p>
<p>lived; but the former sunk under the tidings that I</p>
<p>bore. I see him now, excellent and venerable old</p>
<p>man! his eyes wandered in vacancy, for they had</p>
<p>lost their charm and their delight — his niece, his</p>
<p>more than daughter, whom he doated on with all</p>
<p>that affection which a man feels, who, in the decline</p>
<p>of life, having few affections, clings more earnestly</p>
<p>to those that remain. Cursed, cursed be the ﬁend</p>
<p>that brought misery on his grey hairs, and doomed</p>
<p>him to waste in wretchedness! He could not live</p>
<p>under the horrors that were accumulated around</p>
<p>him; an apoplectic ﬁt was brought on, and in a few</p>
<p>days he died in my arms.</p>
<p>What then became of me? I know not; I lost</p>
<p>sensation, and chains and darkness were the only</p>
<p>objects that pressed upon me. Sometimes, indeed,</p>
<p>I dreamt that I wandered in ﬂowery meadows and</p>
<p>pleasant vales with the friends of my youth; but</p>
<p>awoke, and found myself in a dungeon. Melancholy</p>
<p>followed, but by degrees I gained a clear conception</p>
<p>of my miseries and situation, and was then released</p>
<p>from my prison. For they had called me mad; and</p>
<p>during many months, as I understood, a solitary cell</p>
<p>had been my habitation.</p>
<p>But liberty had been a useless gift to me had</p>
<p>I not, as I awakened to reason, at the same time</p>
<p>awakened to revenge. As the memory of past mis-</p>
<p>fortunes pressed upon me, I began to reﬂect on</p>
<p>their cause — the monster whom I had created,</p>
<p>the miserable dæmon whom I had sent abroad into</p>
<p>the world for my destruction. I was possessed by a</p>
<p>maddening rage when I thought of him, and desired</p>
<p>and ardently prayed that I might have him within my</p>
<p>grasp to wreak a great and signal revenge on his</p>
<p>cursed head.</p>
<p>Nor did my hate long conﬁne itself to useless</p>
<p>wishes; I began to reﬂect on the best means of</p>
<p>securing him; and for this purpose, about a month</p>
<p>after my release, I repaired to a criminal judge in</p>
<p>the town, and told him that I had an accusation to</p>
<p>make; that I knew the destroyer of my family; and</p>
<p>that I required him to exert his whole authority for</p>
<p>the apprehension of the murderer.</p>
<p>The magistrate listened to me with attention and</p>
<p>kindness: “Be assured, sir,” said he, “no pains or</p>
<p>exertions on my part shall be spared to discover</p>
<p>the villain.”</p>
<p>“I thank you,” replied I; “listen, therefore, to the</p>
<p>deposition that I have to make. It is indeed a tale</p>
<p>so strange, that I should fear you would not credit</p>
<p>it, were there not something in truth which, how-</p>
<p>ever wonderful, forces conviction. The story is too</p>
<p>connected to be mistaken for a dream, and I have</p>
<p>no motive for falsehood.” My manner, as I thus</p>
<p>addressed him, was impressive, but calm; I had</p>
<p>formed in my own heart a resolution to pursue my</p>
<p>destroyer to death; and this purpose quieted my</p>
<p>agony, and provisionally reconciled me to life. I now</p>
<p>related my history brieﬂy, but with ﬁrmness and pre-</p>
<p>cision, marking the dates with accuracy, and never</p>
<p>deviating into invective or exclamation.</p>
<p>The magistrate appeared at ﬁrst perfectly in-</p>
<p>credulous, but as I continued he became more at-</p>
<p>tentive and interested; I saw him sometimes shud-</p>
<p>der with horror, at others a lively surprise, unmin-</p>
<p>gled with disbelief, was painted on his countenance.</p>
<p>When I had concluded my narration, I said.</p>
<p>“This is the being whom I accuse, and for whose</p>
<p>detection and punishment I call upon you to exert</p>
<p>your whole power. It is your duty as a magistrate,</p>
<p>and I believe and hope that your feelings as a man</p>
<p>will not revolt from the execution of those functions</p>
<p>on this occasion.”</p>
<p>This address caused a considerable change in</p>
<p>the physiognomy of my auditor. He had heard my</p>
<p>story with that half kind of belief that is given to a</p>
<p>tale of spirits and supernatural events; but when he</p>
<p>was called upon to act ofﬁcially in consequence, the</p>
<p>whole tide of his incredulity returned. He, however,</p>
<p>answered mildly, “I would willingly afford you every</p>
<p>aid in your pursuit; but the creature of whom you</p>
<p>speak appears to have powers which would put all</p>
<p>my exertions to deﬁance. Who can follow an animal</p>
<p>which can traverse the sea of ice, and inhabit caves</p>
<p>and dens, where no man would venture to intrude?</p>
<p>Besides, some months have elapsed since the com-</p>
<p>mission of his crimes, and no one can conjecture</p>
<p>to what place he has wandered, or what region he</p>
<p>may now inhabit.”</p>
<p>“I do not doubt that he hovers near the spot</p>
<p>which I inhabit; and if he has indeed taken refuge in</p>
<p>the Alps, he may be hunted like the chamois, and</p>
<p>destroyed as a beast of prey. But I perceive your</p>
<p>thoughts: you do not credit my narrative, and do not</p>
<p>intend to pursue my enemy with the punishment</p>
<p>which is his desert.”</p>
<p>As I spoke, rage sparkled in my eyes; the mag-</p>
<p>istrate was intimidated; “You are mistaken,” said</p>
<p>he, “I will exert myself; and if it is in my power to</p>
<p>seize the monster, be assured that he shall suffer</p>
<p>punishment proportionate to his crimes. But I fear,</p>
<p>from what you have yourself described to be his</p>
<p>properties, that this will prove impracticable, and</p>
<p>that, while every proper measure is pursued, you</p>
<p>should endeavour to make up your mind to disap-</p>
<p>pointment.”</p>
<p>“That cannot be; but all that I can say will be</p>
<p>of little avail. My revenge is of no moment to you;</p>
<p>yet, while I allow it to be a vice, I confess that it</p>
<p>is the devouring and only passion of my soul. My</p>
<p>rage is unspeakable, when I reﬂect that the mur-</p>
<p>derer, whom I have turned loose upon society, still</p>
<p>exists. You refuse my just demand: I have but one</p>
<p>resource; and I devote myself, either in my life or</p>
<p>death, to his destruction.”</p>
<p>I trembled with excess of agitation as I said this;</p>
<p>there was a phrenzy in my manner, and something,</p>
<p>I doubt not, of that haughty ﬁerceness, which the</p>
<p>martyrs of old are said to have possessed. But to a</p>
<p>Genevan magistrate, whose mind was occupied by</p>
<p>far other ideas than those of devotion and heroism,</p>
<p>this elevation of mind had much the appearance</p>
<p>of madness. He endeavoured to soothe me as a</p>
<p>nurse does a child, and reverted to my tale as the</p>
<p>effects of delirium.</p>
<p>“Man,” I cried, “how ignorant art thou in thy pride</p>
<p>of wisdom! Cease; you know not what it is you say.”</p>
<p>I broke from the house angry and disturbed, and</p>
<p>retired to meditate on some other mode of action.</p>
<p>Chapter VII</p>
<p>MY present situation was one in which all volun-</p>
<p>tary thought was swallowed up and lost. I was</p>
<p>hurried away by fury; revenge alone endowed me</p>
<p>with strength and composure; it modelled my feel-</p>
<p>ings, and allowed me to be calculating and calm,</p>
<p>at periods when otherwise delirium or death would</p>
<p>have been my portion.</p>
<p>My ﬁrst resolution was to quit Geneva for ever;</p>
<p>my country, which, when I was happy and beloved,</p>
<p>was dear to me, now, in my adversity, became hate-</p>
<p>ful. I provided myself with a sum of money, together</p>
<p>371</p>
<p>with a few jewels which had belonged to my mother,</p>
<p>and departed.</p>
<p>And now my wanderings began, which are to</p>
<p>cease but with life. I have traversed a vast portion of</p>
<p>the earth, and have endured all the hardships which</p>
<p>travellers, in deserts and barbarous countries, are</p>
<p>wont to meet. How I have lived I hardly know; many</p>
<p>times have I stretched my failing limbs upon the</p>
<p>sandy plain, and prayed for death. But revenge kept</p>
<p>me alive; I dared not die, and leave my adversary</p>
<p>in being.</p>
<p>When I quitted Geneva, my ﬁrst labour was to</p>
<p>gain some clue by which I might trace the steps of</p>
<p>my ﬁendish enemy. But my plan was unsettled; and</p>
<p>I wandered many hours around the conﬁnes of the</p>
<p>town, uncertain what path I should pursue. As night</p>
<p>approached, I found myself at the entrance of the</p>
<p>cemetery where William, Elizabeth, and my father,</p>
<p>reposed. I entered it, and approached the tomb</p>
<p>which marked their graves. Every thing was silent,</p>
<p>except the leaves of the trees, which were gently</p>
<p>agitated by the wind; the night was nearly dark; and</p>
<p>the scene would have been solemn and affecting</p>
<p>even to an uninterested observer. The spirits of</p>
<p>the departed seemed to ﬂit around, and to cast a</p>
<p>shadow, which was felt but seen not, around the</p>
<p>head of the mourner.</p>
<p>The deep grief which this scene had at ﬁrst ex-</p>
<p>cited quickly gave way to rage and despair. They</p>
<p>were dead, and I lived; their murderer also lived,</p>
<p>and to destroy him I must drag out my weary exis-</p>
<p>tence. I knelt on the grass, and kissed the earth,</p>
<p>and with quivering lips exclaimed, “By the sacred</p>
<p>earth on which I kneel, by the shades that wander</p>
<p>near me, by the deep and eternal grief that I feel, I</p>
<p>swear; and by thee, O Night, and by the spirits that</p>
<p>preside over thee, I swear to pursue the dæmon,</p>
<p>who caused this misery, until he or I shall perish</p>
<p>in mortal conﬂict. For this purpose I will preserve</p>
<p>my life: to execute this dear revenge, will I again</p>
<p>behold the sun, and tread the green herbage of</p>
<p>earth, which otherwise should vanish from my eyes</p>
<p>for ever. And I call on you, spirits of the dead; and</p>
<p>on you, wandering ministers of vengeance, to aid</p>
<p>and conduct me in my work. Let the cursed and</p>
<p>hellish monster drink deep of agony; let him feel the</p>
<p>despair that now torments me.”</p>
<p>I had begun my adjuration with solemnity, and</p>
<p>an awe which almost assured me that the shades</p>
<p>of my murdered friends heard and approved my de-</p>
<p>votion; but the furies possessed me as I concluded,</p>
<p>and rage choaked my utterance.</p>
<p>I was answered through the stillness of night</p>
<p>by a loud and ﬁendish laugh. It rung on my ears</p>
<p>long and heavily; the mountains re-echoed it, and I</p>
<p>felt as if all hell surrounded me with mockery and</p>
<p>laughter. Surely in that moment I should have been</p>
<p>possessed by phrenzy, and have destroyed my mis-</p>
<p>erable existence, but that my vow was heard, and</p>
<p>that I was reserved for vengeance. The laughter</p>
<p>died away: when a well-known and abhorred voice,</p>
<p>apparently close to my ear, addressed me in an au-</p>
<p>dible whisper — “I am satisﬁed: miserable wretch!</p>
<p>you have determined to live, and I am satisﬁed.”</p>
<p>I darted towards the spot from which the sound</p>
<p>proceeded; but the devil eluded my grasp. Sud-</p>
<p>denly the broad disk of the moon arose, and shone</p>
<p>full upon his ghastly and distorted shape, as he ﬂed</p>
<p>with more than mortal speed.</p>
<p>I pursued him; and for many months this has</p>
<p>been my task. Guided by a slight clue, I followed</p>
<p>the windings of the Rhone, but vainly. The blue</p>
<p>Mediterranean appeared; and, by a strange chance,</p>
<p>I saw the ﬁend enter by night, and hide himself in a</p>
<p>vessel bound for the Black Sea. I took my passage</p>
<p>in the same ship; but he escaped, I know not how.</p>
<p>Amidst the wilds of Tartary and Russia, al-</p>
<p>though he still evaded me, I have ever followed</p>
<p>in his track. Sometimes the peasants, scared by</p>
<p>this horrid apparition, informed me of his path;</p>
<p>sometimes he himself, who feared that if I lost all</p>
<p>trace I should despair and die, often left some mark</p>
<p>to guide me. The snows descended on my head,</p>
<p>and I saw the print of his huge step on the white</p>
<p>plain. To you ﬁrst entering on life, to whom care is</p>
<p>new, and agony unknown, how can you understand</p>
<p>what I have felt, and still feel? Cold, want, and</p>
<p>fatigue, were the least pains which I was destined</p>
<p>to endure; I was cursed by some devil, and carried</p>
<p>about with me my eternal hell; yet still a spirit of</p>
<p>good followed and directed my steps, and, when I</p>
<p>most murmured, would suddenly extricate me from</p>
<p>seemingly insurmountable difﬁculties. Sometimes,</p>
<p>when nature, overcome by hunger, sunk under the</p>
<p>exhaustion, a repast was prepared for me in the</p>
<p>desert, that restored and inspirited me. The fare</p>
<p>was indeed coarse, such as the peasants of the</p>
<p>country ate; but I may not doubt that it was set</p>
<p>there by the spirits that I had invoked to aid me.</p>
<p>Often, when all was dry, the heavens cloudless,</p>
<p>and I was parched by thirst, a slight cloud would</p>
<p>bedim the sky, shed the few drops that revived me,</p>
<p>and vanish.</p>
<p>I followed, when I could, the courses of the</p>
<p>rivers; but the dæmon generally avoided these, as</p>
<p>it was here that the population of the country chieﬂy</p>
<p>collected. In other places human beings were sel-</p>
<p>dom seen; and I generally subsisted on the wild</p>
<p>animals that crossed my path. I had money with</p>
<p>me, and gained the friendship of the villagers by</p>
<p>distributing it, or bringing with me some food that I</p>
<p>had killed, which, after taking a small part, I always</p>
<p>presented to those who had provided me with ﬁre</p>
<p>and utensils for cooking.</p>
<p>My life, as it passed thus, was indeed hateful to</p>
<p>me, and it was during sleep alone that I could taste</p>
<p>joy. O blessed sleep! often, when most miserable, I</p>
<p>sank to repose, and my dreams lulled me even to</p>
<p>rapture. The spirits that guarded me had provided</p>
<p>these moments, or rather hours, of happiness, that</p>
<p>I might retain strength to fulﬁl my pilgrimage. De-</p>
<p>prived of this respite, I should have sunk under my</p>
<p>hardships. During the day I was sustained and in-</p>
<p>spirited by the hope of night: for in sleep I saw my</p>
<p>friends, my wife, and my beloved country; again</p>
<p>I saw the benevolent countenance of my father,</p>
<p>heard the silver tones of my Elizabeth’s voice, and</p>
<p>beheld Clerval enjoying health and youth. Often,</p>
<p>when wearied by a toilsome march, I persuaded</p>
<p>myself that I was dreaming until night should come,</p>
<p>and that I should then enjoy reality in the arms of</p>
<p>my dearest friends. What agonizing fondness did</p>
<p>I feel for them! how did I cling to their dear forms,</p>
<p>as sometimes they haunted even my waking hours,</p>
<p>and persuade myself that they still lived! At such</p>
<p>moments vengeance, that burned within me, died</p>
<p>in my heart, and I pursued my path towards the de-</p>
<p>struction of the dæmon, more as a task enjoined by</p>
<p>heaven, as the mechanical impulse of some power</p>
<p>of which I was unconscious, than as the ardent</p>
<p>desire of my soul.</p>
<p>What his feelings were whom I pursued, I can-</p>
<p>not know. Sometimes, indeed, he left marks in</p>
<p>writing on the barks of the trees, or cut in stone,</p>
<p>that guided me, and instigated my fury. “My reign</p>
<p>is not yet over,” (these words were legible in one</p>
<p>of these inscriptions); “you live, and my power is</p>
<p>complete. Follow me; I seek the everlasting ices of</p>
<p>the north, where you will feel the misery of cold and</p>
<p>frost, to which I am impassive. You will ﬁnd near</p>
<p>this place, if you follow not too tardily, a dead hare;</p>
<p>eat, and be refreshed. Come on, my enemy; we</p>
<p>have yet to wrestle for our lives; but many hard and</p>
<p>miserable hours must you endure, until that period</p>
<p>shall arrive.”</p>
<p>Scofﬁng devil! Again do I vow vengeance; again</p>
<p>do I devote thee, miserable ﬁend, to torture and</p>
<p>death. Never will I omit my search, until he or I</p>
<p>perish; and then with what ecstacy shall I join my</p>
<p>Elizabeth, and those who even now prepare for me</p>
<p>the reward of my tedious toil and horrible pilgrim-</p>
<p>age.</p>
<p>As I still pursued my journey to the northward,</p>
<p>the snows thickened, and the cold increased in a</p>
<p>degree almost too severe to support. The peasants</p>
<p>were shut up in their hovels, and only a few of</p>
<p>the most hardy ventured forth to seize the animals</p>
<p>whom starvation had forced from their hiding-places</p>
<p>to seek for prey. The rivers were covered with ice,</p>
<p>and no ﬁsh could be procured; and thus I was cut</p>
<p>off from my chief article of maintenance.</p>
<p>The triumph of my enemy increased with the</p>
<p>difﬁculty of my labours. One inscription that he left</p>
<p>was in these words: “Prepare! your toils only begin:</p>
<p>wrap yourself in furs, and provide food, for we shall</p>
<p>soon enter upon a journey where your sufferings</p>
<p>will satisfy my everlasting hatred.”</p>
<p>My courage and perseverance were invigorated</p>
<p>by these scofﬁng words; I resolved not to fail in</p>
<p>my purpose; and, calling on heaven to support</p>
<p>me, I continued with unabated fervour to traverse</p>
<p>immense deserts, until the ocean appeared at a</p>
<p>distance, and formed the utmost boundary of the</p>
<p>horizon. Oh! how unlike it was to the blue seas</p>
<p>of the south! Covered with ice, it was only to be</p>
<p>distinguished from land by its superior wildness and</p>
<p>ruggedness. The Greeks wept for joy when they</p>
<p>beheld the Mediterranean from the hills of Asia,</p>
<p>and hailed with rapture the boundary of their toils.</p>
<p>I did not weep; but I knelt down, and, with a full</p>
<p>heart, thanked my guiding spirit for conducting me</p>
<p>in safety to the place where I hoped, notwithstand-</p>
<p>ing my adversary’s gibe, to meet and grapple with</p>
<p>him.</p>
<p>Some weeks before this period I had procured</p>
<p>a sledge and dogs, and thus traversed the snows</p>
<p>with inconceivable speed. I know not whether the</p>
<p>ﬁend possessed the same advantages; but I found</p>
<p>that, as before I had daily lost ground in the pursuit,</p>
<p>I now gained on him; so much so, that when I ﬁrst</p>
<p>saw the ocean, he was but one day’s journey in</p>
<p>advance, and I hoped to intercept him before he</p>
<p>should reach the beach. With new courage, there-</p>
<p>fore, I pressed on, and in two days arrived at a</p>
<p>wretched hamlet on the seashore. I inquired of the</p>
<p>inhabitants concerning the ﬁend, and gained ac-</p>
<p>curate information. A gigantic monster, they said,</p>
<p>had arrived the night before, armed with a gun and</p>
<p>many pistols; putting to ﬂight the inhabitants of a</p>
<p>solitary cottage, through fear of his terriﬁc appear-</p>
<p>ance. He had carried off their store of winter food,</p>
<p>and, placing it in a sledge, to draw which he had</p>
<p>seized on a numerous drove of trained dogs, he</p>
<p>had harnessed them, and the same night, to the</p>
<p>joy of the horror-struck villagers, had pursued his</p>
<p>journey across the sea in a direction that led to no</p>
<p>land; and they conjectured that he must speedily</p>
<p>be destroyed by the breaking of the ice, or frozen</p>
<p>by the eternal frosts.</p>
<p>On hearing this information, I suffered a tempo-</p>
<p>rary access of despair. He had escaped me; and</p>
<p>I must commence a destructive and almost end-</p>
<p>less journey across the mountainous ices of the</p>
<p>ocean, — amidst cold that few of the inhabitants</p>
<p>could long endure, and which I, the native of a ge-</p>
<p>nial and sunny climate, could not hope to survive.</p>
<p>Yet at the idea that the ﬁend should live and be</p>
<p>triumphant, my rage and vengeance returned, and,</p>
<p>like a mighty tide, overwhelmed every other feeling.</p>
<p>After a slight repose, during which the spirits of the</p>
<p>dead hovered round, and instigated me to toil and</p>
<p>revenge, I prepared for my journey.</p>
<p>I exchanged my land sledge for one fashioned</p>
<p>for the inequalities of the frozen ocean; and, pur-</p>
<p>chasing a plentiful stock of provisions, I departed</p>
<p>from land.</p>
<p>I cannot guess how many days have passed</p>
<p>since then; but I have endured misery, which noth-</p>
<p>ing but the eternal sentiment of a just retribution</p>
<p>burning within my heart could have enabled me to</p>
<p>support. Immense and rugged mountains of ice</p>
<p>often barred up my passage, and I often heard the</p>
<p>thunder of the ground sea, which threatened my</p>
<p>destruction. But again the frost came, and made</p>
<p>the paths of the sea secure.</p>
<p>By the quantity of provision which I had con-</p>
<p>sumed I should guess that I had passed three</p>
<p>weeks in this journey; and the continual protrac-</p>
<p>tion of hope, returning back upon the heart, often</p>
<p>wrung bitter drops of despondency and grief from</p>
<p>my eyes. Despair had indeed almost secured her</p>
<p>prey, and I should soon have sunk beneath this</p>
<p>misery; when once, after the poor animals that car-</p>
<p>ried me had with incredible toil gained the summit</p>
<p>of a sloping ice mountain, and one sinking under</p>
<p>his fatigue died, I viewed the expanse before me</p>
<p>with anguish, when suddenly my eye caught a dark</p>
<p>speck upon the dusky plain. I strained my sight to</p>
<p>discover what it could be, and uttered a wild cry</p>
<p>of ecstacy when I distinguished a sledge, and the</p>
<p>distorted proportions of a well-known form within.</p>
<p>Oh! with what a burning gush did hope revisit my</p>
<p>heart! warm tears ﬁlled my eyes, which I hastily</p>
<p>wiped away, that they might not intercept the view I</p>
<p>had of the dæmon; but still my sight was dimmed by</p>
<p>the burning drops, until, giving way to the emotions</p>
<p>that oppressed me, I wept aloud.</p>
<p>But this was not the time for delay; I disencum-</p>
<p>bered the dogs of their dead companion, gave them</p>
<p>a plentiful portion of food; and, after an hour’s rest,</p>
<p>which was absolutely necessary, and yet which was</p>
<p>bitterly irksome to me, I continued my route. The</p>
<p>sledge was still visible; nor did I again lose sight of it,</p>
<p>except at the moments when for a short time some</p>
<p>ice rock concealed it with its intervening crags. I</p>
<p>indeed perceptibly gained on it; and when, after</p>
<p>nearly two days’ journey, I beheld my enemy at no</p>
<p>more than a mile distant, my heart bounded within</p>
<p>me.</p>
<p>But now, when I appeared almost within grasp</p>
<p>of my enemy, my hopes were suddenly extin-</p>
<p>guished, and I lost all trace of him more utterly than</p>
<p>I had ever done before. A ground sea was heard;</p>
<p>the thunder of its progress, as the waters rolled and</p>
<p>swelled beneath me, became every moment more</p>
<p>ominous and terriﬁc. I pressed on, but in vain. The</p>
<p>wind arose; the sea roared; and, as with the mighty</p>
<p>shock of an earthquake, it split, and cracked with</p>
<p>a tremendous and overwhelming sound. The work</p>
<p>was soon ﬁnished: in a few minutes a tumultuous</p>
<p>sea rolled between me and my enemy, and I was</p>
<p>left drifting on a scattered piece of ice, that was</p>
<p>continually lessening, and thus preparing for me a</p>
<p>hideous death.</p>
<p>In this manner many appalling hours passed;</p>
<p>several of my dogs died; and I myself was about</p>
<p>to sink under the accumulation of distress, when</p>
<p>I saw your vessel riding at anchor, and holding</p>
<p>forth to me hopes of succour and life. I had no</p>
<p>conception that vessels ever came so far north, and</p>
<p>was astounded at the sight. I quickly destroyed part</p>
<p>of my sledge to construct oars; and by these means</p>
<p>was enabled, with inﬁnite fatigue, to move my ice-</p>
<p>raft in the direction of your ship. I had determined, if</p>
<p>you were going southward, still to trust myself to the</p>
<p>mercy of the seas, rather than abandon my purpose.</p>
<p>I hoped to induce you to grant me a boat with which</p>
<p>I could still pursue my enemy. But your direction</p>
<p>was northward. You took me on board when my</p>
<p>vigour was exhausted, and I should soon have sunk</p>
<p>under my multiplied hardships into a death, which I</p>
<p>still dread, — for my task is unfulﬁlled.</p>
<p>Oh! when will my guiding spirit, in conducting</p>
<p>me to the dæmon, allow me the rest I so much</p>
<p>desire; or must I die, and he yet live? If I do, swear</p>
<p>to me, Walton, that he shall not escape; that you will</p>
<p>seek him, and satisfy my vengeance in his death.</p>
<p>Yet, do I dare ask you to undertake my pilgrimage,</p>
<p>to endure the hardships that I have undergone?</p>
<p>No; I am not so selﬁsh. Yet, when I am dead, if</p>
<p>he should appear; if the ministers of vengeance</p>
<p>should conduct him to you, swear that he shall not</p>
<p>live — swear that he shall not triumph over my</p>
<p>accumulated woes, and live to make another such</p>
<p>a wretch as I am. He is eloquent and persuasive;</p>
<p>and once his words had even power over my heart:</p>
<p>but trust him not. His soul is as hellish as his form,</p>
<p>full of treachery and ﬁend-like malice. Hear him</p>
<p>not; call on the manes of William, Justine, Clerval,</p>
<p>Elizabeth, my father, and of the wretched Victor,</p>
<p>and thrust your sword into his heart. I will hover</p>
<p>near, and direct the steel aright.</p>
<p>WALTON, in continuation.</p>
<p>August 26th, 17— .</p>
<p>YOU have read this strange and terriﬁc story, Mar-</p>
<p>garet; and do you not feel your blood congealed</p>
<p>with horror, like that which even now curdles mine?</p>
<p>Sometimes, seized with sudden agony, he could</p>
<p>not continue his tale; at others, his voice broken,</p>
<p>yet piercing, uttered with difﬁculty the words so re-</p>
<p>plete with agony. His ﬁne and lovely eyes were now</p>
<p>lighted up with indignation, now subdued to down-</p>
<p>cast sorrow, and quenched in inﬁnite wretchedness.</p>
<p>Sometimes he commanded his countenance and</p>
<p>tones, and related the most horrible incidents with a</p>
<p>tranquil voice, suppressing every mark of agitation;</p>
<p>then, like a volcano bursting forth, his face would</p>
<p>suddenly change to an expression of the wildest</p>
<p>rage, as he shrieked out imprecations on his perse-</p>
<p>cutor.</p>
<p>His tale is connected, and told with an appear-</p>
<p>ance of the simplest truth; yet I own to you that the</p>
<p>letters of Felix and Saﬁe, which he shewed me, and</p>
<p>the apparition of the monster, seen from our ship,</p>
<p>brought to me a greater conviction of the truth of his</p>
<p>narrative than his asseverations, however earnest</p>
<p>and connected. Such a monster has then really</p>
<p>existence; I cannot doubt it; yet I am lost in surprise</p>
<p>and admiration. Sometimes I endeavoured to gain</p>
<p>from Frankenstein the particulars of his creature’s</p>
<p>formation; but on this point he was impenetrable.</p>
<p>“Are you mad, my friend?” said he, “or whither</p>
<p>does your senseless curiosity lead you? Would you</p>
<p>also create for yourself and the world a demonia-</p>
<p>cal enemy? Or to what do your questions tend?</p>
<p>Peace, peace! learn my miseries, and do not seek</p>
<p>to increase your own.”</p>
<p>Frankenstein discovered that I made notes con-</p>
<p>cerning his history: he asked to see them, and then</p>
<p>himself corrected and augmented them in many</p>
<p>places; but principally in giving the life and spirit to</p>
<p>the conversations he held with his enemy. “Since</p>
<p>you have preserved my narration,” said he, “I would</p>
<p>not that a mutilated one should go down to poster-</p>
<p>ity.”</p>
<p>Thus has a week passed away, while I have</p>
<p>listened to the strangest tale that ever imagination</p>
<p>formed. My thoughts, and every feeling of my soul,</p>
<p>have been drunk up by the interest for my guest,</p>
<p>which this tale, and his own elevated and gentle</p>
<p>manners have created. I wish to soothe him; yet</p>
<p>can I counsel one so inﬁnitely miserable, so desti-</p>
<p>tute of every hope of consolation, to live? Oh, no!</p>
<p>the only joy that he can now know will be when</p>
<p>he composes his shattered feelings to peace and</p>
<p>death. Yet he enjoys one comfort, the offspring</p>
<p>of solitude and delirium: he believes, that, when</p>
<p>in dreams he holds converse with his friends, and</p>
<p>derives from that communion consolation for his</p>
<p>miseries, or excitements to his vengeance, that</p>
<p>they are not the creations of his fancy, but the real</p>
<p>beings who visit him from the regions of a remote</p>
<p>world. This faith gives a solemnity to his reveries</p>
<p>that render them to me almost as imposing and</p>
<p>interesting as truth.</p>
<p>Our conversations are not always conﬁned to</p>
<p>his own history and misfortunes. On every point</p>
<p>of general literature he displays unbounded knowl-</p>
<p>edge, and a quick and piercing apprehension. His</p>
<p>eloquence is forcible and touching; nor can I hear</p>
<p>him, when he relates a pathetic incident, or endeav-</p>
<p>ours to move the passions of pity or love, without</p>
<p>tears. What a glorious creature must he have been</p>
<p>in the days of his prosperity, when he is thus noble</p>
<p>and godlike in ruin. He seems to feel his own worth,</p>
<p>and the greatness of his fall.</p>
<p>“When younger,” said he, “I felt as if I were des-</p>
<p>tined for some great enterprise. My feelings are pro-</p>
<p>found; but I possessed a coolness of judgment that</p>
<p>ﬁtted me for illustrious achievements. This senti-</p>
<p>ment of the worth of my nature supported me, when</p>
<p>others would have been oppressed; for I deemed it</p>
<p>criminal to throw away in useless grief those talents</p>
<p>that might be useful to my fellow-creatures. When I</p>
<p>reﬂected on the work I had completed, no less a one</p>
<p>than the creation of a sensitive and rational animal,</p>
<p>I could not rank myself with the herd of common</p>
<p>projectors. But this feeling, which supported me in</p>
<p>the commencement of my career, now serves only</p>
<p>to plunge me lower in the dust. All my speculations</p>
<p>and hopes are as nothing; and, like the archangel</p>
<p>who aspired to omnipotence, I am chained in an</p>
<p>eternal hell. My imagination was vivid, yet my pow-</p>
<p>ers of analysis and application were intense; by the</p>
<p>union of these qualities I conceived the idea, and</p>
<p>executed the creation of a man. Even now I cannot</p>
<p>recollect, without passion, my reveries while the</p>
<p>work was incomplete. I trod heaven in my thoughts,</p>
<p>now exulting in my powers, now burning with the</p>
<p>idea of their effects. From my infancy I was imbued</p>
<p>with high hopes and a lofty ambition; but how am I</p>
<p>sunk! Oh! my friend, if you had known me as I once</p>
<p>was, you would not recognize me in this state of</p>
<p>degradation. Despondency rarely visited my heart;</p>
<p>a high destiny seemed to bear me on, until I fell,</p>
<p>never, never again to rise.”</p>
<p>Must I then lose this admirable being? I have</p>
<p>longed for a friend; I have sought one who would</p>
<p>sympathize with and love me. Behold, on these</p>
<p>desert seas I have found such a one; but, I fear, I</p>
<p>have gained him only to know his value, and lose</p>
<p>him. I would reconcile him to life, but he repulses</p>
<p>the idea.</p>
<p>“I thank you, Walton,” he said, “for your kind</p>
<p>intentions towards so miserable a wretch; but when</p>
<p>you speak of new ties, and fresh affections, think</p>
<p>you that any can replace those who are gone? Can</p>
<p>any man be to me as Clerval was; or any woman</p>
<p>another Elizabeth? Even where the affections are</p>
<p>not strongly moved by any superior excellence, the</p>
<p>companions of our childhood always possess a cer-</p>
<p>tain power over our minds, which hardly any later</p>
<p>friend can obtain. They know our infantine dispo-</p>
<p>sitions, which, however they may be afterwards</p>
<p>modiﬁed, are never eradicated; and they can judge</p>
<p>of our actions with more certain conclusions as to</p>
<p>the integrity of our motives. A sister or a brother can</p>
<p>never, unless indeed such symptoms have been</p>
<p>shewn early, suspect the other of fraud or false deal-</p>
<p>ing, when another friend, however strongly he may</p>
<p>be attached, may, in spite of himself, be invaded</p>
<p>with suspicion. But I enjoyed friends, dear not only</p>
<p>through habit and association, but from their own</p>
<p>merits; and, wherever I am, the soothing voice of</p>
<p>my Elizabeth, and the conversation of Clerval, will</p>
<p>be ever whispered in my ear. They are dead; and</p>
<p>but one feeling in such a solitude can persuade me</p>
<p>to preserve my life. If I were engaged in any high</p>
<p>undertaking or design, fraught with extensive util-</p>
<p>ity to my fellow-creatures, then could I live to fulﬁl</p>
<p>it. But such is not my destiny; I must pursue and</p>
<p>destroy the being to whom I gave existence; then</p>
<p>my lot on earth will be fulﬁlled, and I may die.”</p>
<p>September 2d.</p>
<p>MY BELOVED SISTER,</p>
<p>I write to you, encompassed by peril, and igno-</p>
<p>rant whether I am ever doomed to see again dear</p>
<p>England, and the dearer friends that inhabit it. I am</p>
<p>surrounded by mountains of ice, which admit of no</p>
<p>escape, and threaten every moment to crush my</p>
<p>vessel. The brave fellows, whom I have persuaded</p>
<p>to be my companions, look towards me for aid; but</p>
<p>I have none to bestow. There is something terri-</p>
<p>bly appalling in our situation, yet my courage and</p>
<p>hopes do not desert me. We may survive; and if</p>
<p>we do not, I will repeat the lessons of my Seneca,</p>
<p>and die with a good heart.</p>
<p>Yet what, Margaret, will be the state of your</p>
<p>mind? You will not hear of my destruction, and</p>
<p>you will anxiously await my return. Years will pass,</p>
<p>and you will have visitings of despair, and yet be</p>
<p>tortured by hope. Oh! my beloved sister, the sick-</p>
<p>ening failings of your heart-felt expectations are, in</p>
<p>prospect, more terrible to me than my own death.</p>
<p>But you have a husband, and lovely children; you</p>
<p>may be happy: heaven bless you, and make you</p>
<p>so!</p>
<p>My unfortunate guest regards me with the ten-</p>
<p>derest compassion. He endeavours to ﬁll me with</p>
<p>hope; and talks as if life were a possession which</p>
<p>he valued. He reminds me how often the same</p>
<p>accidents have happened to other navigators, who</p>
<p>have attempted this sea, and, in spite of myself, he</p>
<p>ﬁlls me with cheerful auguries. Even the sailors feel</p>
<p>the power of his eloquence: when he speaks, they</p>
<p>no longer despair; he rouses their energies, and,</p>
<p>while they hear his voice, they believe these vast</p>
<p>mountains of ice are mole-hills, which will vanish</p>
<p>before the resolutions of man. These feelings are</p>
<p>transitory; each day’s expectation delayed ﬁlls them</p>
<p>with fear, and I almost dread a mutiny caused by</p>
<p>this despair.</p>
<p>September 5th.</p>
<p>A scene has just passed of such uncommon in-</p>
<p>terest, that although it is highly probable that these</p>
<p>papers may never reach you, yet I cannot forbear</p>
<p>recording it.</p>
<p>We are still surrounded by mountains of ice,</p>
<p>still in imminent danger of being crushed in their</p>
<p>conﬂict. The cold is excessive, and many of my</p>
<p>unfortunate comrades have already found a grave</p>
<p>amidst this scene of desolation. Frankenstein has</p>
<p>daily declined in health: a feverish ﬁre still glim-</p>
<p>mers in his eyes; but he is exhausted, and, when</p>
<p>suddenly roused to any exertion, he speedily sinks</p>
<p>again into apparent lifelessness.</p>
<p>I mentioned in my last letter the fears I enter-</p>
<p>tained of a mutiny. This morning, as I sat watching</p>
<p>the wan countenance of my friend — his eyes half</p>
<p>closed, and his limbs hanging listlessly, — I was</p>
<p>roused by half a dozen of the sailors, who desired</p>
<p>admission into the cabin. They entered; and their</p>
<p>leader addressed me. He told me that he and his</p>
<p>companions had been chosen by the other sailors</p>
<p>to come in deputation to me, to make me a demand,</p>
<p>which, in justice, I could not refuse. We were im-</p>
<p>mured in ice, and should probably never escape;</p>
<p>but they feared that if, as was possible, the ice</p>
<p>should dissipate, and a free passage be opened, I</p>
<p>should be rash enough to continue my voyage, and</p>
<p>lead them into fresh dangers, after they might hap-</p>
<p>pily have surmounted this. They desired, therefore,</p>
<p>that I should engage with a solemn promise, that if</p>
<p>the vessel should be freed, I would instantly direct</p>
<p>my coarse southward.</p>
<p>This speech troubled me. I had not despaired;</p>
<p>nor had I yet conceived the idea of returning, if</p>
<p>set free. Yet could I, in justice, or even in possi-</p>
<p>bility, refuse this demand? I hesitated before I an-</p>
<p>swered; when Frankenstein, who had at ﬁrst been</p>
<p>silent, and, indeed, appeared hardly to have force</p>
<p>enough to attend, now roused himself; his eyes</p>
<p>sparkled, and his cheeks ﬂushed with momentary</p>
<p>vigour. Turning towards the men, he said —</p>
<p>“What do you mean? What do you demand of</p>
<p>your captain? Are you then so easily turned from</p>
<p>your design? Did you not call this a glorious expedi-</p>
<p>tion? and wherefore was it glorious? Not because</p>
<p>the way was smooth and placid as a southern sea,</p>
<p>but because it was full of dangers and terror; be-</p>
<p>cause, at every new incident, your fortitude was</p>
<p>to be called forth, and your courage exhibited; be-</p>
<p>cause danger and death surrounded, and these</p>
<p>dangers you were to brave and overcome. For this</p>
<p>was it a glorious, for this was it an honourable un-</p>
<p>dertaking. You were hereafter to be hailed as the</p>
<p>benefactors of your species; your name adored, as</p>
<p>belonging to brave men who encountered death</p>
<p>for honour and the beneﬁt of mankind. And now,</p>
<p>behold, with the ﬁrst imagination of danger, or, if</p>
<p>you will, the ﬁrst mighty and terriﬁc trial of your</p>
<p>courage, you shrink away, and are content to be</p>
<p>handed down as men who had not strength enough</p>
<p>to endure cold and peril; and so, poor souls, they</p>
<p>were chilly, and returned to their warm ﬁre-sides.</p>
<p>Why, that requires not this preparation; ye need not</p>
<p>have come thus far, and dragged your captain to</p>
<p>the shame of a defeat, merely to prove yourselves</p>
<p>cowards. Oh! be men, or be more than men. Be</p>
<p>steady to your purposes, and ﬁrm as a rock. This</p>
<p>ice is not made of such stuff as your hearts might</p>
<p>be; it is mutable, cannot withstand you, if you say</p>
<p>that it shall not. Do not return to your families with</p>
<p>the stigma of disgrace marked on your brows. Re-</p>
<p>turn as heroes who have fought and conquered,</p>
<p>and who know not what it is to turn their backs on</p>
<p>the foe.”</p>
<p>He spoke this with a voice so modulated to the</p>
<p>different feelings expressed in his speech, with an</p>
<p>eye so full of lofty design and heroism, that can you</p>
<p>wonder that these men were moved. They looked</p>
<p>at one another, and were unable to reply. I spoke; I</p>
<p>told them to retire, and consider of what had been</p>
<p>said: that I would not lead them further north, if they</p>
<p>strenuously desired the contrary; but that I hoped</p>
<p>that, with reﬂection, their courage would return.</p>
<p>They retired, and I turned towards my friend;</p>
<p>but he was sunk in languor, and almost deprived of</p>
<p>life.</p>
<p>How all this will terminate, I know not; but I</p>
<p>had rather die, than return shamefully, — my pur-</p>
<p>pose unfulﬁlled. Yet I fear such will be my fate; the</p>
<p>men, unsupported by ideas of glory and honour,</p>
<p>can never willingly continue to endure their present</p>
<p>hardships.</p>
<p>September 7th.</p>
<p>The die is cast; I have consented to return, if</p>
<p>we are not destroyed. Thus are my hopes blasted</p>
<p>by cowardice and indecision; I come back ignorant</p>
<p>and disappointed. It requires more philosophy than</p>
<p>I possess, to bear this injustice with patience.</p>
<p>September 12th.</p>
<p>It is past; I am returning to England. I have</p>
<p>lost my hopes of utility and glory; — I have lost my</p>
<p>friend. But I will endeavour to detail these bitter</p>
<p>circumstances to you, my dear sister; and, while I</p>
<p>am wafted towards England, and towards you, I will</p>
<p>not despond.</p>
<p>September 19th, the ice began to move, and</p>
<p>roarings like thunder were heard at a distance, as</p>
<p>the islands split and cracked in every direction. We</p>
<p>were in the most imminent peril; but, as we could</p>
<p>only remain passive, my chief attention was oc-</p>
<p>cupied by my unfortunate guest, whose illness in-</p>
<p>creased in such a degree, that he was entirely con-</p>
<p>ﬁned to his bed. The ice cracked behind us, and</p>
<p>was driven with force towards the north; a breeze</p>
<p>sprung from the west, and on the 11th the passage</p>
<p>towards the south became perfectly free. When</p>
<p>the sailors saw this, and that their return to their</p>
<p>native country was apparently assured, a shout</p>
<p>of tumultuous joy broke from them, loud and long-</p>
<p>continued. Frankenstein, who was dozing, awoke,</p>
<p>and asked the cause of the tumult. “They shout,” I</p>
<p>said, “because they will soon return to England.”</p>
<p>“Do you then really return?”</p>
<p>“Alas! yes; I cannot withstand their demands. I</p>
<p>cannot lead them unwillingly to danger, and I must</p>
<p>return.”</p>
<p>“Do so, if you will; but I will not. You may give</p>
<p>up your purpose; but mine is assigned to me by</p>
<p>heaven, and I dare not. I am weak; but surely the</p>
<p>spirits who assist my vengeance will endow me with</p>
<p>sufﬁcient strength.” Saying this, he endeavoured to</p>
<p>spring from the bed, but the exertion was too great</p>
<p>for him; he fell back, and fainted.</p>
<p>It was long before he was restored; and I often</p>
<p>thought that life was entirely extinct. At length he</p>
<p>opened his eyes, but he breathed with difﬁculty,</p>
<p>and was unable to speak. The surgeon gave him</p>
<p>a composing draught, and ordered us to leave him</p>
<p>undisturbed. In the mean time he told me, that my</p>
<p>friend had certainly not many hours to live.</p>
<p>His sentence was pronounced; and I could only</p>
<p>grieve, and be patient. I sat by his bed watching</p>
<p>him; his eyes were closed, and I thought he slept;</p>
<p>but presently he called to me in a feeble voice, and,</p>
<p>bidding me come near, said — “Alas! the strength</p>
<p>I relied on is gone; I feel that I shall soon die, and</p>
<p>he, my enemy and persecutor, may still be in be-</p>
<p>ing. Think not, Walton, that in the last moments</p>
<p>of my existence I feel that burning hatred, and ar-</p>
<p>dent desire of revenge, I once expressed, but I feel</p>
<p>myself justiﬁed in desiring the death of my adver-</p>
<p>sary. During these last days I have been occupied</p>
<p>in examining my past conduct; nor do I ﬁnd it blame-</p>
<p>able. In a ﬁt of enthusiastic madness I created a</p>
<p>rational creature, and was bound towards him, to</p>
<p>assure, as far as was in my power, his happiness</p>
<p>and well-being. This was my duty; but there was</p>
<p>another still paramount to that. My duties towards</p>
<p>my fellow-creatures had greater claims to my atten-</p>
<p>tion, because they included a greater proportion of</p>
<p>happiness or misery. Urged by this view, I refused,</p>
<p>and I did right in refusing, to create a companion</p>
<p>for the ﬁrst creature. He shewed unparalleled ma-</p>
<p>lignity and selﬁshness, in evil: he destroyed my</p>
<p>friends; he devoted to destruction beings who pos-</p>
<p>sessed exquisite sensations, happiness, and wis-</p>
<p>dom; nor do I know where this thirst for vengeance</p>
<p>may end. Miserable himself, that he may render</p>
<p>no other wretched, he ought to die. The task of</p>
<p>his destruction was mine, but I have failed. When</p>
<p>actuated by selﬁsh and vicious motives, I asked you</p>
<p>to undertake my unﬁnished work; and I renew this</p>
<p>request now, when I am only induced by reason</p>
<p>and virtue.</p>
<p>“Yet I cannot ask you to renounce your country</p>
<p>and friends, to fulﬁl this task; and now, that you are</p>
<p>returning to England, you will have little chance of</p>
<p>meeting with him. But the consideration of these</p>
<p>points, and the well-balancing of what you may</p>
<p>esteem your duties, I leave to you; my judgment and</p>
<p>ideas are already disturbed by the near approach</p>
<p>of death. I dare not ask you to do what I think right,</p>
<p>for I may still be misled by passion.</p>
<p>“That he should live to be an instrument of mis-</p>
<p>chief disturbs me; in other respects this hour, when</p>
<p>I momentarily expect my release, is the only happy</p>
<p>one which I have enjoyed for several years. The</p>
<p>forms of the beloved dead ﬂit before me, and I has-</p>
<p>ten to their arms. Farewell, Walton! Seek happiness</p>
<p>in tranquillity, and avoid ambition, even if it be only</p>
<p>the apparently innocent one of distinguishing your-</p>
<p>self in science and discoveries. Yet why do I say</p>
<p>this? I have myself been blasted in these hopes,</p>
<p>yet another may succeed.”</p>
<p>His voice became fainter as he spoke; and at</p>
<p>length, exhausted by his effort, he sunk into silence.</p>
<p>About half an hour afterwards he attempted again to</p>
<p>speak, but was unable; he pressed my hand feebly,</p>
<p>and his eyes closed for ever, while the irradiation of</p>
<p>a gentle smile passed away from his lips.</p>
<p>Margaret, what comment can I make on the</p>
<p>untimely extinction of this glorious spirit? What can</p>
<p>I say, that will enable you to understand the depth</p>
<p>of my sorrow? All that I should express would be</p>
<p>inadequate and feeble. My tears ﬂow; my mind is</p>
<p>overshadowed by a cloud of disappointment. But</p>
<p>I journey towards England, and I may there ﬁnd</p>
<p>consolation.</p>
<p>I am interrupted. What do these sounds por-</p>
<p>tend? It is midnight; the breeze blows fairly, and the</p>
<p>watch on deck scarcely stir. Again; there is a sound</p>
<p>as of a human voice, but hoarser; it comes from the</p>
<p>cabin where the remains of Frankenstein still lie. I</p>
<p>must arise, and examine. Good night, my sister.</p>
<p>Great God! what a scene has just taken place!</p>
<p>I am yet dizzy with the remembrance of it. I hardly</p>
<p>know whether I shall have the power to detail it; yet</p>
<p>the tale which I have recorded would be incomplete</p>
<p>without this ﬁnal and wonderful catastrophe.</p>
<p>I entered the cabin, where lay the remains of my</p>
<p>ill-fated and admirable friend. Over him hung a form</p>
<p>which I cannot ﬁnd words to describe; gigantic in</p>
<p>stature, yet uncouth and distorted in its proportions.</p>
<p>As he hung over the cofﬁn, his face was concealed</p>
<p>by long locks of ragged hair; but one vast hand</p>
<p>was extended, in colour and apparent texture like</p>
<p>that of a mummy. When he heard the sound of</p>
<p>my approach, he ceased to utter exclamations of</p>
<p>grief and horror, and sprung towards the window.</p>
<p>Never did I behold a vision so horrible as his face, of</p>
<p>such loathsome, yet appalling hideousness. I shut</p>
<p>my eyes involuntarily, and endeavoured to recollect</p>
<p>what were my duties with regard to this destroyer. I</p>
<p>called on him to stay.</p>
<p>He paused, looking on me with wonder; and,</p>
<p>again turning towards the lifeless form of his creator,</p>
<p>he seemed to forget my presence, and every fea-</p>
<p>ture and gesture seemed instigated by the wildest</p>
<p>rage of some uncontrollable passion.</p>
<p>“That is also my victim!” he exclaimed; “in his</p>
<p>murder my crimes are consummated; the miser-</p>
<p>able series of my being is wound to its close! Oh,</p>
<p>Frankenstein! generous and self-devoted being!</p>
<p>what does it avail that I now ask thee to pardon me?</p>
<p>I, who irretrievably destroyed thee by destroying all</p>
<p>thou lovedst. Alas! he is cold; he may not answer</p>
<p>me.”</p>
<p>His voice seemed suffocated; and my ﬁrst im-</p>
<p>pulses, which had suggested to me the duty of</p>
<p>obeying the dying request of my friend, in destroy-</p>
<p>ing his enemy, were now suspended by a mixture</p>
<p>of curiosity and compassion. I approached this</p>
<p>tremendous being; I dared not again raise my looks</p>
<p>upon his face, there was something so scaring and</p>
<p>unearthly in his ugliness. I attempted to speak, but</p>
<p>the words died away on my lips. The monster con-</p>
<p>tinued to utter wild and incoherent self-reproaches.</p>
<p>At length I gathered resolution to address him, in</p>
<p>a pause of the tempest of his passion: “Your re-</p>
<p>pentance,” I said, “is now superﬂuous. If you had</p>
<p>listened to the voice of conscience, and heeded</p>
<p>the stings of remorse, before you had urged your</p>
<p>diabolical vengeance to this extremity, Frankenstein</p>
<p>would yet have lived.”</p>
<p>“And do you dream?” said the dæmon; “do you</p>
<p>think that I was then dead to agony and remorse?</p>
<p>— He,” he continued, pointing to the corpse, “he</p>
<p>suffered not more in the consummation of the deed;</p>
<p>— oh! not the ten-thousandth portion of the anguish</p>
<p>that was mine during the lingering detail of its exe-</p>
<p>cution. A frightful selﬁshness hurried me on, while</p>
<p>my heart was poisoned with remorse. Think ye that</p>
<p>the groans of Clerval were music to my ears? My</p>
<p>heart was fashioned to be susceptible of love and</p>
<p>sympathy; and, when wrenched by misery to vice</p>
<p>and hatred, it did not endure the violence of the</p>
<p>change without torture such as you cannot even</p>
<p>imagine.</p>
<p>“After the murder of Clerval, I returned to</p>
<p>Switzerland, heart-broken and overcome. I pitied</p>
<p>Frankenstein; my pity amounted to horror: I ab-</p>
<p>horred myself. But when I discovered that he, the</p>
<p>author at once of my existence and of its unspeak-</p>
<p>able torments, dared to hope for happiness; that</p>
<p>while he accumulated wretchedness and despair</p>
<p>upon me, he sought his own enjoyment in feelings</p>
